The Eternity of Divine Speech: Three Arguments
Nasafi enumerates three arguments for the eternity of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments used by Nasafi to prove that divine speech is an eternal attribute and cannot be originated.

Core Idea
Divine speech cannot be an accident; it must be an eternal attribute of God.

Imam Nasafi presents three distinct arguments to demonstrate that God's speech cannot be a created, temporal phenomenon. Each argument eliminates possible alternatives, leading to the conclusion that speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. This lesson walks through these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical divisions Nasafi presents.
The passage contains three separate arguments.
2
Understand the first argument
Identify the three-part logical division (in God's essence, in another locus, or no locus) and why each possibility is impossible.
This argument eliminates any possible way for speech to be an accident.
3
Understand the second argument
Notice the disjunctive argument: either speech subsists in God or obligations become meaningless.
This shows that denying eternal speech leads to absurdity.
4
Understand the third argument
Consider the argument from deficiency: if not speaking eternally, God would be silent, which is a deficiency.
Deficiency cannot be attributed to God.
5
Connect to theology
Reflect on how these arguments establish that speech is an eternal attribute, not a created accident.
This is foundational for understanding the divine attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حادثا لكان الأمر لا يخلو إما أن يكون حادثا في ذات الله تعالى، وإما أن يكون حادثا في محل آخر سوى ذاته تعالى، وإما أن يكون حادثا لا في محل، ولا تصور لقسم رابع، وهذه الأقسام كلها ممتنعة، ويدلل على امتناع كل قسم منها. وبعد ذلك يقول : إما أن يقال إن كلام الله تعالى قائم بذاته، وهو متكلم آمر ناه، وإما أن ينكر أن يكون الله تعالى كلام وأمر ونهي، وبذلك تبطل فرضية العبادات، لانعدام أمره ونهيه. ويقول : إنه تعالى لو لم يكن في الأزل متكلما لكان موصوفا بضد من أضداد الكلام كالسكوت، واتصافه تعالى بذلك محال لأنه نقص، والنقص من أمارات الحدوث.
If it were originated, the matter would not be free from either being originated in the essence of God Most High, or being originated in another locus besides His essence Most High, or being originated not in a locus, and there is no conception of a fourth division. All these divisions are impossible, and he provides evidence for the impossibility of each division. After that he says: either it is said that the speech of God Most High subsists in His essence, and He is a speaker, commander, prohibitor; or it is denied that God Most High has speech, command, and prohibition, and thereby the obligation of acts of worship is invalidated due to the absence of His command and prohibition. And he says: if He Most High were not in pre-eternity a speaker, He would be described with an opposite of the opposites of speech, like silence, and His being described with that is impossible because it is deficiency, and deficiency is a sign of origination.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage contains three arguments demonstrating that divine speech cannot be originated and must be an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. First, Nasafi presents a logical division: if speech were originated (hadith), it would have to be either (1) an accident in God's essence, (2) an accident in another locus, or (3) an accident not in a locus. A fourth possibility is inconceivable. All three are impossible: the first is impossible because it would imply change in God; the second would make speech a property of another subject; the third is contradictory because accidents cannot exist without a locus. Thus, originating speech in God is impossible. Second, he gives a disjunctive argument: either we affirm that God's speech subsists in His essence, making Him a commanding and prohibiting speaker, or we deny that God has speech, command, and prohibition. Denial would nullify all religious obligations since there would be no divine command or prohibition, which is absurd. Therefore, speech must be an eternal attribute. Third, he argues that if God were not speaking from eternity, He would be described by the opposite of speech, such as silence. But silence is a deficiency, and deficiency is a sign of origination. Since God is beyond deficiency, He must be eternally speaking. These three arguments converge on the conclusion that God's speech is an uncreated, eternal attribute.

Key Terms

حادث

قائم بالذات

نقص
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that divine speech could be an accident (like human speech) without leading to absurdities.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if speech were an accident, it would have to be in God's essence (implying change), in another locus (making it not God's speech), or no locus (impossible). Therefore, speech cannot be an accident.

Checkpoint
What is the first logical division Nasafi uses to reject originated divine speech?
A) Speech must be either eternal or created, with no third option.
B) If speech were originated, it would have to be an accident in God's essence, or in another locus, or not in a locus, all of which are impossible.
C) Speech can only be an accident in a body, so it cannot be attributed to God.
D) Speech is either composed of letters or meanings; both are impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is not a created accident but an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is eternal and uncreated, then the Quran is not just a book but His very attribute. How does this change the way you approach recitation? Are you reciting with the awe that you are engaging with an eternal attribute of Allah? What would need to change in your attitude?
?
The argument from deficiency says that silence is a deficiency impossible for God. Where in your life are you silent about truth when you should speak? How does knowing that God is eternally speaking and commanding call you to be a speaker of truth?
?
If the absence of divine command would make worship invalid, then your acts of worship are only valid because God has eternally commanded them. Does your prayer feel like it is a response to an eternal command, or just a habit? How can you make it more conscious?

Rational Judgments

The Rational Method and the Core Argument for Vision
Nasafi presents his method and the rational argument for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's preference for rational proof and the core Maturidi argument that the cause of visibility is being qaim bi al-dhat.

Core Idea
The absolute cause of visibility is subsistence by oneself, not corporeality.

Nasafi begins by explaining his method: rational proof should precede transmitted evidence, but here he presented the latter first because it establishes both the fact and permissibility of vision. Then he lays out the rational argument: the Mu'tazila claim visibility requires a body, but the Maturidis say the cause is being self-subsistent. This foundational point sets up the entire debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about method
Notice Nasafi's remark about the order of proofs. He usually starts with reason, but here he used transmitted evidence first. Ask yourself why.
This reveals his pedagogical strategy.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the Mu'tazila claim (visibility requires corporeality) and the Maturidi counter (visibility requires qiyam bi al-dhat). Understand the logical connection.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since Allah is qaim bi al-dhat, the Maturidi conclusion is that He can be seen. Contrast with the Mu'tazila conclusion that He cannot.
This is the core of the rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَسْتَدِلُّ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ بِالْعَقْلِ فَيَقُولُ: كَانَ مِنَ الْأَجْدَى الِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالْعَقْلِ أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ تَأْيِيدُ ذَلِكَ بِالنَّقْلِ كَمَا هُوَ مَنْهَجُهُ - إِلَّا أَنَّهُ قَدَّمَ الْأَدِلَّةَ السَّمْعِيَّةَ لِمَا فِيهَا مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الرُّؤْيَةِ وَجَوَازِهَا جَمِيعًا. وَمِنْ أَدِلَّتِهِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ: أَنَّ الْخُصُومَ أَثْبَتُوا أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ الْجِسْمِيَّةُ، فَمَا كَانَ بِجِسْمٍ كَانَ مَرْئِيًّا، وَاللهُ لَيْسَ بِجِسْمٍ فَلَا يَكُونُ مَرْئِيًّا. أَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّسَفِيِّ فَإِنَّ أَصْحَابَهُ قَالُوا بِأَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ كَوْنُهُ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ، فَمَا كَانَ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ تَجُوزُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، وَمَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ يَسْتَحِيلُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى قَائِمٌ بِالذَّاتِ فَكَانَ جَائِزَ الرُّؤْيَةِ. وَيَشْرَحُ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ مُنَاقِشًا الْمُعْتَزِلَةَ فِي كَلَامِهِمْ. ثُمَّ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَقُولُ: نَسْأَلُ الْمُعْتَزِلَةَ لِمَ قُلْتُمْ: إِنَّ تَعَلُّقَ الرُّؤْيَةِ بِهَذِهِ الْأَجْنَاسِ تَعَلُّقُ الْأَحْكَامِ بِالْعِلَلِ، دُونَ كَوْنِ هَذِهِ الْأَجْنَاسِ مِنْ أَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ؟ وَيُطَالِبُونَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَمَا أَجَابُوا بِهِ يَهْدِمُ عَلَيْهِمْ قَوْلَهُمْ. ثُمَّ يَقُولُ: وَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ لَا نَدَّعِيَ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ الْقِيَامُ بِالذَّاتِ أَيْضًا، وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الْمَاتُرِيدِيُّ وَأَصْحَابُهُ كَانَ عَلَى طَرِيقَةِ الْمُسَامَحَةِ وَالْمُسَاهَلَةِ، وَلَا نَشْتَغِلُ بِادِّعَاءِ شَيْءٍ عِلَّةً، فَإِنَّهُمْ هُمُ الْمُدَّعُونَ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ مَا زَعَمُوا. فَلَا مَعْنَى لِلِاشْتِغَالِ بِمُعَارَضَةِ الْعِلَّةِ بِالْعِلَّةِ قَبْلَ الْعَجْزِ عَنْ مَنْعِ دَلِيلِ الْخُصُومِ وَهَدْمِهِ.
After that, he argues for the permissibility of vision by reason, saying: It would have been more suitable to argue by reason first and then support that by transmitted evidence, as is his method – except that he presented the transmitted evidences because they establish both the affirmation and permissibility of vision. And from his rational evidences for the permissibility of vision: that the opponents (al-khusum) established that the absolute cause of vision is corporeality (al-jismiyya); so whatever is a body is visible, and Allah is not a body, therefore He is not visible. However, according to al-Nasafi, his associates said that the absolute cause of vision is His being subsistent by Himself (qa'im bi al-dhat); so whatever is subsistent by itself, its vision is permissible, and whatever is not like that, its vision is impossible. And Allah (Exalted) is subsistent by Himself, so He is possible of vision. And he explains this statement discussing the Mu'tazila in their speech. Then after that he says: We ask the Mu'tazila: why did you say that the attachment of vision to these genera (ajnas) is the attachment of rulings to causes (ilal), rather than these genera being among the attributes of existence (sifat al-wujud)? And they demand proof, so whatever they answer will demolish their own statement. Then he says: And it is advisable that we should not claim that the absolute cause of vision is the subsistence by oneself (al-qiyam bi al-dhat) either, and what al-Maturidi and his associates mentioned was by way of concession and lenience. And we should not occupy ourselves with claiming something as a cause, for they (the opponents) are the ones who claim that the absolute cause of vision is what they alleged. So there is no meaning to occupying oneself with opposing a cause with a cause before being unable to prevent the proof of the opponents and demolishing it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the rational argument for the permissibility of seeing Allah. Nasafi begins by noting his methodological preference for rational proof first, but here he presented transmitted evidence first because it simultaneously establishes affirmation and permissibility. Then he presents the core rational argument: the Mu'tazila claim that the absolute cause of visibility is being a body; therefore God, not being a body, is not visible. But the Maturidis counter that the cause is being subsistent by oneself (qaim bi al-dhat). Since Allah is qaim bi al-dhat, He is possible of vision. Nasafi then explains that he discusses this with the Mu'tazila. Next, he challenges the Mu'tazila: why do they consider visibility to be attached to bodies as causal attributes (ilal) rather than as attributes of existence (sifat al-wujud)? He demands proof from them and claims any answer will demolish their position. Finally, Nasafi advises that we should not claim that the absolute cause is qiyam bi al-dhat either; the Maturidi position was a concession. The better approach is to not engage in asserting a cause at all, but rather to first refute the opponent's arguments.

Key Terms
علة مطلقة
جسمية
قائم بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi argument in this passage, what is the absolute cause of visibility?
A) Being a body (al-jismiyya)
B) Being subsistent by oneself (al-qiyam bi al-dhat)
C) Existence (al-wujud)
D) Being an accident (al-arad)

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi rational argument for the vision of Allah replaces the Mu'tazila's cause of corporeality with being qaim bi al-dhat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believed, like the Mu'tazila, that only bodies can be seen, how would that affect your expectation of seeing Allah in the afterlife? Does this passage challenge you to reconsider what it means to 'see' Allah without physical sight? How can you actively cultivate a longing for that vision in your daily life, even without knowing its modality?
?
The passage shows that the Maturidis shifted the cause from corporeality to self-subsistence. Reflect: In your own understanding of Allah, do you tend to limit Him by your own experiences (like the Mu'tazila did)? What specific assumption about Allah do you need to break today in order to draw closer to Him? Write one concrete step you will take to replace a limited perception with a more transcendent one.
?
The passage mentions that Nasafi's method is to use reason first, then support with transmitted evidence. But here he did the opposite. Does this make you think about the relationship between faith and reason in your own life? Are there times when you rely too much on one or the other? What is one area of your belief where you need to strengthen the harmony between reason and revelation? Commit to a specific action this week.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
4 min
Challenging the Mu'tazila: Cause vs. Attribute of Existence
Nasafi challenges the Mu'tazila's causal theory of visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's challenge to the Mu'tazila: why they consider visibility to be a causal attachment to bodies rather than an attribute of existence, and how this undermines their position.

Core Idea
Visibility is an attribute of existence, not a causal property of bodies.

After presenting the Maturidi alternative, Nasafi questions the Mu'tazila's foundational assumption. He asks: why do you treat visibility as a ruling attached to a cause (corporeality) rather than as a property of existence itself? This move shifts the burden of proof onto the Mu'tazila, demanding they justify their causal framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the challenging question
Find the sentence where Nasafi directly asks the Mu'tazila for their rationale. Notice the key terms: 'ta'alluq al-ahkam bi al-ilal' and 'sifat al-wujud'.
This is a critical dialectical move.
2
Understand the two frameworks
One framework sees visibility as a ruling linked to a cause (corporeality). The other sees it as an attribute of existence. Nasafi suggests the latter is more coherent.
3
Anticipate the Mu'tazila's dilemma
Nasafi claims that whatever they answer will demolish their own statement. Try to think why any answer would be problematic for them.
This is a classic refutation strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَسْتَدِلُّ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ بِالْعَقْلِ فَيَقُولُ: كَانَ مِنَ الْأَجْدَى الِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالْعَقْلِ أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ تَأْيِيدُ ذَلِكَ بِالنَّقْلِ كَمَا هُوَ مَنْهَجُهُ - إِلَّا أَنَّهُ قَدَّمَ الْأَدِلَّةَ السَّمْعِيَّةَ لِمَا فِيهَا مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الرُّؤْيَةِ وَجَوَازِهَا جَمِيعًا. وَمِنْ أَدِلَّتِهِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ: أَنَّ الْخُصُومَ أَثْبَتُوا أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ الْجِسْمِيَّةُ، فَمَا كَانَ بِجِسْمٍ كَانَ مَرْئِيًّا، وَاللهُ لَيْسَ بِجِسْمٍ فَلَا يَكُونُ مَرْئِيًّا. أَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّسَفِيِّ فَإِنَّ أَصْحَابَهُ قَالُوا بِأَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ كَوْنُهُ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ، فَمَا كَانَ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ تَجُوزُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، وَمَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ يَسْتَحِيلُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى قَائِمٌ بِالذَّاتِ فَكَانَ جَائِزَ الرُّؤْيَةِ. وَيَشْرَحُ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ مُنَاقِشًا الْمُعْتَزِلَةَ فِي كَلَامِهِمْ. ثُمَّ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَقُولُ: نَسْأَلُ الْمُعْتَزِلَةَ لِمَ قُلْتُمْ: إِنَّ تَعَلُّقَ الرُّؤْيَةِ بِهَذِهِ الْأَجْنَاسِ تَعَلُّقُ الْأَحْكَامِ بِالْعِلَلِ، دُونَ كَوْنِ هَذِهِ الْأَجْنَاسِ مِنْ أَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ؟ وَيُطَالِبُونَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَمَا أَجَابُوا بِهِ يَهْدِمُ عَلَيْهِمْ قَوْلَهُمْ. ثُمَّ يَقُولُ: وَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ لَا نَدَّعِيَ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ الْقِيَامُ بِالذَّاتِ أَيْضًا، وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الْمَاتُرِيدِيُّ وَأَصْحَابُهُ كَانَ عَلَى طَرِيقَةِ الْمُسَامَحَةِ وَالْمُسَاهَلَةِ، وَلَا نَشْتَغِلُ بِادِّعَاءِ شَيْءٍ عِلَّةً، فَإِنَّهُمْ هُمُ الْمُدَّعُونَ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ مَا زَعَمُوا. فَلَا مَعْنَى لِلِاشْتِغَالِ بِمُعَارَضَةِ الْعِلَّةِ بِالْعِلَّةِ قَبْلَ الْعَجْزِ عَنْ مَنْعِ دَلِيلِ الْخُصُومِ وَهَدْمِهِ.
After that, he argues for the permissibility of vision by reason, saying: It would have been more suitable to argue by reason first and then support that by transmitted evidence, as is his method – except that he presented the transmitted evidences because they establish both the affirmation and permissibility of vision. And from his rational evidences for the permissibility of vision: that the opponents (al-khusum) established that the absolute cause of vision is corporeality (al-jismiyya); so whatever is a body is visible, and Allah is not a body, therefore He is not visible. However, according to al-Nasafi, his associates said that the absolute cause of vision is His being subsistent by Himself (qa'im bi al-dhat); so whatever is subsistent by itself, its vision is permissible, and whatever is not like that, its vision is impossible. And Allah (Exalted) is subsistent by Himself, so He is possible of vision. And he explains this statement discussing the Mu'tazila in their speech. Then after that he says: We ask the Mu'tazila: why did you say that the attachment of vision to these genera (ajnas) is the attachment of rulings to causes (ilal), rather than these genera being among the attributes of existence (sifat al-wujud)? And they demand proof, so whatever they answer will demolish their own statement. Then he says: And it is advisable that we should not claim that the absolute cause of vision is the subsistence by oneself (al-qiyam bi al-dhat) either, and what al-Maturidi and his associates mentioned was by way of concession and lenience. And we should not occupy ourselves with claiming something as a cause, for they (the opponents) are the ones who claim that the absolute cause of vision is what they alleged. So there is no meaning to occupying oneself with opposing a cause with a cause before being unable to prevent the proof of the opponents and demolishing it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the rational argument for the permissibility of seeing Allah. Nasafi begins by noting his methodological preference for rational proof first, but here he presented transmitted evidence first because it simultaneously establishes affirmation and permissibility. Then he presents the core rational argument: the Mu'tazila claim that the absolute cause of visibility is being a body; therefore God, not being a body, is not visible. But the Maturidis counter that the cause is being subsistent by oneself (qaim bi al-dhat). Since Allah is qaim bi al-dhat, He is possible of vision. Nasafi then explains that he discusses this with the Mu'tazila. Next, he challenges the Mu'tazila: why do they consider visibility to be attached to bodies as causal attributes (ilal) rather than as attributes of existence (sifat al-wujud)? He demands proof from them and claims any answer will demolish their position. Finally, Nasafi advises that we should not claim that the absolute cause is qiyam bi al-dhat either; the Maturidi position was a concession. The better approach is to not engage in asserting a cause at all, but rather to first refute the opponent's arguments.

Key Terms
تعليق الأحكام بالعلل
أوصاف الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila assume without proof that visibility is causally linked to corporeality.
Correction: Nasafi challenges this assumption, suggesting visibility is an attribute of existence, thus not limited to bodies.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi ask the Mu'tazila to justify?
A) Why Allah cannot be seen
B) Why they consider visibility attached to bodies as causes rather than as attributes of existence
C) Why they accept transmitted evidence over reason
D) Why they believe in the vision of Allah

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by questioning their causal framework and demanding proof, turning the tables on them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah, do you demand clear proof from revelation and reason, or do you accept it as self-evident like the Mu'tazila? Recall a specific belief you hold about Allah—are you willing to scrutinize its foundation today?
?
Nasafi challenges the Mu'tazila to rethink their causal assumptions. Is there a way you attribute events in your life to secondary causes rather than to Allah's attribute of existence? Commit to reminding yourself daily that all things are from Allah's will and existence.
?
The Mu'tazila reduced visibility to a causal function. Do you ever reduce Allah's attributes—like His mercy or power—to mere functions rather than recognizing them as intrinsic to His being? How can you begin to see His attributes as realities, not just mechanisms?

Dialectical Caution: Refrain from Asserting a Cause
Nasafi advises against asserting a cause for visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's final methodological advice: not to assert any absolute cause for visibility, but to first refute the opponent's proof.

Core Idea
Do not claim a cause; refute the opponent's claim first.

After the rational argument and the challenge, Nasafi offers a key dialectical principle. He warns against asserting that qiyam bi al-dhat is the absolute cause, calling the Maturidi statement a concession. Instead, the proper method is to avoid occupying oneself with asserting a cause altogether and to focus on demolishing the opponent's proof. This is a sophisticated lesson in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the cautionary statement
Find where Nasafi says 'we should not claim that the absolute cause is qiyam bi al-dhat'. Understand why he says this.
He calls the earlier Maturidi statement a concession (musamaha).
2
Understand the dialectical principle
Nasafi advises not to engage in opposing a cause with another cause. Instead, first refute the opponent's proof. This is the stronger argumentative strategy.
3
Apply the lesson
Reflect on why this principle is important in theological debates. It prevents unnecessary commitments and focuses on weak points in the opponent's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَسْتَدِلُّ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ بِالْعَقْلِ فَيَقُولُ: كَانَ مِنَ الْأَجْدَى الِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالْعَقْلِ أَوَّلًا ثُمَّ تَأْيِيدُ ذَلِكَ بِالنَّقْلِ كَمَا هُوَ مَنْهَجُهُ - إِلَّا أَنَّهُ قَدَّمَ الْأَدِلَّةَ السَّمْعِيَّةَ لِمَا فِيهَا مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الرُّؤْيَةِ وَجَوَازِهَا جَمِيعًا. وَمِنْ أَدِلَّتِهِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ عَلَى جَوَازِ الرُّؤْيَةِ: أَنَّ الْخُصُومَ أَثْبَتُوا أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ الْجِسْمِيَّةُ، فَمَا كَانَ بِجِسْمٍ كَانَ مَرْئِيًّا، وَاللهُ لَيْسَ بِجِسْمٍ فَلَا يَكُونُ مَرْئِيًّا. أَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّسَفِيِّ فَإِنَّ أَصْحَابَهُ قَالُوا بِأَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ كَوْنُهُ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ، فَمَا كَانَ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ تَجُوزُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، وَمَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ يَسْتَحِيلُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى قَائِمٌ بِالذَّاتِ فَكَانَ جَائِزَ الرُّؤْيَةِ. وَيَشْرَحُ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ مُنَاقِشًا الْمُعْتَزِلَةَ فِي كَلَامِهِمْ. ثُمَّ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَقُولُ: نَسْأَلُ الْمُعْتَزِلَةَ لِمَ قُلْتُمْ: إِنَّ تَعَلُّقَ الرُّؤْيَةِ بِهَذِهِ الْأَجْنَاسِ تَعَلُّقُ الْأَحْكَامِ بِالْعِلَلِ، دُونَ كَوْنِ هَذِهِ الْأَجْنَاسِ مِنْ أَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ؟ وَيُطَالِبُونَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَمَا أَجَابُوا بِهِ يَهْدِمُ عَلَيْهِمْ قَوْلَهُمْ. ثُمَّ يَقُولُ: وَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ لَا نَدَّعِيَ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ هِيَ الْقِيَامُ بِالذَّاتِ أَيْضًا، وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الْمَاتُرِيدِيُّ وَأَصْحَابُهُ كَانَ عَلَى طَرِيقَةِ الْمُسَامَحَةِ وَالْمُسَاهَلَةِ، وَلَا نَشْتَغِلُ بِادِّعَاءِ شَيْءٍ عِلَّةً، فَإِنَّهُمْ هُمُ الْمُدَّعُونَ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ مَا زَعَمُوا. فَلَا مَعْنَى لِلِاشْتِغَالِ بِمُعَارَضَةِ الْعِلَّةِ بِالْعِلَّةِ قَبْلَ الْعَجْزِ عَنْ مَنْعِ دَلِيلِ الْخُصُومِ وَهَدْمِهِ.
After that, he argues for the permissibility of vision by reason, saying: It would have been more suitable to argue by reason first and then support that by transmitted evidence, as is his method – except that he presented the transmitted evidences because they establish both the affirmation and permissibility of vision. And from his rational evidences for the permissibility of vision: that the opponents (al-khusum) established that the absolute cause of vision is corporeality (al-jismiyya); so whatever is a body is visible, and Allah is not a body, therefore He is not visible. However, according to al-Nasafi, his associates said that the absolute cause of vision is His being subsistent by Himself (qa'im bi al-dhat); so whatever is subsistent by itself, its vision is permissible, and whatever is not like that, its vision is impossible. And Allah (Exalted) is subsistent by Himself, so He is possible of vision. And he explains this statement discussing the Mu'tazila in their speech. Then after that he says: We ask the Mu'tazila: why did you say that the attachment of vision to these genera (ajnas) is the attachment of rulings to causes (ilal), rather than these genera being among the attributes of existence (sifat al-wujud)? And they demand proof, so whatever they answer will demolish their own statement. Then he says: And it is advisable that we should not claim that the absolute cause of vision is the subsistence by oneself (al-qiyam bi al-dhat) either, and what al-Maturidi and his associates mentioned was by way of concession and lenience. And we should not occupy ourselves with claiming something as a cause, for they (the opponents) are the ones who claim that the absolute cause of vision is what they alleged. So there is no meaning to occupying oneself with opposing a cause with a cause before being unable to prevent the proof of the opponents and demolishing it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the rational argument for the permissibility of seeing Allah. Nasafi begins by noting his methodological preference for rational proof first, but here he presented transmitted evidence first because it simultaneously establishes affirmation and permissibility. Then he presents the core rational argument: the Mu'tazila claim that the absolute cause of visibility is being a body; therefore God, not being a body, is not visible. But the Maturidis counter that the cause is being subsistent by oneself (qaim bi al-dhat). Since Allah is qaim bi al-dhat, He is possible of vision. Nasafi then explains that he discusses this with the Mu'tazila. Next, he challenges the Mu'tazila: why do they consider visibility to be attached to bodies as causal attributes (ilal) rather than as attributes of existence (sifat al-wujud)? He demands proof from them and claims any answer will demolish their position. Finally, Nasafi advises that we should not claim that the absolute cause is qiyam bi al-dhat either; the Maturidi position was a concession. The better approach is to not engage in asserting a cause at all, but rather to first refute the opponent's arguments.

Sifting Attributes to Find the Cause of Vision
Nasafi's methodology for identifying the cause of visibility through attribute analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi systematically examines attributes to identify the essential cause that makes vision permissible, and why existence is the only attribute that transfers from the witnessed to the unseen.

Core Idea
To see the unseen, find the common cause that carries across.

Before we can argue that Allah (the unseen) can be seen, we need to determine what makes something visible in the witnessed world. Nasafi, after a long debate with Mu'tazila theologians, presents a rigorous method: we must sift through all attributes of visible objects to separate the essential cause from accidental attributes that happen to accompany them. The passage lists every possible candidate and eliminates all but one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the formal structure: an introduction about methodology, then a long list of attributes, then a conclusion.
The list is exhaustive—Nasafi wants to show he considered all possibilities.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where Nasafi distinguishes between 'attributes of cause' (sifat al-illa) and 'attributes of existence' (sifat al-wujud). This distinction is crucial.
Attributes of existence are accidental; they don't transfer the ruling.
3
Enumerate the attributes listed
Go through the list: body, accident, color, colored, self-subsistent, kawn, mutakawwin, known, masculine, originated, described, enduring, existent. Think about why each might be a candidate.
Some attributes are common to many things, others are specific.
4
Understand the rejection process
Nasafi says he discusses each aspect to reject all except existence. This is a process of elimination. Ask: why would existence be the only one that cannot be eliminated?
Because every visible object must exist, but other attributes are not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ كَلَامٍ طَوِيلٍ مَعَ الْجُبَّائِيِّ وَالنَّظَّامِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا مِنَ الْمُعْتَزِلَةِ يَقُولُ: لَا بُدَّ مِنْ سَيْرِ الْأَوْصَافِ لِيَتَبَيَّنَ لَنَا الْعِلَّةُ الْمُجَوِّزَةُ لِلرُّؤْيَةِ مِنَ الْأَوْصَافِ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ الْمُقْتَرِنَةِ بِهَا، وَالْمُسَمَّاةِ عِنْدَ الْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ بِأَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ، ثُمَّ تُعَدَّى الرُّؤْيَةُ بِتِلْكَ الْعِلَّةِ مِنَ الشَّاهِدِ إِلَى الْغَائِبِ إِذِ التَّعْدِيَةُ مِنَ الشَّاهِدِ إِلَى الْغَائِبِ تَكُونُ بِأَوْصَافِ الْعِلَّةِ دُونَ أَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ. ثُمَّ يَقُولُ: لَا يَخْلُو الْمَرْئِيُّ فِي الشَّاهِدِ مِنْ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَرْئِيًّا لِكَوْنِهِ جِسْمًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ عَرَضًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ لَوْنًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مُتَلَوِّنًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ قَائِمًا بِالذَّاتِ، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ كَوْنًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مُتَكَوِّنًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مَعْلُومًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مُذَكَّرًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مُحْدَثًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مَوْصُوفًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ بَاقِيًا، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ مَوْجُودًا. وَيُنَاقِشُ كُلَّ الْوُجُوهِ لِيَدْفَعَهَا كُلَّهَا مَاعَدَا الْوُجُودَ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى مَوْجُودٌ فَكَانَ جَائِزَ الرُّؤْيَةِ.
And after a long discussion with al-Jubba'i, al-Nazzam, and others from the Mu'tazila, he says: It is necessary to examine the attributes so that the cause that makes vision permissible is distinguished from the accidental attributes that are conjoined with it, which are called by the theologians 'attributes of existence' (sifat al-wujud). Then vision is transferred by that cause from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib), since transfer from the witnessed to the unseen is by the attributes of cause (sifat al-illa), not by attributes of existence. Then he says: The visible in the witnessed world is not free from being visible because it is a body, or because it is an accident, or because it is a color, or because it is colored, or because it is subsistent by itself, or because it is a 'kawn' (being/space), or because it is a 'mutakawwin' (generated), or because it is known, or because it is masculine, or because it is originated, or because it is described, or because it is enduring, or because it is existent. And he discusses each aspect to reject all of them except existence, and Allah (Exalted) is existent, so He is permissible of vision.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage discusses the methodology for identifying the true cause of visibility. Nasafi, after extensive debate with Mu'tazila leaders like al-Jubba'i and al-Nazzam, states that we must sift through attributes to distinguish the essential cause (al-illa al-mujawwiza) from accidental attributes (sifat al-wujud) that happen to accompany the visible object. The transfer (ta'diya) of the ruling of visibility from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) is performed by considering the essential cause, not the accidental attributes. Then he enumerates all possible attributes of visible things in the witness: body, accident, color, colored, self-subsistent, kawn, mutakawwin, known, masculine, originated, described, enduring, existent. He discusses each and rejects all except existence. Since Allah is existent, He is eligible to be seen.

Key Terms
أوصاف العلة (sifat al-illa)
أوصاف الوجود (sifat al-wujud)
تعدية (ta'diya)
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)
وجود (wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that existence itself is the direct efficient cause that makes something visible, like a physical property.
Correction: In kalam, existence is the common attribute that remains after eliminating all other candidates, but it is not an active cause; rather, it is the attribute that is not eliminated and thus serves as the basis for analogy (qiyas). The exact nature of the cause is that it is the attribute that is shared between witnessed and unseen, allowing transfer of the ruling.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which attribute of visible objects in the witness world is not eliminated as a possible cause for visibility?
A) Body
B) Accident
C) Subsistence by self
D) Existence

Key Takeaway
Through systematic elimination of possible attributes, Nasafi identifies existence as the only attribute that transfers visibility from the witnessed to the unseen, allowing the conclusion that Allah, being existent, can be seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi argues that existence itself is the cause of visibility. Reflect: Do you live as though God's existence is the most obvious reality, or do you need other signs to feel His presence? What habit could you change today to see Him more clearly?
?
The passage lists many attributes (color, shape, etc.) that are not necessary for visibility. In your own life, do you sometimes attach conditions to God's love or mercy, forgetting that His mere existence guarantees His attributes? How can you strip away those conditions?
?
If the logical proof that Allah can be seen is sound, what does that imply for your hope of meeting Him? Does this strengthen your longing, or does it remain an abstract concept? What concrete action will you take to prepare for that vision?

The Cause of Visibility is Existence
Nasafi enumerates possible causes of visibility and refutes all except existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi identifies existence (wujūd) as the sole cause of visibility and how this argument supports the possibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
Existence, not any other attribute, is the single cause that makes something visible.

In this passage, Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite denial of seeing God by methodically examining what attribute in created things permits visibility. After listing thirteen possibilities, he eliminates all but one: existence. Since God exists, it is rationally possible to see Him.

Guided Path
1
Understand the method of qiyās
Nasafi uses analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to the unseen. First, he searches for the cause ('illa) of visibility in created things.
2
Examine the list of possible causes
Read through the thirteen attributes Nasafi considers. Notice the pattern: each is a property that could be thought to cause visibility.
The commentary explains the distinction between essential attributes and accidental ones.
3
Identify the accepted cause
Nasafi rejects all except existence. Understand why existence is the only necessary and consistent cause for visibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد كلام طويل مع الجبائي والنظام وغيرهما من المعتزلة يقول: لابد من سير الأوصاف ليتبين لنا العلة المجوزة للرؤية من الأوصاف الاتفاقية المقترنة بها، والمسماة عند المتكلمين بأوصاف الوجود، ثم تُعدَّى الرؤية بتلك العلة من الشاهد إلى الغائب إذ التعدية من الشاهد إلى الغائب تكون بأوصاف العلة دون أوصاف الوجود. ثم يقول: لا يخلو المرئي في الشاهد من أن يكون مرئيا لكونه جسما، أو لكونه عرضا، أو لكونه لونا، أو لكونه متلونا، أو لكونه قائما بالذات، أو لكونه كونا، أو لكونه متكونا، أو لكونه معلوما، أو لكونه مذكرا، أو لكونه محدثا، أو لكونه موصوفا، أو لكونه باقيا، أو لكونه موجودا. ويناقش كل الوجوه ليدفعها كلها ماعدا الوجود، والله تعالى موجود فكان جائز الرؤية.
And after lengthy discussion with al-Jubba'i and al-Nazzam and others of the Mu'tazila, he says: It is necessary to examine the attributes to make clear to us the cause that permits vision from among the accidental attributes associated with it, which are called by the theologians 'attributes of existence', then vision is transferred by that cause from the witnessed to the unseen, since the transfer from the witnessed to the unseen is by the attributes of the cause, not the attributes of existence. Then he says: The seen object in the witnessed cannot be other than visible by virtue of being a body, or by virtue of being an accident, or by virtue of being a color, or by virtue of being colored, or by virtue of being subsisting by itself, or by virtue of being a mode, or by virtue of being a moded, or by virtue of being known, or by virtue of being masculine, or by virtue of being originated, or by virtue of being described, or by virtue of being permanent, or by virtue of being existent. And he discusses all the aspects to refute them all except existence, and God Most High is existent, so He is possible to see.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational proof for the possibility of seeing God. Nasafi begins by referencing a long debate with al-Jubba'i and al-Nazzam, leading Mu'tazilites who denied the vision of God. The method is to search for the attribute in the witnessed world that is the cause ('illa) permitting visibility. Among many attributes that seem to accompany visibility (such as being a body, accident, color, etc.), the theologians distinguish between essential attributes ('illī) and merely existential attributes (sifāt al-wujūd). Nasafi lists thirteen possible attributes that could be the cause. He then states that he refutes each except existence (wujūd). Since God is existent, He falls under the cause, and thus it is possible to see Him. The argument is a qiyās (analogy) from the witnessed to the unseen, a key method in kalām.

Key Terms
علة
وصف وجود
الشاهد
الغائب
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute common between created beings and God can be used to prove the possibility of seeing God.
Correction: Only attributes that are actual causes ('illa) of visibility in the witnessed world are valid; mere existence of a common attribute like 'being known' is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which attribute is the only common cause for visibility between created things and God?
A) Being a body
B) Being an accident
C) Existence
D) Being permanent

Key Takeaway
Existence (wujūd) is the only attribute that consistently causes visibility; since God exists, it is rationally possible to see Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that seeing Allah is rationally possible? If so, how does that belief shape your yearning to meet Him in the Hereafter?
?
Your own existence makes you visible to others. Have you ever reflected that your existence is a gift from Allah, and that He sees you at every moment? How does that awareness change your actions today?
?
If the only cause for visibility is mere existence, then nothing about your appearance, status, or deeds makes you more or less seen by Allah. How does this truth humble your pride and drive you to purify your intentions?

The Cause of Vision: Existence as the Basis
Nasafi summarises that the cause of vision is existence, and accompanying features are not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that existence alone is the cause of visibility, and that accompanying features like distance and facing are not necessary conditions.

Core Idea
Existence is the sole cause of visibility, not accidental features.

In this concluding passage, Imam Nasafi distills the core argument regarding the vision of God. He explains why existence—and not any physical condition—is the cause of visibility, and why we must rely on evidence rather than imagination. Let's unpack his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style: Nasafi packs multiple points into a single sentence.
2
Identify the two key moves
Find where Nasafi attaches vision to existence and then transfers that ruling to the unseen. These are the core logical steps.
3
Understand why accompanying features are dismissed
Nasafi lists separation, facing, and ray connection as attributes of existence in the witnessed world, but argues they are not necessary because they can be absent (as in God's seeing us).
This is the key point of departure from those who require physical conditions.
4
Reflect on evidence vs imagination
Nasafi acknowledges that our minds may not imagine vision without these features, but insists that evidence (dalīl) takes precedence over imagination (wahm). Ask yourself why this principle is important in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يلخص أساس الاختلاف في المسألة في العبارات التالية: "والحاصل أنا لما رأينا الرؤية متعلقة بتلك الأجناس، ولا وصف يجمعها يمكن تعليقها به إلا الوجود علقناها به لوجود دليل تعلقها به تعلق الأحكام بالعلل وعديناها إلى الغائب بتعدي الوجود، ودلتنا رؤيته تعالى إيانا على أن ما اقترن بالرؤية في الشاهد من المباينة والمقابلة، واتصال الشعاع وغير ذلك أوصاف وجود وليست بالقرائن اللازمة لها، لتحققها بدونها، فاتبعنا هذه الدلائل، وإن لم يقع في أوهامنا رؤية إلا بهذه القرائن، لما أن المعول على الدليل دون الوهم."
Then he summarises the basis of the disagreement in the issue in the following statements: "The upshot is that when we saw vision attached to those genera, and no attribute unites them can be attached to except existence, we attached it to it due to the existence of evidence of its attachment to it as the attachment of rulings to causes, and we transferred it to the unseen by the transfer of existence. And His seeing us, may He be exalted, indicated to us that what is associated with vision in the witnessed of separation (mubāyana), facing (muqābala), connection of a ray (ittiṣāl shu'ā'), and other things are attributes of existence and are not necessary concomitants of it, because it is realized without them. So we followed these evidences, even though vision does not occur in our minds except with these concomitants, because reliance is on evidence, not on imagination."
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This concluding passage encapsulates the entire argument about the vision of God. Nasafi restates the core method: we observe that vision in the witnessed world attaches to various kinds of objects (ajnās). The only attribute common to all is existence. Therefore, we conclude that existence is the cause ('illa) of visibility. We then transfer this ruling to the unseen (God) by analogy of existence. Additional features that often accompany vision in the witnessed world—such as separation (mubāyana), facing (muqābala), and ray connection (ittiṣāl shu'ā')—are not necessary conditions (qarā'in lāzima) because we know from God's seeing us that these features are not required. Even if our minds cannot imagine vision without them, we rely on evidence (dalīl), not on imagination (wahm).

Key Terms
علة
أجناس
الوجود
مباينة
مقابلة
اتصال الشعاع
قرائن
وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conditions like distance and facing are necessary for any vision to occur.
Correction: Nasafi argues that these are attributes of existence in the witnessed world, not necessary concomitants, because God's seeing us occurs without them.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the basis for transferring the ruling of visibility from the witnessed to the unseen?
A) The attribute of being a body
B) The attribute of existence
C) The attribute of being an accident
D) The attribute of being a color

Key Takeaway
The cause of visibility is existence alone; accidental features like distance and facing are not required for God's vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your relationship with Allah have you been letting your limited imagination (wahm) dictate what He can or cannot do, rather than submitting to the clear evidence (dalīl) from revelation? What would it cost you to let go of that assumption today?
?
You now know that Allah's vision of you does not depend on any physical conditions—He sees you even when you feel hidden or distant. If you truly lived knowing that His gaze is always upon you, what would you change about your thoughts, words, or actions starting now?
?
Nasafi says we rely on evidence, not imagination. Think of a belief or practice in your life that you hold onto simply because you cannot imagine otherwise, not because you have clear proof from the Qur'an or Sunnah. Are you willing to abandon it for the sake of truth? What is the first step?

The Four Conditions for Any Claim to Be Valid
Al-Nasafi enumerates four necessary conditions for a valid claim and applies them to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four necessary conditions that any claim must satisfy to be considered valid, and how they apply to the claim of prophethood.

Core Idea
A valid claim needs possibility, matching evidence, no grounds for rejection, and no contradiction.

Al-Nasafi presents a logical framework that any serious claim—especially the claim of prophethood—must pass. Before we can accept someone as a prophet, we must verify that their claim meets four rational conditions. This passage lays out those conditions in a clear, sequential manner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the four conditions listed after the initial statement.
The passage begins by stating that any claim necessitates four matters.
2
Identify each condition
Find and list the four conditions: possibility, matching evidence, absence of grounds for rejection, and freedom from contradiction.
Each condition is introduced with 'First', 'Second', etc.
3
Apply to prophethood
Consider how each condition is fulfilled in the claim of Prophet Muhammad. Why are these conditions essential for verifying prophethood?
This strengthens the rational basis for accepting prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يبين النسفي أن أي دعوى يلزمها أمور أربعة، وقد توفرت في رسالة سيدنا محمد عليه السلام. أولها - كون الدعوى في حيز الإمكان. وثانيها إتيان المدعى ببينة مطابقة للدعوى وثالثها . أن تكون دواعي الجرح منتفية عن بينته ورابعها . سلامة الدعوى والبينة عن مناقض يرد عليهما، أو على أحدهما.
Then al-Nasafi explains that any claim necessitates four matters, and they have been fulfilled in the mission of our master Muhammad, peace be upon him. First: the claim being within the realm of possibility. Second: the claimant bringing evidence that matches the claim. Third: that the grounds for rejection are absent from his evidence. Fourth: the safety of the claim and the evidence from a contradiction that refutes them both, or one of them.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the four necessary conditions that any valid claim (da'wa) must meet, applied here to the claim of prophethood. Al-Nasafi states that these conditions are all fulfilled in the case of Prophet Muhammad. The first condition is that the claim must be possible in itself—not impossible like claiming to be a god. The second is that the claimant must produce evidence (bayyina) that precisely matches his claim, not something unrelated. The third is that there must be no grounds for discrediting that evidence, such as inconsistency or unreliability. The fourth is that the claim and evidence must be free from any internal contradiction that would invalidate them. These four form a logical framework for testing any claim to prophethood.

Key Terms
دعوى
إمكان
بينة
دواعي الجرح
مناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four conditions are arbitrary rules invented by theologians.
Correction: They are a logical standard derived from rational necessity for any valid claim.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, which of the following is NOT one of the four conditions that any claim must fulfill?
A) The claim must be possible in itself.
B) The evidence must match the claim.
C) The claim must be made by a large group of people.
D) The claim and evidence must be free from contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The four conditions—possibility, matching evidence, no grounds for rejection, and no contradiction—provide a rational test for any claim, especially the prophethood claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last heard a claim about Islam (from a scholar, a friend, or an online source), did you consciously check its possibility, evidence, absence of grounds for rejection, and consistency? If not, what will you do differently starting today?
?
Think of a belief you hold that may not pass this four-condition test. Are you willing to examine it honestly and, if needed, abandon it? What is one specific step you can take this week to apply this test to your own beliefs?
?
If you knew that applying these four conditions to every claim you accept would cost you a cherished assumption, would you still do it? What would that cost, and are you ready to pay it for the sake of truth?

The Cause-Effect Argument Against Precedence of Istita'a
Al-Nasafi presents the cause-effect argument and refutes the Mu'tazila on precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's rational argument that the relationship between istita'a (power) and the act is like cause and effect, making precedence impossible.

Core Idea
Cause and effect must coexist; power cannot precede action.

Al-Nasafi begins his refutation by establishing a foundational principle: istita'a relates to the act as a cause relates to its effect. Just as a cause cannot exist without its effect, it is impossible for power to precede the action. In this passage, he also critiques the Mu'tazila who claim otherwise, showing their inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Nasafi presents the cause-effect analogy and then uses it to reject the views of Abu Hashim and the Mu'tazila.
The passage also mentions the impossibility of accidents persisting, which supports the argument.
2
Understand the cause-effect analogy
Al-Nasafi says istita'a runs with the act as the cause ('illa) runs with the effect (ma'lul). He argues that it is impossible for a cause to be devoid of its effect.
This is a rational principle derived from observation and theological reasoning.
3
Examine the critique of Abu Hashim
Abu Hashim claimed the cause (power) can be devoid of the effect forever. The Mu'tazila (his followers) say for one moment. Al-Nasafi declares both false.
This critique strengthens the necessity of simultaneity.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This cause-effect reasoning is a key pillar in al-Nasafi's overall case against the precedence of istita'a. It shows that power must accompany the act, not precede it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يبدأ النسفي في مناقشة الخصوم مناقشة تبطل آراءهم، وتبين أن فهمهم للآيات قاصر، وأن شبههم العقلية تدل على تناقضهم، وأن الأعراض يستحيل بقاؤها ، وأن الاستطاعة تجري مع الفعل مجرى العلة من المعلول، وخلو العلة عن المعلول ممتنع. وأبو هاشم يقول بخلوها عنه أبد الدهر ، أما أهل نحلته من المعتزلة فيقولون بخلوها عنه زمانا واحدا، وكل ذلك باطل، ويقول النسفي: إن المحققين من أئمته أبوا تقدم الاستطاعة على الأفعال، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول زمانا، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ، ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ذلك إلى وقد اجتمع أهل القبلة على سؤال الله المعونة والتوفيق في كل لحظة، ولو كانت قدرة الطاعة معطاة للإنسان قبل ذلك لم يكن للطلب معنى، وأدى بقاء العرض. ويبين أدلته بالتفصيل على وجوب مقارنة الاستطاعة للفعل، وليس قبله، ويقول : إن القول بتقدم القدرة على الفعل يوجب استغناء العبد عن الله وقت الفعل، والقول به كفر، وقد تعرض الشيخ أبو منصور لهذا في حديث طويل ملخصه أن إعطاء القدرة قبل الفعل خروج عن الحكمة، ولا يليق ذلك بحكمة الله. وقد تعلق المعتزلة بآيات تثبت سبق القدرة للفعل، ويقول النسفي في ذاك: إن المقصود بهذه الآيات الاستطاعة الأولى التي هي سلامة الأسباب والآلات وهو متفق معهم في ذلك، أما ما عدا ذلك فلا.
Then al-Nasafi begins to discuss the opponents in a discussion that invalidates their views, shows that their understanding of the verses is deficient, that their rational specious arguments indicate their contradiction, that accidents (a'rad) cannot persist, and that istita'a relates to the act as the cause (illa) relates to the effect (ma'lul), and it is impossible for the cause to be devoid of the effect. Abu Hashim says it is devoid of it forever, while the people of his sect from the Mu'tazila say it is devoid of it for one moment, and all that is false. Al-Nasafi says: The verified scholars from his imams refused the precedence of istita'a over acts, because it involves saying that the cause is devoid of the effect for a period of time, and likewise they stated the concomitance of effects with their causes and did not permit the precedence of causes over them. Moreover, the people of the Qibla have agreed on asking Allah for help and success (tawfiq) at every moment; if the power of obedience were given to man before that, the request would have no meaning, and it would lead to the persistence of the accident. And he explains his proofs in detail on the necessity of the concomitance of istita'a with the act, and not before it, and he says: The statement that power precedes the act necessitates the servant's independence from Allah at the time of the act, and holding that statement is unbelief (kufr). And Sheikh Abu Mansur addressed this in a long speech whose summary is that giving power before the act is a departure from wisdom, and that is not befitting the wisdom of Allah. And the Mu'tazila have clung to verses that prove the precedence of power over the act, and al-Nasafi says about that: The intended meaning of these verses is the first istita'a which is safety of causes and instruments, and he agrees with them on that; as for other than that, no.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation (objection-and-answer) of those who claim istita'a (qudra) precedes the act. It begins by summarizing al-Nasafi's approach: he invalidates their views, showing their misunderstanding of verses and rational inconsistencies. He then gives several arguments: (1) Accidents (a'rad) cannot persist, so if power preceded, it would have to persist until the act, which is impossible. (2) The relation of istita'a to the act is like cause to effect; a cause cannot be devoid of its effect. He critiques Abu Hashim (who says power can be devoid of effect forever) and other Mu'tazila (who say for one moment). (3) The consensus of all Muslims (ahl al-qibla) is to ask Allah for tawfiq (divine facilitation) at every moment; if power were already given, that request would be meaningless. (4) Claiming precedence implies the servant is independent of Allah at the moment of action, which is kufr. He then cites Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's view that giving power before the act is against divine wisdom. Finally, he addresses the Mu'tazila's proof-texts from the Quran, explaining that those verses refer to the first type of istita'a (safety of means), which he agrees precedes the act; they do not apply to power (qudra).

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that power can be given to a person before the act, like a stored capacity.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that if power preceded, the cause (power) would exist without its effect (act), which is rational impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the relationship between istita'a and the act?
A) Istita'a precedes the act.
B) Istita'a is like a cause and the act is its effect, so they must coexist.
C) Istita'a follows the act.
D) There is no relationship; acts are independent.

Key Takeaway
The cause-effect relationship between istita'a and the act necessitates their simultaneity; precedence would violate rational principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume you have the ability to act but delay obeying Allah, as if your power can exist without its related deed? How does the cause-effect simultaneity expose that assumption as an illusion?
?
When you make plans and say 'I will do it tomorrow,' are you not presuming that your istita'a (capacity) can precede your action? What change must you make in your view of time and ability?
?
If your ability to act is inseparable from the act itself in the sight of Allah, how should that reshape your prayer when you ask for strength to do good?

The Consensus of Asking for Tawfiq and the Implication of Kufr
Al-Nasafi presents arguments from consensus (tawfiq), implication of kufr, and scriptural reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's arguments from the universal practice of asking Allah for tawfiq and the charge that precedence leads to kufr, as well as his interpretation of the Quranic verses used by the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Asking Allah for help disproves that power is already possessed; precedence implies independence and is kufr.

Al-Nasafi presents two further arguments: First, the consensus of all Muslims to ask Allah for tawfiq at every moment shows that power must be continuously granted, not given beforehand. Second, if power preceded, the servant would be independent of Allah at the moment of action—a belief that constitutes kufr. Finally, he addresses the Mu'tazila proof-texts from the Quran, explaining they refer to a different kind of istita'a.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how al-Nasafi moves from the practice of supplication to the theological charge of kufr, and then to scriptural interpretation.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the argument from tawfiq
All Muslims ask Allah for help and success (tawfiq) at every moment. If power for obedience were already given, this request would be meaningless. Also, it would imply the persistence of an accident (power), which is impossible.
This is a practical consensus-based argument.
3
Examine the charge of kufr
Saying that power precedes the act means the servant does not need Allah at the time of the act. Al-Nasafi declares this belief as kufr (unbelief).
This shows the gravity of the issue.
4
Analyze the scriptural reconciliation
The Mu'tazila cite verses to prove precedence. Al-Nasafi explains that those verses refer to the first istita'a—safety of causes and instruments—which he agrees precedes the act. The contested power (qudra) is different.
This defuses their proof-texts.

What does al-Nasafi say about the Mu'tazila's proof-texts from the Quran?

Key Takeaway
The consensus of asking for tawfiq and the charge of kufr reinforce that istita'a must accompany the act, and the Quranic verses cited by opponents are misinterpreted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for tawfīq in your daily prayers, do you truly believe that without His help you cannot even utter a single word of obedience? Or do you say it mechanically while feeling self-sufficient inside? Today, before each action, pause and say 'Lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh' with the conviction that your power to act is created by Allah at that very moment.
?
Al-Nasafī declared that believing power precedes the act leads to kufr because it implies independence from Allah. Search your heart: are there areas of your life—work, study, relationships—where you rely on your own abilities and credit yourself for success? Repent of that hidden independence and commit to attributing every achievement to Allah's creation of your ability at that instant.
?
The Muʿtazila twisted Quranic verses to support their view, but al-Nasafī humbly reinterpreted them under the guidance of the Salaf. Have you ever twisted a verse of the Quran to justify a sin or a comfortable belief? Will you now submit your understanding to the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna, even if it humbles you? Resolve to seek knowledge with a heart ready to accept the truth, not to defend your pre-existing opinions.

Three Proofs That Power Is Valid for Opposites
Nasafi gives three rational proofs that qudra is valid for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs al-Nasafi provides to establish that qudra (power) is valid for opposites.

Core Idea
The same power can produce opposite acts — otherwise, human responsibility would collapse.

Al-Nasafi tackles a central question in Islamic theology: Does the power (qudra) given by Allah to humans enable them to perform both obedience and sin? He answers with three rational proofs that it does. This lesson walks through those proofs and the opposing view, helping you grasp the logic behind human moral accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a question-answer format.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage presents three rational proofs. Find each one and note the analogy or logical structure used.
Proof 1: tongue analogy. Proof 2: taklif ma la yutaq. Proof 3: snow analogy.
3
Understand the opposing view
After presenting his proofs, Nasafi mentions the opposing argument that power for obedience is tawfiq and for sin is khidhlan. Note how he treats it fairly.
This sets up a dialectic that will be discussed further.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If power were not valid for opposites, what would that mean for human choice and divine justice? How do these proofs support moral responsibility?
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل النسفي هل تصلح القدرة للضدين؟ ويجيب بأن الدلائل التي تثبت أن القدرة صالحة للضدين منها: إن كل سبب من أسباب الفعل يصلح للضدين كاللسان يصلح للصدق والكذب. ومنها : أن القدرة لو لم تصلح للضدين لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق. ومنها أن كل ما يحصل به شيء، ولا يصلح لضده يكون الحاصل به بالطبع لا بالاختيار كالثلج الذي يحصل به التبريد لطبعه، وذلك قول بالاضطرار. أما القائلون بأنها لا تصلح للضدين، وأن قوة الطاعة غير قوة المعصية – فيستدلون بأن قوة الطاعة هي التوفيق، وقوة المعصية هي الخذلان، ويعرض كلامهم بأمانته ويناقشهم.
And al-Nasafi asks: Is power (qudra) valid for both opposites? And he answers that the proofs that establish that power is valid for opposites include: First, every cause of an act is valid for opposites, like the tongue is valid for truth and falsehood. Second, if power were not valid for opposites, it would involve imposing an unbearable obligation (taklif ma la yutaq). Third, everything by which a thing is obtained, and which is not valid for its opposite, produces what it does by nature, not by choice, like snow which produces cooling by its nature; and that is a statement of compulsion. As for those who say that it is not valid for opposites, and that the power for obedience is different from the power for sin, they argue that the power for obedience is divine success (tawfiq) and the power for sin is abandonment (khidhlan); and he presents their words with fairness and discusses them.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a proof passage where al-Nasafi answers the question of whether qudra (power) is valid for both opposites. He provides three proofs: (1) Analogy from everyday causes: each instrument or cause is fit for opposites, e.g., the tongue can speak truth or falsehood. (2) If power were not valid for opposites, then when God commands an act, the person would lack the power for its opposite, yet the opposite is also possible; this would lead to taklif ma la yutaq (imposing an unbearable obligation), which is impossible for God. (3) If something produces only one effect and is incapable of its opposite, that production is by nature, not by choice — like snow naturally cools; if human acts were like that, it would imply compulsion (jahr), contradictory to moral responsibility. After presenting these proofs, al-Nasafi acknowledges the opposing view: some say the power for obedience is tawfiq and the power for sin is khidhlan (divine abandonment). He commits to presenting their arguments fairly and then discussing them, indicating a balanced scholarly approach.

Key Terms
القدرة
الضدين
تكليف ما لا يطاق
بالطبع
بالاختيار
التوفيق
الخذلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if power is valid for opposites, then one can commit sin with the same power given for obedience, but this passage argues that the same power can be used for either, not that it is predestined for one.
Correction: Nasafi's proofs show that power is neutral and can be directed to either obedience or sin by human choice, which is necessary for moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the proofs al-Nasafi gives for the validity of qudra for opposites?
A) Every cause is valid for opposites, like the tongue.
B) Without it, taklif ma la yutaq would occur.
C) Snow naturally cools, so human acts must also be natural.
D) The power for obedience is tawfiq and for sin is khidhlan.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi proves that qudra is valid for opposites through everyday examples, logical consequence, and the distinction between natural compulsion and free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent moment when you faced a choice between truth and falsehood, using your tongue. Did you act as if your power was only for one? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you truly believed that the same power you have now could be used for its opposite – and you chose wrongly? Will you ask Him to help you use that power for what pleases Him today?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that if power were not valid for opposites, you would be compelled like snow cooling. But you are not. So when you sin, you cannot blame compulsion. What is one area of your life where you have been acting as if you are compelled, when in fact you have the power to choose otherwise? What is one concrete step you will take this week to exercise that power for obedience?

Capacity for Opposites: Nasafi's Three Proofs
Nasafi gives three proofs for capacity's suitability for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's rational arguments that human capacity must be suitable for both obedience and disobedience, and the implications for moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Capacity must serve opposites; otherwise, acts become compelled and obligations unjust.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi tackles a central question in Maturidi theology: can the same human capacity (qudra) be used for both good and evil? He answers with three clear proofs, drawing on analogies, the impossibility of unbearable obligation, and the distinction between natural and voluntary action. Understanding this teaches us why humans are truly responsible for their choices.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, noting key terms like القدرة, الضدين, and تكليف ما لا يطاق. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow.
Pay attention to how Nasafi structures his answer: a question, then proofs, then the opposing view.
2
Identify the three proofs
List the three arguments Nasafi gives for capacity being suitable for opposites. Notice the analogies: the tongue (proof 1), the burden of impossible command (proof 2), and the natural vs. chosen act (proof 3).
Each proof targets a different aspect: creation, obligation, and free will.
3
Understand the opposing view
Read the part where Nasafi presents the opponents' claim that the power for obedience (tawfiq) and for disobedience (khidhlan) are distinct. Note that he promises to discuss this fairly.
This sets up a dialectic—Nasafi is not merely stating his own view but engaging with others.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: if capacity were not for both opposites, then God's commands would be impossible to fulfill, or our actions would be forced like natural phenomena. That would undermine moral accountability.
This connects to broader issues of justice and free will in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل النسفي هل تصلح القدرة للضدين؟ ويجيب بأن الدلائل التي تثبت أن القدرة صالحة للضدين منها: إن كل سبب من أسباب الفعل يصلح للضدين كاللسان يصلح للصدق والكذب. ومنها : أن القدرة لو لم تصلح للضدين لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق. ومنها أن كل ما يحصل به شيء، ولا يصلح لضده يكون الحاصل به بالطبع لا بالاختيار كالثلج الذي يحصل به التبريد لطبعه، وذلك قول بالاضطرار. أما القائلون بأنها لا تصلح للضدين، وأن قوة الطاعة غير قوة المعصية – فيستدلون بأن قوة الطاعة هي التوفيق، وقوة المعصية هي الخذلان، ويعرض كلامهم بأمانته ويناقشهم.
And al-Nasafi asks: Is capacity suitable for both opposites? And he answers: The evidences that prove that capacity is suitable for both opposites include: that every cause of an act is suitable for both opposites, like the tongue is suitable for truth and falsehood. And among them: that if capacity were not suitable for both opposites, it would involve the imposition of what is beyond capacity (taklif ma la yutaq). And among them: that everything by which a thing is obtained, and which is not suitable for its opposite, its obtainment is by nature not by choice, like snow by which cooling is obtained due to its nature, and that is a saying of compulsion (idtirar). As for those who say that it is not suitable for both opposites, and that the power of obedience is different from the power of disobedience – they argue that the power of obedience is tawfiq (divine assistance), and the power of disobedience is khidhlan (divine abandonment). And he presents their speech with honesty and discusses it.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents Nasafi's treatment of the question whether human capacity (qudra) can serve both opposites (al-diddan). He first answers affirmatively and gives three proofs. First, the analogy of causes: every cause of an act is suitable for opposites (e.g., the tongue can speak truth or falsehood). Second, if capacity were not suitable for both, then commanding both obedience and forbidding disobedience would impose what is beyond capacity (taklif ma la yutaq), which is impossible. Third, if something produces an effect and cannot produce its opposite, the effect comes by nature not by choice (e.g., snow cools by nature), implying compulsion. Nasafi then presents the opposing view: those who say capacity is not for both, and that the power for obedience is tawfiq and for disobedience is khidhlan. He states he will present their argument honestly and discuss it.

تكليف ما لا يطاق
الطبع
الاختيار
التوفيق
الخذلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if capacity were for both opposites, then God would be unjust in commanding obedience while forbidding disobedience, because the same power would be used for both.
Correction: Nasafi shows the opposite: if capacity were NOT for both opposites, then God's commands would be impossible to fulfill, which would be truly unjust. Capacity serving opposites is necessary for moral responsibility.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that human capacity must be suitable for both opposites to preserve divine justice and human free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Nasafi's second proof—that without the capacity for both, commanding would be impossible—affect your understanding of that moment? What does that mean for your accountability before God?
?
Your tongue can speak truth or falsehood. Right now, think of a recent conversation where you chose one or the other. Did you rely on the same capacity for both? What does Nasafi's analogy teach you about your responsibility? Will you use your tongue differently after this lesson?

The third proof says that if we did not have capacity for both, our actions would be like snow's cooling—compelled. Al-Nasafi's Refutation of Jabriyya: Three Proofs and the Mu'tazili Doubt
Al-Nasafi refutes Jabriyya by three proofs and presents Mu'tazili objections in verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the Jabriyya using revelation, reason, and necessity, and you will see the Mu'tazili doubt about divine and human agency.

Core Idea
Human actions require both divine creation and human acquisition—neither Jabriyya nor Mu'tazila have the full picture.

This passage shows the methodical way al-Nasafi dismantles the Jabriyya position. He first outlines three sources of evidence—revelation, reason, and necessity—that prove human responsibility. Then he presents the Mu'tazili doubt in poetic form, which he plans to refute. This sets up the central debate about how divine power and human choice relate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note the three types of evidence al-Nasafi uses: revelation, reason, and necessity. Think about why each is important.
These three proofs are a standard method in Maturidi theology.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili poet's argument
The poet presents three logical possibilities for actions we are blamed for: they are solely from us, solely from Allah, or shared. Consider the implications of each.
The poet seems to argue that blame is impossible in any case, but al-Nasafi will refute this.
3
Notice the witnessed example
The Mu'tazila argue that in the witnessed world, one act cannot be caused by two agents. This is a key point for their denial of divine involvement in human actions.
This is called the 'impossibility of one maqdur under two qudras'.
4
Prepare for al-Nasafi's response
Ask yourself: How might al-Nasafi respond to these Mu'tazili doubts? Keep in mind his three proofs and the Maturidi concept of acquisition (kasb).
This prepares you for the next part of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يبين وجوه بطلان قول الجبرية بدلالة السمع، والعقل، والضرورة، ويعرض شبه المعتزلة، ويردها، وهي الشبه التي يلخص بعضها الشاعر المعتزلي في الأبيات التالية: لا تعدوا أفعالنا التي نذم بها إحدى ثلاث خصال حين نحصيها إن كان يلحقنا من لائم فيها فيسقط اللوم عنا حين نأتيها ـرد مولانــــ بصنعته أو كان يشركنا فاللوم يلحقه ولَم يكن للآلة في خليقته صنع فما الذنب إلا ذنب جانيها الشاهد. وكذلك هم يقولون باستحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين كما في الشاهد.
Then he shows the aspects of the invalidation of the statement of the Jabriyya by the indication of revelation (al-sam'), reason (al-'aql), and necessity (al-darura). And he presents the doubts (shubah) of the Mu'tazila and refutes them. And these are the doubts which the Mu'tazili poet summarized in the following verses: 'Do not count our actions for which we are blamed [as] one of three characteristics when we enumerate them: If we are followed by a blamer in them, then blame falls away from us when we perform them, [or] ... by His making, or He partners us, then the blame attaches to Him, and if the instrument has no making in its creation, then what is the sin except the sin of its perpetrator?' The witnessed example. Similarly, they say: The impossibility of a single maqdur falling under the power of two agents, as in the witnessed example.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage describes al-Nasafi's refutation of the Jabriyya using three types of evidence: revelation (sam'), reason ('aql), and necessity (darura). He then presents the doubts (shubah) of the Mu'tazila, which he refutes. A Mu'tazili poet summarized these doubts in verses: the actions we are blamed for must be either entirely from us, entirely from Allah, or shared. In each case, the attribution of blame becomes problematic. The Mu'tazila also argue that it is impossible for one act to be under the power of two agents, as is seen in the witnessed world. Al-Nasafi will later refute this.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili verses are the final truth about human action.
Correction: The verses are the doubts of the Mu'tazila, which al-Nasafi will refute. They are not al-Nasafi's own position.
