Rational Proof: No Creative Power for Other Than Allah and Two Powers on One Act
Al-Nasafi demonstrates that only Allah has creative power, and two powers can relate to one act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's rational argument that only Allah has creative power, the servant's act is not compelled, and a single act can be under two powers from different aspects.

Core Idea
Only Allah creates; human acts are acquired, not compelled, and one act can involve both divine and human agency.

In this passage, al-Nasafi refutes Mu'tazili rational objections by clarifying key concepts. He distinguishes between creating (takhliq) and acting (fi'l), showing that the servant's act is not forced. He also argues that it is possible for one act to be under two powers when considered from different aspects. This lesson unpacks these dense arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the text speak first.
Notice the concise style of al-Nasafi; each clause introduces a key claim.
2
Identify the main claims
List the distinct claims: (1) creative power impossible for others, (2) servant has act but not creative power, (3) not compelled, (4) one act under two powers possible from two aspects, (5) bringing evil into existence is not evil, (6) reality of shirk.
This passage packs multiple refutations into a single sentence flow.
3
Understand the takhliq-fi'l distinction
Focus on the difference between creative power (takhliq) and mere action (fi'l). The servant acts but does not create.
This distinction is crucial for the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on kasb (acquisition).
4
Analyze the dual agency concept
How can one act be under two powers? Al-Nasafi says it is possible from two different aspects (e.g., Allah as Creator, servant as acquirer).
This refutes the Mu'tazili claim of impossibility of two powers on one act.
5
Reflect on implications for evil and shirk
Consider why bringing evil into existence is not evil (it depends on the doer) and that shirk is attributing creative power to other than Allah.
These points complete the refutation and reaffirm tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رده على شبههم العقلية يثبت استحالة قدرة التخليق لغير الله، ويبين أن العبد له فعل، وإن لم يكن له قدرة التخليق، وليس بمضطر فيه، وأن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين من جملة المجوزات دون الممتنعات وذلك من جهتين مختلفتين. ويبين أن إيجاد القبيح ليس بقبيح، ويذكر حقيقة الشرك.
And in his refutation of their rational doubts (shubah al-'aqliyya), he establishes the impossibility of the power of creation (qudrat al-takhliq) for other than Allah, and shows that the servant has an act (fi'l) even though he does not have the power of creation, and he is not compelled (mudtarr) in it, and that a single maqdur falling under the power of two agents is among the possible (al-mujawwizat) things, not the impossible (al-mumtani'at), and that from two different aspects. And he shows that bringing evil into existence (ijad al-qabih) is not evil (qabih), and he mentions the reality of shirk.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
In this passage, al-Nasafi provides a rational refutation of the Mu'tazili doubts. He establishes that creative power (takhliq) is impossible for anyone other than Allah. The servant does have an act (fi'l) but it is not creation, and he is not compelled in it. Then he argues against the Mu'tazili claim that one action cannot be under two powers; rather, it is among the possible things (al-mujawwizat) and not impossible, especially when considered from two different aspects (e.g., from Allah as Creator and from the servant as acquirer). He also explains that bringing evil into existence (by Allah) is not itself evil; evil is relative to the doer. And he mentions the reality of shirk (associating partners with Allah), which is to attribute creative power to others.

Key Terms
قدرة التخليق
فعل
مضطر
مقدور
جهتين
إيجاد القبيح
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the servant has an act, he must have creative power.
Correction: Al-Nasafi distinguishes between having an act (fi'l) and having creative power (takhliq); the servant has an act but no creative power.

Checkpoint

B) Yes, it is possible from two different aspects

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi affirms that only Allah creates, the servant acts without compulsion, and one act can involve two agents from different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that only Allah creates, but do you live as if your own efforts produce nothing? Can you honestly say that you attribute every good in your life solely to Allah's creative power, even when you worked hard for it? What would change if you truly internalized that your act is acquired, not created by you?
?

?
The text defines shirk as attributing creative power to others. Examine your heart: in what subtle ways do you rely on created things as if they have independent power over your life? Is there any area where you act as if your wealth, your health, or your relationships are self-generated?

One Act Under Two Powers: The Rational Possibility
Al-Nasafi establishes the permissibility of one act under two powers via invention and acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidis argue that a single act can be subject to both God's creative power and the servant's acquired power.

Core Idea
God creates the act; the servant acquires it—two powers, one act, no contradiction.

Al-Nasafi confronts a key objection: if God is the sole creator, how can humans have real power? He argues that after proving the impossibility of human creation, we must still affirm human power over action. The solution is that one act can fall under two powers in different respects: God invents, the servant acquires.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the proof
Read the Arabic passage and identify the proof that creative power is impossible for the servant.
This is a crucial premise.
2
Notice the agreement with Mu'tazila
The author agrees with the Mu'tazila that two powers cannot combine from the aspect of invention.
3
Understand the point of disagreement
The Maturidi claim is that two powers can combine on the same act from different aspects: invention vs acquisition.
This is the core insight.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the servant is not compelled and has a real action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت بالدليل استحالة قدرة التخليق للعبد ، وثبت أن له القدرة على الفعل عرف جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين وهو يسلم مع المعتزلة أن قدرتين لا تجتمعان على المقدور الواحد من جهة الاختراع، وكذا من جهة الاكتساب أما منشأ الخلاف فهو تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد على جهة الاختراع والاكتساب فإن قدرة الله على المقدور هي الاختراع، وقدرة العبد على نفس المقدور هي الاكتساب. وبهذا يثبت أن العبد ليس بمجبور، وأن له فعلا، وأن قدرته متعلقة بمقدوره وهو فعله، ويفرق بين الكسب والخلق فيقول : كل مقدور وقع في محل قدرته فهو كسب، وما وقع لا في محل قدرته فهو خلق، واسم الفعل يشملهما، وقيل: ما وقع بآلة فهو كسب، وما ليس بآلة فهو خلق، وقيل: ما وقع المقدور به من حيث يصـ [؟] انفراد القادر به فهو خلق، وما ليس كذلك فهو كسب.
And when it is established by proof that creative power (qudrat al-takhlīq) for the servant is impossible, and it is established that he has power over the action, it is known that it is permissible for a single act of power (maqdūr wāḥid) to fall under the power of two powerful agents (qādirān). And he agrees with the Muʿtazila that two powers cannot combine upon one act of power from the aspect of invention (ikhtirāʿ), likewise from the aspect of acquisition (iktisāb). But the origin of the disagreement is the relation of two powers to one act of power in the manner of invention and acquisition: for God's power over the act of power is invention, and the servant's power over the same act of power is acquisition. And by this it is established that the servant is not compelled (majbūr), and that he has an action, and that his power is related to his act of power which is his action. And he distinguishes between acquisition (kasb) and creation (khalq), saying: Every act of power that occurs in the locus of his power is acquisition, and what occurs not in the locus of his power is creation, and the name 'action' encompasses both. And it is said: What occurs with an instrument (āla) is acquisition, and what is without an instrument is creation. And it is said: What occurs, the act of power occurs by it in that [؟] the powerful agent is alone [؟] with it, that is creation, and what is not like that is acquisition.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage establishes the Maturidi doctrine of kasb (acquisition). After proving that the servant cannot create, the author affirms that the servant nonetheless has power over his action. This leads to the concept that a single act can be subject to two powers: God's power of creation (ikhtirāʿ) and the servant's power of acquisition (iktisāb). The Mu'tazila are partially agreed that two powers cannot combine on the same act from the aspect of creation, but the Maturidi position is that they relate to the same act differently—God creates it, the servant acquires it. This avoids both compulsion and independent creation. The passage then gives multiple definitions of kasb vs. khalq: (1) Kasb is what occurs within the locus of the servant's power; khalq is what does not. (2) Kasb is with an instrument; khalq is without. (3) Kasb is when the agent is not solitary; khalq is when the agent is utterly independent. The common thread is that God's creative act is unique, while the servant's act is dependent and acquired.

Key Terms
كسب
خلق
اختراع
اكتساب
مقدور واحد
قدرة قادرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates the act, the servant's power is illusory.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the servant's power is real as acquisition (kasb), not creation.

Checkpoint
What is the point of agreement with the Mu'tazila according to the passage?
A) Two powers can combine on one act in different respects
B) Two powers can never combine on one act
C) Two powers cannot combine on one act from the aspect of invention
D) The servant creates his own actions

Key Takeaway
God creates the act, the servant acquires it — two powers, one act, different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In moments of sin, do you instinctually blame your circumstances or even God, or do you truly own your action as your acquisition? How does knowing that God creates the act but you acquire it reshape the way you repent? This week, catch yourself before you shift blame and say: 'This is my acquisition; I am responsible.'
?
If your power to act is real but not creative—totally dependent on God's creation—does your pride in your achievements still hold up? What would it look like to live every success and failure with both full responsibility and total reliance on Allah? Identify one area where you can practice this balance today.
?
The lesson asserts that the servant is not compelled (majbūr). Do you truly feel free in your choices, or do you sometimes use the belief in divine decree as an excuse for passivity? What is one decision you have been avoiding because you felt 'it's all written'? Commit to making that decision actively, acknowledging that you acquire it while God creates it.

Kasb and Khalq: Defining the Servant's Acquisition
Al-Nasafi defines kasb and khalq with three formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various definitions of kasb and khalq and how they distinguish the servant's action from God's creation.

Core Idea
Kasb is what falls within the servant's power; khalq is what does not — and other definitions.

After establishing that the servant's power relates to his action as acquisition, al-Nasafi now clarifies the terms 'kasb' (acquisition) and 'khalq' (creation). He offers three definitions, all converging on the idea that God's creative act is absolute and independent, while the servant's act is dependent and occurs within a specific locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage listing the three definitions of kasb vs khalq. Compare them.
Notice that the first definition uses 'locus of power', the second uses 'instrument', and the third uses 'solitary agent'.
2
Identify the common theme
Ask yourself what all three definitions have in common: they all contrast dependence vs independence.
The key is that kasb is always dependent on something else.
3
Apply to the previous lesson
Connect these definitions back to the earlier point that the servant's power is acquisition, not creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, you attribute it to yourself as your 'earned' act. But do you truly recognize that every 'earned' action only occurs because God places it within your domain of power? What specific action today can you attribute to God's creation (khalq) rather than your own acquisition (kasb)? Resolve to say 'Allah created this for me' for one action you usually take credit for.
?

But these instruments themselves were created by God. When you use a phone or a car, do you remember that the instrument is just a tool lent to you? The real power is God's. Take a moment: name one instrument you used today and say internally, 'This instrument is a means, but the true agent is Allah.' How does that change your sense of indebtedness?
?
Al-Nasafi's third definition hints at the solitary agent (al-qādir al-munfarid). Only God can create without any dependence. But you, as a servant, depend on God for every atom of power. Think of a moment when you felt utterly in control—a success you attributed to your own effort. Now ask yourself: was I truly independent? The answer is no. This week, when you succeed, will you say 'This is from Allah' with the same intensity you say 'I did it'? Write down one personal achievement and right now, in your heart, dedicate it wholly to Allah as His creation of success, while you merely acquired it.

The Creation and Acquisition of Actions: Nasafi's Summary
Nasafi summarizes the debate on actions and affirms that actions are created by God and acquired by the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's categorization of objections to the doctrine that God creates human actions, and how he defends the Maturidi position of acquisition (iktisab).

Core Idea
God creates all actions; humans acquire moral responsibility.

This concluding passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla summarizes a lengthy debate on human agency. Nasafi identifies three possible reasons for rejecting that God creates human actions and answers each. The passage culminates in affirming the classical Maturidi doctrine: actions are created by God and acquired by the servant, preserving both divine power and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three-part structure of objections Nasafi addresses.
The passage is dense; take time to appreciate the logical flow.
2
Identify the three categories of denial
Nasafi lists three reasons why someone might deny that God creates actions: lack of evidence, impossibility, or that it leads to necessitarianism. Identify each and note how he responds.
This classification is key to understanding the debate.
3
Examine Nasafi's responses
For each category, Nasafi provides a response: rational and scriptural proofs for the first; demanding proof for the second; and refuting the doubt for the third.
Notice that he does not simply assert but engages the opposing views.
4
Understand the final position
The passage concludes with the established doctrine: action relates to God as creation (khalq) and to the servant as acquisition (iktisab). This means the servant is free and not compelled.
This is the Maturidi solution to the problem of free will and divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النهاية يقول : إن الكلام في هذا الموضوع يحتاج إلى مجلد ضخم مثل تبصرة الأدلة. ويلخص الأمر فيها يلي: أن إنكار تخليق أفعال العباد إما أن يكون لما لا دلالة عليها أو للإحالة أو لما القول به من إيجاب الضرورة وامتناع التكليف، وبعد هذا الترديد يقول: إن كان إنكارهم لعدم الدلالة فقد أقمنا الكثير من الدلائل العقلية والسمعية. وإن كان إنكارهم للإحالة فيطالبون بدليلها، ولا دلائل معهم سوى الوهم، وإن كان إنكارهم لما فيه من إيجاب الضرورة فقد أجبنا عن ذلك ودفعنا الشبه، وثبت في النهاية أن الفعل يتعلق بالله سبحانه على طريق الخلق أي الاختراع، وبالعبد على طريق الاكتساب . وهذا يؤدي إلى أن العبد مختار، وليس بمضطر.
And in the end he says: indeed, speech on this topic requires a large volume like Tabsirat al-Adilla. And he summarizes the matter as follows: The denial of the creation of the actions of servants is either due to lack of evidence for it, or due to impossibility, or due to what the statement entails of necessitarianism (ijab al-darura) and the prevention of moral obligation (imtina' al-taklif). After this division, he says: If their denial is due to lack of evidence, then we have produced many rational and scriptural proofs. If their denial is due to impossibility, then they are required to produce evidence for it, and they have no evidence except illusion. If their denial is due to what it entails of necessitarianism, then we have answered that and repelled the doubt. And it is established in the end that the action relates to God Almighty by way of creation (khalq), i.e., invention (ikhtira'), and to the servant by way of acquisition (iktisab). And this leads to the servant being free (mukhtar) and not constrained (mudtarr).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding summary of an earlier debate on the creation of human actions. Nasafi first notes that the topic is vast, requiring a large volume like his own Tabsirat al-Adilla. He then categorizes the reasons why one might deny that God creates the actions of servants into three possibilities: lack of evidence, impossibility (of the concept), or that it would imply necessitarianism (the denial of free will) and thus invalidate moral responsibility. He then addresses each: if the denial is due to lack of evidence, they have provided ample rational and scriptural evidence. If due to impossibility, the objector must prove that impossibility, and they only have illusions. If due to fear of necessitarianism, they have already repelled that doubt. The final established position is that actions are related to God as creation (invention) and to the servant as acquisition. This means the servant is genuinely free (mukhtar) and not compelled.

Key Terms
تخليق
خلق
اكتساب
اضطرار
تكليف
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That acquisition (iktisab) implies the servant creates his own action.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that God alone creates (khalq, ikhtira'), while the servant only acquires (iktisab) the moral quality of the action. The servant does not bring the action into existence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is one of the reasons people deny that God creates human actions?
A) It leads to necessitarianism and prevents moral obligation.
B) It limits God's power.
C) It makes humans responsible for evil.
D) It contradicts revelation.

Key Takeaway
Actions are created by God and acquired by the servant, preserving divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on a sin you committed, do you truly acknowledge that God created that very act, yet you remain accountable? How does this affect your sense of urgency in seeking forgiveness?
?
Have you ever excused your own laziness or failure by thinking, 'It was destined anyway'? How does the doctrine of acquisition (iktisab) confront that excuse and demand you take responsibility for your choices?
?
If God creates every action, including your faith and your doubts, what does that mean for your daily striving? Does this truth make you feel helpless or does it ignite a deeper reliance on Him? Answer honestly: which reaction dominates your heart?

Nasafi's Refutation of the Analogy from the Witnessed
Nasafi refutes the rational argument from analogy that willing folly makes one foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that God would be foolish if He wills foolishness, using three distinct responses based on the nature of divine will and the definitions of foolishness.

Core Idea
God's action cannot be judged by human standards of foolishness.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God wills disbelief or foolish acts, He would be foolish, drawing an analogy from human experience. Nasafi dismantles this analogy by showing that the human case involves prohibition and lack of praiseworthy outcome, neither of which applies to God. In this passage, he presents three counter-arguments drawn from Ash'ari's definition and the Maturidi framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of argument: the Mu'tazilite objection is stated, then Nasafi's three refutations are given.
The passage contains both the objection and the responses in a compressed form.
2
Identify the three refutations
Look for three distinct points: (1) God's willing things that would remove lordship but not allowing them to occur, (2) Ash'ari's definition that foolishness is what is forbidden, so in the unseen there is no prohibition, (3) the Maturidi definition that foolishness lacks a praiseworthy outcome, and God's will always has one.
The second refutation is attributed to al-Ash'ari; the third is the author's own view.
3
Reflect on why the analogy fails
Consider how each refutation breaks the analogy from the witnessed to the unseen. In human cases, willing foolishness is both prohibited and leads to no good outcome. For God, there is no prohibition and His will always aims at a praiseworthy end.
This highlights the error in applying human ethical categories to Allah.
4
Summarize the key difference
Write a one-sentence summary of why God cannot be called foolish for willing what appears foolish to us. The answer: divine will is not constrained by human commands and always serves a higher wisdom.
This will help solidify the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد بعد ذلك على شبهتهم العقلية وهي أن مريد السفه سفيه، ومريد الكفر كافر اعتبارا بالشاهد فيقول : إرادة ما لو كان لأوجب زوال ربوبيته، وإرادته أن لا يكون ما لو يكن لزالت ألوهيته، وقد مر أن الأشعري قال : السفه ما نهى عنه، وفي الشاهد مريد السفه منهي عنه فكان به سفيها، وفي الغائب لا نهي فلم يكن سفيها، وعندنا : أن السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة، فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها، ويضرب لذلك مجموعة من الأمثلة تؤكد كذبهم وتدعم رأيه.
And he refutes after that their rational doubt, which is that the one who wills foolishness is foolish, and the one who wills unbelief is an unbeliever by analogy to the witnessed. He says: Willing what, if it were, would necessitate the removal of His lordship, and His willing that something not be which, if it were not, would remove His divinity. And it has been mentioned that al-Ash'ari said: Foolishness is that which is forbidden. In the witnessed, the one who wills foolishness is forbidden from it, so he becomes foolish by it. But in the unseen, there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish. And according to us: Foolishness is that which is not attached to a praiseworthy consequence. If it were attached to the willing of foolishness, it would not be foolishness. And he gives for that a set of examples that confirm their falsity and support his view.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage refutes the rational objection that willing foolishness makes one foolish. The argument from analogy (قياس الغائب على الشاهد) is used: in human experience, if someone wills foolishness, they are called foolish. Therefore, if God wills foolishness (like creating disbelief), He would be foolish. Nasafi offers three responses. First, he states that God wills things that, if they occurred, would necessitate the removal of His lordship, but He also wills that they not occur, preserving His divinity. Second, he cites al-Ash'ari's definition: foolishness is what is forbidden. In the witnessed, the one who wills foolishness is indeed forbidden from it, so he becomes foolish. But in the unseen (God), there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish. Third, he gives the author's own definition: foolishness is what does not lead to a praiseworthy outcome. If God's willing of something leads to a good outcome (e.g., punishment of disbelievers as a manifestation of justice), then it is not foolish. He supports this with examples (though not listed in the passage) that he claims confirm the falsity of the Mu'tazilite objection.

Key Terms
مريد السفه
اعتبار بالشاهد
النهي
عاقبة حميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy from human experience (shahid) to God (gha'ib) is valid: if a human who wills foolishness is foolish, then God who wills foolishness (e.g., creating unbelievers) must also be foolish.
Correction: Nasafi shows the analogy fails because (1) God's will is not subject to prohibition, (2) His will always has a praiseworthy outcome, and (3) His willing is not comparable to human willing in terms of lordship and divinity.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi definition mentioned in the passage, what makes an action foolish?
A) It causes harm
B) It is forbidden by the Lawgiver
C) It is not attached to a praiseworthy consequence
D) It is done with ignorance

Key Takeaway
The analogy from human will to divine will fails because God is not bound by prohibition and His will always serves a praiseworthy purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or circumstances that seem senseless, do you ever doubt God's wisdom? How does Nasafi's argument that God's will always serves a praiseworthy outcome challenge you to trust His plan even when you cannot see the good?
?
In what areas of your life have you assumed that your own sense of logic or morality should apply to God? What would it look like to surrender that assumption and let God be God?
?
Think of a time you judged someone else's faith as foolish. Are you making the same mistake the Mu'tazilites made by applying human standards to divine matters? How can you repent from that today?

Refutation of the Rational Objection on Willing Kufr
Al-Nasafi refutes the rational objection that willing kufr makes one a kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi refutes the rational objection that willing kufr makes one a kafir, by distinguishing between the witnessed and unseen and by defining foolishness in terms of consequences.

Core Idea
Willing kufr does not make Allah a kafir because the rules of the witnessed do not apply to the unseen.

This passage tackles a common rational objection: if a human who wills foolishness is foolish, then if Allah wills kufr, He must be foolish or a kafir. Al-Nasafi presents two responses: one from Al-Ash'ari based on the absence of prohibition in Allah's case, and his own based on the concept of praiseworthy consequence. The lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the translation. Let the structure emerge.
The passage contains two distinct arguments.
2
Understand the rational objection
Identify the objection: by analogy to humans, if willing foolishness makes one foolish, then Allah's willing kufr would make Him a kafir.
This is based on qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.
3
Examine Al-Ash'ari's response
Notice Al-Ash'ari's definition: foolishness is what is forbidden. In the witnessed, a human who wills foolishness is prohibited, so he becomes foolish. But in the unseen (Allah), there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish.
The key difference is the presence or absence of a prohibition.
4
Examine Al-Nasafi's own view
Al-Nasafi defines foolishness as that which has no praiseworthy consequence. Since Allah's willing kufr has wise purposes (e.g., justice), it is not foolish.
This definition focuses on outcomes rather than prohibition.
5
Reflect on the examples
The passage mentions examples that support Al-Nasafi's position and refute the objection, though they are not detailed here.
Consider what examples might illustrate praiseworthy consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد بعد ذلك على شبهتهم العقلية وهي أن مريد السفه سفيه، ومريد الكفر كافر اعتبارا بالشاهد فيقول: إرادة ما لو كان لأوجب زوال ربوبيته، وإرادته أن لا يكون ما لو يكن لزالت ألوهيته، وقد مر أن الأشعري قال: السفه ما نهى عنه، وفي الشاهد مريد السفه منهي عنه فكان به سفيها، وفي الغائب لا نهي فلم يكن سفيها، وعندنا: أن السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة، فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها، ويضرب لذلك مجموعة من الأمثلة تؤكد كذبهم وتدعم رأيه.
And he responds after that to their rational objection, which is that the one who wills foolishness is foolish, and the one who wills disbelief is a disbeliever, by analogy to the witnessed (al-shahid). He says: Willing something which, if it were, would necessitate the loss of His lordship, and His willing that something not be, which if it were not, would cause the loss of His divinity. And it has passed that al-Ash'ari said: Foolishness (al-sufh) is what is forbidden. In the witnessed, the one who wills foolishness is forbidden (from it), so by it he becomes foolish. But in the unseen (al-gha'ib), there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish. According to us (al-Nasafi's view): Foolishness is what no praiseworthy consequence attaches to. So if a praiseworthy consequence attaches to the willing of foolishness, it would not be foolishness. And he gives for that a set of examples that affirm their falsity and support his view.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage refutes the rational objection that if Allah wills kufr, He must be a kafir, based on analogy with human behavior (shahid). Al-Nasafi presents two arguments. First, a subtle point: Allah's willing something that would negate His lordship or divinity is not comparable because His essence is necessarily existent. Second, he cites al-Ash'ari's definition: foolishness (sufh) is what is forbidden. In the witnessed world, one who wills foolishness is prohibited from it, so he becomes foolish by doing it. But in the case of Allah (al-gha'ib), there is no prohibition upon Him, so He is not foolish. Then al-Nasafi gives his own (Maturidi) view: foolishness is that which has no praiseworthy consequence (aqiba hamida). If Allah wills kufr, it has praiseworthy consequences (e.g., punishment of disbelievers, manifestation of justice), so it is not foolish. He then gives examples to support this, which are not detailed here but are said to demonstrate the falsity of the opponent's claim.

Key Terms
شبهة عقلية
الشاهد
الغائب
السفه
عاقبة حميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that willing evil necessarily makes the willer evil, without considering the difference between Creator and creature or the presence of wisdom.
Correction: Allah's will is not subject to human moral categories; His actions have wise consequences that prevent them from being called foolish.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's own view, what makes an action foolish?
A) It is forbidden by revelation
B) It has no praiseworthy consequence
C) It is done by a foolish person
D) It contradicts divine nature

Key Takeaway
Allah's willing of disbelief is not foolish because His actions have praiseworthy consequences and are not subject to human prohibitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil or suffering in the world, do you instinctively question Allah's wisdom? This lesson calls you to trust that His will has a praiseworthy consequence beyond your sight. What specific situation in your life today requires you to surrender that judgment and affirm His perfect wisdom?
?
You often judge yourself and others by human standards of morality. But this lesson shows that Allah is not bound by those standards. Are you willing to let go of expecting Him to act according to your sense of fairness? What inner resistance do you feel, and how will you actively work to submit to His decree?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's willing of disbelief has a praiseworthy purpose, how does that change your view of those who disbelieve? Does it make you more compassionate or more resigned? Pray for guidance on how to reconcile your heart with this truth—then write one concrete step you will take this week to live it out.

The Consensus on Realities and Knowledge
The author states the consensus of rational beings on realities and knowledge, except for a fringe denier group.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all rational beings agree on the existence of knowledge and realities for things, and the extreme nature of the position held by those who deny all reality and knowledge.

Core Idea
Denying all reality is a position even animals would reject.

After establishing the definition and reality of knowledge, the author now presents the universal agreement of rational beings on the existence of knowledge and realities. He also highlights a fringe group from ancient times who pretended ignorance and claimed that nothing has any reality. This lesson examines that consensus and the absurdity of the denialist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the strong claim of consensus (ijma') and the description of the deniers.
Pay attention to the phrase 'رتبة تستنكف البهائم عنها' – even animals would disdain such a rank.
2
Identify the consensus
Find the statement of consensus: 'أجمع العقلاء على ثبوت العلم والحقائق للأشياء'. Understand that this is a claim of universal agreement among rational beings.
3
Analyze the exception
The exception is a group from the ancients (طائفة من الأوائل). Read how they are described and what they claimed. Ask yourself: Why would the author emphasize that even animals would disdain their rank?
This hyperbole shows the irrationality of their position.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the implication: If someone denies all reality and knowledge, is it possible to have intellectual discourse with them? The author will address this in the next lesson.
This lesson sets the stage for the discussion on debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات الحقائق والعلوم: وإذا عرف حد العلم وحقيقته نقول: أجمع العقلاء على ثبوت العلم والحقائق للأشياء، سوى طائفة من الأوائل تجاهلت ورضيت لنفسها رتبة تستنكف البهائم عنها، فزعمت أن لا حقيقة لشيء ولا علم بشيء، وإنما هي ظنون وحسبانات. وأجمع العقلاء على أن لا مناظرة بيننا وبين من هذا قوله لأن بيننا فائدة المناظرة أن يثبت بالدلائل صحة قول وبطلان قول آخر، والعلم الحاصل عن النظر في الدلائل وإن كان يبلغ النهاية في القوة فطريقه أخفى من طريق علم الحواس والبداهة، ومن بلغ في الوقاحة والعناد مبلغاً لا يبالي من إنكار ما يثبت من العلوم والحقائق بالحواس والبداهة في العقول، لا يرجى منه قبول العلم الثابت بالاستدلال، ولأن المناظرة تكون بين اثنين بينهما أصول مسلمة حكمها الإثبات، وأصول أخرى مسلمة حكمها النفي، ووجد فرع له شبه بكلام النوعين في الأصول بوجه من الوجوه، فيختلف اثنان أن إلحاقه بأي الأصلين أولى، وشبهه بأيهما كان بوصف العلة، وبأيهما كان بوصف الوجود، أعني ما وجد اتفاقا من غير أن يكون علة الحكم الثابت في العلم فيناظران ليظهر وصف العلة من وصف الوجود الواقع اتفاقا فيلحق بالأصل الذي يشاركه في وصف علة الحكم الثابت فيه، وإذا لم يكن لهؤلاء المتجاهلة أصل مجمع عليه لا يتصور مناظرتهم.
Discussion on establishing realities and knowledges: And when the definition and reality of knowledge are known, we say: All rational beings have agreed on the existence of knowledge and realities for things, except a group from the ancients who pretended ignorance and were content with a rank that even animals would disdain, and they claimed that nothing has any reality and there is no knowledge of anything, and that they are only opinions and conjectures. And all rational beings have agreed that there is no debate between us and one who says this, because the benefit of debate is to establish by evidences the correctness of a statement and the falsehood of another, and the knowledge obtained from examining evidences, even if it reaches the utmost strength, its path is more hidden than the path of sensory and intuitive knowledge. And one who has reached such a level of insolence and obstinacy that he does not care to deny what is established of knowledges and realities by the senses and intuition in minds cannot be hoped to accept knowledge established by inference. And because debate takes place between two persons who have agreed-upon premises whose judgment is affirmation, and other agreed-upon premises whose judgment is negation, and a branch is found that resembles both types in the premises in some respect, so two persons differ as to which of the two originals it should be attached, and its resemblance to which of them is by the description of the cause, and to which of them is by the description of existence, that is, what is found coincidentally without being the cause of the judgment established in knowledge; so they debate to make apparent the description of the cause from the description of existence occurring coincidentally, so it is attached to the original with which it shares the description of the cause of the judgment established therein. And if these pretenders have no agreed-upon original, then their debate is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing the reality of knowledge and objects (ḥaqāʾiq) and the impossibility of debating those who deny them. The author begins with a heading: 'Discussion on establishing realities and knowledges.' Then he says: After defining knowledge and its reality, we say: All rational beings (al-ʿuqalāʾ) have agreed on the existence of knowledge and realities for things, except a group from the ancients who pretended ignorance and were content with a rank that even animals would disdain. They claimed that nothing has reality and there is no knowledge of anything, only opinions and conjectures. Then the author states that all rational beings also agree that there can be no debate (munāẓara) between us and someone who holds this view. The reason is that the benefit of debate is to establish by evidence the correctness of one statement and the falsehood of another. The knowledge obtained from examining evidences, though it may reach the utmost strength, its path is more hidden than the path of sensory and intuitive knowledge. Someone who has reached such a level of insolence and obstinacy that he does not care to deny what is established by the senses and intuition cannot be hoped to accept knowledge established by inference. Moreover, debate takes place between two persons who share agreed-upon premises (uṣūl musallama) that have the judgment of affirmation (ithbāt) and other premises that have the judgment of negation (nafy). Then a branch (farʿ) is found that resembles both types of premises in some respect. The two differ as to which of the two original premises the branch should be attached. They debate to make clear which descriptive feature is the cause (ʿilla) and which is merely accompanying. If the deniers have no agreed-upon premise at all, then debate with them is inconceivable.

Key Terms
العقلاء
الحقائق
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is dismissing all disagreement or philosophical skepticism in general.
Correction: The author is specifically targeting those who deny all reality and knowledge entirely, not those who question specific claims. The consensus is on the existence of realities, not on every detail.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did all rational beings agree upon?
A) That knowledge is acquired only through senses.
B) That knowledge and realities exist for things.
C) That debate is always possible.
D) That animals have knowledge.

Key Takeaway
All rational beings agree that things have real essences and knowledge is possible, except for a radical group that no one can rationally debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the skeptics 'were content with a rank that even animals would disdain.' Is there any area of your faith or knowledge where you have settled for a lower standard, content with doubt or ignorance instead of seeking certainty? What will you do about it today?
?
All rational beings agree that knowledge and realities exist. But do you live as if you truly believe that? When faced with a trial or confusion, do you act on the certainty that truth is real and God knows all, or do you drift into apathy as if all is mere opinion?
?
The skeptics claimed 'there is no knowledge of anything.' Do you ever act as if that's true by neglecting to learn about your Creator, His commands, or your purpose? What would it take for you to become a true 'عاقل' (rational being) in this sense?

The Impossibility of Debating Deniers of Realities
The author explains the purpose of debate and why it requires shared premises, concluding that deniers of all realities have no such common ground.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical conditions required for a valid debate (munazara) and why it is impossible to debate with someone who denies all agreed-upon premises.

Core Idea
No debate is possible without shared premises.

Having established the consensus of rational beings on realities, the author now explains that debate (munazara) requires agreed-upon premises (usul musallama). He describes the structure of a valid debate involving two types of premises and the process of determining the true cause. This lesson unpacks that structure and shows why deniers of all realities cannot be engaged in debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the main claim: all rational beings agree that there is no debate with deniers of realities.
The phrase 'لا مناظرة بيننا وبين من هذا قوله' is the key.
2
Understand the benefit of debate
The author states the purpose: 'فائدة المناظرة أن يثبت بالدلائل صحة قول وبطلان قول آخر'. Understand that debate aims to establish truth and falsehood through evidence.
3
Analyze the difficulty of inferential knowledge
Notice that knowledge from inference (istidlal) is more hidden than sensory or intuitive knowledge. The denier rejects even sensory knowledge, so he cannot accept inferential knowledge.
This shows the denier's obstinacy is total.
4
Examine the conditions for debate
The passage describes a scenario: two parties have shared premises (one affirming, one negating). A branch case arises that resembles both. They debate to determine which premise it belongs to based on the descriptive cause (illa) versus mere coincidence. This requires common ground.
If no agreed-upon premises exist, debate is inconceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات الحقائق والعلوم: وإذا عرف حد العلم وحقيقته نقول: أجمع العقلاء على ثبوت العلم والحقائق للأشياء، سوى طائفة من الأوائل تجاهلت ورضيت لنفسها رتبة تستنكف البهائم عنها، فزعمت أن لا حقيقة لشيء ولا علم بشيء، وإنما هي ظنون وحسبانات. وأجمع العقلاء على أن لا مناظرة بيننا وبين من هذا قوله لأن بيننا فائدة المناظرة أن يثبت بالدلائل صحة قول وبطلان قول آخر، والعلم الحاصل عن النظر في الدلائل وإن كان يبلغ النهاية في القوة فطريقه أخفى من طريق علم الحواس والبداهة، ومن بلغ في الوقاحة والعناد مبلغاً لا يبالي من إنكار ما يثبت من العلوم والحقائق بالحواس والبداهة في العقول، لا يرجى منه قبول العلم الثابت بالاستدلال، ولأن المناظرة تكون بين اثنين بينهما أصول مسلمة حكمها الإثبات، وأصول أخرى مسلمة حكمها النفي، ووجد فرع له شبه بكلام النوعين في الأصول بوجه من الوجوه، فيختلف اثنان أن إلحاقه بأي الأصلين أولى، وشبهه بأيهما كان بوصف العلة، وبأيهما كان بوصف الوجود، أعني ما وجد اتفاقا من غير أن يكون علة الحكم الثابت في العلم فيناظران ليظهر وصف العلة من وصف الوجود الواقع اتفاقا فيلحق بالأصل الذي يشاركه في وصف علة الحكم الثابت فيه، وإذا لم يكن لهؤلاء المتجاهلة أصل مجمع عليه لا يتصور مناظرتهم.

?

?

?
Have you ever insisted on debating someone who rejects obvious realities, hoping to convince them with logic? After reading that such debate is inconceivable, will you change your approach—perhaps to prayers or silence instead? What will you do differently the next time you encounter such a person?

The Intellect as a Necessary Cause of Knowledge
Nasafi argues intellect is a necessary cause of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that the intellect's role as a cause of knowledge is known by necessity, not by proof, and how he refutes skeptical objections.

Core Idea
The intellect's reliability is self-evident like sensory knowledge.

Imam Nasafi tackles a fundamental question: how do we know that the intellect is a valid source of knowledge? He rejects circular arguments and presents a compelling case that this knowledge is necessary and self-evident, akin to knowing that the whole is greater than its part.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the dialectical style: Nasafi presents objections before stating the truth.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author poses a dilemma: either the intellect's status is known by itself (circular) or by something else. Identify the two horns and how he rejects both.
This is a classic anti-skeptical strategy.
3
Understand the examples of necessary knowledge
Nasafi gives two examples: whole greater than part, and equal times implying equal finitude. Reflect on why these are self-evident and cannot be doubted.
Compare to sensory knowledge—also necessary and not subject to doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل سبب أصيل للمعرفة]: وأما الكلام في العقل أنه من أسباب المعارف، فمن أنكر ذلك يتعلق بكون قضاياه متناقضة، ولأن كون العقل من أسباب المعارف، وكون الاستدلال (۷) والنظر به مما يفضيان إلى العلم، إما أن يعرف بالعقل، وإما أن يعرف بغير العقل، فإن ذلك؟ قلتم : بأنه يعرف بالعقل ففيه سئلتم ، وفي النظرية ) (1) نوزعتم، وإن قلتم : عرف ذلك بدليل آخر، قيل لكم ما ذلك الدليل؟ فإن قلتم : هو الخبر، قيل: بخبر من عرفتم وبم عرفتم أن المخبر صادق فيما قال ؟ وإن قلتم بالحس، فبأي حس عرفتم(2) ذلك؟ أشيروا إليه وعينوه ليظهر تعنتكم ومكابرتكم، ولأنا)(3) أرباب الحواس السليمة ولا يختلف أرباب الحواس (4) السليمة في معرفة المحسوسات وأهل الحق - نصرهم الله - يقولون : كون (5) العقل من أسباب (6) المعارف يعلم بالضرورة، فإن العلم الثابت (7) ببديهة العقل ضروري كعلم الحواس، فإن العلم بأن الشيء أعظم من جزئه وأن جزءه ) (8) أصغر من كله ،ضروري، فإن زيدا بكليته أعظم من يده وحدها، إذ في كله يد وزيادة، ويده أصغر من كله، وكذا العلم بأن المستويين في زمان إذا اتصف أحدهما بالتناهي (في الوجود (كان الآخر أيضا متناهيا)(9) في الوجود ) (10) كمن علم أن ولادة زيد وعمرو كانت في ساعة واحدة، ثم علم أن أحدهما ابن عشرين سنة، علم ضرورة أن الآخر ابن عشرين سنة، حتى إن شيئا من الشبه (11) والشكوك لا يعتريه، ولو أراد تشكيك (12) نفسه في ذلك لعجز، وعرف الشبه(11) من نفسه أنه مكابر ، كما في العلم الحاصل بالحواس. فإذا كان كذلك فمن أنكر على الإطلاق كون العقل من أسباب المعارف، فقد أنكر العلم الضروري، وتجاهل
[The intellect is a fundamental cause of knowledge]: As for the discussion concerning the intellect, that it is among the causes of knowledge: whoever denies that clings to the fact that its propositions are contradictory. And because the fact that the intellect is among the causes of knowledge, and that inference (istidlāl) and consideration (naẓar) by it lead to knowledge, either it is known by the intellect or it is known by other than the intellect. Then that? You said: it is known by the intellect, so in that you are questioned, and in the theory you are disputed. And if you said: it is known by another proof, it is said to you: what is that proof? If you said: it is the report (khabar), it is said: by whose report did you know, and by what did you know that the informant is truthful in what he said? And if you said: by sense perception, then by which sense did you know that? Point to it and specify it so that your obstinacy and stubbornness become apparent. And we are people of sound senses, and the people of sound senses do not differ in the knowledge of sensible objects. And the people of truth — may God support them — say: that the intellect is among the causes of knowledge is known by necessity (ḍarūra). For the knowledge established by the intuitive grasp of the intellect is necessary like the knowledge of the senses. For the knowledge that the whole is greater than its part and that its part is smaller than the whole is necessary. For Zaid in his entirety is greater than his hand alone, since in his whole there is a hand and more, and his hand is smaller than the whole. And likewise the knowledge that two things equal in time, if one of them is described as finite in existence, then the other is also finite in existence — like one who knows that the birth of Zaid and Amr was in the same hour, then knows that one of them is twenty years old, he knows necessarily that the other is twenty years old — so much so that no doubt or suspicion affects it. And if he wanted to doubt himself in that, he would be unable, and he would know from himself that he is stubborn. Such is the knowledge obtained by the senses. So if it is so, then whoever absolutely denies that the intellect is among the causes of knowledge has denied necessary knowledge and feigned ignorance.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the intellect is a fundamental cause of knowledge, known by necessity. It begins by presenting the position of those who deny the intellect as a source of knowledge, claiming its propositions are contradictory. The author then poses a dilemma: either the fact that intellect is a source of knowledge is known by the intellect itself (which leads to circularity) or by something other than the intellect. If by another proof, then that proof is examined. If the proof is a report, we ask whose report and how we know the reporter is truthful. If the proof is sense perception, we ask which sense. The author then asserts that the truth is that it is known by necessity (ḍarūra). He provides examples of necessary knowledge: the whole is greater than its part, and if two things are equal in time and one is finite, the other is necessarily finite. These are self-evident truths that no rational person can doubt. He compares this to sensory knowledge, which is also necessary. Therefore, denying the intellect's role is a denial of necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
عقل
ضرورة
بديهة العقل
استدلال
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the intellect's reliability is only conventional or based on other sources like revelation or senses.
Correction: Nasafi argues it is self-evident and necessary, like knowledge that the whole is greater than its part, and cannot be proven by other means without circularity.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, how is the fact that the intellect is a cause of knowledge known?
A) By the intellect itself through circular reasoning
B) By sense perception
C) By necessary knowledge (ḍarūra)
D) By transmitted reports

Key Takeaway
The intellect's role as a cause of knowledge is self-evident and necessary, analogous to sensory knowledge, and cannot be denied without contradicting reason itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about a self-evident truth (e.g., 'the whole is greater than its part'), do you trust your immediate certainty, or do you search for proof? What does that hesitation reveal about your reliance on your intellect?
?
Think of a time you stubbornly denied something obvious—like a clear logical contradiction. How does Nasafi's example of the person unable to doubt himself expose the inner conflict you felt? Will you consciously submit to necessary truths, even when they are inconvenient?
?
If the intellect's basic principles are necessary and self-evident, why do you sometimes act against them (e.g., ignoring clear cause and effect)? What change must you make to align your actions with your inherent knowledge?

Reason as a Source of Knowledge Known by Necessity
The author proves that reason is a source of knowledge known by necessity, using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that reason's role as a source of knowledge is a necessary truth, using examples from part-whole relations and coevals.

Core Idea
Reason is a necessary source of knowledge, as self-evident as the part being smaller than the whole.

In this passage, the author engages with a skeptic who questions whether reason can be a source of knowledge. He shows that any alternative appeal to report or sense leads to an infinite regress. Then he affirms that the people of truth hold that reason's cognitive role is known by necessity, illustrated by two undeniable examples. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow. Notice the dialectical style: the author addresses an opponent.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the dialectical structure
The passage begins with an objection: 'You said that it is known by reason...' Then the author counters each possible alternative (report, sense). Map out the exchange.
3
Examine the two examples
The author provides two examples: the part-whole relation and the equality of coevals. These illustrate necessary rational knowledge. Understand why each is self-evident.
The part-whole example uses Zayd and his hand; the coevals example uses Zayd and Amr born at the same time.
4
Understand the conclusion about sophistry
The author concludes that denying reason as a source of knowledge is denial of necessary knowledge and aligns one with the Sophists, who reject obvious truths.
The Sophists are often associated with radical skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتم : بأنه يعرف بالعقل ففيه سئلتم ، وفي النظرية نوزعتم، وإن قلتم : عرف ذلك بدليل آخر، قيل لكم ما ذلك الدليل؟ فإن قلتم : هو الخبر، قيل: بخبر من عرفتم وبم عرفتم أن المخبر صادق فيما قال ؟ وإن قلتم بالحس، فبأي حس عرفتم ذلك؟ أشيروا إليه وعينوه ليظهر تعنتكم ومكابرتكم، ولأنا أرباب الحواس السليمة ولا يختلف أرباب الحواس السليمة في معرفة المحسوسات وأهل الحق - نصرهم الله - يقولون : كون العقل من أسباب المعارف يعلم بالضرورة، فإن العلم الثابت ببديهة العقل ضروري كعلم الحواس، فإن العلم بأن الشيء أعظم من جزئه وأن جزءه أصغر من كله ،ضروري، فإن زيدا بكليته أعظم من يده وحدها، إذ في كله يد وزيادة، ويده أصغر من كله، وكذا العلم بأن المستويين في زمان إذا اتصف أحدهما بالتناهي في الوجود كان الآخر أيضا متناهيا في الوجود كمن علم أن ولادة زيد وعمرو كانت في ساعة واحدة، ثم علم أن أحدهما ابن عشرين سنة، علم ضرورة أن الآخر ابن عشرين سنة، حتى إن شيئا من الشبه والشكوك لا يعتريه، ولو أراد تشكيك نفسه في ذلك لعجز، وعرف الشبه من نفسه أنه مكابر ، كما في العلم الحاصل بالحواس. فإذا كان كذلك فمن أنكر على الإطلاق كون العقل من أسباب المعارف، فقد أنكر العلم الضروري، وتجاهل والتحق بالسوفسطائية، فيعامل بما يعامل به السوفسطائية، وإن أقر به فقد أقر في الجملة بكون العقل من أسباب المعارف
You said: that it is known by reason, so in that you have been questioned, and concerning theory you have been disputed. And if you say: that is known by another proof, it is said to you: what is that proof? If you say: it is report, it is said: by whose report do you know, and how did you know that the reporter is truthful in what he said? And if you say: by sense, then by which sense did you know that? Point to it and specify it so that your obstinacy and stubbornness become apparent, and because we are possessors of sound senses, and the possessors of sound senses do not differ in knowledge of sensible things. And the people of truth - may God support them - say: that reason is among the causes of knowledge is known by necessity. For the knowledge established by the intuition of reason is necessary, like sensory knowledge. For the knowledge that a thing is greater than its part, and that its part is smaller than its whole, is necessary. For Zayd in his entirety is greater than his hand alone, because in his whole there is a hand and more, and his hand is smaller than his whole. Likewise, the knowledge that two coevals, when one of them is described as finite in existence, the other also is finite in existence, like one who knows that the birth of Zayd and Amr was in the same hour, then knows that one of them is twenty years old, he knows necessarily that the other is twenty years old, such that no doubt or suspicion can affect him. And if he wished to doubt himself in that, he would be unable, and he would know from himself that he is stubborn, just as in knowledge obtained through the senses. So if this is the case, then whoever denies absolutely that reason is among the causes of knowledge has denied necessary knowledge, acted ignorantly, and joined the Sophists. He is to be dealt with as the Sophists are dealt with. And if he acknowledges that, then he has acknowledged in general that reason is among the causes of knowledge.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an argument that reason is a source of knowledge known by necessity. The author begins by addressing an opponent who claims that something is known by reason, then the author questions them, showing that if they resort to another proof, that proof itself requires justification. If they say it is known by report, we ask whose report and how we know the reporter is truthful. If they say by sense, we ask which sense and demand specification. The author then aligns with the people of truth who assert that reason's role as a cause of knowledge is known necessarily. He provides two examples: the part-whole relation and the equality of coevals in duration. These are self-evident truths that cannot be doubted. Denial of such necessary knowledge amounts to sophistry (sufisṭāʾiyya). The passage concludes that if the opponent admits this, they have conceded the general principle.

Correction: The author shows that rational intuitions (like part-whole and coevals) are equally necessary and indubitable.

Key Takeaway
Reason is a necessary source of knowledge, as demonstrated by self-evident examples like part-whole relations and coevals; denying it is sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If reason is a necessary source of knowledge, then using it is not optional for a believer. What is one area of your aqeedah or daily practice where you have relied on blind imitation rather than applying the rational proofs that are available to you? What will you do to change that this week?

Proof that Inference Leads to Knowledge
The author proves that inference leads to knowledge using analogy and human nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses analogy and human nature to prove that inference leads to knowledge.

Core Idea
Inference is like a road: if traveled correctly, it inevitably leads to knowledge.

The author presents two compelling arguments to establish that inference (النظر) is a valid path to knowledge. First, he draws an analogy with a disputed road: the only way to know if it leads to a destination is to travel it. Second, he points to human nature itself—when faced with uncertainty, people naturally turn to reflection and reasoning. This lesson unpacks these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Get a feel for the author's reasoning.
2
Understand the analogy of the road
Focus on the comparison between inference and a road to Samarkand. Notice how the dispute is settled by testing the road.
The analogy shows that empirical testing confirms a path's validity.
3
Examine the argument from human nature
The author argues that turning to inference in ambiguity is instinctive, like using senses. Reflect on this claim.
Even deniers use inference in worldly matters, showing its inevitability.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage establishes that inference leads to knowledge when conditions are met. Consider why this matters for theology.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to check comprehension.

So if he acknowledges that, but denies inference and reasoning, it is said to him: The proof that inference is a way to knowledge is that whoever engages in it and fulfills the conditions of inference arrives at knowledge inevitably. So it is known that it is a way to knowledge, like two people disputing about a road whether it is a road to Samarkand or not. The least that can settle the dispute is to say: Take this road; if it leads you to Samarkand, it is a road to it; if it does not lead you to it, it is not a road to it. So if he takes it and it leads him to it, he knows that it is a road to Samarkand. And also because everyone turns to inference when a matter is ambiguous to him, and reflects on it, and this is ingrained in human nature. Just as he turns, when something among visible things is ambiguous, to the sense of sight, and likewise when every sense-object is ambiguous, he turns to the sense prepared for perceiving that type of sense-object. So it indicates that the knowledge that reason is among the causes of knowledge, and that inference leads to knowledge, is something ingrained in human nature. Likewise, all rational people examine gains and distinguish between beneficial and harmful ones, and they occupy themselves with the beneficial profitable ones and avoid the harmful losing ones. And if one were to consider the state of this denier, he would find him engaged with it in most situations for ordering his worldly affairs. His livelihood would not be arranged, nor would the means of spending his life be organized, except

Commentary
This passage is a proof that inference (نظر) leads to knowledge. The author presents two arguments. First, an analogy: two people disputing whether a road leads to Samarkand. The way to settle it is to test the road by traveling it; if it leads to Samarkand, it is a valid road. Similarly, if one engages in inference with its proper conditions, it leads to knowledge. Second, the author argues from human nature: when in doubt, people naturally resort to inference and reflection, just as they use their senses when confused about sensed objects. This shows that the role of both reason and inference as sources of knowledge is ingrained in human nature. He further notes that even those who deny inference use it in worldly matters, like managing livelihood, which shows they implicitly acknowledge it.

If you suppress it in religious questions, are you living in denial of how God created you? Today, let that instinct lead you to seek knowledge of Him.
?
The author says that even the one who denies inference uses it constantly in daily life. Where in your own life do you deny the very tool you rely on every moment? Will you continue to ignore this contradiction, or will you admit that reason is a gift meant to bring you closer to the truth?

The Path-to-Samarkand Analogy: Proving the Validity of Nazar
Al-Nasafi uses a practical analogy to prove nazar's validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the path-to-Samarkand analogy demonstrates that rational reflection (nazar) is a valid method for attaining knowledge.

Core Idea
The only way to test a method is to use it and see the results.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful analogy to settle a dispute about the validity of rational inquiry. By comparing nazar to a physical path, he shows that the truth of a method is proven by its outcome. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the key terms and the flow from the opponent's position to the analogy.
Pay attention to how the author sets up the hypothetical opponent.
2
Identify the structure
Break down the passage into three parts: the opponent's admission (intellect is a cause of knowledge) and denial (of nazar), the challenge to prove nazar's validity, and the Samarkand analogy as the proof.
The analogy is the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on the analogy's logic
Consider why the author uses a physical path analogy for a rational method. How does 'traveling the path' correspond to engaging in reflection? Why is this a decisive refutation?
The analogy shifts the burden of proof to practical experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحق بالسوفسطائية، فيعامل بما يعامل به السوفسطائية، وإن أقر به فقد أقر في الجملة بكون العقل من أسباب المعارف، فلو أنه أقر بذلك، وأنكر النظر والاستدلال، فقيل له: الدليل على أن النظر طريق العلم أن من اشتغل به، واستوفى شرائط النظر، أفضى به إلى العلم لا محالة، فعرف أنه طريق العلم كاثنين يختلفان في طريق سمرقند أم ليس بطريق لها، فأدنى ما يقطع به الخصومة أن يقال : اسلك هذا الطريق فإن أفضى بك إلى سمرقند كان طريقا إليها، وإن لم يفض بك إليها لم يكن طريقا إليها، فإذا سلكه وأفضى به إليها علم أنه طريق سمرقند
And he joins the sophists, so he is treated as the sophists are treated. And if he acknowledges it, he has acknowledged in general that intellect is among the causes of knowledges. So if he acknowledges that, but denies reflection (nazar) and inference, then it is said to him: The evidence that reflection is a path to knowledge is that whoever engages in it and fulfills the conditions of reflection, it leads him necessarily to knowledge, so he knows that it is a path to knowledge. Like two persons who disagree about a path to Samarkand — is it a path to it or not? The least that settles the dispute is to say: Travel this path; if it leads you to Samarkand, it is a path to it, and if it does not lead you to it, it is not a path to it. When he travels it and it leads him to it, he knows that it is the path to Samarkand.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (tamthil) for the validity of rational inference (nazar). The author first sets up a hypothetical person who acknowledges that intellect is a source of knowledge but denies the need for reflection (nazar). To refute him, the author presents a practical test: the method of empirical verification. The analogy of two people disputing the road to Samarkand is used to show that the only way to resolve the dispute is to actually traverse the path and see where it leads. Similarly, the test for whether reflection leads to knowledge is to actually engage in it with its proper conditions. If it leads to certain knowledge, then it is a valid method. The author emphasizes that this is the 'least that cuts off the dispute' — it is a decisive empirical demonstration.

Key Terms
النظر
العلم
الاستدلال
طريق
السوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy is meant to prove that all reasoning leads to truth, or that the outcome is guaranteed.
Correction: The analogy shows that the validity of a method can be tested by its results, but it does not guarantee that every instance of reflection will produce knowledge — only that when conditions are met, it consistently does.

Checkpoint

A) Arguing about it
B) Consulting experts
C) Traveling it and seeing where it leads
D) Measuring the distance

Key Takeaway
Rational reflection is validated by its consistent production of knowledge, just as a path is validated by reaching its destination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your beliefs about God and the afterlife, do you honestly fulfill the 'conditions of reflection'—such as sincerity, seeking truth, and avoiding bias? If not, which condition will you work on starting today?
?
The analogy demands that you actually travel the path to know it works. Are you merely discussing nazar intellectually, or do you actively engage in reflective practice that leads you to certainty? What one step can you take this week to move from debate to genuine reflection?
?
If someone challenged the validity of your reasoning in faith matters, could you point to concrete experiences where your reflection led you to sure knowledge? Or have you settled for inherited opinions without personal verification? Commit now to undertake a reflective journey on one fundamental question within the next month.

The Universal Human Reliance on Reason
Proof from human disposition and worldly practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the natural human disposition to use reason in everyday life proves that reflection leads to knowledge, and that the denial of this is mere obstinacy.

Core Idea
Humans are naturally wired to reason; denying it is like denying your own eyesight.

The author presents a compelling argument from human nature. He observes that everyone, even those who deny the validity of reasoning in theology, relies on it for daily survival. This lesson explores that proof and its implication for the obstinate denier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: two parallel arguments from natural disposition and worldly practice.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
The first part argues from innate human reaction to ambiguity (like using eyesight). The second part points to everyone's use of reason in daily affairs to distinguish benefit from harm.
Both parts show that reflection is universal and necessary.
3
Understand the conclusion about the denier
The author concludes that since the denier uses reason for worldly matters, his denial in religion is not genuine but obstinate—a way to escape religious obligations.
This is a key point: the denier is inconsistent, not intellectually honest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن كلا يفزع إلى النظر، عند اشتباه الأمر عليه، ويتأمل في ذلك، وهذا جبل عليه الخلق، حتى إن العاقل لو أراد الامتناع عنه، إذا حزبه أمر ونابته نائبة، لم تطاوعه نفسه، كما يفزع عند اشتباه شيء من المرئيات إلى حاسة البصر، وكذا عند اشتباه كل محسوس يفزع إلى الحاسة المعدة لإدراك ذلك النوع من المحسوس. فدل أن العلم بأن العقل من أسباب المعارف، وأن النظر مما يفضي إلى العلم، مما جبل عليه البشر وكذا العقلاء بأسرهم ينظرون في المكاسب ويميزون بين النافعة منها والضارة فيشتغلون بالنافعة منها الرابحة، ويجتنبون الضارة الخاسرة، ولو تؤمل حال هذا الجاحد لوجد مشتغلا به في أكثر حالات لتسوية أموره الدنيوية لا يلتأم له أمر معيشته، ولا ينتظم أسباب تزجية عمره إلا بذلك، ودل بقاؤه إلى هذه الحالة على وجود هذا الصنيع منه، فدل أنه في الإنكار معاند، يروم بذلك التقصي عن ربقة التكليف، والتخلص عن لوازم الأمر والنهي
And because everyone resorts to reflection when a matter is ambiguous to him and contemplates it, and this is a disposition upon which creation is formed, to the extent that if the rational person wanted to refrain from it when a matter befalls him and a calamity strikes, his soul would not obey him. Just as he resorts, when something among visible things is ambiguous, to the sense of sight, and likewise when every sensory thing is ambiguous, he resorts to the sense prepared to perceive that type of sensed thing. Thus it indicates that the knowledge that intellect is among the causes of knowledges, and that reflection leads to knowledge, is something upon which humans are disposed. And likewise, all rational people engage in reflection in their livelihoods and distinguish between beneficial and harmful ones, and they occupy themselves with the beneficial profitable ones, and avoid the harmful loss-making ones. And if one were to consider the state of this denier, one would find him engaged in reflection in most states for arranging his worldly affairs; his livelihood is not set in order, nor are the means of passing his life organized, except by that. And his survival to this state indicates the existence of this action from him, so it indicates that in his denial he is obstinate, seeking thereby to slip away from the yoke of obligation and to escape from the demands of command and prohibition.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a two-part proof from human nature (fitra) and common practice. First, the author argues that it is instinctive for humans to resort to reasoning when faced with uncertainty. This is a natural disposition (jibillah), like using eyesight for visible ambiguities. Therefore, the connection between reasoning and knowledge is innate. Second, the author observes that all rational people use reasoning in their daily affairs to distinguish benefit from harm. Even the person who denies the validity of reasoning in theological matters still uses it in worldly matters; otherwise his life would be impossible. Thus his denial is not genuine but obstinate, motivated by a desire to escape religious obligations. The author concludes that such a denier is a stubborn opponent (mu'anid).

Key Terms
النظر
الجبلة
الخلق
المعاند
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The denier genuinely rejects all use of reason.
Correction: The author shows that the denier uses reason extensively for worldly affairs, so his rejection of reason in theology is selective and obstinate, not principled.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the denier of reflection is 'obstinate'?
A) Because he refuses to use his senses
B) Because he relies on reflection in worldly matters but denies it in religion
C) Because he admits intellect is a source of knowledge
D) Because he is a sophist

Key Takeaway
The natural human reliance on reason in practical life proves that reflection is a valid path to knowledge, and denying it in theology is just an excuse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The denier in the passage escapes from the 'yoke of obligation' by denying reason. In what area of your life are you tempted to deny what you know to avoid a duty? Write down one specific step to confront that denial today.
?
If the instinct to reason is as natural as sight, then ignoring it in matters of ultimate truth is like closing your eyes and claiming darkness. Where have you willingly closed your mind to avoid the light of knowledge? What will you do this week to open your eyes?
