Self-Refutation of the Denier of Nazar
The author proves that denial of nazar is self-refuting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the denial of nazar (rational reflection) necessarily affirms it, because the denier must use nazar to make his claim.

Core Idea
Denying nazar is a self-refuting act that logically affirms what it denies.

The author presents a powerful logical argument that any attempt to deny rational reflection (nazar) is self-defeating. The denier must provide a proof, and the only possible proofs either fail or themselves rely on nazar. Thus, the denial actually establishes the necessity of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the argument flow naturally before dissecting it.
The passage is dense but self-contained.
2
Identify the three possible sources of knowledge
The denier might claim his denial is based on senses, intuition, or reason. Notice how the author systematically refutes each possibility.
3
Trace the refutation of each claim
Follow how the author shows that senses and intuition fail, and that the accusation of obstinacy is symmetrical and leads only to nazar as the remaining method.
Pay attention to the phrase 'Nothing remains for him except reflection.'
4
Understand the conclusion
Grasp the final point: nazar is established by consensus and by the opponent's own admission, and the self-refutation argument applies to any denial that ends up affirming its subject.
This is an ilzam (self-refutation) argument, a classic dialectical tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن النظر في نفيه إثباته، إذ نافي النظر ينفيه به، إذ ليس له دليل سوى النظر، فإنه لو ادعى معرفة صحة نفيه بالحواس أو بالبداهة، لطولب بإحالة ذلك إلى حاسة معينة، فيظهر حينئذ تعنته، وكذلك لو ادعى معرفته بالبداهة، ويقال له: إنا أرباب الحواس السليمة، والعقول الوافرة، فما بالنا لا نعرف؟ فإن قال: إنكم تعرفون ذلك غير أنكم تعاندون لا ينفصل ممن يقلب عليه الأمر ويقول له: إنك تعرف صحة قولي وفساد قول نفسك، غير أنك تعاند، فلم يبق له إلا النظر، وبه استدل حيث قال: إن قضاياه متناقضة فدل أن نافيه مثبت ضرورة، ومثبته يثبته أيضا، فكان ثابتا بإجماع العقلاء، وكذا باتفاق الخصم الذي هو من أصدق الشهادات، وكذا كل شيء في نفيه إثباته كان ثابتا ضرورة
And because reflection (nazar) is proven by its own denial, for the denier of reflection denies it by means of reflection, since he has no proof other than reflection. For if he claimed to know the correctness of his denial through the senses or through intuition (badiha), he would be required to refer it to a specific sense, whereupon his obstinacy would appear. Likewise, if he claimed to know it through intuition, it would be said to him: We are possessors of sound senses and abundant intellects; why do we not know it? If he says: You know that but you are obstinate, he is no different from someone who turns the matter around and says: You know the correctness of my statement and the unsoundness of your own statement, but you are obstinate. So nothing remains for him except reflection, and by it he argued, for he said: Its propositions are contradictory. Thus it indicates that its denier affirms it necessarily, and its affirmer also affirms it. So it is established by consensus of the rational people, and likewise by the agreement of the opponent, which is among the truest testimonies. And likewise, everything whose denial implies its affirmation is necessarily established.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a self-refutation (ilzam) argument. The author argues that any attempt to deny nazar is itself an act of nazar, because the denier must provide a proof for his denial. The only possible proofs are sensory, intuitive, or rational. Sensory proof fails because it would require specifying a sense that could disprove a rational method. Intuitive proof fails because the denier cannot claim others have the same intuition if they do not. If the denier then claims others are obstinate, the argument turns against him. Therefore, the only remaining method is rational inference, which the denier uses when he says his propositions contradict others. Hence, the denial itself affirms nazar. The author concludes that nazar is established by consensus and by the opponent's own admission. This is a powerful logical argument that turns the opponent's position against itself.

Key Terms
النظر
النفي
الإثبات
البداهة
الحواس
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that self-refutation is a psychological contradiction or a mere rhetorical trick.
Correction: The self-refutation is a logical necessity: the denial of nazar cannot be coherently stated without presupposing nazar, so the denial itself proves its truth.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if the denier of nazar claims his denial is based on intuition (badiha)?
A) He is believed
B) He is asked why others don't have that intuition
C) His denial is accepted
D) He must provide a new method

Key Takeaway
The denial of nazar is self-refuting because it requires nazar to be made, thus nazar is necessarily established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a reasoned argument simply because it challenged a comfortable belief? That dismissal itself relies on reason. Will you commit to examining your own beliefs with the same nazar you demand of others? Write one area where you tend to avoid rational scrutiny and choose to investigate it this week.
?
The denier of nazar is condemned by his own act of denial. Consider a time you accused someone of being irrational. Were you using the very tool you denied them? How can you ensure your criticism of others does not rebound? Resolve to check your own reasoning before dismissing another's.
?
The passage shows that no alternative (senses, intuition, obstinacy) escapes the need for nazar. What in your life are you relying on that you have not subjected to nazar—perhaps a habit, a tradition, or a prejudice? Are you willing to set aside time today to apply rational reflection to that matter, trusting that Allah has given you intellect for guidance?

Answering the Objection: The Validity of Nazar Proven by Its Own Practice
The author answers the objection to nazar's validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the opponent's own reasoning to prove the validity of nazar.

Core Idea
The objection itself is proof of the method's validity.

The author anticipates a natural challenge: if nazar is the method for knowing theological truths, then how do we know that nazar itself is valid? In this passage, he offers a clever reply that turns the objection inside out—using the opponent's own question as evidence and citing the universal experience that reasoning leads to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two-part structure of the answer.
The passage ends mid-sentence in the source, but the argument is complete.
2
Identify the two arguments
First, the author says the very question is nazar, so the opponent implicitly accepts its validity. Second, we have experienced that nazar leads to knowledge, just as the opponent uses it in worldly affairs.
3
Reflect on the non-circular nature
Consider why this answer is not circular: it uses the opponent's own behavior and common experience as evidence, not the method itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي سؤالهم أنكم بم عرفتم ثبوته؟ قلنا : هذا منكم نظر فاشتغالكم به إقرار منكم بثبوته، ثم قال: عرفناه بكونه مفضيا إلى العلم إذ سلكناه فأفضى بنا إليه، كما سلكتم أنتم فأفضى بكم إلى العلم في أكثر أمور دنياكم وأفضى بكم بزعمكم إلى
Their remaining question is: 'By what do you know its validity?' We say: This (question) from you is nazar; your engagement in it is an acknowledgment from you of its validity. Then he said: We know it by it leading to knowledge, when we traversed it and it led us to knowledge, just as you traversed it and it led you to knowledge in most of your worldly affairs, and it led you, in your claim, to
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses an anticipated objection: 'How do you know that nazar is a valid method for acquiring knowledge?' The author provides a twofold answer. First, the very act of asking this question is itself an act of nazar—the objector is reasoning about the validity of reasoning. By engaging in nazar, he implicitly acknowledges its validity. Second, the author says that we know nazar's validity through experience: we have actually used it and it has led us to certain knowledge, just as the objector uses it in his daily worldly affairs and it leads him to practical knowledge. The phrase 'in your claim' (بزعمكم) is a rhetorical slight, implying that the opponent's claim is not fully honest. This answer effectively turns the objection back on the objector, using his own practice as evidence.

Key Terms
النظر
الإقرار
الإفضاء
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's answer is circular; it assumes the validity of nazar to prove its validity.
Correction: The answer is not circular. It uses the opponent's own behavior as evidence and cites common experience, not the method itself.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the question 'How do you know nazar is valid?'?
A) By citing revelation
B) By saying the question itself is nazar
C) By avoiding the question
D) By appealing to authority

Key Takeaway
The validity of nazar is proven by the fact that the opponent engages in reasoning when questioning it, and by the consistent experience that reasoning leads to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The author points out that the objector uses nazar in everyday life but hesitates in theology. In what areas of your daily life do you rely on your mind, yet hesitate to rely on it for matters of faith? Pray to Allah to remove that hesitation and grant you certainty.

The Self-Refutation of Denying Nazar
Imam Nasafi proves the necessity of nazar through self-refutation of its denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi proves the necessity of nazar by showing that its denial is self-refuting.

Core Idea
Denying nazar requires nazar, so its denial affirms it.

How can someone deny the very tool of reasoning when every denial itself relies on reasoning? Imam Nasafi presents a powerful argument that the denial of nazar (speculative reasoning) is self-defeating. In this lesson, we will explore his dialectical method and see why nazar is established necessarily by the consensus of rational people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the opening line: 'the denial of nazar is its affirmation.' This paradoxical statement is the key. Understand that the denier must use nazar to deny it.
2
Follow the dialectic alternatives
Nasafi considers possible grounds for denial: senses or intuition (badāha). See how each alternative is challenged, leaving no escape.
The challenges force the denier to either specify a sense or face that others with sound senses don't know.
3
Note the reversal argument
If the denier accuses others of obstinacy, the argument can be reversed. This shows the weakness of the denial.
Reversal: the accuser can be accused of the same.
4
See the forced conclusion
The denier is left with only nazar as a tool. Thus, in denying nazar, he affirms it. The passage concludes that nazar is established by consensus of rational people.
5
Understand the method of demand
Nasafi introduces a direct question: 'Did you negate nazar by nazar or by something else?' This seals the argument: either admit nazar or require proof that again needs nazar.
This method is called 'tariq al-muṭālaba'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن النظر في نفيه إثباته، إذ نافي النظر ينفيه به، إذ ليس له دليل سوى النظر، فإنه لو ادعى معرفة صحة نفيه بالحواس أو بالبداهة، لطولب بإحالة ذلك إلى حاسة معينة، فيظهر حينئذ تعنته، وكذلك لو ادعى معرفته بالبداهة، ويقال له: إنا أرباب الحواس السليمة، والعقول الوافرة، فما بالنا لا نعرف؟ فإن قال: إنكم تعرفون ذلك غير أنكم تعاندون لا ينفصل ممن يقلب عليه الأمر ويقول له: إنك تعرف صحة قولي وفساد قول نفسك، غير أنك تعاند، فلم يبق له إلا النظر، وبه استدل حيث قال: إن قضاياه متناقضة فدل أن نافيه مثبت ضرورة، ومثبته يثبته أيضا، فكان ثابتا بإجماع العقلاء، وكذا كل شيء في نفيه إثباته كان ثابتا ضرورة، ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريقة المطالبة فيقال له انتفى النظر بالنظر أم بغير النظر؟ فإن قال: بالنظر فقد أقر بثبوته، وإن قال بغير النظر طولب بذلك على حساب ما مر
And because [the act of] nazar (speculative reasoning) in its denial is its affirmation, since the denier of nazar denies it by means of it, for he has no proof other than nazar. For if he claimed knowledge of the validity of his denial through the senses or through intuition (badāha), he would be required to refer that to a particular sense, then his obstinacy would become apparent. Likewise, if he claimed knowledge of it through intuition, it would be said to him: 'We are possessors of sound senses and ample intellects; why do we not know?' If he says: 'You know that but you are obstinate,' he cannot be distinguished from one who reverses the argument and says to him: 'You know the truth of my statement and the falsity of your own statement, but you are obstinate.' So nothing remains for him but nazar, and by it he argued when he said: 'His premises are contradictory,' so it indicates that its denier is an affirmer of necessity, and its affirmer also affirms it, so it is established by the consensus of the rational people. Likewise, everything in whose denial is its affirmation is established necessarily. And this speech proceeds according to the method of demand, so it is said to him: 'Did you negate nazar by nazar or by something else? If he says: 'By nazar,' he has acknowledged its existence; and if he says: 'By something else,' he is required to provide that according to what has passed.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of nazar (speculative reasoning) through the self-refutation of its denial. The argument proceeds as follows: The denier of nazar has no proof other than nazar itself, because if he claimed sense perception or intuition, he would be challenged to specify the sense or would face the objection that others with sound senses do not perceive it. If he claims others know but are obstinate, the argument can be reversed. Thus the denier is forced to use nazar to deny nazar, which means his denial affirms it. The author then introduces a dialectical method: ask the denier whether he negated nazar by nazar or by something else. If by nazar, he admits its existence; if by something else, he must prove that something else, which again requires nazar. The conclusion is that nazar is established necessarily by the consensus of rational people, and any concept whose denial is self-referential is necessarily true.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
بداهة (badāha)
تعنت (obstinacy)
مطالبة (demand method)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That denying nazar is a rational position independent of reasoning.
Correction: The passage shows that any denial of nazar must itself use nazar, making the position self-refuting.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for the necessity of nazar in this passage?
A) Nazar leads to knowledge in worldly affairs.
B) Denying nazar requires nazar, so it is self-defeating.
C) All rational premises are non-contradictory.
D) Nazar is innate in human beings.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is necessarily established because its denial self-destructs; every rational person must affirm it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you dismissed a truth claim without examining it, assuming your intuition was sufficient? How does this passage expose the hidden nazar in that very dismissal?
?
If nazar is inescapable, are you using it carefully and sincerely to seek truth, or are you using it carelessly to defend positions you already hold? What would change if you treated every denial as an affirmation of the very tool you're using?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with the argument that you 'just knew' something without reflection. Would that excuse hold? How does this lesson press you to take intellectual responsibility for what you believe?

Three Positive Proofs for the Validity of Nazar
Imam Nasafi gives three positive proofs that nazar is valid and leads to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs Imam Nasafi offers to establish the validity of nazar (speculative reasoning) and how he refutes the denier's objection.

Core Idea
Nazar is self-validating: it leads to knowledge, is innate, and can prove itself without circularity.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to the question: 'How do you know that nazar is valid?' He offers three distinct positive proofs: first, nazar leads to knowledge in worldly matters; second, people are naturally disposed to use it in difficult situations; and third, a self-referential argument shows that nazar can prove itself. This lesson will walk through each proof and the concluding refutation of the denier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the question-and-answer structure at the beginning.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage presents three positive proofs for nazar: experiential, innate disposition, and self-referential. Locate each one in the text.
The first proof is about knowledge in worldly affairs, the second about human nature, and the third is a logical analogy.
3
Understand the self-referential analogy
The third proof uses an analogy: 'every speech is an accident' applies to itself. Compare this to how a specific nazar can prove nazar in general.
This shows that self-reference is not automatically invalid when supported by evidence.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Notice the conclusion: the denier's negation of nazar by means of nazar is an impossible self-contradiction. Consider why this is so.
This ties back to the first proof: even denial uses nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي سؤالهم أنكم بم عرفتم ثبوته؟ قلنا: هذا منكم نظر فاشتغالكم به إقرار منكم بثبوته، ثم قال: عرفناه بكونه مفضيا إلى العلم إذ سلكناه فأفضى بنا إليه، كما سلكتم أنتم فأفضى بكم إلى العلم في أكثر أمور دنياكم وأفضى بكم بزعمكم إلى العلم بأنه ليس ثابت إذ نفيتموه به وعرفنا أيضا بكون الخلائق مجبولين عليه إذا حزبهم أمور يتعذر عليهم الوقوف عليها بالحواس والبداهة، ويقال أيضا: عرفنا النظر في الجملة بنوع نظر داخل في الجملة مفض إلى العلم فثبت صحة هذا النظر، الذي أدانا إلى كون النظر في الجملة ثابتا إذ هو من جملة النظر، كما لو قال إنسان: كل قول محدث عرض، كان هذا القول إخباراً أنه بنفسه عرض لأنه إخبار عن أن كل قول محدث عرضي، وهذا القول من جملة القول المحدث، لو قال: كل عرض مفتقر إلى محل، أو قال: كل عرض مستحيل البقاء، كان هذا إخباراً عن نفسه، فدل أن هذا جائز غير ممتنع فيجب القول به عند قيام الدليل، قام الدليل على ما مر فأما نفي شيء ما بإثباته فمحال وأنتم نفيتم النظر بالنظر فكان فيه نفيه بإثباته
Their question remains: 'By what did you know its existence?' We say: 'This [question] from you is nazar, so your engagement in it is an acknowledgment from you of its existence.' Then he said: 'We know it by virtue of its leading to knowledge, for we pursued it and it led us to knowledge, just as you pursued it and it led you to knowledge in most of your worldly affairs, and led you, according to your claim, to knowledge that it is not established when you denied it by it. And we also know it by virtue of people being innately disposed to it when difficult matters befall them that they cannot grasp through the senses and intuition. And it is also said: We know nazar in general by a type of nazar that is included in the general [category] which leads to knowledge, so the validity of this nazar is established, which led us to the fact that nazar in general is established, for it is part of nazar. Just as if a man says: 'Every speech is an accident (ʿaraḍ),' this statement is an information that it is itself an accident because it is information that every speech is an accidental speech, and this statement is part of speech that is accidental. If he said: 'Every accident is dependent on a substrate,' or said: 'Every accident is impossible to persist,' this is information about itself. So this indicates that this is permissible and not impossible, so it is necessary to affirm it when proof is established. The proof has been established as mentioned. As for negating something by its affirmation, it is impossible, and you have negated nazar by nazar, so in it there is negation by affirmation.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents three positive proofs for the validity of nazar, responding to the question 'By what did you know its existence?' The first proof is experiential: nazar leads to knowledge in worldly matters, and even deniers use it to deny its own existence. The second proof is innate disposition: when people face difficult issues beyond senses and intuition, they naturally turn to nazar. The third proof is a self-referential argument: we know nazar in general through a specific act of nazar that is part of it, analogous to a statement like 'every speech is an accident' which reflects on itself. The author concludes that just as such self-referential statements are valid when supported by proof, so too is the affirmation of nazar through nazar itself. He then reiterates that the denier's negation of nazar by means of nazar is an impossible self-contradiction.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
بداهة (badāha)
عرض (accident)
مفض إلى العلم (leading to knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using nazar to prove nazar is circular reasoning.
Correction: The self-referential argument is analogous to statements like 'every speech is an accident' which apply to themselves, and such self-reference is valid when supported by proof.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the proofs for nazar mentioned in this passage?
A) Nazar leads to knowledge in worldly affairs.
B) People are innately disposed to use nazar in difficult situations.
C) Nazar is proven by a self-referential argument.
D) Nazar is known through direct sensory perception.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi establishes the validity of nazar through three independent proofs: experiential, innate, and self-referential, showing that nazar is both effective and logically sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you turn to nazar (reasoned reflection) or do you rely on feelings or authority? Are you willing to admit that your very act of questioning is already an acknowledgment of nazar?
?
The passage shows that people naturally use nazar in worldly affairs. Why do you hesitate to apply the same careful reasoning to matters of the unseen? What would change if you committed to consistent nazar in your religious life?
?
You have just seen that even denying nazar requires nazar. This means your intellect cannot escape its own dependence on reasoned thought. How will this truth reshape your approach to your doubts and your relationship with the proofs of Islam?

Why Rational Premises Are Never Contradictory
Nasafi refutes the claim that rational premises are contradictory and identifies the true causes of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rational premises themselves are never contradictory; contradictions arise only from flaws in the inquirer's method, not from reason itself.

Core Idea
Rational premises are never contradictory; only faulty inquiry causes error.

A common objection against rational inquiry is that rational premises often contradict one another. Imam Nasafi addresses this directly, explaining that the fault lies not with reason but with the reasoner. He clarifies that when the conditions of proper inquiry are met, error is impossible. This lesson explores his refutation and the importance of objective inquiry free from personal desire.

1

This reinforces the core idea that reason is reliable when used correctly.

The passage introduces an example that is cut off, but the main refutation is clear.

Why Reason Never Contradicts Itself
Nasafi refutes the claim that rational propositions are contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that rational propositions are never contradictory and where error actually originates.

Core Idea
Reason is never the problem; the inquirer's shortcoming is.

This passage addresses a common objection against rational theology: that rational arguments often contradict each other, making reason unreliable. Nasafi responds by shifting the blame from reason itself to the way it is used. Let's examine his rebuttal closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the structure: an objection followed by a refutation.
Notice the emphatic 'qatt' (never) in Nasafi's denial.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection is that most rational propositions are contradictory. Nasafi's answer is a flat denial and an explanation of the true cause of error.
He doesn't just reject the claim; he provides an alternative account.
3
Understand 'fulfilling the conditions of inquiry'
Nasafi says falling into falsehood is due to taqsir (shortcoming) and following hawa (whim) instead of aql (intellect). Fulfilling the conditions (shara'it al-nazar) prevents error.
This is a key concept in kalam: proper method safeguards truth.
4

If reason itself is reliable, then disagreements among rational thinkers are due to human failure, not reason's flaws. This defends the validity of rational theology.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. The opponents claim that most rational propositions (qadaya al-aql) are contradictory, implying that rational inquiry (nazar) is unreliable. Nasafi answers by denying the premise: rational propositions are never (qatt) contradictory. This leads to conjecture (zann) mistaken for certainty. If the inquirer had used the correct method and verified each premise, his inquiry would never lead to error. Thus the fault lies in the inquirer, not in reason itself.

The Magian's Error: How One False Premise Leads to False Belief
Nasafi gives the example of a Magian to show how error in one premise leads to false belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single error in reasoning, following whim instead of intellect, can lead to a false theological conclusion, as illustrated by Nasafi's example of the Magian.

Core Idea
One mistaken premise, driven by whim, can demolish many correct ones.

Imam Nasafi provides a vivid example to show that rational inquiry (nazar) only yields correct knowledge when every premise is examined with intellect, not whim. He walks through the reasoning of a Magian who gets many premises right but errs in one judgment, leading him to embrace dualism. This lesson walks through that example and extracts the principle that inquiry must be governed by intellect throughout.

Guided Path
1
Read the Magian's reasoning process
Read the passage to follow the sequence of premises the Magian considered.
Notice how many premises he got correct.
2
Identify the correct premises
List the premises the Magian got right: world's origination, existence of evil, need for originator, wisdom of Maker.
3
Spot the erroneous premise
Identify the premise where he went wrong: judging the creation of evil as folly, based on whim.
This is the key error.
4
Understand the consequence and the principle
See how that one error led to a second creator, and note the condition for nazar to lead to knowledge.
The passage ends with a general principle about inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثاله: أن المجوسي نظر في أقسام العالم، فوجدها محدثة، فاعتقد حدوثها وهو صحيح، ثم وجد في العالم الشرور والقبايح والأقذار والأنتان فاعتقد ثبوتها وهو صحيح، ثم اعتقد حدوثها وهو أيضا صحيح، ثم اعتقد أن المحدث لا بد له من المحدث وهو صحيح، ثم اعتقد أن صانع العالم حكيم وهو صحيح، ثم اعتقد أن إيجاد الشرور والقبايح والأقذار والأنتان سفه وهذا خطأ، واعتقد أنها لما كانت محدثة فلابد لها من محدث والصانع حكيم لا يسفه لم يخلق هذه الأشياء، ولابد لضرورة اقتضاء المحدثات محدثا أن يكون سوى الباري جل وعلا، صانع سفيه يتولى تخليق هذه الأشياء فوقع في الباطل لنظره في مقدمة واحدة بهواه دون عقله، ولو تأمل بعقله لعرف أن إيجاد هذه الأشياء حكمة على ما نبين إن شاء الله تعالى. فلم يقع في الباطل، والنظر يكون مقضيا إلى العلم بشريطة حصوله في جميع المقدمات بالعقل دون الهوى وثبت أن لا تناقض في قضايا العقل، ولا خلل في كون النظر مفضيا إلى العلم عند وجوده بشرطه
An example of that: The Magian examined the divisions of the world and found them originated, so he believed in their origination—and that is correct. Then he found in the world evils, ugly things, filth, and stench, so he believed in their existence—and that is correct. Then he believed in their origination—and that is also correct. Then he believed that the originated necessarily has an originator—and that is correct. Then he believed that the Maker of the world is wise—and that is correct. Then he believed that bringing into existence evils, ugly things, filth, and stench is folly—and this is an error. And he believed that since they were originated, they must have an originator, and the Wise Maker does not act foolishly, so He did not create these things; and by necessity of originated things requiring an originator, there must be other than the Blessed and Exalted Creator—a foolish maker who undertakes the creation of these things. So he fell into falsehood because of his inquiry in one premise following his whim without his intellect. Had he reflected with his intellect, he would have known that bringing these things into existence is wisdom, as we will explain, God willing. Thus he did not fall into falsehood—and inquiry leads to knowledge on condition that it occurs in all premises by intellect without whim. And it is established that there is no contradiction in rational propositions and no defect in inquiry leading to knowledge when it exists under its condition.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an illustrative example (mithal) demonstrating the previous point. The Magian (al-majusi) reasoned correctly about many premises: the world's origination, the existence of evil, the need for an originator, the wisdom of the maker. But he made one error: he judged that creating evil is 'folly' (safah) because he followed his whim (hawa) instead of his intellect ('aql). This single false premise led him to posit a second, foolish creator—resulting in dualism. The author notes that if he had used his intellect, he would have seen wisdom (hikma) in creating evil. The conclusion reaffirms that inquiry (nazar) leads to knowledge only when all premises are investigated with intellect, not whim. The error was not in nazar itself but in the defective premise.

Misconception: That the Magian's error proves that rational inquiry into divine attributes is invalid.
Correction: The Magian's error was not in using rational inquiry, but in allowing whim to override intellect in one premise. Proper inquiry (nazar) with sound premises leads to correct knowledge.

Defending Nazar: Self-Refutation and Analogy from Sense Perception
Nasafi defends nazar through self-refutation and analogy from sensory error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi defends the validity of rational inquiry (nazar) against opponents by showing that their objection is self-refuting and that occasional errors do not invalidate a method.

Core Idea
Even a single error does not destroy the validity of nazar, just as false reports or optical illusions do not destroy testimony or sight.

In this passage, Nasafi counters critics who claim that rational inquiry (nazar) is unreliable because some reasoning leads to false conclusions. He employs two strategies: first, a self-refutation showing that the opponents' own argument is itself a form of nazar, and second, an analogy demonstrating that occasional errors in a source of knowledge do not negate its overall validity. This lesson unpacks these arguments and their significance for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Nasafi's concise, dialectical style.
Pay attention to the conditional 'لو كان...' structure.
2
Understand the self-refutation argument
Identify how Nasafi turns the opponents' critique back on themselves: if all nazar is invalid, then their own statement 'this speech is nazar' would be invalid.
This is a classic ilzam (binding argument) in kalam.
3
Analyze the analogy from sense perception
See how Nasafi extends the logic: if occasional error in nazar makes it invalid, then testimony and sense perception would also be invalid due to occasional lies and optical illusions. Since no one rejects those, nazar should not be rejected.
The analogy functions by parity of reasoning.
4
Reflect on the role of the 'distinguishing criterion'
Notice that Nasafi mentions a criterion (qanun) to separate sound from unsound reasoning. This implies that nazar is not an undifferentiated method but one with standards.
This anticipates the need for a methodology like logic (mantiq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: هذا الكلام نظر، فيجب أن يكون باطلا، ثم يقال لهم: لو كان فساد ما فسد من النظر والاستدلال يوجب فساد كل نظر مع قيام الدليل على صحته، وثبوت القانون المميز بين صحيحه وفاسده، لكان ينبغي أن يخرج الخبر والحس من أسباب المعارف، لوجود الكذب في الخبر والغلط في الحس عند البعد إذ يرى كبير الجثة صغيرا عنده وكذا عند الآفة، إذ الأحول يرى الشيء شيئين وحيث لم يبطل الخبر والحس لم يبطل العقل والنظر والله الموفق.
Then we say: This speech is inquiry, so it must be invalid. Then it is said to them: If the corruption of what has been corrupted of inquiry and deduction necessitates the corruption of every inquiry—despite the existence of proof of its validity and the establishment of the criterion distinguishing between its sound and corrupt—then it would be necessary to exclude testimony and sense perception from the causes of knowledge, due to the existence of falsehood in testimony and error in sense perception at a distance, when one sees a large body as small, and likewise due to a defect, when the squinting person sees one thing as two. And since testimony and sense perception are not invalidated, reason and inquiry are not invalidated. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments. First, a self-refutation (ilzam): if all nazar is invalid (as opponents claim), then their own statement—'this speech is nazar'—would itself be invalid, which is absurd. Second, an analogy (qiyas): if occasional error in nazar made the entire method invalid, then by the same logic, testimony (khabar) and sense perception (hiss) would also be invalid because they contain errors (lies in testimony, optical illusions in sight). But no one discards these sources of knowledge entirely. Therefore, nazar remains valid. The author emphasizes that there is a distinguishing criterion (qanun) to separate sound reasoning from unsound.

Key Terms
هذا الكلام
الفساد
الخبر
الحس
الآفة
الأحول
القانون المميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi is rejecting nazar itself.
Correction: He is actually defending nazar against wholesale rejection by showing that the opponents' objection is self-refuting and that occasional errors do not invalidate the method.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Nasafi use to defend nazar?
A) Analogy from divine revelation
B) Analogy from sense perception and testimony
C) Analogy from historical reports
D) Analogy from dreams

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends nazar by showing that its rejection is self‑defeating and that occasional error is not a valid reason to abandon a reliable method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith stemming from a flawed argument, do you then reject all rational inquiry, or do you seek the criterion (qanun) to distinguish sound reasoning from corrupt? How has Nasafi's defense exposed your own tendency to discard a method because of one failure?
?
Recall a time you dismissed an entire line of evidence because of a single mistake you or someone else made. How does the analogy with testimony and sense perception challenge you to reconsider that dismissal? Will you now practice a more measured response?
?
If the opponent's very objection to nazar is self-refuting, what does that say about the objections you raise against divine commands? Are your own reasons against obedience consistent, or do they collapse under their own weight?

Objection and Answer: Clarity of Knowledge
Nasafi addresses the objection that theoretical knowledge lacks clarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that theoretical knowledge lacks the clarity of necessary knowledge and Nasafi's initial response that proper inquiry removes this difference.

Core Idea
Proper inquiry yields the same certainty as intuitive knowledge.

This lesson presents an objection raised against the possibility of certain theoretical knowledge. The opponents argue that necessary knowledge (like intuitive and sensory truths) is self-evident, while theoretical knowledge remains hidden. Nasafi begins his response by denying that such a difference holds when the conditions of inquiry are fulfilled. This passage sets the stage for a defense of the validity of nazar.

1
Read the passage carefully

Identify the objection's logic

3
Analyze Nasafi's reply

The answer is cut off, but the intended point is that proper nazar yields certainty equal to necessary knowledge.

The Clarity of Theoretical Knowledge
Nasafi answers the objection that theoretical knowledge is less clear than intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theoretical knowledge is not inherently less clear than intuitive knowledge, and how they differ in preservation.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge is as clear as intuition, but requires active preservation.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a common objection against theological reasoning: that theoretical knowledge is less clear and certain than intuitive knowledge. He denies the premise and explains that the real difference lies in how each type of knowledge is preserved, not in its inherent clarity. This is crucial for defending the validity of arguments about unseen matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from opponents
Start by reading the first part of the passage (Arabic and translation) where the opponents' claim is presented: that self-evident and sensory knowledge is clearer than theoretical knowledge.
Notice the terms بداهة (self-evident), محسوسات (sensibles), and نظريات (theoretical).
2
Identify Nasafi's denial
Find the sentence where Nasafi says the claim of difference in clarity is rejected. This is his initial response.
The phrase 'ممنوعة' means 'rejected'.
3
Understand the condition for equal clarity
Read the next part where Nasafi explains that when one fulfills the conditions of reasoning (شرائط النظر), theoretical knowledge becomes as clear as intuitive knowledge.
This is a key point: the clarity depends on proper reasoning.
4
Grasp the distinction in preservation
Examine the later part where Nasafi says the real difference is not in clarity or essence, but in how each type of knowledge is preserved. Intuitive knowledge is preserved by Allah without human effort, while theoretical knowledge requires recollection of proofs and repelling doubts.
The terms تذكر الأدلة and دفع الشبهة are important.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final sentence that summarizes that in terms of knowledge, clarity, and hiddenness, they do not differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتعلق به الخصوم أيضا، أن حال واحد منا البداهة والمحسوسات يخالف حاله مع النظريات في التجلي والكشف، وإذا كان للضروريات زيادة كشف وتجلي، كان بمقابلته للنظريات خفاء، والخفاء ينافي العلم، ويجاب عنه: أن دعوى هذه المفارقة في الجلاء والخفاء ممنوعة، فإن من نظر واستوفي شرائط النظر كانت حاله النظريات كحاله مع الضروريات في الجلاء والخفاء وإنما مع يقال أحدهما أخفي من الآخر في التعارف، لتفاوت طريقيهما في الخفاء والانكشاف لا لتفاوتهما في أنفسهما غير أنهما يفترقان من وجه، وهو أن بقاء العلم في الضروريات لا يقف على فعل في هذا العالم، كما كان حصوله لا يقف على فعل من جهته، بل وجد بتولي الله تعالى اختراعه في العبد من غير صنع من قبله، وكذا يتولى إبقاءه من غير صنع من العبد والنظري كما لم يحصل إلا بصنع وجد من قبل العالم وهو النظر والاستدلال، لا يبقى إلا بصنع وجد من قبله وهو تذكر الأدلة ودفع الشبهة المعترضة، فأما في أصل العلم والجلاء والخفاء فلا يفترقان، والله الموفق. وفي المسألة كلام كثير غير أن كتابنا هذا يضيق عن استيفاء ذلك، والله الموفق.
And also among what the opponents bring up is that the state of each of us with self-evident things (badahiyyat) and sensibles (mahsusat) differs from his state with theoretical things (nazariyyat) in clarity and disclosure. And if necessary things have additional disclosure and clarity, then theoretical things would correspondingly have hiddenness, and hiddenness contradicts knowledge. The answer to it is: The claim of this difference in clarity and hiddenness is rejected. For whoever engages in reasoning (nazar) and fulfills the conditions of reasoning, his state with theoretical things is like his state with necessary things in clarity and hiddenness. It is only said that one is more hidden than the other in common usage, due to the difference of their two paths in hiddenness and unveiling, not due to a difference in themselves. However, they differ in one respect: the preservation of knowledge of necessary things does not depend on an action in this world, just as its attainment did not depend on an action on his part; rather it occurs by Allah's undertaking its creation in the servant without any action from him, and likewise He undertakes its preservation without any action from the servant. As for theoretical knowledge, just as it is not attained except by an action from the learned person, namely reasoning and inference, so it does not remain except by an action from him, namely recollecting the proofs and repelling the objection that arises. As for the essence of knowledge, clarity, and hiddenness, they do not differ. And God is the granter of success. And there is much talk on the issue, but our book is too limited to exhaust that. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection from opponents who argue that self-evident and sensory knowledge is clearer than theoretical knowledge, implying that theoretical knowledge is less certain. The author answers by denying the premise: when reasoning is performed correctly with all conditions fulfilled, the resulting knowledge is as clear as intuitive knowledge. The apparent difference in clarity is due to the different paths of acquisition, not a difference in the knowledge itself. However, the author notes a real difference: intuitive knowledge does not require constant effort to be preserved, while theoretical knowledge requires ongoing recollection of proofs and warding off doubts. This difference is in maintenance, not in initial clarity or certainty. The author concludes by saying that more could be said but the book is limited.

شرائط النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theoretical knowledge is inherently less clear and certain than intuitive knowledge.
Correction: Nasafi argues that when reasoning conditions are fulfilled, theoretical knowledge is equally clear; the difference is only in how it is preserved.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the difference between intuitive and theoretical knowledge?
A) Theoretical knowledge is less clear
B) Intuitive knowledge does not require effort to preserve
C) Theoretical knowledge is not real knowledge
D) They differ in essence

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge achieved through proper reasoning is as clear as intuitive knowledge; they differ only in that intuitive knowledge is preserved without effort while theoretical knowledge requires ongoing recollection and defense against doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there areas of your faith that feel unclear or distant? Have you honestly fulfilled the conditions of proper reasoning (شرائط النظر) to bring them to clarity, or have you settled for vagueness?
?
This passage warns that theoretical knowledge does not remain without an action from you. What specific doubt or objection have you faced recently? Did you actively repel it (دفع الشبهة) or let it linger? What will you do differently starting today?

Equality and Difference of Necessary and Theoretical Knowledge
The author compares necessary and theoretical knowledge in clarity and dependence on human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that necessary and theoretical knowledge are equal in intrinsic clarity but differ in their dependence on human acts for acquisition and persistence.

Core Idea
Necessary and theoretical knowledge are equally clear, but only theoretical knowledge requires human effort.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi addresses a common question: Is theoretical knowledge inherently less clear than necessary knowledge? The author argues that in their essence, they share the same level of clarity and obscurity. The difference lies not in the knowledge itself but in how we acquire and maintain it. Let's examine the precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's main claim: theoretical and necessary knowledge are equal in clarity.
Pay attention to the phrase 'في الجلاء والخفاء' (in clarity and obscurity).
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author says they differ. Note that necessary knowledge is acquired and preserved without any human act, while theoretical knowledge depends on reflection (naẓar) and recollection (tadhakkur).
The contrast is between 'لا يقف على فعل' (does not depend on an act) and 'إلا بصنع وجد' (except by an act).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that in essence, clarity, and obscurity, they do not differ. The difference is only in the pathway and mode of maintenance.
Reflect on why this distinction matters for theological epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر كانت حاله النظريات كحاله مع الضروريات في الجلاء والخفاء، وإنما يقال أحدهما أخفي من الآخر في التعارف، لتفاوت طريقيهما في الخفاء والانكشاف لا لتفاوتهما في أنفسهما غير أنهما يفترقان من وجه، وهو أن بقاء العلم في الضروريات لا يقف على فعل في هذا العالم، كما كان حصوله لا يقف على فعل من جهته، بل وجد بتولي الله تعالى اختراعه في العبد من غير صنع من قبله، وكذا يتولى إبقاءه من غير صنع من العبد، والنظري كما لم يحصل إلا بصنع وجد من قبل العالم وهو النظر والاستدلال، لا يبقى إلا بصنع وجد من قبله وهو تذكر الأدلة ودفع الشبهة المعترضة، فأما في أصل العلم والجلاء والخفاء فلا يفترقان، والله الموفق. وفي المسألة كلام كثير غير أن كتابنا هذا يضيق عن استيفاء ذلك، والله الموفق.
The theoretical (nazarī) is in the same state as the necessary (ḍarūrī) in terms of clarity and obscurity. It is only said that one is more hidden than the other in common parlance due to the difference in their pathways in obscurity and disclosure, not due to a difference in themselves. However, they differ in one respect: the persistence of necessary knowledge does not depend on an act in this world, just as its acquisition did not depend on an act from the person; rather, it came about by Allah's undertaking its creation in the servant without any action from him. Similarly, He undertakes its preservation without any action from the servant. As for theoretical knowledge, just as it was not acquired except by an act from the knower, namely reflection (al-naẓar) and inference (al-istidlāl), so it does not persist except by an act from him, namely recollecting the proofs (tadhakkur al-adilla) and repelling the opposing doubt (dafʿ al-shubha al-muʿtariḍa). As for the essence of knowledge and its clarity and obscurity, they do not differ. And Allah is the giver of success. There is much discussion on this issue, but this book of ours cannot accommodate its full treatment. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the relationship between necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (nazarī) knowledge. The author begins by stating that in terms of clarity and obscurity (al-jalāʾ wa al-khafāʾ), theoretical knowledge is on par with necessary knowledge. When people say one is more hidden than the other, it is only due to the different paths by which they are known, not because of any intrinsic difference. Then the author identifies one point of distinction: the persistence (baqāʾ) of necessary knowledge does not require any act from the knower in this world, just as its initial acquisition did not require the knower's act. Instead, Allah creates it directly in the servant and also preserves it without the servant's action. In contrast, theoretical knowledge is both acquired and maintained through the knower's act—namely, reflection (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl) for its acquisition, and recollecting proofs and repelling doubts for its persistence. However, the author concludes that in their essential nature as knowledge and in the quality of clarity/obscurity, they do not differ. The passage ends with the author noting that the topic is vast but cannot be covered fully in this book.

جلاء (jalāʾ)

نظر (naẓar)
استدلال (istidlāl)
تذكر الأدلة (tadhakkur al-adilla)
دفع الشبهة (dafʿ al-shubha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theoretical knowledge is inherently less clear than necessary knowledge.
Correction: The author explains that they are equal in clarity and obscurity; the perceived difference is only in their pathways and dependence on human acts.

Checkpoint
According to the author, in what respect do necessary and theoretical knowledge differ?
A) In their intrinsic clarity
B) In their dependence on human acts for acquisition and persistence
C) In their essential nature as knowledge
D) In their certainty

Key Takeaway
Necessary and theoretical knowledge are intrinsically equal in clarity, but they differ in that only theoretical knowledge requires human effort for its acquisition and persistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says theoretical knowledge only persists if you actively recall proofs and repel doubts. When was the last time you deliberately recalled a proof for Allah's existence to strengthen your faith? If it has been a while, what does that say about the state of your conviction? Will you commit to reviewing a proof today?
?
You likely feel certain about many necessary truths (like your own existence). Do you feel the same level of certainty about the truths you have reasoned to, such as the existence of God or the truth of Islam? If not, is it because you have neglected the act of reflecting on those proofs and defending them against doubts? What one doubt will you actively address this week?
?
Allah sustains necessary knowledge in you without any effort from you, but theoretical knowledge requires your constant maintenance. Are you grateful for the necessary knowledge He has given you, and are you taking responsibility for the theoretical knowledge He has enabled you to acquire? How can you make the remembrance of proofs a regular practice starting tomorrow?

The Hardship Argument for Faith's Benefit and the Sunni Response
Presents the Mu'tazila hardship argument and the Sunni tafaddul response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that faith's benefit comes from hardship, and the Sunni response that reward is a favor from Allah.

Core Idea
Reward is from Allah's favor, not from human hardship.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila claim that faith is only beneficial if it involves hardship in seeking evidence. The Sunni position, however, affirms that reward is a sheer favor from Allah, not a direct exchange for hardship. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the opening lines where the Mu'tazila state that belief without evidence is either not faith or not beneficial.
2
Understand the hardship argument
Note how they link benefit to hardship: reward corresponds to hardship, and there is no hardship in tasdiq itself only in reflection.
3
Learn the Sunni counter-position
The Sunni response is that reward is from Allah's favor (tafaddul) and He has made it correspond to tasdiq directly.
Except for cases like witnessing punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن هذا الاعتقاد ليس نفسه بإيمان أو هو إيمان غير أنه ليس بنافع ما لم يكن مبنيا على الدليل، فحينئذ يقع بيننا وبينهم كلام في أصل المسألة، ثم هؤلاء الذين يشترطون كون الاعتقاد والتصديق مبنيًّا على الدليل لكونه نافعا إن زعموا أن خروجه عن النفع إنما يكون لانعدام تحمل المشقة، والثواب يقابل تحمل المشقة، ومباشرة المكروه طلبا لمرضاة الله ولا مشقة في نفس التصديق بل المشقة في الوصول إلى التصديق بحصول العلم بالتأمل في الدليل، ولهذا لا ينفع التصديق الحاصل عند اليأس وفي الآخرة، فالكلام لعامة الفقهاء ومتكلمي أهل السنة منهم على ما مر من نيل الثواب بوعد الله تعالى تفضلا منه فيكون بمقابلة ما قابله به وجعل ذلك بمقابلة التصديق فيقابله، إلا ما خص منه كإيمان من عاين العذاب أو كان مضطرا فيه على ما بينا، فأما من يقول: إنما اشترطنا اقتران الدليل بالتصديق ليصير الاعتقاد بدونه مبنيا على الدليل إيمانا، فيحتاج إلى التكلم معهم فنقول: بأنهم يقولون: إن التصديق إذا لم يقترن به دليل، يكون مجرد اعتقاد نشأ لا عن دليل، ومثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون علما، إذ العلم المحدث لابد له من سبب ضروريا كان أو استدلاليا، ولا سبب لهذا الاعتقاد، فلم يكن علما، ولأن العلم المحدث نوعان ضروري واستدلالي لا ثالث لهما، وهذا الاعتقاد لم يثبت عن أحدهما، فليس بعلم.
For if they say: 'This belief is not itself faith, or it is faith but it is not beneficial unless it is based on evidence,' then at that point a discussion occurs between us and them concerning the root of the issue. Then those who stipulate that belief and affirmation be based on evidence in order for it to be beneficial—if they claim that its lack of benefit is only due to the absence of bearing hardship, and reward corresponds to bearing hardship, and undertaking what is disliked seeking Allah's pleasure, and there is no hardship in the very act of affirmation, rather the hardship is in reaching affirmation through attaining knowledge by reflecting on the evidence, and for this reason the affirmation that occurs at the time of despair and in the afterlife is not beneficial—then the statement of the general jurists and the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Sunnah among them is based on what has preceded concerning attaining reward by the promise of Allah Most High as a favor from Him, so it is in exchange for what He has made it correspond to, and He made it correspond to affirmation, so it corresponds to it, except what is excluded from that, like the faith of one who witnesses the punishment or is under compulsion, as we have explained. As for one who says: 'We only stipulated the conjunction of evidence with affirmation so that the belief without it becomes based on evidence as faith,' it is necessary to discuss with them, and we say: They say that when affirmation is not conjoined with evidence, it is mere belief arising not from evidence, and such belief does not constitute knowledge, because originated knowledge necessarily has a cause, whether necessary or deductive, and this belief has no cause, so it is not knowledge. And because originated knowledge is of two types: necessary and deductive, with no third type; this belief is not established by either of them, so it is not knowledge.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a multi-layered response to the Mu'tazila who condition evidence for faith to be beneficial. The author first frames the debate: if the Mu'tazila argue that belief without evidence is either not faith at all, or is faith but not beneficial unless based on evidence, then the discussion shifts to the root issue of what makes faith beneficial. The author then addresses those who claim that lack of benefit is due to absence of hardship, since reward corresponds to hardship, and there is no hardship in tasdiq itself—only in the process of reflection. Against this, the Sunni position (based on earlier discussion) is that reward is a favor from Allah (tafaddul) and He has made it correspond to tasdiq itself, except in cases of coercion or witnessing punishment. Then the author turns to those who say evidence is needed for the belief to be valid as faith. Their argument is that tasdiq without evidence is mere belief without cause, and knowledge must be either necessary (daruri) or acquired (istidlali). Since this belief has no such cause, it is not knowledge ('ilm). The author leaves this part as an argument to be addressed, implying that the Sunni response will challenge the exclusivity of these categories or the denial of knowledge in such belief. The passage thus lays out the Mu'tazila position in detail, setting up a Sunni refutation.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
إيمان
دليل
نفع
مشقة
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward is earned solely through human effort and hardship.
Correction: The Sunni position is that reward is a favor from Allah, not a direct exchange for hardship.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument in this passage, what is the source of reward for faith?
A) The affirmation itself
B) The hardship of seeking evidence
C) Allah's favor
D) The number of deeds

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response affirms that reward is a favor from Allah, directly linked to tasdiq, not contingent on hardship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that your spiritual efforts—your study, your fasting, your prayers—earn you a degree of favor from Allah, rather than being responses to His already-given grace? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that your very faith is a gift and not an achievement?
?
When you find yourself lacking deep knowledge or evidence for a certain belief, do you feel that your faith is somehow less valuable or acceptable to Allah? What does this lesson say about where the real benefit of faith lies, and how does that confront your anxiety?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded evidence for faith to be profitable; are there areas of your life where you withhold trust from Allah until you see proof? What step of tasdiq can you take today without any guarantee of worldly outcome, relying solely on His favor?

The Knowledge Argument: Tasdiq without Evidence is Not 'Ilm
Presents the Mu'tazila argument that tasdiq without evidence is not 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that tasdiq without evidence does not qualify as knowledge because it lacks a causal basis.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires a cause; tasdiq without evidence is causeless and thus not true knowledge.

In this section, the Mu'tazila present a more philosophical argument: that originated knowledge must be either necessary or acquired. Since tasdiq without evidence is neither, it cannot be considered knowledge. This lesson examines that reasoning and its implications for the definition of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
They argue that tasdiq without evidence is mere belief without a cause, and knowledge requires a cause.
2
Understand the two types of knowledge
Knowledge is either daruri (necessary) or istidlali (deductive). This belief fits neither.
3
Consider the implication
If it is not knowledge, then it cannot be beneficial faith according to their criterion.
The Sunni response is not given here but will challenge this dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن هذا الاعتقاد ليس نفسه بإيمان أو هو إيمان غير أنه ليس بنافع ما لم يكن مبنيا على الدليل، فحينئذ يقع بيننا وبينهم كلام في أصل المسألة، ثم هؤلاء الذين يشترطون كون الاعتقاد والتصديق مبنيًّا على الدليل لكونه نافعا إن زعموا أن خروجه عن النفع إنما يكون لانعدام تحمل المشقة، والثواب يقابل تحمل المشقة، ومباشرة المكروه طلبا لمرضاة الله ولا مشقة في نفس التصديق بل المشقة في الوصول إلى التصديق بحصول العلم بالتأمل في الدليل، ولهذا لا ينفع التصديق الحاصل عند اليأس وفي الآخرة، فالكلام لعامة الفقهاء ومتكلمي أهل السنة منهم على ما مر من نيل الثواب بوعد الله تعالى تفضلا منه فيكون بمقابلة ما قابله به وجعل ذلك بمقابلة التصديق فيقابله، إلا ما خص منه كإيمان من عاين العذاب أو كان مضطرا فيه على ما بينا، فأما من يقول: إنما اشترطنا اقتران الدليل بالتصديق ليصير الاعتقاد بدونه مبنيا على الدليل إيمانا، فيحتاج إلى التكلم معهم فنقول: بأنهم يقولون: إن التصديق إذا لم يقترن به دليل، يكون مجرد اعتقاد نشأ لا عن دليل، ومثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون علما، إذ العلم المحدث لابد له من سبب ضروريا كان أو استدلاليا، ولا سبب لهذا الاعتقاد، فلم يكن علما، ولأن العلم المحدث نوعان ضروري واستدلالي لا ثالث لهما، وهذا الاعتقاد لم يثبت عن أحدهما، فليس بعلم.
For if they say: 'This belief is not itself faith, or it is faith but it is not beneficial unless it is based on evidence,' then at that point a discussion occurs between us and them concerning the root of the issue. Then those who stipulate that belief and affirmation be based on evidence in order for it to be beneficial—if they claim that its lack of benefit is only due to the absence of bearing hardship, and reward corresponds to bearing hardship, and undertaking what is disliked seeking Allah's pleasure, and there is no hardship in the very act of affirmation, rather the hardship is in reaching affirmation through attaining knowledge by reflecting on the evidence, and for this reason the affirmation that occurs at the time of despair and in the afterlife is not beneficial—then the statement of the general jurists and the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Sunnah among them is based on what has preceded concerning attaining reward by the promise of Allah Most High as a favor from Him, so it is in exchange for what He has made it correspond to, and He made it correspond to affirmation, so it corresponds to it, except what is excluded from that, like the faith of one who witnesses the punishment or is under compulsion, as we have explained. As for one who says: 'We only stipulated the conjunction of evidence with affirmation so that the belief without it becomes based on evidence as faith,' it is necessary to discuss with them, and we say: They say that when affirmation is not conjoined with evidence, it is mere belief arising not from evidence, and such belief does not constitute knowledge, because originated knowledge necessarily has a cause, whether necessary or deductive, and this belief has no cause, so it is not knowledge. And because originated knowledge is of two types: necessary and deductive, with no third type; this belief is not established by either of them, so it is not knowledge.

Commentary
This passage presents a multi-layered response to the Mu'tazila who condition evidence for faith to be beneficial. The author first frames the debate: if the Mu'tazila argue that belief without evidence is either not faith at all, or is faith but not beneficial unless based on evidence, then the discussion shifts to the root issue of what makes faith beneficial. The author then addresses those who claim that lack of benefit is due to absence of hardship, since reward corresponds to hardship, and there is no hardship in tasdiq itself—only in the process of reflection. Against this, the Sunni position (based on earlier discussion) is that reward is a favor from Allah (tafaddul) and He has made it correspond to tasdiq itself, except in cases of coercion or witnessing punishment. Their argument is that tasdiq without evidence is mere belief without cause, and knowledge must be either necessary (daruri) or acquired (istidlali). Since this belief has no such cause, it is not knowledge ('ilm). The author leaves this part as an argument to be addressed, implying that the Sunni response will challenge the exclusivity of these categories or the denial of knowledge in such belief. The passage thus lays out the Mu'tazila position in detail, setting up a Sunni refutation.

Key Terms
علم
ضروري
استدلالي
سبب
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any belief, even without evidence, can be considered knowledge.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that knowledge must have a causal basis, so causeless belief is not knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what are the two types of knowledge?
A) Intuitive and empirical
B) Necessary and deductive
C) Sacred and profane
D) Certain and probable

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that faith without evidence is not knowledge because it lacks a causal basis, challenging the Sunni view of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm your belief in Allah, can you point to a clear cause—either a necessary realization or a deductive proof—for that belief? If not, the Mu'tazila would say your tasdiq is not 'ilm. Does that unsettle you? What will you do today to ensure your faith is built on evidence?
?
The passage claims that knowledge must have a cause. Have you ever examined the cause of your own certainty about the unseen? If your faith is mere imitation, you are not safe. Will you commit to studying the rational proofs for God's existence this week?
?
If your belief lacks a causal basis, it is mere i'tiqad, not 'ilm. Could it be that you have never truly sought knowledge of Allah, only followed others? What is one concrete step you can take right now to turn your belief into knowledge?

Two Types of Revealed Matters
Classification of revealed matters into two types based on rational accessibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi classification of revealed matters into those knowable by reason and those only knowable through revelation.

Core Idea
Reason can know some truths, but revelation is needed for others.

The author presents a key classification that defines the relationship between reason and revelation in Islamic theology. He argues that truth is distinguished by prevailing proof, and then explains that the prophet's message includes two categories: matters that reason can discover through reflection, and matters that are beyond reason's reach. This sets the stage for understanding the scope of rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins by discussing reasoning against opponents in creedal matters, not juristic ones. Note that truth is determined by the strength of proof and exposure of fallacies.
This sets the stage for the classification that follows.
2
Identify the two types
Look for the phrase 'على نوعين' which introduces the two divisions. The first type includes matters that reason can know through reflection (e.g., existence of God, attributes). The second includes matters that reason cannot know (e.g., details of worship).
3
Reflect on examples
Notice the examples given: first type includes knowledge of the world's creation, God's existence, oneness, attributes, prophethood. Second type includes modalities of worship, conditions, times, punishments.
These examples clarify the boundary of rational knowledge.
4
Connect to the role of miracles
The author emphasizes that the prophet's speech, supported by miracles, is a source of knowledge. This is why we can trust the prophet even on matters beyond reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعليل (۱) على من خالفنا في المسائل الاعتقادية دون من خالفنا في المسائل الاجتهادية) (۲) وبهذا يمتاز الحق من الباطل لأن الحق ما غلبت حجته، وأظهرت أسباب التمويه في غيره، فأما بدون إظهار أسباب التمويه فيما يقابل هذا الرأي، فلا يوصف بكونه حقا إلا على غالب الرأي والظن هذا هو زبدة كلام المعتزلة.
وأما القائلون: إن الشرط لصيرورة الإيمان نافعا أو صيرورة التصديق إيمانا، هو اقتران الدليل به غير أن ذلك الدليل قد يكون كلام الرسول الذي عرف ما المعتقد له قيام المعجزات على يده فيثبت له العلم بخبره بجميع يجب اعتقاده في حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وغيره، إذ خبر من تأيد بالمعجزة من أسباب العلم، وكان الاعتقاد مبنيا على الدليل الموجب للعلم فإنهم يقولون ما ورد الرسل صلوات الله عليهم به من الشرائع المتضمنة لصالح العاجلة والآجلة فهو على نوعين: أحدهما في (۳) ما في العقول إمكان الوقوف عليه، والوصول إليه بالتأمل والفكر كمعرفة حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته واتصافه بصفات الكمال، وتنزيهه عن (4) سمات النقص، وثبوت النبوات، وأشباه ذلك . والآخر: ما ليس في العقول إمكان الوقوف عليه ككيفيات العبادات وشروط جوازها، وأوقات أدائها، وتقدير الحدود والكفارات وأشباه ذلك.
The reasoning (التعليل) [is] against those who oppose us in matters of belief, not against those who oppose us in juristic matters. And by this, truth is distinguished from falsehood, because truth is that whose proof prevails and the means of obfuscation are shown in others. But without showing the means of obfuscation in what opposes this view, it is not described as being truth except by predominant opinion and conjecture. This is the core of the Mu'tazila's speech. And as for those who say: The condition for faith becoming beneficial or for affirmation (التصديق) becoming faith is the accompaniment of evidence, except that that evidence may be the speech of the messenger who is known to have miracles performed at his hand, so he attains certainty of his report concerning everything that must be believed regarding the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Maker, and other matters; for the report of one supported by a miracle is among the causes of knowledge, and belief is based on the evidence that necessitates knowledge. They say: What the messengers (peace of God be upon them) have brought of laws comprising the benefit of the immediate and the future is of two types: One of them is what there is in the intellects the possibility of comprehending it and reaching it through reflection and thought, such as knowledge of the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Maker, His Oneness, His being described with attributes of perfection, His transcendence above attributes of deficiency, the establishment of prophethoods, and the like. The other: What there is not in the intellects the possibility of comprehending it, such as the modalities of worship, the conditions of their validity, the times of their performance, the specification of legal punishments and expiations, and the like.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a classification and theological argument about the types of revealed matters. The author begins by clarifying that the reasoning (التعليل) applies only to those who oppose in creedal matters, not juristic ones. He notes that truth is distinguished by the prevalence of proof and exposure of fallacies. Then he presents the view of those who hold that evidence must accompany faith for it to be beneficial. They say that the evidence can be the speech of the prophet, proven by miracles, which yields certainty. From this, they state that the laws brought by the prophets fall into two categories: (1) matters that reason can know through reflection, such as the existence and attributes of God and prophethood; (2) matters that reason cannot know, such as details of worship, penalties, etc. The first type is accessible to rational inquiry, while the second relies solely on revelation. This classification underscores the complementary roles of reason and revelation in Islamic theology.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all revealed matters are beyond reason and cannot be known rationally.
Correction: This passage clarifies that some revealed matters, like God's existence and attributes, are accessible to reason through reflection, while others are not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of matters brought by the prophets?

B) Matters accessible to reason and matters not accessible to reason

Key Takeaway
The prophets brought two types of matters: those reason can discover and those that require pure revelation.

?

?
If you truly believe that some truths are beyond reason, how does that affect your humility before the Quran and Sunnah? Where do you need to submit without fully comprehending?
