The Commoner's Knowledge and the Sufficiency of Proof
Nasafi uses the commoner analogy and cites scholarly agreement to define knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge in Islamic theology can be established by a valid proof even if the person cannot articulate the reasoning or refute objections, as illustrated by the commoner's inference of a builder from a building.

Core Idea
A valid proof suffices for knowledge, even if you can't explain it.

Imam Nasafi addresses a key theological question: When does belief count as knowledge? He gives a vivid example of a commoner who sees a building and knows it has a builder, despite being unable to reason formally. This shows that knowledge does not require full articulation of the proof. He then extends this to believers facing doubts, citing the agreement of the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the commoner analogy
Read the passage about the commoner who sees a building and believes it has a builder. Notice that Nasafi calls this belief knowledge because it is based on evidence, even if the commoner doesn't understand the logical details.
This is the core illustration.
2
Identify the key condition for knowledge
Look for the condition that makes the commoner's belief knowledge: it is built on evidence (dalil). The passage stresses that ignorance of the 'how' does not negate knowledge.
3
Explore the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions
Read how Nasafi cites both schools to show that a person who has a valid proof but cannot answer subsequent doubts remains knowledgeable. Note the conditions they set.
This shows consensus on the principle.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for a believer today: having a correct belief based on proof is enough; inability to refute every objection does not destroy knowledge.
This is the practical takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجلي له المعلوم كعامي رأى بناءً فاعتقد أن له بانيا، كان اعتقاده علما، لأنه بناه على الدليل إذ البناء دليل الباني، وإن كان لا يعرف كيفية دلالة الدليل، وهي أن البناء لما كان يتصور أن يكون أعلى من هذا أو أسفل، وأغلظ منه أو أدق، وأوثق منه أو أوهى، وأحسن منه أو أردأ، وكان من الجائز أن يكون على هذا فاختصاصه بهذا الوجه الذي هو عليه مع جواز أن لا يكون عليه بل يكون على غيره لن يكون إلا بتخصيص مخصص قادر مختار، وجهله بكيفية هذه الدلالة لا تخرج عقيدته من أن تكون علما، فكذا هذا يحققه أن الخصوم ساعدوا على هذا وقالوا بمثله فإن عندي الأشعري لو عرف دليلا واحدا في كل مسألة وبنى عليه اعتقاده، ثم وردت عليه شبهة عجز عن دفعها ولم يتزلزل اعتقاده بل بقى متقررا على ما اعتقده معتقدا أن تلك شبهة لا حجة، وأن وراءها من يدفعها من متكلمي أهل الإسلام، بقي مؤمنا وإن كان تعارض الأدلة يخرج الرأي عن حد العلم إلى الظن والشك والوقوف ولكن لما كان هذا الرجل بقي بانيا اعتقاده على دليل موجب للعلم، واعتقد تلك الشبهة باطلة كان اعتقاده علما لكونه مبنيا على دليل موجب للعلم وإن كان لا يهتدي إلى جهة بطلان الشبهة، وكيفية دلالة دليله على بطلان الشبهة فكذا هذا، وكذا عند المعتزلة من وجد منهم شرط صحة إيمانه وحكم له بالإيمان ثم اعترضت عليه بعد ذلك شبهة ولم يكن عنده من الجواب ما به يوجب دفعها لانعدام هذه الشبهة على خاطره فيما مضى من الأزمنة، ويحتاج إلى التأمل فيها ليقف على وجه دفعها لم يبطل إيمانه في هذه المدة، وإن تعارضت الأدلة ولم يهتد إلى كيفية وجه الدفع، ولكن لما اعتقد صحة دينه بانيا اعتقاده على دليل واعتقد بطلانها عن دليل يوجب بطلانها كان اعتقاده علما ينافي جميع أضداده ويتجلى له به المعلوم
And it reveals to him the known thing: like a commoner who saw a building and believed that it has a builder. His belief was knowledge, because he built it upon evidence, since the building is evidence of the builder, even if he does not know the manner of indication of the evidence, which is that the building, since it can be imagined to be higher or lower than this, thicker or finer, stronger or weaker, more beautiful or worse, and it was possible that it be in this way, so its specification with this particular aspect that it is upon, despite the possibility that it not be upon it but rather upon something else, would only be by the specification of a specifying agent, powerful, free-choosing. And his ignorance of the manner of this indication does not remove his belief from being knowledge. So likewise, this is verified by the fact that the opponents agreed upon this and said the like. For according to me, al-Ash'ari: if a person knew one evidence in every issue and built his belief upon it, then an objection came upon him which he was unable to repel, and his belief did not waver but remained firm upon what he believed, believing that that objection is not a proof, and that behind it there are those who repel it from the mutakallimun of the people of Islam, he remains a believer. Even though conflicting evidences may take opinion out of the limit of knowledge to conjecture, doubt, and suspension, nevertheless since this man remained building his belief upon evidence that necessitates knowledge, and he believed that objection to be false, his belief was knowledge because it was built upon evidence that necessitates knowledge, even if he does not find the way to the invalidity of the objection and the manner of indication of his evidence upon the invalidity of the objection. So likewise this. And likewise with the Mu'tazila: whoever among them fulfilled the condition of the soundness of his faith and was judged to be a believer, then an objection arose against him afterwards and he did not have from the answer that which would oblige its repulsion, due to this objection not having occurred to his mind in past times, and he needs to reflect upon it to stop at the way of its repulsion, his faith is not invalidated in this period, even if the evidences conflict and he does not find the way of its repulsion; but since he believed the correctness of his religion, building his belief upon evidence, and he believed its falsehood upon evidence that necessitates its falsehood, his belief was knowledge that contradicts all its opposites, and it reveals to him the known thing.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and an appeal to scholarly agreement. Nasafi begins with the example of an illiterate commoner who sees a building and immediately knows it has a builder. This belief is knowledge (ilm) because it is based on evidence (dalil), the building itself. Even though the commoner cannot articulate the formal logical reasoning that the specific form of the building requires a choosing agent, his ignorance of the 'how' of the evidence does not strip his belief of the status of knowledge. Nasafi then extends this to the case of a believer who has a valid proof for a religious truth but later encounters a doubt (shubha) he cannot answer. He cites the positions of both the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila to show they agree: as long as the person's original belief was built on a valid proof and he remains convinced that the doubt is false, his knowledge remains intact. For the Ash'ari, such a person remains a believer even if he cannot refute the doubt. For the Mu'tazila, similarly, the person's faith is not invalidated during the period he reflects on the doubt, because his conviction is based on proof. Thus the core point is that knowledge depends on having a valid proof and firm conviction, not on the ability to articulate the proof's details or to refute every objection.

Key Terms
علم
دليل
شبهة
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge requires the ability to articulate a complete syllogism and refute all doubts.
Correction: The passage shows that as long as one's belief is based on a valid proof (even if implicit) and conviction remains firm, it counts as knowledge, even if one cannot explain the reasoning or answer objections.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the commoner's belief that the building has a builder count as knowledge?
A) Because he has a proof even if he cannot articulate it.
B) Because he is certain without any doubt.
C) Because he heard it from a trustworthy source.
D) Because he saw the builder.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in Islamic theology is established by having a valid proof and firm conviction, not by the ability to articulate reasoning or refute objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith that you cannot answer, do you remain firm in your belief, or does your conviction waver? What does that reveal about whether your belief is built on a true proof?
?
The commoner could not explain how the building points to a builder, yet his belief was knowledge. In what area of your faith do you rely on a simple proof that you might not be able to articulate fully? Are you comfortable with that?
?
Think of a time you encountered a skeptical argument that unsettled you. Did you dismiss it as invalid even without a response, or did you feel your faith shaken? How does this lesson challenge your response and call you to strengthen the foundation of your belief?

Universal Innate Reasoning in All Muslims
Nasafi argues for universal innate reasoning among Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Nasafi, every Muslim, regardless of learning, possesses an innate form of reasoning (istidlal) about God and faith, even if they cannot articulate it.

Core Idea
Even the simplest Muslim reasons about God, naturally.

Al-Nasafi addresses a key question: does faith require formal theological reasoning? He argues that all Muslims, from scholars to commoners, inherently use reasoning when they acknowledge God's attributes in the face of creation. In this passage, he presents a hypothetical case and then affirms that no Muslim is devoid of this innate istidlal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice how Nasafi moves from a hypothetical scenario to a general claim.
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Nasafi begins with a person isolated from Islam who believes after hearing about miracles. This sets up the contrast with those already within the Islamic world.
3
Identify the main claim
Nasafi asserts that all people of the Islamic world, regardless of social status, are believers and possess some form of reasoning (istidlal). Locate the sentence where he states this clearly.
Key phrase: 'لن يخلو أحد منهم عن ضرب الاستدلال'
4
Examine the evidence from natural phenomena
Nasafi gives an example: when a common person sees thunder, wind, or darkness, they spontaneously glorify God and attribute these events to His power and will. This shows their instinctive reasoning.
This is a practical demonstration of implicit istidlal.
5
Note the theological consensus
Nasafi mentions that there is no disagreement among Maturidi scholars or between them and the Ash'aris on this point. Only the Mu'tazila differ. Reflect on why this matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينشأ في قطر من الأقطار، أو شاهق جبل من الجبال، لم تبلغه الدعوى ولا علم بثبوت هذه الملة، فشاهده مسلم فدعاه إلى الدين، وبين له ما يجب اعتقاده، وأُخْبِرَ رسولا لنا بلغ هذا الدين عن الله ودعانا إليه وقد ظهرت على يده المعجزات الناقضات للعادة، فصدقه هذا الإنسان في جميع ذلك واعتقد الدين من غير سابقة تأمل وتفكر هذا هو الذي اختلفوا فيه، فأما أهل دار الإسلام عوامهم وخواصهم نسوانهم وصبيانهم، العاقلون أهل الأمصار والرساتيق والقرى، وسكان الصحاري والبراري فكلهم مؤمنون مسلمون عارفون الله تعالى ووحدانيته وغير ذلك لن يخلو أحد منهم عن ضرب الاستدلال، وإن كان لا يهتدي إلى العبارة عن دليله، ولا يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة حتى إن واحدا منهم متى عاين هؤلاء من الأهوال كرعد هائل وهبوب ريح عاصفة أو ظلمة راكدة يسبح للحال، ويصف الله تعالى بكمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وتمام العلم والحكمة علما منه أن لا تعلق لهذه الأفزاع إلا بقدرته التامة ومشيئته النفاذة، وهو الذي خلق السماوات بغير عمد ممدودة وأطناب مشدودة، وجعل فيها الأفلاك الدائرة، والنجوم السائرة، وخلق الأرض وجعل فيها الجبال الواسعة، وشق فيها الأنهار الجارية، على هذا أهل جميع الأسواق والنواحي والقرى والرساتيق والنسوان والعقلاء من الصبيان، فلم يكن في هؤلاء بين مشايخنا خلاف ولا بيننا وبين الأشعري، وإنما الخلاف فيهم بيننا وبين المعتزلة على ما مر، والحمد لله على التوفيق والهداية.
A person arises in a region or on a high mountain, not reached by the call and not knowing of the establishment of this religion - then a Muslim sees him and calls him to the religion, and explains to him what he must believe, and informs him that a messenger for us conveyed this religion from God and called us to it, and miracles breaking custom appeared at his hand. So this person believes him in all that and believes the religion without prior reflection and thought. This is what they differed about. As for the people of the abode of Islam, their commoners and elite, their women and children, the rational ones among the people of cities, rural districts and villages, and the inhabitants of deserts and wildernesses, all of them are believers, Muslims, knowing God Most High and His oneness and other things. Not one of them is devoid of some kind of reasoning (istidlal), even if he does not find expression for his proof and cannot repel objections. So much so that when one of them witnesses some of these terrors, like a tremendous thunder, the blowing of a violent wind, or a spreading darkness, he instantly glorifies and describes God Most High as having complete power, effective will, perfect knowledge and wisdom, knowing that these fears have no connection except to His complete power and effective will. And He is the One who created the heavens without extended pillars and tightened ropes, and placed in them the revolving spheres and the moving stars, and created the earth and placed in it the vast mountains, and cut through it the flowing rivers. On this are all the people of the markets, districts, villages, rural areas, women, and rational children. There was no disagreement among our teachers regarding these people, nor between us and al-Ash'ari. Rather the disagreement regarding them is between us and the Mu'tazila as previously mentioned. Praise be to God for success and guidance.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an argument that even the simplest Muslims possess an innate form of reasoning (istidlal) about God and faith. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, begins by presenting a hypothetical scenario: a person grows up in an isolated region where the Islamic da'wa never reached. When a Muslim encounters him, explains the religion, and mentions a messenger with miracles, the man believes without prior reflection. This case is disputed. Then Nasafi turns to the people of the Islamic world: commoners, elite, women, children, city dwellers, villagers, nomads—all are believers and knowers of God and His oneness. Not one of them is without some form of reasoning, even if they cannot articulate their proof or refute objections. As evidence, he notes that when such a person witnesses awe-inspiring natural events like thunder, wind, or darkness, they spontaneously glorify God and attribute these phenomena to His power, will, and wisdom. They inherently recognize that such events depend solely on God. This natural response shows that basic reasoning is universal. Nasafi then states that there is no disagreement among Maturidi scholars or between them and the Ash'aris on this matter; only the Mu'tazila disagree. The passage concludes with praise to God. The key point is that faith even without formal theological reasoning still involves an implicit rational acknowledgment of God's attributes.

Key Terms
استدلال (istidlal)
إيمان (iman)
قدرة (qudra)
مشيئة (mashī'a)
علم (ʿilm)
حكمة (ḥikma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simple faith requires no reasoning at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that even commoners have a basic implicit reasoning.

Checkpoint

A) They have no reasoning and follow only blind imitation.
B) They all possess some kind of reasoning, even if they cannot articulate it.
C) Only the scholars have reasoning; commoners do not.
D) They are not considered Muslims because they lack reasoning.

Key Takeaway
All Muslims possess an innate reasoning about God, even if they cannot explain it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face sudden danger or overwhelming natural events, what is the first thought in your heart? Does it reveal a deep trust in Allah's power and wisdom, or something else? What does that reveal about the state of your innate reasoning?
?
This lesson teaches that even the simplest believer has an implicit reasoning about God. Have you ever looked down on someone who cannot explain their faith as well as you? What does that reveal about your own understanding of iman?
?
Imagine you were born in complete isolation without any exposure to Islam. When you saw a thunderstorm or a starry sky, would your heart still recognize its Creator? Why or why not? Let this question challenge you to examine whether your faith is merely inherited or truly rooted in your fitrah.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
8 min
Proof of Indivisible Atoms
Nasafi proves the existence of indivisible atoms by refuting infinite divisibility and answering objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses rational arguments to prove the existence of indivisible atoms, refuting infinite divisibility and responding to objections.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility leads to absurdity, proving atoms must exist.

Imam al-Nasafi now turns to a fundamental debate in Islamic theology: whether matter is infinitely divisible or composed of indivisible atoms. He presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum to show that infinite divisibility contradicts sensory experience, and then addresses a sophisticated objection from God's attributes. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the decisive proof from the nature of aggregation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the density of argument; each sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the first argument
Find the initial argument against infinite divisibility—the comparison of a mustard seed and a mountain. Why does infinite divisibility make them equal?
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The opponents raise an objection from God's knowledge and power. How does Nasafi respond? Pay attention to the distinction between actual existent things and non-existent things.
This clarifies that infinity can be compared only in the created, finite realm.
4
Understand the aggregation argument
Read the second argument about who created aggregation in bodies. Follow the logical steps: if God can separate parts, then after separation each part becomes indivisible.
This argument ties atoms to the contingency of accidents.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that atoms exist and notes the vast literature on the topic. Why does he not delve deeper?
The lesson's key takeaway is that infinite divisibility is rationally impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في هذه الأقسام التي بيناها اختلاف بين المتكلمين فأنكر هشام بن الحكم والنظام وكثير من الأوائل والحساب وجود ما سميناه جوهرا وهو الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وزعموا أن الجزء وإن قل فهو يتجزأ إلى ما لا نهاية له وهذا يؤدي إلى أن الخردلة لا تكون أصغر من الجبل، ولا يكون الجبل أكبر منها إذ ما لا نهاية لأجزائه لا يكون أكبر مما لا نهاية لأجزائه، إذ الكبر في الأجسام يراد به كثرة الأجزاء وزيادة أجزاء أحدهما على الآخر، وما لا نهاية له لا يكون أزيد مما لا نهاية له. والقول بأن الجبل ليس بأعظم من الخردلة إنكار المشاهدة. فإن قالوا إن معلومات الله تعالى أكثر من مقدوراته لما أن ذاته معلوم له وليس مقدور له، وإن كان لا نهاية لمعلوماته ومقدوراته فيثبت بهذا جواز كون ما لا نهاية له أكثر مما لا نهاية له. قلنا : هذا محال لما بينا والمحال لا يصحح بالنظر. ثم قيل له : ما من معلومات الله تعالى ومقدوراته محصورة ومعلوماته أكثر، فأما ما لم يوجد من معلوماته ومقدوراته فلا نهاية لهما، ولا يقال فيهما إن إحداهما أكثر من الأخرى، فما أثبتنا فيه التزايد والكثرة فهو محصور متناه، وما لا تناهي له لم يثبت فيه الكثرة فيطلب فيه المعارضة ويقال لهم : من الذي خلق الاجتماع في أجزاء هذا الجسم المعين ؟ فلابد من أن يقولوا الله تعالى، فيقال لهم: وهل يقدر على دفع الاجتماع وتخليق الافتراق بعد الاجتماع، فإن قالوا: لا، فقد عجزوا الله تعالى عن إعدام ما خلقه من الأعراض وتخليق ضده بدله، وإن قالوا: نعم، قلنا: إذا ارتفع الاجتماع عن الأجزاء لم يبق جزء قابل للتجزؤ إذ القابل للتجزؤ ما كان مجتمعا في نفسه، وما ليس بمجتمع فليس بقابل للتجزي، فإذا بقي كل جزء غير قابل للتجزي، فكانت أجزاء لا تتجزأ وهي المعنية بقولنا: جواهر، وهذه مسألة عظيمة فيها حجج كثيرة وشبه جمة صنف المتكلمون فيها تصانيف كثيرة من الجانبين، ولا حاجة بنا إلى إيراد ذلك على أن كتابنا هذا لا يتسع للكلام في مثل تلك المسائل، فأعرضنا عن ذلك إيثارا للتخفيف، والله الموفق.
Then in these divisions which we have mentioned there is disagreement among the theologians: Hisham ibn al-Hakam, al-Nazzam, and many of the ancients and mathematicians denied the existence of what we have called a substance (jawhar), which is the indivisible part. And they claimed that the part, however small, is divisible into infinity, and this leads to the mustard seed not being smaller than the mountain, and the mountain not being greater than it, because that which has no end to its parts cannot be greater than that which has no end to its parts, since largeness in bodies is intended to mean multiplicity of parts and the increase of parts of one over the other, and that which has no end cannot be more than that which has no end. And the saying that the mountain is not greater than the mustard seed is a denial of sense perception. Then if they say that the objects of God's knowledge are more than His objects of power, because His essence is known to Him and not an object of power, and if there is no end to His objects of knowledge and His objects of power, then this establishes the possibility of one infinite being more than another infinite. We say: This is impossible because of what we have explained, and the impossible cannot be validated by reasoning. Then it was said to him: The objects of God's knowledge and His objects of power that exist are limited, and His objects of knowledge are more. As for what does not exist of His objects of knowledge and His objects of power, there is no end to them both, and it is not said of them that one is more than the other. So what we have established concerning increase and multiplicity is that it is limited and finite; and for that which is infinite, multiplicity is not established, so that opposition is not sought in it. And it is said to them: Who created the aggregation in the parts of this particular body? They must say: Allah, exalted is He. Then it is said to them: Is He able to remove the aggregation and create separation after aggregation? If they say: No, then they have declared Allah, exalted is He, incapable of annihilating what He created of accidents and creating their opposite in their place. If they say: Yes, we say: When aggregation is removed from the parts, no part remains divisible, because that which is divisible is what is aggregated in itself, and what is not aggregated is not divisible. So if every part remains indivisible, then they are parts that do not divide, which are what we mean by our saying: substances (jawahir). This is a great issue containing many proofs and numerous specious arguments; the theologians have composed many works on both sides. And we have no need to present that, given that our book does not accommodate speech on such issues, so we have turned away from that in preference for brevity. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of indivisible atoms (jawahir) and a refutation of the view that atoms are infinitely divisible. The author first states the disagreement: Hisham ibn al-Hakam, al-Nazzam, and many early thinkers and mathematicians denied the existence of an indivisible part, claiming infinite divisibility. Then he presents a reductio ad absurdum: if a part is infinitely divisible, then a mustard seed and a mountain would have the same number of parts, so neither would be larger than the other, which contradicts sensory evidence. He then handles an objection: the opponents cite God's knowledge and power as cases where one infinite can be greater than another (since God's objects of knowledge are more than His objects of power, yet both are infinite). The author answers by distinguishing between actual existent things (which are finite and can be more or less) and non-existent things (which are infinite and cannot be compared). Thus, the objection does not apply to actual bodies. He then presents a second argument: whoever denies indivisible parts must explain who aggregates parts of a body. They will say God. Then ask: can God separate them? If not, God is impotent; if yes, then after removal of aggregation each part would be indivisible, because divisibility requires aggregation. Hence, parts remain indivisible, proving the existence of atoms. The author concludes by noting the abundance of arguments and says he will not elaborate further for brevity.

Key Terms
جوهر
جزء لا يتجزأ
الخردلة
الجبل
معلومات
مقدورات
الاجتماع
الافتراق
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite divisibility is logically coherent and does not lead to contradictions.
Correction: Nasafi shows that infinite divisibility leads to the absurd conclusion that a mustard seed equals a mountain in size, contradicting sense perception. He also shows that the comparison of infinite sets only applies to finite actualities, not to pure infinities.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author draw from the claim that a part is divisible into infinity?
A) The mountain would be larger than the mustard seed.
B) The mustard seed would be larger than the mountain.
C) Neither the mountain nor the mustard seed would have any size.
D) The mustard seed and the mountain would be equal in size.

Key Takeaway
Infinite divisibility is rationally impossible; matter must consist of indivisible units called atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces you to confront that even the smallest speck has a definite size and structure. When you look at a tiny seed or a massive mountain, do you recognize the Hand of Allah in their very composition, or do you see them as mere objects? What would it take for you to see His creation as a sign, not just a thing?
?
The opponents in this lesson doubted the existence of atoms, but the proof stands. Is there a truth about Allah or His creation that you have doubted because it seemed abstract or hard to grasp? What would change in your heart if you accepted such a truth fully?
?
Nasafi refutes the objection by saying that for infinite non-existent things, comparison does not apply. This teaches humility: some matters are beyond human comparison. Where in your life do you try to compare things that are beyond comparison, especially regarding Allah's actions? How can you submit in those areas?

Proof for the Existence of Accidents from Color Change
Al-Nasafi presents a proof for the existence of accidents from color change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi proves the existence of accidents using the observation of color change in a body and why this proof is foundational for establishing the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
Color change proves accidents, not essence.

Al-Nasafi begins by stressing the importance of proving accidents, as they lead to establishing the world's temporal origination. He then presents a clever argument from color change: when a body turns from black to white, the change cannot be due to the body's essence, so accidents must exist. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the importance of proving accidents
Read the opening lines where al-Nasafi mentions the many types of accidents and the sects that deny them. Note why he says proving accidents is crucial: it leads to proving the temporal origination of the world.
This sets the stage for the proof.
2
Follow the proof from color change
Work through the argument: We observe a black body that becomes white while the body remains. Then consider the two possibilities: blackness is due to the body's essence or due to a separate meaning (accident). Trace the reasoning for each branch.
Pay attention to the logical steps and why the essence branch leads to a contradiction.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the essence branch is impossible, the blackness must be due to an accident that perishes, and the whiteness due to a new accident. This proves that accidents exist.
Reflect on how this argument refutes those who deny accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشكوك، والرطوبات واليبوسات وغيرها، وهي عند التحصيل قريبة من ثلاثين نوعا في أكثرها نزاع بين مثبتيها، ونفاتها أصلا طوائف الدهرية والثنوية وأبي بكر الأصم من جملة المعتزلة، ويجب صرف العناية إلى إقامة الدليل على ثبوتها معاني غير الجواهر، إذ بثبوتها يتوصل إلى إثبات حدوث العالم. الدليل على ثبوت الأعراض: فنقول : الدليل على ثبوتها أنا نرى جسما أسود ثم رأيناه أبيض، وغير ذلك الجسم باق فنقول : إما أن كان أسود لذاته، وإما أن كان أسود لمعنى غير ذاته. فإن كان أسود لذاته لن يتصور أن لا يبقى أسود وذاته الذي هو علة اتصافه بكونه أسود قائم، ولا جائز أيضا أن يصير أبيض وذاته الذي هو علة كونه أسود موجود، وكذا إذا صار أبيض لذاته وإما أن صار أبيض لمعنى غير ذاته، ولا جائز أن صار أبيض لذاته لما أن هذا الذات كان موجودا، ولم يكن متصفا بكونه أبيض، وحيث صار أبيض مع بقاء الذات الذي كان أسود دل أنه ما صار أبيض لذاته ولا كان قبل هذا أسود لذاته بل كان أسود لمعنى انعدم والذات باق، وصار أبيض لمعنى حدث والذات غير حادث في هذه الحالة، فكان هذا دليلا على ثبوت الأعراض.
And doubts, and moistures and drynesses and others, and they are upon investigation close to thirty types, in most of which there is dispute between those who affirm them and those who negate them, originally sects: the Dahriyya, the Dualists, and Abu Bakr al-Asamm from among the Mu'tazila. And attention must be directed to establishing the proof for their existence as meanings other than substances, for by their existence the proof of the temporal origination of the world is reached. The proof for the existence of accidents: We say: The proof for their existence is that we see a black body, then we see it white, and that body remains (the same). So we say: Either it was black due to its essence, or it was black due to a meaning other than its essence. For if it was black due to its essence, it would not be conceivable that it not remain black while its essence which is the cause of its being qualified with blackness persists, nor is it permissible that it becomes white while its essence which is the cause of its being black exists. Likewise, if it becomes white due to its essence — and it is not permissible that it becomes white due to its essence because this essence existed and it was not qualified with being white — and when it becomes white with the persistence of the essence that was black, it indicates that it did not become white due to its essence, nor was it before this black due to its essence, but rather it was black due to a meaning that ceased to exist while the essence remained, and it became white due to a meaning that occurred while the essence did not become temporally originated in this state. So this is a proof for the existence of accidents.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of accidents (a'rad). The author begins by noting the many types of accidents (about thirty) and the disagreement about them, mentioning the Dahriyya, Dualists, and the Mu'tazilite Abu Bakr al-Asamm who denied accidents. He then states the necessity of proving them because their existence leads to proving the temporality of the world. The proof itself uses the observation of a body changing color from black to white while the body itself persists. The argument proceeds by dichotomy: either the blackness belongs to the body's essence or to an accident. If it were essential, the body would always be black and could not become white while the essence remains. Since we observe change, the blackness must be due to an accident that perishes, and the whiteness due to a new accident. Thus accidents exist.

Key Terms
عَرَض
جَوْهَر
ذات
مَعْنَى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Color change means the substance itself changes.
Correction: The substance remains constant; change is due to accidents.

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of proving the existence of accidents according to this passage?
A) To prove the existence of the Creator
B) To prove the temporal origination of the world
C) To prove that substances change
D) To refute the Dahriyya

Key Takeaway
Accidents exist because observable changes in bodies cannot be explained by the essence of the body itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that your essence remains constant while attributes come and go. Think of a character flaw you have struggled with for years. Do you define yourself by that flaw, or do you see it as an accident that can be removed? What would it practically mean for you to treat it as an accident—something that can leave you without changing who you are? Commit today to one concrete action that treats that flaw as temporary.
?
The deniers of accidents rejected this obvious proof because of their assumptions. Consider an area of your life where you ignore clear evidence—perhaps about a relationship, a habit, or your own spiritual state. What truth about yourself are you avoiding? Ask Allah for the courage to accept it, and then write down one step you will take this week to align your life with that truth.

Why Negation Cannot Explain Blackness
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection that blackness is not due to essence nor accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a simple negation (no cause) cannot account for why a body is black rather than white, and why this leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Negation never specifies; only positive attribution can.

This short but powerful passage presents a typical objection—that blackness might be neither essential nor accidental—and demolishes it with a single logical point: negation does not pick out one of two opposites. Before we can attribute a quality to something, we need a reason, not a denial of reasons.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims blackness is not due to essence nor due to an accident (i.e., no cause at all). Notice that this is a pure negation.
The objector thinks 'no cause' is a possible explanation.
2
Spot the refutation's key point
Al-Nasafi says negation cannot necessitate specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) to one of two descriptions. Why? Because saying 'neither X nor Y' doesn't make X more likely than Y.
3
Work through the absurd conclusion
If blackness has no cause at all, then at the moment the body is black, being white would be equally possible. But that contradicts the actual observation that the body is black. So the negation is false.
This shows that every quality must have a cause—either essential or accidental.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
This argument teaches a general rule: denial alone never grounds a positive state. In kalām, all observable attributes must be traced back to a real cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال إنه كان أسود لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، لأن هذا نفي، والنفي لا يوجب الاختصاص بأحد الوصفين فيقتضى هذا أن لا يكون كونه أسود حال كونه أسود أولى من كونه أبيض وهذا محال.
And it should not be said that it was black neither due to its essence nor due to a meaning, because this is a negation, and negation does not necessitate specification to one of the two descriptions, so it would require that its being black when it is black is not more fitting than its being white, and this is absurd.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector claims that the blackness of the body is neither due to its essence nor due to an accident. The author refutes this by pointing out that this is a simple negation, and negation cannot explain why the body happens to be black rather than white at a given moment. If blackness were not attached by either essence or accident, then the body would be equally likely to be black or white, which is not the case. Therefore, the negation fails.

Key Terms
نَفْي
اخْتِصَاص
وَصْف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'no cause' is a valid explanation for a quality.
Correction: The author shows that a mere negation cannot explain why one specific quality exists rather than its opposite. A cause is always required.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that blackness is neither from essence nor from accident?
A) Because negation cannot specify which color the body has
B) Because essence always causes blackness
C) Because accidents are necessary
D) Because the body cannot be black

Key Takeaway
Negation alone cannot ground a positive quality; every observed attribute requires a real cause (essence or accident).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality about yourself that you have accepted as 'just the way I am'—neither due to your core nature (essence) nor any external factor. After reading this passage, can you still honestly hold that view? If not, what is the real cause you must now seek?
?
Identify a habit you have that you have never questioned, like procrastination or impatience. This passage teaches that every observed attribute requires a cause. Are you willing to stop treating it as a mere 'negation' (no cause) and instead find the actual cause? What would that require from you today?
?
The argument shows that without a cause, blackness would be equally likely to be whiteness. Consider your own belief that something is 'just how it is.' Could this be a hidden excuse to avoid taking responsibility? What would it mean for your life to insist on finding the real cause behind your actions?

Refutation of Dual Causation for a Single Attribute
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that blackness is due to both essence and accident simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a rational judgment cannot have two distinct causes and how this refutes the claim that blackness is due to both essence and accident.

Core Idea
One effect, one cause: rational judgments are single-sourced.

Al-Nasafi here tackles a subtle objection: what if blackness is caused by both the essence and an accident? He shows this is logically impossible because a rational judgment requires a single cause. The objector's position collapses into contradiction and inadvertently admits the existence of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the contradiction
The passage presents three rational judgments. Find each one and notice how Sanusi defines it before giving its rule.
3
Recognize the implication
Ask yourself why these three categories are the first thing a student of kalam must learn. The commentary will help you answer.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال بأنه يكون أسود لعينه ولمعنى، لأنك إذا قلت: لعينه يوجب أن لا يكون لمعنى، وإذا قلت لمعنى يوجب أن لا يكون لعينه، إذ الحكم العقلي لا يثبت إلا بعلة واحدة فيصير في التقدير كأنك قلت: كان أسود لا لعينه ولا لمعنى، وقد بينا فساده، مع أن هذا اعتراف منك بثبوت معنى إلى هذا ندعوك.
And it should not be said that it is black due to its own self and due to a meaning, because if you say 'due to its own self' it necessitates that it is not due to a meaning, and if you say 'due to a meaning' it necessitates that it is not due to its own self, since a rational judgment is not established except by a single cause. So in estimation it becomes as if you said: it was black neither due to its own self nor due to a meaning, and we have already shown its corruption. Moreover, this is an admission from you of the existence of a meaning, to which we call you.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that blackness is due to both the essence and an accident. The author argues that this is contradictory because a single effect (blackness) cannot have two distinct causes: if it is due to the essence, it cannot be due to an accident, and vice versa. The rational judgment of blackness cannot arise from two principles. Thus, the objection collapses into the already refuted position of no cause. Additionally, by admitting 'meaning' (accident), the objector implicitly concedes the existence of accidents, which is what the proof aims to establish.

Key Terms
عَيْن
عِلَّة
حُكْم عَقْلِيّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An attribute like blackness can be caused by both essence and accident simultaneously.
Correction: A rational judgment cannot have two distinct causes; it must be by a single cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction the author points out in the claim that blackness is due to both essence and accident?
A) Essence and accident are opposites
B) A single judgment cannot have two causes
C) Accidents cannot cause blackness
D) The body cannot be black

Key Takeaway
A rational judgment (like blackness) cannot have two distinct causes; attributing it to both essence and accident is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to trust in Allah alone, do you sometimes rely on other causes, as if your success comes from both Allah and your own effort? This passage shows that claiming two causes for one effect is illogical. Examine your heart: are there areas where you attribute your success to both Allah and your own effort, as if they are separate causes? Commit to affirming that Allah is the sole cause of everything.
?
The objector inadvertently admits the existence of accidents. What beliefs do you hold that inadvertently admit something you claim to deny? Is there a contradiction in your faith that you need to resolve? Take a specific example from your life and resolve it today.
?
This lesson shows that a rational judgment cannot have two causes. Reflect on your intention in worship: is it for Allah alone, or do you mix in desire for people's praise? Resolve to purify your intention today.

Refuting the Maker's Act Objection
Al-Nasafi refutes an objection about blackness coming from a maker's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the objection that blackness is directly caused by a maker's act, and how the argument reduces to the essential/accidental dichotomy.

Core Idea
A maker's act must work through essence or accident — not outside them.

Having established that blackness is either essential or accidental, a potential objection arises: could blackness be produced directly by a maker's act? Al-Nasafi anticipates this and shows that even if we say blackness comes from a maker's 'making', the making itself must be analyzed as either affecting the essence or creating an accident. Thus the original dichotomy stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection is introduced and then refuted.
The passage is a single logical move.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The author says the maker's making must be either making the essence necessitate blackness or making a meaning (accident) necessitate it. Locate both alternatives in the text.
3
Trace the conclusion
The author concludes that both alternatives reduce to the original dichotomy: blackness is either essential or accidental. See how the objection does not introduce a new category.
This is the refutation.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself: why does this refutation work? Notice it forces the opponent's position into the existing framework, showing consistency.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال كان أسود لجعل جاعل لأن جعل الجاعل لا يخلو إما أن يكون لأجل جعله ذاته مقتضيا لكونه أسود أو لأجل جعله معنى مقتضيا لذلك، فآل الأمر إلى ما قلنا من كونه أسود لذاته أو لمعنى.
And it should not be said that it was black due to a maker's making, because the maker's making is not free: either it is due to His making its essence necessitate its being black, or due to His making a meaning necessitate that. So the matter reduces to what we said: it being black due to its essence or due to a meaning.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that blackness is caused by a maker (jāʿil) directly. The author argues that the act of making (jaʿl) must result in either the essence itself being the cause of blackness or a separate accident (maʿnā) being the cause. In either case, it falls back into the original dichotomy: blackness is either essential or accidental. Thus, the objection does not undermine the proof; it merely restates the options.

Key Terms
جَعْل
جَاعِل
مُقْتَضِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine action directly causes properties without any intermediate cause, bypassing essence or accident.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that even if we attribute blackness to a maker's act, that act must still work through the essence or an accident. Thus no new category is introduced.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the maker's making in relation to blackness?
A) It creates blackness from nothing
B) It either makes the essence or an accident the cause
C) It bypasses essence and accident
D) It is the same as essence

Key Takeaway
Blackness, if caused by a maker, must be either essential or accidental, thus the objection does not change the original argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
You often judge others by their actions, but do you consider the root cause? This lesson says all causes are either essential or accidental. When you next feel anger at someone, will you stop to ask whether their flaw is essential or merely accidental, and respond with mercy instead of blame?

Refutation of the State (Hal) Objection
Al-Nasafi begins refuting the objection that blackness is due to a state by reducing the state to essence or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of 'state' (ḥāl) as a third category is dismissed by reducing it to either essence or accident.

Core Idea
A state (ḥāl) must belong to the essence or to an accident — there is no third option.

In this passage, al-Nasafi responds to an objection that blackness could be explained by a 'state' rather than directly by an accident. He argues that the state itself must be attributable either to the essence or to something else, effectively collapsing the objection into the same dichotomy. This brief argument demonstrates the principle of parsimony in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical structure of the refutation.
2
Understand the objection being refuted
The objection claims that blackness could be due to a 'state' (ḥāl) rather than directly an accident. Consider why this would be problematic for the author's position.
The author's aim is to show that 'state' is not a separate category.
3
Analyze the reduction
The author argues that the state must either go back to the essence itself or to something else (an accident). Ask yourself: why does this two-option dichotomy refute the objection?
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال كان أسود لكونه على حال لأن تلك الحال إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الذات أو إلى ما ليس بعين.
And it should not be said that it was black due to its being in a state, because that state either goes back to the essence or to what is not the self.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the objection that blackness is due to a state (ḥāl). The author argues that a state must either be attributable to the essence itself or to something else (i.e., an accident). Thus, it reduces to the same dichotomy of essence or accident. However, the text cuts off, so the full refutation is not preserved here.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The state (ḥāl) is a third category of attribute distinct from essence and accident.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that a state must be either inherent to the essence or to something else (an accident), so it collapses into the existing categories.

Checkpoint

A) Only essence
B) Only accident
C) Either essence or accident
D) Neither essence nor accident

Key Takeaway
The concept of a state (ḥāl) does not create a third category; it ultimately refers to either the essence or an accident.

The Reality of Accidents: A Rational Proof
Nasafi proves the existence of accidents through a numerical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses a legal example to prove that accidents have real existence.

Core Idea
Accidents are real; denying them leads to absurdity.

Imam Nasafi begins with a legal ruling on the punishment for adultery to demonstrate a deeper metaphysical truth. The increase from eighty to one hundred lashes is not merely a numerical change; it implies the reality of the act of striking itself. This passage grounds the existence of accidents in rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the legal example
Notice the specific numbers: 80 and 100. Understand that the example is about the punishment for adultery, but the point is about the increase.
The judge must acknowledge that 100 exceeds 80 by 20.
3
Grasp the argument from absurdity
Follow the logical chain: if the act of striking (an accident) had no existence, then 'nothing' would be more than 'nothing' by 20. This is absurd. Therefore the accident must exist.
The argument works because the increase implies a real change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمانون، وفي حد الزنا لابد من أن يقول : مائة، ولابد من (۱) أن يعترف أن المائة أزيد من الثمانين بعشرين وليس الضارب بمتعدد ولا المضروب، ولا السوط الذي آلة الضرب، ولو لم يكن للضرب الذي هو عرض وجود لكان لا شيء أكثر من لا شيء بعشرين، وهذا محال لا يخفى بطلانه على المجانين فضلا عن العقلاء، وكذا هذا في أعداد ركعات الصلوات(۲)، والله الموفق.
هو
وإذا(۳) عرف أن العالم بأسره ما ذكرنا من الأعراض والأعيان مركبة وغير مركبة لا يوجد للعالم (٤) قسم إلا وهو داخل تحت ما ذكر سفليًّا كان أو علويا جمادا كان أو ناميا نباتا كان أو حيوانا عجما (٥) كان أو ناطقا (٦) إذ لا واسطة بين ما يقوم بنفسه وبين ما لا يقوم بنفسه)()
Eighty, and in the punishment for adultery he must say: one hundred, and he must (1) acknowledge that one hundred exceeds eighty by twenty, and the one who strikes is not multiple, nor the one struck, nor the whip which is the instrument of striking; and if the striking—which is an accident—had no existence, then nothing would be more than nothing by twenty, and this is an impossibility whose falsity is not hidden from madmen, let alone from rational people; and likewise this applies to the numbers of prayer cycles (2). And God is the giver of success.
He
And when (3) it is known that the entire world—consisting of what we mentioned of accidents and essences, composite and non-composite—there exists no division of the world (4) except that it falls under what we mentioned, whether lower or upper, inanimate or growing, plant or animal, non-rational (5) or rational (6), for there is no intermediate between what is self-subsistent and what is not self-subsistent.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of accidents and then classifies the world into two categories. The example begins with a legal ruling: in the punishment for adultery, the prescribed number of lashes is one hundred. The judge must acknowledge that one hundred exceeds eighty by twenty. The discussion then shifts to the metaphysical reality of the act of striking. The act of striking is an accident (ʿaraḍ). If this accident had no existence, then the statement 'one hundred exceeds eighty by twenty' would mean that nothing (the non-existent accident) exceeds nothing by twenty—an absurdity. This argument shows that accidents must have real existence. The same reasoning applies to the numbers of prayer cycles. After concluding with 'and God is the giver of success,' the author adds a separate point: once it is known that the entire world is composed of accidents (aʿrāḍ) and essences (aʿyān), whether composite or non-composite, there is no part of the world that falls outside these two categories—whether lower or upper, inanimate or living, plant or animal, non-rational or rational. This is because there is no intermediate between what is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-nafsihi, i.e., substance) and what is not self-subsistent (i.e., accident). Thus the world is exhaustively divided into substances and accidents.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
وجود (existence)
لا شيء (nothing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents are merely mental concepts or qualities without real existence.
Correction: This argument shows that accidents must have real existence, otherwise numerical comparisons would be absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the basic argument used to prove that accidents (like striking) have real existence?
A) If the accident did not exist, then the comparison of numbers would involve something exceeding nothing by a quantity.
B) The Quran explicitly states that accidents exist.
C) All rational people agree that accidents exist.
D) The accident of striking is necessary for punishment to be carried out.

Key Takeaway
Accidents have real existence; denying them leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever denied something about yourself because it was uncomfortable, even though its existence was as obvious as the accident of striking? What would facing that truth cost you, and are you willing to pay that price today?
?
If the judge must acknowledge a simple numerical truth, what truths about your own character or beliefs have you been avoiding acknowledging because they require you to change your life? Write down one specific truth you will stop avoiding and commit to acting on it this week.
?
The passage says even madmen see the falsity of denying accidents. Is there an area of your 'aqeedah or daily practice where you are clinging to a position that a rational person would reject? What step will you take today to align yourself with the clear truth?

The World: Only Accidents and Substances
Nasafi classifies the world into accidents and substances exclusively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every entity in the world is either self-subsistent (substance) or not (accident), with no third category.

Core Idea
Everything is either a substance or an accident.

Having established the reality of accidents, Imam Nasafi now provides a comprehensive classification of all created things. He asserts that the entire world, from the highest heavens to the lowest earth, falls into two categories: substances (self-subsistent) and accidents (dependent). This division is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the enumeration of types of things (upper/lower, inanimate/growing, etc.)
2
Identify the two categories
The passage states clearly that everything is either self-subsistent (substance) or not self-subsistent (accident). There is no intermediate.
Self-subsistent means it exists without needing a substratum; accident needs a substance to exist.
3
See the exhaustive nature
The passage lists many kinds of things (plants, animals, etc.) but all are substances. Accidents are not listed because they are non-self-subsistent. The point is that nothing falls outside this binary division.
For example, a stone is a substance, its color is an accident.

Misconception: Some entities (like qualities or relations) are neither substances nor accidents.

?

Substances Are Never Devoid of Accidents
Proof that substances always have accidents via dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why substances must always be accompanied by accidents like association, separation, motion, or rest, and how the author proves this necessity using rational argument.

Core Idea
Substances cannot exist without accidents; they are inseparable from motion, rest, association, or separation.

In this passage, the author builds on the proven origination of accidents to show that substances themselves are never free from them. He examines two pairs of accidents—association and separation, motion and rest—and argues that there is no third possibility between the two states. This establishes that accidents are essential concomitants of substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from establishing accidents to defining the four terms and then the logical argument.
The passage ends with an objection that the author will answer later.
2
Identify the definitions of the four accidents
Locate the definitions of association (اجتماع), separation (افتراق), motion (حركة), and rest (سكون). Notice how each is defined in relation to substances and place.
Association and separation are about the spatial relation between two substances; motion and rest about a single substance in place.
3
Understand the dichotomy argument
The author argues that for two substances, there is no intermediate between having an interval (separation) and not having one (association). Similarly, a substance in place must either move (motion) or stay (rest). Therefore, substances cannot be empty of these accidents.
The objection at the end questions how a substance at the moment of origination can be said to have motion or rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت حدوث الأعراض وتقرر، فتأملنا بعد ذلك في حال الأعيان فوجدناها غير متعربة من الأعراض التي ثبت حدوثها بما لا شبهة فيه من الدلائل، ثم تأملنا فوجدنا تعريها عن الأعراض وخلوها عنها ممتنعا مستحيلا، وذلك لأنا رأينا الاجتماع والافتراق معنيين وراء المفترق والمجتمع، وكذا الحركة والسكون على ما بينا في فصل إثبات الأعراض ثم رأينا أن الاجتماع تماس الجوهرين حتى لا يكون بينهما مكان والافتراق تباين الجوهرين حتى يكون لثالث بينهما مكان، وقيل الاجتماع كون جوهر بجنب جوهر بحيث ليس بينهما حيز، والافتراق كون جوهر لا بجنب جوهر، والحركة هي الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، والسكون هو القرار في مكان في زمانين فصاعدا ثم رأينا أن وجود جوهرين لا يخلو من أن يكون بينهما حيزا أو لا يكون، ومن أن يكون كل واحد منهما بجنب صاحبه أو يكون لا بجنب صاحبه، ولا يتصور بين هاتين الحالتين واسطة، فكان إذا خلو الجوهرين عن الاجتماع والافتراق محالا، وكذا عن الحركة والسكون لأن المتمكن في مكان، إما أن ينتقل عنه فيكون متحركا وإما أن يستقر فيه فيكون ساكنا، ولا واسطة بينهما، فإذا كان خلو الجوهرين عنهما جميعا محال فإن قلت: أليس أن الجوهر عندكم في أول أحوال وجوده يكون خاليا عن الحركة والسكون جميعا، إذ هو عندكم في حال حدوثه ليس بمتحرك ولا ساكن، فما أنكرتم
Then, when the origination of accidents is established and confirmed, we afterwards contemplated the state of substances and found them not free from accidents whose origination has been established by proofs free of any doubt. Then we contemplated and found their being stripped of accidents and empty of them to be impossible and inconceivable. That is because we saw that association (اجتماع) and separation (افتراق) are two meanings beyond the separated and the associated, and likewise motion (حركة) and rest (سكون) as we explained in the chapter on establishing accidents. Then we saw that association is the contact of two substances such that there is no space between them, and separation is the divergence of two substances such that there is a space for a third between them. And it was said: association is a substance being adjacent to a substance such that there is no interval between them, and separation is a substance not being adjacent to a substance. Motion is the transfer from one place to another, and rest is remaining in a place for two times or more. Then we saw that the existence of two substances is not free from either having an interval between them or not, and from each one being adjacent to its companion or not adjacent. And there is no conceivable intermediate between these two states. So if the two substances were empty of association and separation, it would be impossible. And likewise [empty] of motion and rest, because a thing occupying a place either moves away from it, thus being in motion, or stays in it, thus being at rest, and there is no intermediate between them. So if the substances being empty of both is impossible, then if you say: Is it not the case that the substance, according to you, at the first moment of its existence is empty of both motion and rest, since at the moment of its origination it is neither moving nor resting? So what prevents you...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a proof that substances (جواهر) are never devoid of accidents (أعراض). The author begins by affirming that accidents are proven to be originated (حادثة). Then he examines substances and finds that they are never free from these accidents. Specifically, he argues that any two substances must either be in a state of association (اجتماع) – meaning they are contiguous with no space between them – or separation (افتراق) – meaning there is space between them. There is no third possibility. Therefore, it is impossible for two substances to exist without one of these two accidents. Similarly, any substance occupying a place must either move from it (motion) or stay in it (rest), with no intermediate. Hence, substances cannot be empty of motion and rest. The author then raises an objection: a substance at the moment of its origination is neither moving nor resting, so how can it be said to always have motion or rest? The passage cuts off here; the answer is given on the following pages. The proof establishes that accidents are essential concomitants of substances, and thus substances cannot exist without them.

Key Terms
الجواهر
الأعراض
الاجتماع
الافتراق
الحركة
السكون
المتمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A substance at the moment of its creation has no accident, especially motion or rest.
Correction: The author argues that even at the first moment, a substance must have at least association or separation with respect to another substance, or else it would be inconceivable. The objection raises this point, but the answer (in later pages) shows that the substance does have the accident of 'being created' or similar.

Checkpoint
What two pairs of accidents does the author argue substances must always have?
A) Colour and shape
B) Association and separation, motion and rest
C) Life and knowledge
D) Heat and cold

Key Takeaway
Substances are never devoid of accidents; they always have either association or separation, and either motion or rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If substances cannot exist without accidents even for an instant, what does that imply about your own existence? Are you truly independent for even a moment? What does this constant dependency demand of you in your relationship with the Creator?
?
The proof shows that your body is never free from accidents like motion or rest. How does this awareness of constant change affect your sense of purpose? Where are you moving towards, and what are you resting in?
?
This passage cuts off with an objection about the first moment of existence. Consider your own first moment of existence: were you not dependent on Allah for that very instant? How does this truth change how you view your past, present, and future?

The Impossibility of a Substance Being Free from Motion or Rest in the State of Remaining
Al-Nasafi proves that a substance in the state of remaining necessarily has motion or rest, but the first state is exempt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's argument that the first moment of a substance's existence cannot be classified as motion or rest, while subsequent moments must be one or the other.

Core Idea
A substance in its first instant has a single kawn—neither motion nor rest.

Al-Nasafi presents a crucial argument for the temporality of substances. He distinguishes between the first moment of existence and all subsequent moments. In the state of remaining, a substance must be either moving or at rest, but the first state escapes that classification because it lacks a second kawn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the full literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become familiar before analyzing.
Notice how the author uses straightforward conditions: if in same place, rest; if different, motion.
2
Understand the definition of motion and rest
The author defines motion as two 'kawns' in two places, and rest as two 'kawns' in one place. Grasp this definition clearly.
Kawn (كون) is an accident of being in a place at a time.
3
Analyze the first state vs. state of remaining
Contrast the single kawn of the first state with the two kawns required for motion/rest. See why the first state cannot be classified as motion or rest.
The first state is a single instant without a prior referent for comparison.
4
See the argument's conclusion
Recognize that by admitting the first state as origination, one proves substances are temporal. The opponent cannot avoid this conclusion.
Even if they deny the first state qualifies as origination, they must hold that substances are never free from motion/rest, which is impossible in the first moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه في الأزل يكون خاليا عنهما ؟ قيل عرف ببديهة العقل معرفة لا يعارضها شك أن الجوهر في حالة البقاء لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون لأنه إما أن يكون في الزمان الثاني في المكان الذي كان فيه الزمان الأول، وإما أن يكون في غيره فإن كان في المكان الأول فهذا سكون، وإن كان في المكان الثاني فهو حركة، ولا يتصور خلوه عن ذلك فأما في أول أحوال وجوده فتلك حالة واحدة لم يتصل بها حالة أخرى ليكون فيها في المكان الأول فيكون ساكنا أو في مكان آخر فيكون متحركا وفي حالة البقاء الحكم بخلافه، يوضحه أن الحركة هي كونان في مكانين والسكون كونان في مكان واحد، وفي الحالة الأولى لن يتصور إلا كون واحد وهو ليس بحركة ولا بسكون، فأما في حالة البقاء فيوجد كونان، فلابد من أن يكون حركة أو سكونا، فإن سلمتم للجوهر حالة الحدوث فقد أقررتم بحدوث الجواهر، فوقعت لنا الغنية عن إثباته بالدليل، وإن لم تسلموا ذلك فلن يتصور عندكم خلو الجواهر عن الحركة والسكون، على أن عندنا في أول أحوال الوجود لن يخلو الجوهر عن الكون وهو عرض أن كان خلا عن الحركة والسكون، لما أن كل واحد منهما عرضان وهما كونان ويستحيل وجود كونين في حالة واحدة ووجود كون واحد كاف لاستحالة خلوه عن العرض
That it in pre-eternity would be free from them? He said: Known by the primary intellect a knowledge not contested by doubt that the substance in the state of remaining is not free from motion and rest, because it is either in the second time in the place where it was in the first time, or it is in other than it. If it is in the first place, this is rest; if it is in the second place, it is motion. And it is not conceivable that it be free from that. As for the first of the states of its existence, that is a single state not connected to another state such that it would be in it in the first place so as to be at rest or in another place so as to be in motion. And in the state of remaining the judgment is contrary to that. He clarifies this by saying that motion is two 'kawns' in two places, and rest is two 'kawns' in one place. In the first state only one 'kawn' can be conceived, which is neither motion nor rest. As for the state of remaining, two 'kawns' exist, so it must be either motion or rest. If you admit for the substance a state of origination, then you have admitted the temporality of substances, and we have no need to prove it by evidence. And if you do not admit that, then according to you substances cannot be conceived free from motion and rest. Moreover, according to us in the first of the states of existence, the substance is not free from the 'kawn', which is an accident, even if it is free from motion and rest, because each of those two (motion and rest) are two accidents and are two 'kawns', and it is impossible for two 'kawns' to exist in a single state. The existence of a single 'kawn' suffices to prevent it from being free from an accident.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument showing that substances must be temporal because they never exist without accidents. The author begins by stating that a substance in the state of remaining (i.e., after it has come into existence and persists) cannot be free from either motion or rest. The reason is that in any given moment after the first, the substance must either remain in its previous location (which is rest) or change location (which is motion). There is no third possibility. However, the first moment of existence is different: it is a single instant without a prior moment, so it cannot be compared. Motion and rest are each defined as two 'kawns' (occurrences of being in a place). Motion is being in two places, rest is being in one place twice. In that first moment, only one 'kawn' exists, so it cannot be motion or rest. The author uses this to force the opponent to admit that the first state is a state of origination (hadith), which automatically proves the temporality of the substance. If the opponent denies that first state counts as origination, then they would have to hold that the substance is never free from motion/rest, which is impossible in the first moment. The author also notes that even if the first moment lacks motion/rest, it still contains a 'kawn' (the accident of being in a place), so the substance is always conjoined with an accident, which makes it temporal. This argument relies on the distinction between the first moment (one kawn) and subsequent moments (two kawns).

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
حركة (motion)
سكون (rest)
كون (kawn)
عرض (accident)
حالة البقاء (state of remaining)
أول أحوال الوجود (first state of existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a substance stays in the same place at the first moment, that should be considered rest.
Correction: Rest requires two kawns (being in one place at two times). The first moment has only one kawn, so it cannot be rest.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many 'kawns' are required for a substance to be considered in motion or at rest in the state of remaining?
A) One kawn
B) Two kawns
C) Three kawns
D) None

Key Takeaway
The first moment of a substance's existence has only one 'kawn', making it neither motion nor rest, and this establishes the substance's temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very first moment of existence was a single 'kawn' – placed, not moving, not at rest. You did not choose where or when to begin. If that origin was entirely given and you contributed nothing to it, then how can you claim ownership over your life as if you created yourself? What would change if you lived today with the constant awareness that you are radically contingent, dependent at every moment? Will you spend even five minutes after this lesson sitting with the reality that your existence is a gift, not a right?
?
After that first moment, every instant of your life has been either motion (changing) or rest (staying). You have never had a single moment that was not one of these two. This means you are constantly either moving toward something or standing still. What are you moving toward right now? What are you resting in? Are you comfortable resting in that which fades, or will you set yourself in motion toward the Eternal? Do not let this reflection pass without a concrete intention: name one specific, immediate change in your direction or stillness.
