Refutation of Existence Without Self or Meaning
Nasafi refutes the view that something exists neither by itself nor by a meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's reductio argument against the possibility of an existent that is not by itself nor by a meaning, and why this establishes the necessity of a cause for every existent.

Core Idea
Nothing can exist without a cause – either its essence or an attribute.

This passage from Nasafi presents a crisp logical argument that exposes the impossibility of a self-less, meaning-less existence. Before we can discuss what attributes Allah must have, we need a clear method for distinguishing real existence from imaginary claims. Nasafi gives us that method here: a reductio ad absurdum that any rational person must accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by a consequence and then a verification by analogy.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The argument has two clear parts: the direct reductio (if something existed neither by itself nor by a meaning, it would simultaneously exist and not exist) and the analogical proof (comparing to blackness).
3
Understand 'by itself' and 'by a meaning'
Analyse the concepts of 'by itself' (li nafsihi) and 'by a meaning' (li ma'nan). In kalam, 'by itself' means by its own essence, and 'by a meaning' means by an attribute or cause. These are the only two possible causes for a thing's existence.
This is crucial for understanding the argument's scope.
4
Examine the analogy with blackness
Reflect on why the analogy works: blackness is blackness by its own essence, so saying it is blackness not by itself leads to absurdity. Similarly, existence must be by itself or by a meaning.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider that this argument establishes that every existent must have a cause for its existence, which is foundational for later proofs of God's existence in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه لو كان موجودا لا لنفسه ولا لمعنى لم يكن ما يوجب اختصاصه بالوجود، فينبغي أن لا يكون موجودا في حال كونه موجودا، وهذا باطل. يحققه أنه لو جاز له أن يقول: الموجود موجود لا لنفسه ولا لمعنى الجاز لغيره أن يقول: السواد سواد لا لنفسه ولا لمعنى، وإذا كان هذا باطلا كان ذلك باطلا.
That if something existed not by itself and not by a meaning, there would be nothing that necessitates its specific existence, so it should not exist while it is existing, and this is false. He verifies it by that if it were permissible for him to say: the existent exists not by itself and not by a meaning, then it would be permissible for another to say: blackness is blackness not by itself and not by a meaning. And if this is false, then that is false.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the claim that an entity could exist neither by itself (li nafsihi) nor by a meaning (li ma'nan). The author argues that if such a thing were possible, there would be no cause for its specific existence, and it would simultaneously exist and not exist, which is contradictory. To clarify, he provides an analogy: if one were allowed to say 'the existent exists not by itself and not by a meaning', then one could similarly say 'blackness is blackness not by itself and not by a meaning', which is false because blackness is blackness by itself. Thus, the original claim is also false.

Key Terms
موجود
نفسه
معنى
باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'by itself' means by its own physical power or autonomy.
Correction: In kalam terminology, 'by itself' (li nafsihi) means by its own essence or quiddity – its identity is self-standing without needing another attribute to define it.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence of saying something exists neither by itself nor by a meaning according to this passage?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would be non-existent.
C) It would exist and not exist simultaneously.
D) It would be similar to blackness.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's reductio shows that nothing can exist without a cause, either its own essence or an attribute, otherwise a logical contradiction arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You act as if your existence is self-evident. But what cause sustains your existence at this very moment? If everything that exists has a cause either in itself or in an attribute, what is the cause of YOUR existence? Are you content to be dependent on something else? That dependence demands an answer. Will you look for that cause, or continue to assume you just are? This is not theoretical; your existence hangs on it.
?
Imagine that your faith wavers. You might think that your belief exists without a real cause. But Nasafi challenges you: if belief exists not by itself and not by a meaning, then it should not exist. So what is the cause of your belief? Is it rooted in your essence or in a meaning you have grasped? If neither, then perhaps your belief is false. Examine its cause today.
?
If your goodness were not by your essence and not by any acquired attribute, on what basis could you claim to be good? The same logic applies: everything must have a basis. Either your goodness flows from your essential nature or from a quality you have earned. Which is it? And what does that imply about your accountability before the One who is Good by His essence?

The Paradox of Self-Attributed Difference
Nasafi argues that difference-by-self leads to similarity, refuting the Mu'tazilite view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing difference to the essence (self) leads to the contradictory claim that different things are similar in the respect they differ.

Core Idea
If difference comes from the self, then different things share that self-attribute and become similar.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a key argument from the Mu'tazila about how entities differ. He shows that if each thing differs by its own self, then they end up sharing that attribute of 'being different by self', which would make them similar. This paradox exposes a flaw in that position. Let's walk through his logical reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The passage starts with the Mu'tazilite view that two different things differ not by themselves. Nasafi draws out the implication if they differ by themselves.
The opponent's view is that difference is not a self-attribute.
2
Follow the logical implication
Nasafi argues that if each differs by its own self, they share the self-attribute of 'differing from the other', which makes them similar in that regard.
Similarity follows from sharing the same self-attribute.
3
Examine the example of colors
The argument is illustrated with blackness and whiteness, and then blackness and redness, showing how each color's self-definition leads to similarity.
These examples make the abstract argument concrete.
4
Recognize the absurdity
Nasafi concludes that this is clearly false, and the argument applies to other scholars like al-Jubba'i.
The conclusion refutes the original doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق بطلان هذا المذهب هو أن من مذهبهم أن المختلفين يختلفان لا بنفسهما، فكان كل واحد منهما مخالف للآخر لنفسه، فلزم أن يكون المختلفان متماثلين من حيث اختلفا لاشتراكهما في الصفة النفسية وهي مخالفة كل واحد منهما صاحبه وهي الصفة الراجعة إلى النفس، وكذا هذا لازم على أبي هاشم لأن السواد يخالف البياض لكونه سوادا وكذا البياض يخالف السواد لكونه بياضا، والسواد سواد لنفسه والبياض بياض لنفسه وبهما تقع المخالفة بينهما وهما من أخص أوصاف النفس فينبغي أن يقع بينهما مماثلة بعين ما وقع به بينهما مخالفة، وهذا مما لا يخفي فساده على ذي لب ولأن السواد يخالف البياض بأخص أوصافه لنفسه، وكذا الحمرة مخالفة للبياض بأخص أوصافها لنفسها، فإذا السواد والحمرة يشتركان في أخص الصفات النفسية، فلزم أن يكونا متماثلين، وكذا هذا عند الجبائي من الصفات النفسية فيلزمه على نحو ما بينا.
And what verifies the falsity of this doctrine is that it is part of their doctrine that two different things differ not by themselves, so each one of them is different from the other by itself, so it is necessary that the two different things be similar in respect of their difference due to their sharing the self-attribute which is the difference of each one from its companion, and it is the attribute that refers back to the self. And likewise this is necessary for Abu Hashim, because blackness differs from whiteness in that it is blackness, and likewise whiteness differs from blackness in that it is whiteness, and blackness is blackness by itself and whiteness is whiteness by itself, and by these the difference between them occurs, and they are among the most specific attributes of the self, so it should be that between them occurs a similarity exactly by that by which difference occurred between them, and this is not hidden in its corruption from one of intellect. And because blackness differs from whiteness by its most specific attributes for itself, and likewise redness differs from whiteness by its most specific attributes for itself, so blackness and redness share in the most specific self-attributes, so it is necessary that they be similar. And likewise this, according to al-Jubba'i, is from the self-attributes, so it is necessary for him in the manner we have shown.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage critiques the view that two distinct things differ not by themselves but by something else. The author argues that if each thing differs from the other by itself (nafsihi), then they share the attribute of 'differing' as a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya). Consequently, they would be similar (mutamathilan) in that respect, leading to a contradiction. He applies this to specific examples: blackness and whiteness. According to Abu Hashim, blackness is blackness by itself, and whiteness is whiteness by itself; these are their most specific self-attributes that cause them to differ. If so, then they also share this attribute of being by themselves, making them similar. The same logic applies to blackness and redness: they share the self-attribute of being what they are, so they would be similar, which is absurd. The argument also applies to al-Jubba'i's view of self-attributes.

Key Terms
مختلفان
متماثلان
الصفة النفسية
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that things are really similar.
Correction: Nasafi is not claiming that things are similar; he is showing that if you accept the opponent's premise, you get an absurd consequence, thereby refuting the premise.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what follows if two things differ by themselves?
A) They are completely dissimilar.
B) They are similar in the respect they differ.
C) They are identical.
D) They have nothing in common.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the idea that difference is a self-attribute by showing it implies similarity, which is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about yourself that you thought was an unchangeable 'self-attribute' (like 'I am just not good at math')? How would logically extending that belief affect your view of your own potential or similarity to others? Will you now re-examine one such self-label this week?
?
This passage shows how an apparently reasonable claim (difference by self) leads to absurdity. Think of a time you insisted on a distinction between yourself and someone else based on an inherent quality. What would it cost you to question that distinction? Are you ready to face the logical consequences?
?
Nasafi's argument forces us to see that attributing difference to the essence can collapse into similarity. In your relationships, do you ever treat differences as absolute or essential? How might that prevent you from recognizing shared humanity? What step can you take today to bridge such a divide?

Similarity and Difference Can Coexist
Imam Nasafi argues for the compatibility of similarity and difference from different aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why similarity and difference can coexist in two things when considered from different relational aspects, and how this follows from the nature of relational attributes.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference are relational; they can coexist when aspects differ.

This passage tackles a subtle but important point in kalam: how can two things be both similar and different at the same time? Imam Nasafi defends the possibility by showing that similarity and difference are relational attributes that depend on the aspect of consideration. Using clear analogies, he demonstrates that opposing relational descriptions can coexist in a single subject without contradiction. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: Imam Nasafi acknowledges the Ash'ari view, presents an objection, and then refutes it.
Pay attention to the phrase 'من جهة دون جهة' – 'in one aspect but not another'.
2
Identify the key concepts: correlatives and aspect
The author classifies similarity and difference as 'mutadha'ifat' (correlatives) and 'idafiyyat' (relational attributes). Understand that they require two relata and that their application depends on the aspect (jiha).
Correlatives always come in pairs and gain meaning from their relation.
3
Analyze the analogies
Examine the father/son and near/far analogies. See how a single person can be both father and son without contradiction because the aspect differs. Apply the same logic to similarity and difference.
The key is that impossibility only arises when the aspect is unified.

ونحن أيضا هكذا نقول هذا هو المذكور في كتاب شرح الاعتقاد فأما المذكور في كتاب تعجيز المعتزلة، وكتاب ترتيب المذهب، فعلى ما ذكره غيره من الأشعرية، وما يقوله الأشعرية إنا سبرنا الأحوال فوجدنا المتماثلين يتماثلان بما ذكرنا، نحن نساعدهم أنهما كانا مثلين ليسد كل واحد منهما مسد صاحبه إلا أنا نقول : قد يسد أحد الشيئين مسد الأخر بجهة، ولا يسد بجهة فكانا مثلين من جهة دون جهة، وما قالوا من ثبوت المخالفة بينهما بجهة، وذلك مانع من ثبوت المماثلة لمنافاة بينهما. قلنا هذا اعتراض على إجماع العقلاء على ما قررت. ثم نقول : المخالفة والمماثلة لن يتصور ثبوتهما إلا بشيئين فكانا من قبيل المتضايفات، ولا استحالة في اجتماع الوصفين المتنافيين من الإضافيات في محل عند اختلاف الجهات وإنما الاستحالة في جهة واحدة فإن شخصا واحدا يكون بالإضافة إلى شخص آخر ابنا وبالإضافة إلى شخص آخر أبا ولا منافاة بينهما عند اختلاف الجهة، إنما المنافاة عند اتحاد الجهة فيستحيل أن يكون الشخص الواحد أبا لشخص ابنا لذلك الشخص، وكذا القرب والبعد فإن الشخص الواحد يكون قريبا من شخص بعيدا من آخر ومجتمعا مع شخص ومفترقا مع آخر، والاستحالة في كونه بعيدا ممن هو قريب منه ومجتمعا مع من هو مفترق عنه، وإذا كان كذلك. ثم المماثلة والمخالفة كانتا من هذا القبيل فلا يستحيل ثبوت المماثلة بجهة مع ثبوت المخالفة بجهة أخرى، ألا ترى أنه لا يستحيل ثبوت المماثلة ذات مع ثبوت المخالفة مع ذات أخرى، وإنما يستحيل ثبوت المماثلة بالجهة التي بها تثبت المخالفة، ونحن لا ندعي هذا بل ندعي ذلك بجهتين، وقد أقمنا عليه الدلالة الكافية.
And we also say thus: this is what is mentioned in the book Sharh al-I'tiqad. As for what is mentioned in the book Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila and the book Tartib al-Madhhab, then it is according to what other Ash'aris mentioned. And what the Ash'aris say — 'We surveyed the states and found that two similars are similar by what we mentioned' — we help them that they are similars in that each one can take the place of its companion, except that we say: one thing may take the place of the other in one aspect and not take its place in another aspect, so they are similars in one aspect but not another. And what they said about the existence of difference between them in one aspect, and that prevents the existence of similitude due to contradiction between them. We say: this is an objection to the consensus of the rational people on what we established. Then we say: Difference and similitude cannot be conceived to exist except between two things, so they belong to the category of correlatives (al-mutadha'ifat). And there is no impossibility in the conjunction of two contradictory descriptions from among relational attributes (al-idafiyyat) in a locus when the aspects differ; impossibility is only in one aspect. For one person can be a son in relation to one person and a father in relation to another person, and there is no contradiction between them when the aspect differs. Contradiction only occurs when the aspect is unified, so it is impossible that the same person be a father of a person and a son of that same person. Similarly, nearness and distance: one person can be near to one person and far from another, and together with one person and separate from another. Impossibility is in his being far from one from whom he is near and together with one from whom he is separate. If this is so, and similitude and difference are of this category, then it is not impossible to establish similitude in one aspect along with the establishment of difference in another aspect. Do you not see that it is not impossible to establish similitude in essence along with the establishment of difference in another essence? Rather, it is impossible to establish similitude in the same aspect by which difference is established. And we do not claim this, but we claim that by two aspects, and we have established sufficient proof for it.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the compatibility of similarity and difference between two things when considered from different aspects, contrary to a possible objection. The author, Imam Nasafi, begins by stating his agreement with the Ash'ari position that two similar things can substitute for each other in a certain sense, but he nuances it: they are similar in some aspects but not in others. The opponent argues that if difference exists in any aspect, it prevents absolute similarity. The author responds that this objection contradicts the consensus of rational people. He then explains that similarity and difference are correlatives (mutadha'ifat), i.e., they require two relata. There is no logical impossibility for two opposing relational descriptions to coexist in a single subject when the aspects of relation differ. He uses the concrete example of a man being both a father (to one person) and a son (to another) without contradiction. Similarly, nearness and distance can coexist in one person relative to different others. Applying this to similarity and difference: it is possible for two things to be similar in one respect and different in another respect simultaneously, because the aspect (jiha) differs. The author emphasizes that impossibility only arises if both descriptions are attributed under the same aspect. He concludes that his position is sound and supported by sufficient proof.

Key Terms
المماثلة
المخالفة
المتضايفات
الإضافيات
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things differ in any way, they cannot be considered similar at all.
Correction: Similarity and difference are relational; they can coexist in the same two things when considered from different aspects. Difference in one aspect does not negate similarity in another.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, why is it not impossible for two things to be both similar and different?
A) Because similarity and difference are both essential attributes.
B) Because they are considered under different relational aspects.
C) Because similarity is impossible in created things.
D) Because difference is always accidental.

Key Takeaway
Two things can be similar in one respect and different in another because similarity and difference are relational attributes that depend on the aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person you have categorized as 'entirely different' from you. Can you identify one aspect in which you are actually similar? How does recognizing similarity in one aspect while preserving difference in others change your attitude toward them? What concrete step will you take today to acknowledge that similarity?
?
The passage teaches that impossibility arises only when aspects are unified. Where in your own life have you insisted on a unified aspect where there are actually multiple? For example, do you assume that a person's one mistake defines their entire character? Write down one such situation and commit to separating the aspects from today.
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes fall into the trap of absolute similarity (tashbih) by imagining His attributes like human ones, or absolute difference (tanzih) by denying any meaningful comparison? Choose one of Allah's attributes (e.g., mercy). Describe one way it is similar to human mercy and one way it is profoundly different, and ask Allah to guide you to the correct balance.

Similarity and Difference Can Coexist from Different Aspects
Nasafi argues that similarity and difference can coexist from different aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how similarity and difference can coexist between two entities when considered from different aspects, and why this logical principle is crucial for defending divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference can both be true of the same two things — just not in the same way.

A common assumption is that if two things are similar in some way, they cannot also be different. But Imam Nasafi shows us that this is only a problem if we consider the same aspect. By distinguishing between different aspects (jihāt), we can affirm both similarity and difference without contradiction. This logical principle becomes a key tool in defending divine transcendence and understanding God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice how the argument flows from an example to a general principle.
The passage uses analogical reasoning, so pay attention to the initial example.
2
Identify the initial example
The author gives an example of something being 'together with a person and separated from another' and 'far from one who is near him and together with one who is separated'. Observe how this shows that opposite relations can coexist from different perspectives.
This example sets up the analogy for similarity and difference.
3
Understand the main claim
The author states that similarity and difference can be established in different aspects without impossibility. He clarifies that the only impossibility would be if they were in the very same aspect.
Note the key phrase: 'بجهة' (in an aspect) and 'بجهتين' (in two aspects).
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument matters for Islamic theology. How does it help reconcile affirming God's attributes (similarity in some sense) while maintaining His absolute uniqueness (difference)?
This principle underpins the Maturidi approach to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع من آخر ومجتمعا مع شخص ومفترقا مع آخر ، والاستحالة في كونه بعيدا ممن هو قريب منه ومجتمعا مع من هو مفترق عنه، وإذا كان كذلك. ثم المماثلة و المخالفة كانتا من هذا القبيل فلا يستحيل ثبوت المماثلة بجهة مع ثبوت المخالفة بجهة أخرى، ألا ترى أنه لا يستحيل ثبوت المماثلة ذات مع ثبوت المخالفة مع ذات أخرى، وإنما يستحيل ثبوت المماثلة بالجهة التي بها تثبت المخالفة، ونحن لا ندعي هذا بل ندعي ذلك بجهتين، وقد أقمنا عليه الدلالة الكافية. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك صح ما ذهبنا إليه
With another and being together with a person and separated from another, and impossibility in being far from one who is near him and together with one who is separated from him, and if it is so. Then similarity and difference were of this kind, so it is not impossible to establish similarity in one aspect with the establishment of difference in another aspect. Do you not see that it is not impossible to establish similarity of an essence with the establishment of difference with another essence? It is only impossible to establish similarity in the aspect by which difference is established. And we do not claim this, but rather we claim that with two aspects, and we have established sufficient proof for it. And if the matter is so, what we have gone to is correct.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument establishing that similarity (mumathalah) and difference (mukhalafah) can coexist in the same two entities when considered from different aspects. The author begins by noting that it is possible for one thing to be together with a person and separate from another, and for something to be far from someone near and close to someone far. Similarly, similarity and difference can both hold between the same two things as long as they are from different angles. He illustrates with the example of two essences: one essence can be similar to another essence in one respect while being different in another respect. The only impossibility would be to assert similarity and difference in the very same aspect. The author clarifies that he is not claiming the latter, but rather that the two apply from two different aspects. He declares that sufficient evidence has been provided, and thus his position is validated. This argument is foundational for defending divine transcendence (tanzih) while acknowledging the reality of God's attributes.

Key Terms

المخالفة (mukhalafah)
الجهة (aspect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similarity and difference are mutually exclusive in all respects, meaning that if two things are similar, they cannot be different at all.
Correction: The passage shows that similarity and difference can coexist as long as they are considered from different aspects; the impossibility only arises when they are in the same aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is impossible regarding similarity and difference?
A) That they coexist between two essences
B) That they coexist in the same aspect
C) That they are both real
D) That they are both from God

Key Takeaway
Similarity and difference can coexist between two entities as long as they are from different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own relationships, do you ever assume that because someone is similar to you in one way, they must be similar in all ways? How does this lesson challenge you to see others more fairly?
?
In your understanding of God, have you ever fallen into the trap of either completely likening Him to creation or completely distancing Him? How does the principle of different aspects help you worship Him with both intimacy and awe?
?
Think of a time you judged someone harshly because you focused only on the aspect where you differ. Can you now intentionally find an aspect of similarity to build understanding, even while acknowledging differences?

The Argument from Meaning for Origination
Nasafi presents a reductio argument against accidents in the Eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of accidents inhering in the Eternal would undermine the proof for the origination of matter, and how this argument refutes the Karramiyyah position.

Core Idea
Accidents in a thing signal its origination; God cannot receive them.

Imam Nasafi presents a concise yet powerful argument that relies on a core principle: the presence of an accident (maʿnā) in an essence is a sign of that essence's temporal origination. He then uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that if accidents could inhere in the Eternal, the entire proof for the world's origination would be invalidated, leading to the impossible conclusion that either God is originated or matter is eternal. This passage refutes the Karramiyyah claim that God can be a locus for accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: claim, conditional, conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the statement: 'The meaning in the essence is a sign of its origination.' This is the foundational premise. Ask yourself: what kind of 'meaning' is meant here? (It refers to an accident, a temporal property.)
3
Trace the conditional argument
Notice the 'if...then' structure: If an accident could occur in the Eternal, then the proof for matter's origination would be lost. Understand why that would be a problem: the proof for matter's origination relies on observing accidents in matter.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Recognize the two absurd consequences
The passage says the Karramiyyah position ('this statement') forces one of two absurdities: either the Creator is originated or matter is eternal. Both are impossible, so the original assumption must be false.
This refutes the idea that God can be a locus for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعنى في الذات أمارة حدوثه، ولو جاز حدوث معنى في القديم لم يبق لنا على حدوث الهيولى دليل، والقول بحدوث الصانع محال، وكذا القول بقدم الهيولى، وهذا القول يوجب أحد أمرين على ما قررناه.
The meaning (al-maʿnā) in the essence is a sign (amārat) of its origination (ḥudūth). And if it were permissible for a meaning to occur in the Eternal (al-Qadīm), there would remain for us no proof for the origination of matter (al-hayūlā). And the statement that the Creator is originated is impossible (muḥāl), and likewise the statement that matter is eternal (qadīm). And this statement necessitates one of two matters, as we have established.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) that accidents (maʿānī) in a substance indicate its temporal origination. The argument proceeds as follows: The presence of a meaning (accident) in an essence (dhāt) is a sign that the essence is originated. If it were possible for an accident to inhere in the Eternal (i.e., God), then the very proof we use to show that physical matter (hayūlā) is originated would be invalidated. But we know that the Creator cannot be originated (that is absurd), and matter cannot be eternal (also absurd). Therefore, the initial assumption—that an accident could subsist in the Eternal—leads to one of two absurdities: either the Creator is originated or matter is eternal. Since both are impossible, the assumption is false. The argument uses the method of reductio ad absurdum to reject the Karramiyyah claim that God can be a locus for accidents.

Key Terms
المعنى
الذات
أمارة
الحدوث
القديم
الهيولى
الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this passage denies that God has any attributes at all.
Correction: The passage does not deny that God has attributes; it denies that accidents (maʿānī) in the sense of temporal contingents can subsist in His essence. Divine attributes are eternal and not accidents.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what would follow if an accident could inhere in the Eternal?
A) The Creator would be eternal.
B) The proof for the origination of matter would be lost.
C) The Creator would be limited.
D) Matter would be both eternal and originated.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a temporal accident in a thing proves its origination; therefore, God, being eternal, cannot be a locus for such accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even unconsciously, thought of God as subject to change or temporality, like a being that 'responds' or 'reacts'? How does this argument confront that tendency? Commit to purifying your concept of God from any association with change.
?
When you reflect on your own existence, you see countless changes and accidents within yourself. How does this undeniable reality of your own origination move you to recognize the Eternal who is free from all change? What would it mean for you if you were to truly internalize that God alone is unchangeable?
?
This argument uses a logical proof to defend God's transcendence. Yet, the heart often struggles with such abstract reasoning. Is your trust in God built more on intellectual conviction or on emotional experience? How can you strengthen the rational foundation of your faith without losing its warmth?

Refuting the Argument from Above and Below
Nasafi presents and refutes the first intellectual doubt about two self-subsisting existents and spatial direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that two self-subsisting beings must be spatially related by appealing to the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen, and by noting that 'below' implies deficiency, which cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
What applies to physical objects does not automatically apply to God.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles the first of several intellectual doubts raised by opponents of Islamic theology. The doubt asserts that if two self-subsisting beings exist, they must be spatially related—one above the other. Nasafi refutes this by making a simple yet profound distinction: what is true of created things in the witnessed world (al-shahid) does not necessarily hold for the unseen divine reality (al-gha'ib), especially when deficiency is involved. The opponent's own concession that God cannot be 'below' reveals a differentiating factor that breaks the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its English translation. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
Notice the conditional phrasing: 'If they say... then...'
2
Identify the opponent's doubt
The opponent claims that two self-subsisting beings must be in a spatial relationship—one above the other. Find this claim in the text.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation strategy
Nasafi uses a reductio: either the opponent allows the spatial relation for God (which they deny) or they disallow it, thus contradicting their own analogy. He then introduces the differentiating proof (dalil al-tafriqa) based on deficiency.
The key point is that the unseen is different from the witnessed when deficiency is involved.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this argument safeguards God's transcendence. The lesson shows that rational arguments must respect God's unique nature.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبه العقلية للخصوم والرد عليها
فأما ما ذهب إليه الخصوم من الشبهات العقلية فنقول – وبالله التوفيق -: أما حل الشبهة الأولى وهي: أن الموجودين القائمين بالذات لا يخلوان من أن يكون كل واحد منهما جهة من صاحبه فنقول وبالله التوفيق - الموجودان القائمان بالذات كل واحد منهما في الشاهد يجوز أن يكون فوق صاحبه، والآخر تحته. أتجوزون هذا في الغائب؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. تركوا مذهبهم فإنهم لا يجوزون أن يكون الباري جل وعلا تحت العالم. وإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم. فإن قالوا : إنما لم تجوز هذا في الغائب لأن جهة تحت جهة ذم ونقيصة والباري جل وعلا منزه عن النقائص وأوصاف الذم . قيل لهم : فإذا أثبتم التفرقة بين الشاهد والغائب عند وجود دليل التفرقة حيث لم تجوزوا أن يكون الغائب بجهة تحت وإن كان ذلك في الشاهد جائزا لثبوت دليل التفرقة وهو استحالة النقيصة وصف الذم على الغائب،
The intellectual doubts of the opponents and the refutation thereof. As for what the opponents have adopted of intellectual doubts, we say—and with Allah is success—: As for the resolution of the first doubt, which is: that two self-subsisting existents cannot avoid each one being a direction from its companion. We say—and with Allah is success—: The two self-subsisting existents, each one in the witnessed [world], may be above its companion and the other below it. Do you allow this in the unseen? If they say: Yes, they have abandoned their doctrine, for they do not allow that the Exalted and Glorious Creator be under the world. If they say: No, they have invalidated their proof. If they say: We only did not allow this in the unseen because the direction 'under' is a direction of blame and deficiency, and the Exalted Creator is transcendent above deficiencies and attributes of blame. It is said to them: So you have established the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen when there exists a differentiating proof, since you do not allow that the unseen be in a direction of 'under', even though that is permissible in the witnessed, due to the existence of the differentiating proof, which is the impossibility of deficiency and attribute of blame for the unseen.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the first intellectual doubt and its rebuttal. The opponent argues that two self-subsisting beings (like God and the world) must be in a spatial relationship—one above the other. The author refutes by noting that in the witnessed world, one being can be above another, but this does not apply to God (the unseen) because being 'below' implies deficiency, and God is beyond deficiency. Thus, the analogy fails because there is a differentiating factor (deficiency). The opponent's own admission that God cannot be below shows they accept a distinction between witnessed and unseen, which then undercuts their proof.

Key Terms
الشبه العقلية
الموجودان القائمان بالذات
الشاهد
الغائب
جهة تحت
الذم والنقيصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because physical objects are above/below each other, God must be in a spatial relationship with the world.
Correction: God is not like physical objects; the analogy fails because 'below' implies deficiency, which is impossible for God. The witnessed/unseen distinction prevents automatic transfer of properties.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject applying the 'above/below' relationship to God?
A) Because God is not a self-subsisting existent.
B) Because being below is a deficiency, and God is free from all deficiencies.
C) Because the world is not a self-subsisting existent.
D) Because the doubt is not rational.

Key Takeaway
The first intellectual doubt about spatial direction is refuted by showing that the analogy from the witnessed world fails because God is unlike created objects and is beyond deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever catch yourself imagining Him in a specific direction or location, even if subconsciously? How does this passage expose that as a limitation from your own experience? What will you do to correct this in your worship?
?
The opponents admitted that 'under' implies deficiency, so they could not apply it to God. But are there other human concepts you might be wrongly applying to God, such as 'time', 'emotion', or 'physical attributes'? How can this passage help you become more vigilant in purifying your understanding of God?
?
If you were to explain to someone that God is not 'above' in a physical sense, how would you use the argument from this passage? How would you ensure your own heart fully accepts that God is beyond all spatial limitations, and that this impacts how you pray and supplicate?

The First Doubt: Direction Between Two Self-Subsisting Existents
Nasafi states the first doubt and gives the initial refutation via the above/below question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first intellectual doubt posed by opponents: that any two self-subsisting existents must be in a directional relation, and Nasafi's initial refutation by questioning the applicability of spatial relations to God.

Core Idea
What is true in the witnessed world may not apply to the unseen without a proof of distinction.

Opponents argue that God and the world, both self-subsisting, must occupy some direction relative to each other. Nasafi begins his refutation by asking whether they would allow God to be below the world, which they reject, thus exposing an inconsistency in their analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and initial question
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi states the doubt: two self-subsisting existents cannot avoid being in a direction from each other. Then note his question: in the witnessed world, one can be above and the other below. Do they allow this for the unseen?
2
Examine the opponents' possible responses
Nasafi offers two possible answers: if they say yes, they contradict their own belief (God cannot be below). If they say no, they invalidate their own analogy. Consider why both are problematic.
3
Analyze the 'deficiency' defense
The opponents claim a lower direction is a deficiency, so God cannot be below. Nasafi counters by giving examples of rulers and guards where spatial height does not imply honor. Understand that deficiency is not about location but about the entity itself.
4
Reflect on the proof of distinction
Nasafi points out that the opponents themselves already accept a proof of distinction (deficiency) that prevents applying the witnessed rule to the unseen. So why do they not apply the same logic here? This shows their inconsistency.
This lesson covers only the first part of the refutation.

الشبه العقلية للخصوم والرد عليها فأما ما ذهب إليه الخصوم من الشبهات العقلية فنقول – وبالله التوفيق -: أما حل الشبهة الأولى وهي: أن الموجودين القائمين بالذات لا يخلوان من أن يكون كل واحد منهما جهة من صاحبه فنقول وبالله التوفيق - الموجودان القائمان بالذات كل واحد منهما في الشاهد يجوز أن يكون فوق صاحبه، والآخر تحته. أتجوزون هذا في الغائب؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. تركوا مذهبهم فإنهم لا يجوزون أن يكون الباري جل وعلا تحت العالم. وإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم. فإن قالوا : إنما لم تجوز هذا في الغائب لأن جهة تحت جهة ذم ونقيصة والباري جل وعلا منزه عن النقائص وأوصاف الذم . قيل لهم : فإذا أثبتم التفرقة بين الشاهد والغائب عند وجود دليل التفرقة حيث لم تجوزوا أن يكون الغائب بجهة تحت وإن كان ذلك في الشاهد جائزا لثبوت دليل التفرقة وهو استحالة النقيصة ووصف الذم على الغائب، وجواز ذلك على الشاهد فلم قلتم إن دليل التفرقة فيما نحن فيه لم يوجد؟ بل وجد أنه يوجب الحدوث وهو ممتنع على الغائب جائز بل واجب على الشاهد ثم نقول لهم: كون جهة تحت جهة ذم ونقيصة غير مسلم إذ لا نقيصة في ذلك ولا رفعة في علو المكان، إذ كم من حارس فوق السطح وأمير في البيت وطليعة على ما ارتفع من الأماكن وسلطان في ما انهبط من الأمكنة، ثم نقول لهم: كل قائم بالذات في الشاهد جوهر وكل جوهر قائم بالذات أفتستدلون بذلك على أن الغائب جوهر؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. فقد تركوا مذهبهم ووافقوا النصارى، وإن قالوا: لا. نقضوا دليلهم ثم نقول لهم : إنما يجب التعدية من الشاهد إلى الغائب إذا تعلق أحد الأمرين بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، كما في العلم والعالم، والحركة والمتحرك، وذلك مما لا يقتصر على مجرد الوجود، بل يشترط فيه زيادة شرط، وهو أن يستحيل إضافته إلى غيره، ألا ترى أن العالم كما لا ينفك عن العلم والعلم عن العالم، يستحيل إضافة كونه عالما إلى شيء وراء العلم. فعلم أنه كان عالما لأن له علما فوجبت التعدية إلى الغائب والجوهرية مع القيام بالذات إن كان لا ينفكان في الشاهد ولكن لما لم يكن جوهرا لقيامه بالذات بل لكونه أصلا يتركب منه الجسم لم يجب تعدية كونه جوهرا يتعدى كونه قائما بالذات وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فلم قلتم : إنهما كانا في الشاهد موجودين قائمين بالذات لأن كل واحد منهما بجهة من صاحبه أو كان كل واحد منهما بجهة صاحبه لأنهما موجودان قائمان بالذات ثم نقول لهم: لو كانا موجودين قائمين بالذات لأن كل واحد منهما بجهة من صاحبه، لكان الموجود القائم بالذات بالجهة وإن لم يكن معه غيره، ولكان الباري جل وعلا
The intellectual doubts of the opponents and the reply to them. As for what the opponents have resorted to from intellectual doubts, we say—and with Allah is success—: As for the resolution of the first doubt, which is: that two existents subsisting by themselves cannot avoid each one being a direction from its companion. We say—and with Allah is success—: The two existents subsisting by themselves—each one of them in the witnessed (al-shāhid) may be above its companion and the other below it. Do you allow this in the unseen (al-ghā'ib)? If they say: Yes, they abandon their doctrine, for they do not allow that the Blessed and Most High be beneath the world. If they say: No, they invalidate their proof. If they say: We only did not allow this in the unseen because a direction below a direction is blame and deficiency, and the Blessed and Most High is transcendent above deficiencies and attributes of blame. It is said to them: So when you have established the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen in the presence of a proof of distinction, since you do not allow the unseen to be in the lower direction even though that is permissible in the witnessed, due to the establishment of the proof of distinction—which is the impossibility of deficiency and attribute of blame for the unseen, and the permissibility of that for the witnessed—why do you say that the proof of distinction in our case does not exist? Rather it exists: it necessitates temporality (ḥudūth), which is impossible for the unseen [but] permissible, indeed obligatory, for the witnessed. Then we say to them: That a direction below a direction is blame and deficiency is not conceded, for there is no deficiency in that nor elevation in highness of place, for how many a guard is above the roof and an amir in the house and a scout in the elevated places and a sultan in the low places? Then we say to them: Every self-subsisting existent in the witnessed is a substance, and every substance is self-subsisting. Do you then deduce from that that the unseen is a substance? If they say: Yes, they abandon their doctrine and agree with the Christians. If they say: No, they refute their proof. Then we say to them: The transition (ta'diya) from the witnessed to the unseen is obligatory only when one of the two matters is related to the other as a cause to an effect, as in knowledge and the knower, and motion and the mover. This is not limited to mere existence, but an additional condition is required: that it is impossible to attribute it to something else. Do you not see that just as the knower is inseparable from knowledge and knowledge from the knower, it is impossible to attribute his being knowing to something other than knowledge? So it is known that he was knowing because he had knowledge, so the transition to the unseen became obligatory. As for substantiality along with self-subsistence, even though they are inseparable in the witnessed, but since it is not a substance because of its self-subsistence, but because it is a basic component from which the body is composed, it is not obligatory to transfer its being a substance—it would be transferring its being self-subsisting. And since the matter is so, why do you say that they are in the witnessed two existents self-subsisting because each one is a direction from its companion, or each one is a direction of its companion because they are two existents self-subsisting? Then we say to them: If they were two existents self-subsisting because each one is a direction from its companion, then the self-subsisting existent would be with direction even if no other is with it, and the Blessed and Most High would be...
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first intellectual doubt (al-shubha al-ʿaqliyya al-ūlā) raised by the opponents. The doubt: two self-subsisting existents cannot avoid being in a direction relative to each other, so if God and the world both exist, God must be in some direction from the world. Nasafi's refutation proceeds in several steps: 1) He asks the opponents whether they allow that in the unseen (ghā'ib), i.e., God, could be below the world as is possible in the witnessed (shāhid). If they say yes, they contradict their own position; if no, they undermine their analogy. 2) They argue that a lower direction is a deficiency, but Nasafi denies that being below is inherently deficient—examples from rulers and guards show no honor in spatial height. 3) He then attacks their method by applying the same analogy to prove God is a substance (jawhar), which they would reject. 4) He establishes the correct condition for analogy: it is only valid when there is a causal relationship (ʿilla-maʿlūl) and it is impossible to attribute the property to something else, as in knowledge and the knower. Spatial direction does not meet this condition. 5) Finally, he argues that if direction were necessary for self-subsistence, then even a solitary self-subsisting entity would have a direction, which is absurd. The passage ends incomplete, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
موجود قائم بالذات (mawjūd qā'im bidh-dhāt)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā'ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any observed property in the world (like having a direction) automatically applies to God without evidence.
Correction: Nasafi shows that analogy requires careful reasoning; the opponents themselves admit a proof of distinction (deficiency) that blocks the analogy in the lower direction case, so they inconsistently reject that proof in the original doubt.

Checkpoint
What is the first intellectual doubt that Nasafi refutes?
A) That God is a substance
B) That two self-subsisting existents cannot avoid having a direction relative to each other
C) That God is located above the Throne
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Nasafi exposes the opponents' inconsistency by asking whether they would allow God to be below the world, which they reject, thereby undermining their own analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that the opponents were inconsistent: they accepted a proof of distinction for deficiency but not for temporality. Where in your own life do you hold double standards regarding what you accept from revelation versus your own reasoning? Will you commit to being consistent in submitting to all of Allah's proofs, even when inconvenient?
?
Nasafi points out that direction and physical location do not determine honor or deficiency. Do you measure people's worth by their social status, wealth, or position—things that are mere accidents of location and circumstance? How can you train yourself to see people as Allah sees them, not by their worldly rank?
?
The argument relies on the principle that what holds in the witnessed world may not hold in the unseen without a proof. Are there areas in your faith where you have imposed your limited experience onto Allah's majesty? What step can you take today to affirm His transcendence over your mental assumptions?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

Refutation Through Substance Analogy and Causal Condition
Nasafi uses the substance analogy and causal condition to further refute the doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's further refutation: using the substance analogy to show the flaw in the opponents' method, and establishing the correct condition for analogy (causal relation) that prevents applying direction to God.

Core Idea
Analogy requires a causal link, not just shared existence.

Nasafi continues his refutation by turning the opponents' own method against them: if every self-subsisting existent in the witnessed is a substance, then by their logic God would be a substance—an absurd conclusion they reject. He then lays out the correct criterion for analogy: it must be based on a causal relationship, as in knowledge and the knower, not on mere coexistence of properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the substance analogy
Read the part where Nasafi says: every self-subsisting existent in the witnessed is a substance. Do you then deduce that the unseen is a substance? Understand that this would force the opponents to accept that God is a substance, which they deny.
2
Analyze the condition for correct analogy (ta'diya)
Nasafi explains that analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is only valid when there is a cause-effect relationship (علة ومعلول), as in knowledge (cause) and the knower (effect). Mere co-occurrence is insufficient.
3
Apply the condition to direction
Direction is not causally linked to self-subsistence; it is accidental. Therefore, the analogy fails. Nasafi also argues that if direction were necessary, a single self-subsisting entity would still have direction, which is absurd.
4
Note the incompleteness
The passage ends mid-sentence. The full conclusion is not provided, but the argument is clearly leading to the rejection of the doubt.

Commentary

The doubt: two self-subsisting existents cannot avoid being in a direction relative to each other, so if God and the world both exist, God must be in some direction from the world. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any property found together with another in the witnessed can be applied to the unseen if the witnessed property is also present.
Correction: Nasafi shows that a causal relationship is required; mere coexistence (like self-subsistence and direction) is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when is analogy (ta'diya) from the witnessed to the unseen correct?
A) When both share any attribute
B) When there is a causal relationship between the attributes
C) When the unseen is similar to the witnessed in all respects
D) When the attribute is spatial

Key Takeaway
Nasafi establishes that valid analogy requires a causal connection, not just shared existence, thereby refuting the opponents' argument about direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use analogies to understand Allah's attributes, do you check whether the connection you assume is genuinely causal, or do you settle for superficial similarity? How might such shortcuts lead to a flawed understanding of divine transcendence? Will you commit to examining your reasoning before reaching conclusions about Allah?
?
This passage shows how easily people liken Allah to creation without a sound basis. In your own heart, are there hidden comparisons between Allah and created things that you have not confronted? What specific step can you take today to identify and repent from such hidden shirk in your thoughts?
?
Nasafi insists that a valid analogy requires a cause-effect relationship. Reflect on your own reasoning about Allah's attributes: have you ever assumed that because a quality exists in creation, a similar quality must exist in Allah without verifying the causal link? Are you willing to pause and seek knowledge before forming beliefs about Allah? Make a firm intention to guard your aqeedah from false analogies.

Life as a Condition for Attributes: Rational Refutation of al-Salihi
Refutes al-Salihi's claim that life is not a condition for knowledge and power using rational analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sound intellect necessarily affirms life as a condition for knowledge and power, and why the denial of this condition leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If life and death cannot coexist, neither can death and knowledge.

This passage presents a powerful rational proof that life is a necessary condition for attributes like knowledge and power. Imam al-Nasafi refutes the claim of al-Salihi and the Karramiyya that these attributes can exist in the dead. Through a careful analogy and a reductio ad absurdum, he shows that such a claim violates the basic principles of sound reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the flow from analogy to absurdity to conclusion.
The passage uses a tight argumentative style.
2
Identify the core analogy
Focus on the first part: sound intellects reject the coexistence of life and death, and similarly reject the coexistence of knowledge/power with death. The author treats them as equally impossible.
This analogy is the foundation of the proof.
3
Examine the reductio ad absurdum
The author then shows that if knowledge could exist in the dead, then absurd consequences follow: every beautiful thing could be made by inanimate objects, rocks could wage war, etc. This demonstrates the irrationality of the claim.
The reductio strengthens the argument by showing its logical implications.
4
Understand the final ruling
The passage concludes by explicitly declaring the statements of al-Salihi and the Karramiyya as false, affirming that life is a condition for attributes like knowledge, power, hearing, and sight.
This is the doctrinal takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقول السليمة كما تأبى قبول قول من أخبر عن اجتماع الموت والحياة والسواد والبياض والحركة والسكون، تأبى قبول قول من يجوز ثبوت العلم والقدرة للميت، وتعرف امتناع اجتماعهما مع الموت، كما تعرف امتناع اجتماع الحياة والموت ولا تفرق بينهما، فلو جاز ذا لجاز الأول، ولو امتنع الأول لامتنع هذا لانعدام ما يوجب التفرقة بين الأمرين في العقول الصحيحة يحققه أن ذلك لو جاز لجاز أن يكون كل ديباج نفيس، وكل صورة مؤنقة، وكل قصر عال في العالم، كانت حاصلة عن فعل الجمادات والموتى. ولعل كل تصنيف دقيق في فن من العلوم، كان من عمل الجمادات، ولعل صخور العراق وجبالها وأشجارها، تريد مقاتلة ما يجانسها من ذلك بخراسان وما وراء النهر، فتجر العساكر وتضرب الطبول وتنفخ في البوقات، ويقاتلون مقاتلة عظيمة وتجويز هذا كله هذيان وخروج عن قضية العقول والتحاق بالمتجاهلة.
قول الصالحي والكرامية في أن الحياة ليست بشرط لبقية الصفات قول باطل.
وعرف بهذا بطلان قول أبي الحسين الصالحي : إن الحياة ليست بشرط القدرة والعلم، ويجوز وجودهما في الموتى والأجسام المواتية وكذا السمع والبصر.
Sound intellects, just as they refuse to accept the statement of one who reports the coexistence of death and life, blackness and whiteness, movement and stillness, so they refuse to accept the statement of one who permits the existence of knowledge and power for the dead, and they recognize the impossibility of their coexistence with death, just as they recognize the impossibility of the coexistence of life and death, and they do not distinguish between the two. For if this were permissible, the first would be permissible, and if the first were impossible, this would be impossible due to the lack of something that would necessitate a distinction between the two matters in sound intellects. It verifies that if that were permissible, it would be permissible that every precious brocade, every beautiful image, and every lofty palace in the world were produced by the action of inanimate objects and the dead. And perhaps every precise classification in a field of sciences was the work of inanimate objects, and perhaps the rocks of Iraq, its mountains, and its trees want to fight those similar to them in Khurasan and Transoxiana, so they drag armies, beat drums, blow trumpets, and fight a great battle. Permitting all this is raving and departure from the judgment of intellects and attachment to the ignorant.
The statement of al-Salihi and al-Karramiyya that life is not a condition for the remaining attributes is a false statement.
And by this, the falsity of the statement of Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi is known: that life is not a condition for power and knowledge, and it is permissible for them to exist in the dead and inanimate bodies, and likewise hearing and sight.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof that life is a necessary condition for attributes like knowledge and power. It begins with an argument from analogy: just as sound intellects reject the coexistence of death and life, blackness and whiteness, or movement and stillness, they also reject the possibility of knowledge and power existing in a dead being. The intellect recognizes the impossibility of these pairs together, and there is no distinction between the cases of life/death and knowledge/death. The author then strengthens this by showing the absurd consequences: if such coexistence were allowed, one could attribute every elaborate creation to inanimate objects and dead bodies, even complex scholarly works, and imagine rocks and trees waging war. Such ideas are dismissed as raving and contrary to reason. Then the passage explicitly declares the statement of al-Salihi and al-Karramiyya false, and specifically refutes Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi's claim that life is not a condition for power, knowledge, hearing, and sight, concluding that these attributes cannot exist in dead or inanimate bodies.

Key Terms
العقول السليمة
العلم
القدرة
الحياة
الموت
السمع
البصر
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That life is not a condition for knowledge and power; these attributes can exist in the dead (as held by al-Salihi and al-Karramiyya).
Correction: Sound intellect necessarily requires life for such attributes; their coexistence with death is as impossible as life and death coexisting.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the sound intellect reject regarding the attributes of the dead?
A) It rejects only the possibility of life and death together.
B) It rejects the possibility of knowledge and power coexisting with death, just as it rejects life and death together.
C) It rejects the possibility of any attributes in the dead.
D) It rejects the claim that life is a condition for attributes.

Key Takeaway
Sound intellect affirms that life is a necessary condition for knowledge and power, and to claim otherwise leads to irrational absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge and power are gifts that will cease when your life ends. Are you using them today as if you believe that, or do you act as if you have infinite time? What is one concrete action you will take this week to invest your knowledge and power for the sake of Allah, knowing that death will end them?
?
The lesson shows that sound intellect cannot accept the idea of knowledge without life. Yet, how often do you seek knowledge without intending to act on it before you die? Does your pursuit of knowledge reflect the urgency of someone who knows that knowledge is tied to this fleeting life?
?
If you truly internalized that your hearing and sight are only possible because of your life, would you be more careful about what you listen to and look at? What will you change today in how you use your senses, aware that they are loaned to you only until death?

God's Power and the Impossibility of Contradictory States
A reductio argument showing God's power cannot produce contradictory states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God's power does not extend to creating contradictory states, and why such contradictions are impossible.

Core Idea
God's power cannot create logical impossibilities, like a body being both moving and still at once.

This passage presents a subtle reductio argument used by Maturidi theologians. The opponent claims that God needs life as a prerequisite for creating power and knowledge. The author shows that this line of reasoning would lead to an absurd consequence: a body being simultaneously moving and still. Since that is impossible, the opponent's claim collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the logical structure: premise, hypothetical, impossibility, conclusion.
The argument uses 'لو' (if) to set up the hypothetical.
2
Identify the impossible consequence
Find the part where the author says what would happen if God created motion in a locus of stillness. What is the contradictory state?
The key phrase is 'متحركا ساكنا' (moving and still).
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the impossibility of the consequence leads to the rejection of the opponent's original claim. Why does the author say the opponent's reasoning is not valid?
This shows that divine power is restricted by logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يقال كما أن الله تعالى لا يستعين بشيء لا يمنعه أيضا شيء، فينبغي أن يقال إنه تعالى لو خلق في محل سكونا لكان قادرا أن يخلق في محل السكون الحركة ويجوز أن يخلق فيصير الجسم متحركا ساكنا في حالة واحدة وكذا في جميع المتضادات، وحيث كان هذا مستحيلا، دل أن الآخر مستحيل، وما هذا سبيله لا يعد منيعا ولا احتجاجا ولا استعانة.
And likewise it is said: just as God Almighty does not seek help from anything, nothing prevents Him either. So it should be said: if He created stillness in a locus, He would be able to create motion in the locus of stillness, and it would be permissible for Him to create, so the body would become moving and still in one state—and likewise for all opposites. But since this is impossible, it indicates that the other (the objector's claim) is impossible. And such a thing is not counted as a prevention, an argument, or a seeking of help.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument. The author begins by noting that God is not assisted by anything, nor is anything a hindrance to Him. However, the objector's line of reasoning would imply that if God creates stillness in a place, He could then create motion in that same place at the same time, resulting in a body being both moving and still—which is impossible. Since this leads to an impossibility, the objector's original claim (that God needs life as a prerequisite for creating power and knowledge) is also impossible. The author concludes that such reasoning is not a valid objection or argument, nor does it indicate any limitation on God's power. The passage defends divine omnipotence while upholding logical consistency.

Key Terms
قدرة
حركة
سكون
مضادة
استعانة
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can create contradictory states like a body being both moving and still.
Correction: Logical impossibilities are not within the scope of divine power because they are inherently unintelligible and self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
What impossible consequence does the author derive from the opponent's reasoning?
A) A body being both moving and still at the same time.
B) God being unable to create motion.
C) Life being created without a body.
D) Knowledge existing without life.

Key Takeaway
God's power is absolute but cannot produce logical contradictions, as they are inherently impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself expecting God to act in contradictory ways—like asking for forgiveness while refusing to let go of a sin you love? In what area of your life are you asking God to make something both white and black at the same time?
?
The passage declares that 'nothing prevents Him' from acting. Is there some fear in your heart that something—your past, your circumstances, or even your own unworthiness—can actually block God's will for your life? What would change if you truly believed that nothing can prevent Him?
?
When life feels impossible, do you ever subtly doubt God's power because you cannot see a way out? How does this lesson's reminder that God's power works within the realm of logical possibility—not fantasy—reshape your trust in Him today?

The Logical Absurdity of Denying Divine Attributes
Imam Nasafi demonstrates the self-contradiction in saying God knows without knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying divine attributes leads to a logical contradiction based on self-evident innate cognitions.

Core Idea
Denying God's attributes is a self-contradictory absurdity exposed by innate rational truths.

Imam Nasafi uses a powerful logical argument to demonstrate that saying God knows without knowledge or is powerful without power is an obvious contradiction, even to the most ignorant. This argument is rooted in the concept of innate cognitions (shahādāt al-maʿārif), which are primary, self-evident truths ingrained in every discerning human. This lesson unpacks the argument and its implications for the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the forceful language used by Imam Nasafi.
The passage is a continuous argument; do not break it prematurely.
2
Identify the central contradiction
Find where Imam Nasafi states the core idea: that claiming God knows without knowledge is an impossible contradiction (muḥāl mutanāqiḍ).
This is the anchor of the whole argument.
3
Compare to other absurd statements
The Imam compares this to saying 'the second god' or 'God is not powerful'. Realize how these are immediately rejected by any rational person.
This comparison highlights the obviousness of the contradiction.
4
Learn the technical terms
Understand the terms 'shahādāt al-maʿārif' (testimony of innate cognitions) and 'al-istidlāl bi al-ārā' al-zā'ifa' (argument by false opinions).
These are logical categories that classify the argument.
5
Reflect on the rhetorical question
End with Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's question: what rational person could choose such a contradictory belief?
This underscores the self-evident absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن القول بأن الله تعالى عالم بما لا علم له به، وقادر على ما لا قدرة له عليه، محال متناقض لا يخفي تناقضه على أغبى خليقة الله تعالى. وكذا لا يختلف على أسماع أهل اللسان قول القائل : الله الثاني. وكذا قوله: الله تعالى ليس بقادر على شيء، وقوله : لا قدرة له على شيء فإذا على هذا ما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة من جملة الآراء المستشنعة المسترذلة التي يتسارع كل سامع إلى إكفار قائله ونسبته إلى المناقضة، وهذا النوع من الاستدلال يسمى عند أرباب المنطق الاستشهاد بشهادات المعارف. ويعنون بالمعارف العلوم الأولية الثابتة في أصل خلقة كل مميز وجبلته، ولهذا يقولون: إن من تمسك بمثل هذا الرأي ينبغي أن تصور عقيدته للدهماء ليقابلوه بالطعن والاستهزاء، ويسمى هذا الاستدلال عندهم أيضا الاستدلال بالآراء الزائفة. واستدل الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور – رحمه الله بذلك الدليل ثم قال في أثناء كلامه : أي قلب يصر على القول بأنه عالم بما لا علم له به يحتمل أن يختاره عاقل فضلا عن أن يصيب غيره؟!
What confirms it is that the statement that Allah, exalted, is knowing without having knowledge, and powerful without having power, is an impossible contradiction whose contradiction is not hidden from the most ignorant of Allah's creatures. Similarly, the statement of one who says 'the second god' does not differ from the ears of the people of language. Likewise his saying: 'Allah, exalted, is not powerful over anything' and his saying: 'He has no power over anything.' So upon this, what the Mu'tazila have gone to is among the repugnant, contemptible opinions that every hearer hastens to declare its speaker an unbeliever and to attribute him to contradiction. And this type of argument is called by the masters of logic 'testimony of the innate cognitions' (al-istishhād bi shahādāt al-maʿārif). By 'cognitions' they mean the primary sciences established in the origin of the creation and constitution of every discerning person. For this reason they say: whoever adheres to such an opinion, it is appropriate that his belief be presented to the common people so that they meet him with criticism and mockery. And this argument is also called by them 'argument by false opinions' (al-istidlāl bi al-ārā' al-zā'ifa). And the Shaykh al-Imām Abū Manṣūr – may Allah have mercy on him – argued with this evidence and then said in the course of his speech: 'What heart persists in the statement that He is knowing without having knowledge – can a rational person choose it, let alone attain what is other than it?!'
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof against the Mu'tazila who deny attributes. The Imam begins by stating that saying God is knowing without knowledge or powerful without power is an obvious contradiction, even to the most ignorant person. He compares it to saying 'God is second' or 'God is not powerful' – such statements are immediately rejected by any native speaker. He concludes that the Mu'tazila view is among the repugnant opinions that lead listeners to declare its speaker an unbeliever. Then he notes that this type of argument is called in logic 'testimony of innate cognitions' (shahādāt al-maʿārif), referring to primary, self-evident truths ingrained in human nature. Because of this, advocates of such absurd opinions deserve to be publicly ridiculed. The argument is also called 'argument by false opinions'. Finally, he quotes Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who asks rhetorically what rational person could choose such a contradictory statement.

Key Terms
محال متناقض
شهادات المعارف
آراء زائفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this is merely emotional rhetoric or name-calling.
Correction: Imam Nasafi explicitly grounds his argument in logical categories (shahādāt al-maʿārif), showing it is based on self-evident rational truths, not just emotion.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Nasafi describe the type of argument that exposes the contradiction in the Mu'tazila position?
A) It is a scriptural argument based on consensus.
B) It is called 'testimony of innate cognitions' and 'argument by false opinions'.
C) It is an argument from the consensus of scholars.
D) It is a purely philosophical argument.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God knows without knowledge is a self-contradictory absurdity that violates the self-evident truths ingrained in human nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah knows everything', do you truly believe that He possesses real, eternal knowledge, or do you treat it as a mere metaphor? This passage insists on the reality of attributes—does your heart affirm that without hesitation?
?
The passage says even the most ignorant recognize the contradiction. Have you ever casually accepted a theological claim that your own innate reason would reject if you examined it? What is one belief about Allah you hold that you should test against the self-evident truths He placed in you?
?
If the Mu'tazila's opinion is described as 'repugnant' and 'contemptible' for denying attributes, how do you treat others who hold such views? Do you respond with the same intellectual clarity and spiritual urgency, or do you tolerate confusion? Will you commit to learning the proofs for Allah's attributes so you are not swayed by false arguments?

Refuting Contradictions with Self-Evident Truths
Al-Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite view using self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses self-evident truths (primary cognitions) to refute the Mu'tazilite position on God's knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths expose theological contradictions instantly.

Imam al-Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazilite claim about God's knowledge and power is inherently contradictory, and any rational person can see this. He then explains that such refutations rely on self-evident truths called 'primary cognitions' that are innate in every discerning person. In this lesson, we will examine his argument and the logical method behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how al-Nasafi begins with a strong claim of verification.
2
Identify the core claim and examples
Al-Nasafi lists examples of contradictory statements about God. Find them and notice how he ties them to the Mu'tazilite view.
The examples are: 'God knows what He does not know' and 'God is not capable of anything'.
3
Understand the reasoning method
Al-Nasafi explains that this type of reasoning appeals to self-evident truths known as 'shahadat al-ma'arif' (primary cognitions). Reflect on why these truths are considered universal and innate.
He contrasts this with 'reasoning by spurious opinions' (al-istidlal bi al-ara' al-za'ifa).
4
Reflect on the rhetorical conclusion
Note that al-Nasafi suggests that proponents of such views should be publicly displayed for mockery. What does this say about the gravity of holding contradictory theological positions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن القول بأن الله تعالى عالم بما لا علم له به، وقادر على ما لا قدرة له عليه، محال متناقض لا يخفي تناقضه على أغبى خليقة الله تعالى. وكذا لا يختلف على أسماع أهل اللسان قول القائل : الله الثاني. وكذا قوله: الله تعالى ليس بقادر على شيء، وقوله : لا قدرة له على شيء فإذا على هذا ما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة من جملة الآراء المستشنعة المسترذلة التي يتسارع كل سامع إلى إكفار قائله ونسبته إلى المناقضة، وهذا النوع من الاستدلال يسمى عند أرباب المنطق الاستشهاد بشهادات المعارف. ويعنون بالمعارف العلوم الأولية الثابتة في أصل خلقة كل مميز وجبلته، ولهذا يقولون: إن من تمسك بمثل هذا الرأي ينبغي أن تصور عقيدته للدهماء ليقابلوه بالطعن والاستهزاء، ويسمى هذا الاستدلال عندهم أيضا الاستدلال بالآراء الزائفة.
It is verified that the statement that Allah, exalted, is knowledgeable of what He has no knowledge of, and capable of what He has no power over, is impossible and contradictory, and its contradiction is not hidden from the most dull-witted of Allah's creation. Likewise, the saying of one who says 'the second god' is not disputed among the ears of the people of language. Similarly, his saying 'Allah, exalted, is not capable of anything,' and his saying 'He has no power over anything.' So according to this, what the Mu'tazilites went to is among the disgraceful, despicable opinions that every listener hastens to declare its proponent a disbeliever and to attribute him to contradiction. This type of reasoning is called by the logicians 'testimony by primary cognitions'. And by 'cognitions' they mean the primary sciences established in the original creation and disposition of every discerning person. For this reason they say: Whoever clings to such an opinion, his belief should be depicted to the common people so that they meet it with criticism and mockery. This reasoning is also called by them 'reasoning by spurious opinions'.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position regarding God's knowledge and power. Imam al-Nasafi begins by asserting that it is verified that stating Allah knows what He does not know or is capable of what He has no power over is inherently contradictory, and the contradiction is apparent even to the dullest intellect. He then extends this to other related statements, such as saying 'God is not capable of anything' or 'He has no power over anything.' He claims that such statements are immediately recognized as false by any speaker of the language. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite view that leads to such conclusions is among the most disgraceful and despicable opinions, and any listener would be quick to declare its holder a disbeliever because it contradicts clear reason. Al-Nasafi then explains that this type of reasoning is called by logicians 'istishhad bi-shahadat al-ma'arif' (testimony by primary cognitions), meaning that it appeals to self-evident truths that are innate in every rational person. He notes that the proponents of such faulty reasoning should have their beliefs publicly displayed so that the common people can criticize and mock them. He also mentions that this reasoning is called 'reasoning by spurious opinions' (al-istidlal bi-al-ara' al-za'ifa). This passage demonstrates the method of using self-evident truths to refute false doctrines.

Key Terms
