Misconception: The Mu'tazilite position on God's knowledge and power is a legitimate theological nuance that requires careful reflection.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the position leads to self-evident contradictions that any rational person can immediately recognize, making it not a subtle nuance but a basic error.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Three Positions on the Seen-Unseen Analogy
Classification of three groups on using analogy to prove Creator and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on using analogy between the seen and unseen to prove God's existence and attributes, and the Maturidi response.

Core Idea
Analogy between seen and unseen must be used correctly to affirm both existence and attributes.

Imam al-Nasafi presents three groups who differ on how to apply the analogy between the seen world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib) when proving the Creator and His attributes. Understanding these positions clarifies why the Maturidi method insists on proper equivalence and submission to the evidence. Let's examine each group and the author's own stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage lists three groups and then states the author's own method.
2
Identify the three groups
The passage describes three positions: those who equate fully, those who differentiate completely (denying the Maker), and those who equate on existence but differentiate on attributes. Identify each and note the consequences.
The first group is only implied; the second and third are explicitly described.
3
Understand the Maturidi method
The author states his own basis: inference from the seen with proper equivalence and submission (inqiyaad) to the indication of the essence. Ask yourself why submission is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتسوية بين الغائب والشاهد عند وجود التسوية. وإما أن يفرقوا بين الشاهد والغائب في كل ذلك ويقولوا بأن المفعول وإن دل على وجود الفاعل وقدرته وإحكامه وإن دل على علمه في الشاهد، ففي الغائب لا يدل على شيء، لا على وجود الفاعل ولا على قدرته وعلمه فينكرون الصانع وقدرته وعلمه، فيلزمهم إنكار الصانع، وإن وجد دليل وجوده، كما أنكروا قدرته وعلمه، وإن وجد دليل وجودهما وإما أن يسووا بين الشاهد والغائب في حق دلالة المفعول على الفاعل، ويقروا بثبوت الصانع، ويفرقوا بين الشاهد والغائب في حق دلالته على القدرة والعلم ويقولوا : وإن كان ذلك دليلا عليهما في الشاهد وليس بدليل عليهما في الغائب فيصيروا مناقضين من وجهين: أحدهما بالتفرقة بين الدلالة على الذات والدلالة على الصفات والآخر بالتفرقة بين الشاهد والغائب في الدلالة على الصفات ومتعلقنا في ذلك الاستدلال بالشاهد ووجود التسوية بينه وبين الغائب والاستدلال بالانقياد للدلالة على الذات والقول به والله الموفق. واعتراض أبى الهذيل على هذه الطريقة الجواب عنه على ما مر.
And the equivalence between the unseen and the seen when there is equivalence. Or they differentiate between the seen and the unseen in all of that and say that the effect (al-maf'ul), even if it indicates the existence of the agent and his power and his perfection and even if it indicates his knowledge in the seen, in the unseen it does not indicate anything, neither the existence of the agent nor his power and knowledge, so they deny the Maker and His power and knowledge, so they are compelled to deny the Maker, even if the proof of His existence exists, just as they denied His power and knowledge even though the proof of their existence exists. Or they equate between the seen and the unseen with respect to the indication (dalaala) of the effect upon the agent, and affirm the existence of the Maker, but differentiate between the seen and the unseen with respect to its indication upon power and knowledge and say: Even if that is evidence for them in the seen, it is not evidence for them in the unseen, so they become contradictory in two respects: one is the differentiation between the indication upon the essence and the indication upon the attributes, and the other is the differentiation between the seen and the unseen in the indication upon the attributes. And our basis (muta'alliquna) in that is the inference from the seen and the existence of equivalence between it and the unseen, and the inference by submission (al-inqiyaad) to the indication upon the essence and affirmation of it. And God is the Giver of success. And the objection of Abu al-Hudhayl to this method, the answer to it is as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three groups regarding the use of analogy (qiyaas al-ghaa'ib 'ala al-shahid) to prove the existence and attributes of God. The first group equates the seen and unseen in all respects, but the author does not elaborate here. The second group differentiates completely, claiming that the effect (maf'ul) indicates the agent only in the seen but not in the unseen, leading them to deny the Maker and His attributes. The third group equates regarding the existence of the Maker but differentiates regarding the attributes, leading to contradiction. The author then states his own method (Maturidi): inference from the seen with proper equivalence and submission to the indication of the essence. He ends by noting that Abu al-Hudhayl's objection to this method has been answered earlier.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
المفعول
الدلالة
الصانع
التسوية
التفريق
الانقياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all groups who use analogy between shahid and gha'ib end up denying attributes.
Correction: Rather, some affirm attributes correctly through proper analogy, as the Maturidi method does with equivalence and submission.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what results when a group equates the seen and unseen regarding the indication of the effect on the agent but differentiates regarding the indication on power and knowledge?
A) They become consistent in their affirmation of attributes.
B) They become contradictory in two respects.
C) They deny the existence of the Maker.
D) They accept the answer of Abu al-Hudhayl.

Key Takeaway
The correct approach is to use the seen-unseen analogy with equivalence and submission to the evidence, affirming both essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you truly submit to the evidence that points to the Creator's existence and attributes, or do you hold back in some area of your life, trying to keep God at a distance?
?
The third group accepted God's existence but denied His attributes, and the text says they fell into contradiction. Are there aspects of God's nature—like His power over your life or His complete knowledge of your secrets—that you acknowledge intellectually but resist living out?
?
This lesson teaches that partial acceptance of the evidence leads to inconsistency. Is there any part of your faith where you are 'differentiating'—accepting God in theory but denying His involvement in the details of your daily choices? What would it take for you to submit fully?

Refuting the Claim that Knowledge of the Creator is Self-Evident
Author refutes the claim that knowledge of the Creator is self-evident by listing those who disagree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that knowledge of the Creator is self-evident by demonstrating that rational people disagree on this point.

Core Idea
Disagreement among rational people proves that knowledge of the Creator is not self-evident.

The author counters the claim that every rational person innately knows that the Creator is knowing. He points to historical groups who hold contrary views. Since these groups are sound-minded and agree on truly self-evident truths, their disagreement here shows the claim is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's argumentative tone and the list of groups.
The passage begins with 'Then it is said to them:' indicating a rebuttal.
2
Identify the four groups and their views
List each group mentioned: natural philosophers, astrologers, Mu'tazila, and Thumāma ibn al-Ashras. Note what each group attributes well-ordered acts to.
3
Understand the logical structure
The author argues that since these groups are rational and do not differ on truly self-evident matters, their disagreement on this specific claim proves that the claim is not self-evident.
The key premise is that they are 'people of sound intellects'.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: if knowledge of the Creator is not self-evident, it must be inferred from evidence, which is the foundation of kalam arguments.
This lesson prepares you to understand why inference (istidlāl) is central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إن كثيرا من أهل الطبائع يجوزون الفعل المحكم من الطبائع، وإن المنجمين منهم من يجوز ذلك من الكواكب، والمعتزلة يجوزون حصول الأفعال المحكمة على طريق التولد عن الميت والنائم والساهي وثمامة بن الأشرس يجعل المتولدات أفعالا لا فاعل لها. وهؤلاء كلهم من أرباب العقول السليمة، لا يختلفون في البديهيات، فدل أن دعوتهم كون العلم بأن الفاعل عالم بديهيا باطلة مجحودة. والله الموفق.
Then it is said to them: Indeed, many of the people of natural philosophy allow the well-ordered act from natures, and the astrologers among them allow that from the stars, and the Mu'tazila allow the occurrence of well-ordered acts by way of generation (tawallud) from the dead, the sleeping, and the heedless, and Thumāma ibn al-Ashras makes generated acts acts that have no agent. And all of these are among the people of sound intellects, they do not differ regarding the self-evident (badīhī) matters. So this indicates that their claim that knowledge that the agent is knowing is self-evident is false and manifestly invalid. And Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that knowledge of the Creator being knowing is self-evident (badīhī). The author argues by pointing out that many rational people hold views that contradict this necessitation: natural philosophers allow well-ordered acts to arise from blind natures; astrologers attribute them to stars; Mu'tazila allow such acts to be generated from inanimate or unconscious beings; and Thumāma ibn al-Ashras even denies any agent for generated acts. Since these groups are rational and agree on truly self-evident matters, their disagreement on this point proves that the claim of its necessity is false. The conclusion is that the alleged self-evident knowledge is actually a false claim (bāṭila majḥūda).

Key Terms
بديهي (badīhī, self-evident)
فعل محكم (fi'l muḥkam, well-ordered act)
طبائع (ṭabā'i', natural elements)
تولد (tawallud, generation)
فاعل (fā'il, agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of the Creator is self-evident (badihi) and all rational minds recognize it.
Correction: The author shows that many rational groups do not recognize it, so it is not self-evident but requires inference.

Checkpoint
Why does the author cite the views of natural philosophers, astrologers, and Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) To show that they are all irrational
B) To prove that well-ordered acts require a knowledgeable agent
C) To demonstrate that rational people disagree on whether the act necessitates knowledge of the agent, so it cannot be self-evident
D) To argue that generation (tawallud) is impossible

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that since rational people disagree on whether a well-ordered act proves a knowing agent, the claim that such knowledge is self-evident is false; it must be inferential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the existence and knowledge of the Creator were truly self-evident to you, why do you still find yourself living at times as if He is absent? In what areas of your life have you silently attributed order to 'nature' or 'chance' rather than to a knowing agent? This passage demands that you confront those moments and choose to infer His presence intentionally.
?
The author lists rational people who deny the necessity of a knowing agent. Have you ever allowed intellectual doubts or modern naturalistic explanations to dull your certainty in the Creator? What specific step will you take today to actively remind yourself that the well-ordered world demands a knowing Maker—rather than slipping into passive assumption?

The Inversion Argument: Knowledge of the Agent's Knowledge Is Inferential
Author presents an inversion argument that knowledge of the agent's knowledge is inferential, not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of an agent only necessitates necessary knowledge that the agent has knowledge, but not that the agent is essentially knowing without inference.

Core Idea
The act gives necessary knowledge of knowledge's existence, not of the agent's essential knowing.

This passage presents a subtle inversion argument (ʿaks) against those who claim that a well-ordered act directly implies that its agent is essentially knowing. The author distinguishes between two types of knowledge: necessary knowledge that the agent possesses knowledge as an attribute, and inferential knowledge that the agent is 'knowing' as a description of His essence. By drawing an analogy with the disagreement over the existence of accidents, the argument shows that inference is required whenever rational people disagree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the structure: first the inversion claim, then the reasoning, then the analogy.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow each clause.
2
Identify the two claims about knowledge
Notice that the passage distinguishes between 'necessary knowledge of the existence of knowledge' and 'knowledge of the agent being knowing'. The first is necessary, the second is inferential.
This distinction is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the distinction
The author argues that knowledge cannot subsist without a substrate (it is an accident). Therefore, knowing that the act indicates the existence of knowledge does not automatically tell us that the agent is essentially knowing without further inference.
This is based on the nature of accidents.
4
Examine the analogy with accidents
The author compares the case to knowledge of the existence of accidents, which is also inferential due to disagreement among rational people. Similarly, the agent's being knowing is only known by inference because there is disagreement.
The analogy reinforces the need for inference.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument refutes the opponent's claim that the act gives direct, necessary knowledge of the agent's essential attribute. The lesson is that inference is required in such cases.
This is a common pattern in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم من أن يعكس عليهم فيقال: العلم بأن الفعل المحكم يوجب العلم الضروري بوجود علم فاعله ولا يوجب وجود فاعله عالما إلا بالاستدلال، لما علم أن قيام العلم بذاته من غير ذات يقوم به محال ويقال له: إن كان العلم بثبوت الأعراض استدلاليا ضروريا لاختلاف العقلاء فيه، فكذا لا يعلم كون الفاعل قادرا عالما إلا بالاستدلال، لوقوع الاختلاف فيه بين العقلاء على ما ذكرنا.
And it is said to them: What prevents you from being refuted by an inversion (ʿaks) such that it is said: The knowledge that the well-ordered act necessitates necessary knowledge of the existence of the knowledge of its agent, but it does not necessitate the existence of the agent being knowing except by inference (istidlāl), because it is known that the subsistence of knowledge in a self without a substrate in which it subsists is impossible. And it is said to him: If knowledge of the existence of accidents is inferential, not necessary, due to the disagreement of rational people concerning it, then likewise the agent's being powerful and knowing is not known except by inference, due to the occurrence of disagreement concerning it among rational people as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is an argument from inversion (ʿaks) against those who claim that the act directly implies the agent's attribute of knowledge. The author argues that the act only gives necessary knowledge that the agent has knowledge (as an attribute), but it does not give necessary knowledge that the agent is 'knowing' (as an essential description) without inference. This is because knowledge is an accident that subsists in a substrate; the agent's being 'knowing' is a judgment about the essence. Since rational people disagree about whether the act indicates knowledge or the attribute, the knowledge of the agent's being knowing must be inferential, just as the knowledge of the existence of accidents is inferential due to disagreement.

Key Terms
عكس (ʿaks, inversion)
علم ضروري (ʿilm ḍarūrī, necessary knowledge)
علم وجود العلم (ʿilm wujūd al-ʿilm, knowledge of the existence of knowledge)
استدلال (istidlāl, inference)
اختلاف العقلاء (ikhtilāf al-ʿuqalā', disagreement of rational people)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the well-ordered act directly and necessarily gives knowledge of the agent's essential attribute (being knowing) without any inference.
Correction: The act only gives necessary knowledge that the agent has knowledge as an attribute subsisting in Him. Knowledge that the agent is essentially 'knowing' requires inference, because knowledge as an accident cannot subsist without a substrate, and rational people disagree on the matter.

Checkpoint
According to the inversion argument, what does the well-ordered act directly necessitate?
A) Necessary knowledge that the agent is knowing essentially
B) Necessary knowledge that the agent has knowledge as an attribute subsisting in Him
C) Direct knowledge that the agent is powerful
D) No knowledge at all about the agent

Key Takeaway
The act only gives necessary knowledge that the agent has knowledge, not that the agent is essentially knowing without inference; the latter requires inference due to the nature of accidents and disagreement among rational people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order in the universe, do you ever assume you directly know God's essence without any reasoning? How does this inversion argument challenge your intellectual humility before the Creator?
?
The argument says that disagreement among rational people about God's attributes shows the need for inference. Have you ever dismissed a theological disagreement as mere confusion, rather than seeing it as a sign that the matter requires deeper reasoning?
?
If knowledge of God's attributes is only inferential, what does that mean for your daily reliance on Him? Are you willing to engage in the hard work of inference, or do you settle for easy assumptions that may not hold?

Refutation of the First Ignorance: The Denied Premise and Circular Reasoning
Nasafi refutes the first ignorance by showing the premise is denied and leads to circular reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi identifies the first ignorance as building an argument on a denied premise and how that leads to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
An argument built on an unproven premise collapses into circular reasoning.

Nasafi turns to the first of four ignorances, showing that the opponent's entire argument rests on a claim that they cannot prove without presupposing the very point in dispute. He exposes the circularity of their reasoning and illustrates it with vivid analogies. This lesson will guide you through his refutation and the logical flaw of circular reasoning (dawr).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Nasafi first states the opponent's premise, then denies it, then explains why it's denied, and finally exposes the circularity.
The passage uses technical terms like muqaddima majhuda and dawr.
2
Identify the denied premise
The opponent claims that eternity (al-qidam) is the most specific attribute of God. Nasafi says this is denied (mamnu'). Ask yourself: why would the opponent say that? And why does Nasafi reject it?
3
Follow the circular reasoning
Nasafi shows that proving this premise requires assuming the conclusion. Pay attention to the analogies of the well and the locked door.
Circular reasoning makes both the premise and the conclusion unprovable.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this refutation protects Islamic theology from flawed arguments. The core insight is that a valid proof must not rely on the very point it aims to establish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان هذه الجهالات : أما الأولى منها فنقول : بنوا هذا الكلام على كون القدم من أخص أوصاف الباري جل وعلا وهذا ممنوع ، يقال لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ فإن قالوا : قلنا ذلك لأن الموجود وصف عام يستحيل على القديم والمحدث، وإذا قيل: إنه تعالى قديم فهو وصف الموجود بصفة يختص بها عن غيره من الموجودات ثم ليس في القديم صفة أخص من القدم، وكان هو وصفا خاصا لا أخص منه. قيل: وهل وقع النزاع إلا في هذا؟ أليس أن خصومكم يقولون إن القديم إما أن يكون قائما بالذات وهو ذات الباري جل وعلا، وإما أن يكون غير قائم بالذات وهو صفاته تعالى فكان وصف، وعندكم لا أخص من القديم، فإذا هذه مقدمة مجحودة، لا يمكنهم إثبات هذه المقدمة إلا بإقامة الدليل على نفي قديم غير قائم بالذات، و اختصاص صفة القديم بالذات فحسب، ولو أمكنكم إثبات هذا لوقعت لكم الغنية عن التمسك بهذه المقدمة، فإذا هذا منكم تعلق بدليل لا يثبت صحته إلا بثبوت المدلول وكل دليل هذا سبيله فهو باطل، لأنه لا يمكن إثبات الملول إلا بثبوت الدليل، ولا يمكن إثبات الدليل إلا بثبوت المدلول فلا يتوصل إلى إثبات أحدهما البتة. كمن وقع في بئر ولا يمكنه الخروج إلا بإحضار الحبل، ولا يمكن إحضار الحبل إلا بخروجه فامتنع كل واحد منهما. أو كمن أقفل بابا وترك المفتاح في البيت، ولا يمكن فتح الباب إلا بإخراج المفتاح، ولا إخراج المفتاح إلا بفتح الباب، فامتنع الأمران جميعا فكذا هذا . والله الموفق.
And the explanation of these ignorances: As for the first of them, we say: They built this speech on the fact that eternity (al-qidam) is among the most specific attributes of the Creator (al-Bari) – exalted is He – and this is denied. It is said to them: Why did you say that? If they say: We said that because existence (al-mawjud) is a general attribute that applies to both the eternal and the originated, and when it is said that He – exalted – is eternal, it is a description of the existent by an attribute by which He is distinguished from other existents, and then there is no attribute in the eternal that is more specific than eternity, and it became a specific attribute with nothing more specific than it. It is said: Is the dispute anything but about this? Do not your opponents say that the eternal is either self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat) which is the essence of the Creator – exalted is He – or not self-subsistent which are His attributes – exalted – so it was a description, and according to you there is nothing more specific than the eternal. So this is a premise denied; they cannot prove this premise except by establishing a proof for negating an eternal that is not self-subsistent and for confining the attribute of eternity to the essence alone. And if you could prove this, you would be independent of needing to adhere to this premise. So this is from you an attachment to a proof whose correctness is not established except by the establishment of the proven, and every proof of this way is invalid, because it is not possible to prove the proven except by establishing the proof, and it is not possible to establish the proof except by establishing the proven, so there is no way to prove either of them at all. Like one who falls into a well and cannot get out except by bringing a rope, and cannot bring the rope except by getting out, so each one is impossible. Or like one who locks a door and leaves the key inside the house, and cannot open the door except by taking out the key, and cannot take out the key except by opening the door, so both actions are impossible. So is this. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first of the four ignorances that Nasafi identified in the opponent's argument. The first ignorance is building their entire argument on the unproven claim that eternity (al-qidam) is the most specific attribute of God. Nasafi explains that this premise is denied (mamnu'). He then presents the opponent's attempted justification: they argue that existence is a general attribute common to both eternal and originated beings, so when we say God is eternal, we describe Him with an attribute that distinguishes Him from others, and there is no attribute more specific than eternity. Nasafi retorts by pointing out that the dispute is precisely about this: the opponents themselves say that the eternal is either self-subsistent (the essence of God) or not self-subsistent (His attributes), and that according to them, nothing is more specific than the eternal. Therefore, this premise is denied and cannot be proven without circularity. He illustrates the circularity with two analogies: (1) a man in a well who needs a rope to get out but cannot get the rope unless he gets out, and (2) a locked door with the key inside, where opening the door requires the key, but retrieving the key requires opening the door. Similarly, the opponent's proof depends on the very point under dispute, making it invalid. Nasafi concludes that God is the Grantor of success.

Key Terms
القديم (al-qadim: the eternal)
المقدمة المجحودة (al-muqaddima al-majhuda: the denied premise)
الدليل (al-dalil: proof)
المدلول (al-madlul: the proven/proposition)
القائم بالذات (al-qa'im bi al-dhat: self-subsistent)
الوصف (al-wasf: description/attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that circular reasoning (dawr) is acceptable in theological arguments.
Correction: Nasafi destroys this by showing it leads to impossibility, as illustrated by the analogies of the well and the locked door.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi identify as the first ignorance (jahala)?
A) Ignoring the condition of similarity
B) Building on a denied premise
C) Misusing the term 'eternal'
D) Applying the ruling to the opponent's principle

Key Takeaway
The first ignorance is relying on a denied premise, which leads to circular reasoning that makes the argument invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief that is central to your worldview, do you ever rely on a premise you have not examined critically? What would it cost you to truly test it?
?
Consider a time you insisted that something was 'obvious' or 'self-evident' to someone who denied it. Could it be that you were building on an unproven assumption, like the opponent in this lesson? How can you open yourself to genuine investigation?
?
Nasafi showed that circular reasoning makes an argument impossible. In your own life, are there areas where you go in circles—justifying one action by another, without ever reaching solid ground? Will you commit to breaking that cycle today?

The Mu'tazila's Analogy: Witnessed and Unseen Speech
The Mu'tazila use analogy between witnessed and unseen to prove speech is temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's rational argument that uses analogy between the witnessed and unseen to conclude that Allah's speech must be temporal.

Core Idea
If speech in this world is composed of sounds, Allah's speech must be too.

The Mu'tazila were known for their rational approach to theology. They argued that since our reason applies consistently to both the visible world and the unseen, any attribute of Allah must conform to what we know from experience. In this passage, they apply this principle to the attribute of speech, concluding that divine speech must be temporal and composed of letters and sounds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of analogy
The Mu'tazila hold that rational judgment requires consistency between the witnessed (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib) in all genera and differentiate. This is called 'taswiya'.
This principle is foundational to their proof.
2
See examples of application
They illustrate with movement: in the witnessed, movement is transference; so in the unseen it must be transference too. Similarly for blackness, whiteness, conjunction, separation.
These examples show how the principle works for other attributes.
3
Apply to speech
Speech in the witnessed world is of the genus of letters and sounds. Therefore, by analogy, Allah's speech must also be of that genus.
This leads to the conclusion that divine speech is temporal.
4
Recognize the conclusion
If one affirms speech in the unseen that is not letters and sounds, that would be as irrational as affirming a movement without transference.
The conclusion forces the temporality of Allah's speech.
5
Consider the limitation
This passage presents only the Mu'tazila argument. The Maturidi response, which rejects the analogy due to Allah's incomparability, is not included here.
Be aware this is one side of a theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة العقلية للمعتزلة على حدوث كلام الله. والمعقول لهم أن التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب ثابتة في العقول في الأجناس والفصول، فإن الحركة لما كانت نقلة في الشاهد، كانت كذا في الغائب ومن أثبت الحركة في الغائب ليست بنقلة عد خارجا عن المعقول، وكذا هذا في السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق، ثم الكلام في الشاهد من جنس الحروف والأصوات، فمن أثبت في الغائب كلاما على خلاف ما هو المعقول في الشاهد كان خارجا عن قضية العقول، مضاهيا من أثبت في الغائب حركة وليست من جنس النقلة أو سكونا ليس من جنس القرار أو سوادا خارجا عن جنس الألوان.
The rational proofs of the Mu'tazila for the temporality of the speech of Allah. Their rational argument is that the analogy between the witnessed (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib) is established in intellects regarding genera and differentiae. For movement, when it was a transference in the witnessed, it is likewise in the unseen; and whoever affirms movement in the unseen that is not a transference is considered outside of reason. Similarly in blackness, whiteness, conjunction, and separation. Then speech in the witnessed is of the genus of letters and sounds. So whoever affirms in the unseen speech contrary to what is intelligible in the witnessed is outside the dictate of intellects, resembling one who affirms in the unseen a movement not of the genus of transference, or a stillness not of the genus of rest, or a blackness outside the genus of colors.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's rational argument for the temporality of Allah's speech, based on the principle of analogy between the witnessed (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib). They argue that in the witnessed world, things like movement, blackness, whiteness, conjunction, and separation have specific natures that are universally recognized by reason. For example, movement is essentially a transference; if one were to claim an unseen movement that is not a transference, it would be irrational. Similarly, speech in the witnessed world is composed of letters and sounds. Therefore, any speech attributed to Allah in the unseen must also be of that same genus. If one were to affirm a different kind of speech, it would be as absurd as claiming a movement that is not a transference or a blackness outside the genus of colors. Thus, Allah's speech must be temporal like witnessed speech.

Key Terms
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)
تسوية (taswiya)
أجناس (ajnās)
فصول (fuṣūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy between witnessed and unseen is universally valid and accepted by all Muslim theologians.
Correction: The Maturidi school rejects this analogy because Allah is incomparable to creation; attributes of Allah are not bound by the categories of the witnessed world.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Mu'tazila's rational proof for the temporality of speech?
A) That speech in the witnessed is letters and sounds.
B) That Allah's attributes are unlike creation.
C) That the Quran is miraculous.
D) That the Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila base their argument for the temporality of divine speech on the principle that what holds for the witnessed must hold for the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Mu'tazila claim that Allah's speech must be like human speech, do you feel a tension between your reason and your faith? Which side pulls harder on you, and why?
?
The Mu'tazila demand consistency between the seen and unseen. But if Allah is absolutely unique, should we expect such consistency? How does your own belief about Allah's attributes handle this tension without falling into either denial or anthropomorphism?
?
Imagine you are standing before Allah and He asks you: 'Did you believe that My speech is eternal, or did you reduce it to created letters and sounds?' What will your answer be, and on what evidence do you base it—not from a book, but from your heart's conviction?

The Impossibility of Originated Speech Without a Locus
Al-Nasafi proves that originated speech cannot exist without a locus (substance or accident).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why originated speech cannot exist without a locus, based on the rational categories of substance and accident.

Core Idea
An accident without a locus is rationally impossible.

Al-Nasafi addresses a fundamental question in Islamic theology: can speech exist as an independent entity, separate from a subject? The Mu'tazilites claimed that God's speech is an accident that does not inhere in His essence. Al-Nasafi demonstrates the impossibility of any accident existing without a locus by first showing that speech cannot be a substance, then proving that it must be an accident, and finally establishing the rational necessity of a locus for accidents. This passage lays the groundwork for the orthodox position that divine speech is an eternal attribute of God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi sets up the two divisions and focuses on the second.
The passage is a proof; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Understand why speech cannot be a substance
Al-Nasafi argues that speech is an attribute (sifa) and a substance (jawhar) is what is qualified by attributes, not what qualifies. Therefore, speech cannot be a substance.
This is key to the argument.
3
See why speech must be an accident and needs a locus
Since speech is not a substance, it must be an accident. It is a rational principle that an accident cannot subsist without a locus.
Al-Nasafi emphasizes that no rational person has claimed otherwise for other accidents.
4
Consider the historical examples
Al-Nasafi cites the Dahriyya, Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu al-Hudhayl, and al-Jubba'i as examples of those who allowed things to exist without a locus. These are considered foolish.
These examples strengthen the argument by showing the consequences of rejecting the principle.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
If originated speech needs a locus, then God's speech, being eternal, cannot be an accident. This refutes the Mu'tazilite view.
This connects to broader Maturidi doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان امتناع القسم الثاني: وأما القسم الثاني وهو القول بحدوث الكلام لا في محل فهو أيضا ممتنع، لأن الكلام المحدث يستحيل أن يكون جوهرا لأن الكلام من قبيل الصفات، والصفة ما يتميز به الذات المتصف به عما لا اتصاف له به من الذوات والجوهر هو الممتاز بالصفات عما لا اتصاف له بمثل تلك الصفة التي اتصف هو بها، ولا يمتاز به عما لم يتصف به فكان الجوهر من قبيل ما يتصف لا من قبيل ما يتصف به غيره، فكان القول بكون الكلام جسما أو جوهرا محال، وإذا لم يكن جوهرا كان عرضا لانحصار المحدثات على هذين القسمين، ثم العرض مما تقرر في أوائل الأدلة وبداية العقول استحالة قيامه بذاته غير مفتقر إلى محل يقوم به حتى صار ذلك من الآراء الزائفة التي يتصارع أرباب العقول السليمة إلى الحكم بتسفيه من أجازه، ولهذا لم يقل أحد من العقلاء بوجود حركة أو سكون أو اجتماع أو افتراق أو شيء من الألوان أو رائحة أو طعم لا في محل. ولهذا اتفق جمهور على نسبة الدهرية إلى الغباوة بتجويزهم الصور متجردة عن محالها وعلى إخراج ابن الراوندي في تجويزه وجود تكوين قائم لا في محل وأبي الهذيل في تجويزه وجود خطاب كن لا في محل والجبائي وابنه أبي هاشم في تجويزهما
Demonstration of the impossibility of the second division: As for the second division—the statement that speech occurs not in a locus—it is also impossible. Because originated speech cannot be a substance (jawhar), since speech belongs to the category of attributes (sifat). An attribute is that by which the essence qualified by it is distinguished from essences not qualified by it; a substance is that which is distinguished by attributes from what is not qualified by similar attributes. So the substance belongs to the category of what is qualified, not to the category of what qualifies something else. Thus, the statement that speech is a body or a substance is impossible. If it is not a substance, then it is an accident (arad), because originated things are confined to these two categories. Moreover, it is established in the early proofs and the beginning of intellects that an accident cannot subsist by itself without depending on a locus in which it subsists—so much so that this has become among the false opinions that sound-minded people compete to declare foolish the one who allows it. For this reason, none of the rational people have ever said that a motion, rest, conjunction, separation, any color, smell, or taste exists not in a locus. For this reason, the majority agreed to attribute stupidity to the Dahriyya for allowing forms to be stripped of their loci, and to exclude Ibn al-Rawandi for allowing the existence of takwin (origination) subsisting not in a locus, and Abu al-Hudhayl for allowing the existence of the command 'Be' (kun) not in a locus, and al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim for allowing...
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a proof that originated speech cannot exist without a locus (i.e., not in a subject). Al-Nasafi divides the possible cases: either speech occurs in a locus or not. He is dealing with the second case. He argues that originated speech cannot be a substance (jawhar), because speech is an attribute (sifat), and an attribute is what qualifies another thing, whereas a substance is what is qualified. Thus speech is not a substance. Since originated things are only substances or accidents, speech must be an accident. But it is a well-established rational principle that an accident cannot subsist by itself; it requires a locus (mahall) in which to inhere. Therefore, speech cannot exist without a locus. Al-Nasafi notes that no rational person has ever claimed that accidents like motion, color, etc., exist without a locus. He then cites historical examples: the Dahriyya (materialists) were considered stupid for allowing forms without matter; Ibn al-Rawandi allowed takwin (the creative act) without a locus; Abu al-Hudhayl allowed the command 'Be' (kun) without a locus; al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim allowed something similar. All these views are dismissed as absurd against the consensus of sound minds.

Key Terms
كلام
جوهر
عرض
صفة
محل
ذات
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's speech (like 'Be') is a command that could exist without a subject, as the Mu'tazilites claimed.
Correction: Kalam theologians maintain that all accidents must inhere in a substance; therefore, originated speech cannot exist without a locus. God's speech is an eternal attribute, not an accident.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi conclude that originated speech cannot be a substance (jawhar)?
A) Because speech is an accident and accidents must inhere in a substance.
B) Because speech belongs to the category of attributes, and attributes are what qualify other things, while substances are what are qualified.
C) Because speech is a body and bodies cannot exist without a locus.
D) Because speech is eternal and substances are originated.

Key Takeaway
Originated speech is an accident and therefore must inhere in a substance; it cannot exist independently without a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your words are accidents that inhere in your character. Is there a 'speech' you have uttered that you treat as detached from who you are—something you claim was not really 'you'? How will you take ownership of it today?
?
Al-Nasafi calls those who deny the necessity of a locus for accidents 'foolish' and 'sound-minded people compete to declare them foolish'. Where in your life do you entertain thoughts or justifications that have no anchor in action or reality? Will you root them out or let them float unchallenged?
?
The heretical views of Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu al-Hudhayl were rejected because they violated a principle that reason itself deems necessary. Is there any belief you hold that, upon honest reflection, contradicts a clear rational or revealed principle? Will you submit your mind to the truth even if it unsettles you?

The Objection of Deriving 'Speaker' for the Locus of Speech
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri objects and the author refutes him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's objection that the locus of speech must be called a speaker by derivation, and the Maturidi response questioning who is obligated to perform the derivation.

Core Idea
Derivation is not automatic; it depends on who is obligated to assign the name.

In this passage, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri raises a sharp objection against the Maturidi position that speech cannot be created in a locus. He argues that if God created speech in a locus, that locus would necessarily be called a speaker by derivation. The response cleverly shifts the burden: from whom is this derivation required? If from God, He is not obligated; if from humans, the Mu'tazilite position denies human obligation. This moves the discussion from a linguistic claim to a theological principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. Note how he frames the necessity of deriving a name for the locus of speech if speech were created in it.
2
Identify the key question in the response
The response asks 'from whom is it necessary to derive?' This question reframes the objection entirely. Identify this pivot.
3
Understand the two logical possibilities
The response examines two possibilities: derivation from God or from humans. Consider why each fails according to Mu'tazilite principles.
This shows the power of logical refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض لأبي الحسين البصري: فمن ذلك ما ذكره أبو الحسن البصري فإنه حكي كلامنا لا على ما سبق منا السوق، فزعم أنهم يقولون: لو أحدث الله الكلام في محل لوجب أن يشتق لذلك المحل من أخص الكلام أو من أعمه فيوصف بأنه متكلم ثم أجاب فقال : يقال لهم قولكم وجب أن يشتق لمحل الكلام من الكلام اسم من الذي يجب عليه أن يشتق له؟ فإن قالوا: الباري عز وجل، قلنا الباري عندكم لا يجب عليه شيء، وإن قالوا: يجب على غيره أن يشتق لمحل الكلام اسما قيل : عندكم لا يجب على الناس شيء إلا ما
An objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: Among that is what Abu al-Hasan al-Basri mentioned, for he narrated our statement not in the manner we presented it. He claimed that they say: If God originated speech in a locus, it would be necessary to derive for that locus from the most specific of speech or the most general, so it is described as speaking. Then he answered and said: It is said to them: Your saying 'it is necessary to derive for the locus of speech from speech a name' – from whom is it necessary to derive it? If they say: the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, we say: the Creator according to you is not obligated to do anything. And if they say: it is necessary for other than Him to derive for the locus of speech a name, it is said: according to you, nothing is obligatory upon people except what
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (or Abu al-Hasan) against the Maturidi position that if speech were created in a locus, that locus would be called a speaker by derivation. The answer turns on who has the obligation to derive the name: if it is God, He is not obligated; if it is humans, Mu'tazila deny that anything is obligatory upon people except what is known through reason or revelation. This is a logical refutation.

Key Terms
اشتقاق
محل
متكلم
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Derivation of a name from an act is automatic and necessary, regardless of agency.
Correction: Derivation requires an agent who has the obligation or authority to assign the name; it is not a logical necessity independent of agency.

Checkpoint
What is the key question in the response to Abu al-Husayn's objection?
A) What is speech?
B) From whom is it necessary to derive?
C) Is God a speaker?
D) Is speech created?

Key Takeaway
The objection from derivation fails because it assumes a necessary derivation that no one is obligated to perform.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson reveals that derivation is not automatic. How can you ensure your understanding of God's names is rooted in revelation, not in rational obligation? Will you study one of His names this week with fresh eyes?
?
The objection assumes that if speech is in a locus, that locus must be called 'speaker'. Have you ever assumed that a certain attribute or judgment necessarily follows from a situation without checking the grounds? Think of a recent opinion you held about someone. Did you derive a label without considering the obligations involved? How can this lesson's emphasis on questioning necessity change your attitude?

Refutation of the Denial of Sequential Letters Constituting Speech
Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that letters cannot form speech due to sequential existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the argument that sequential letters cannot form speech and the importance of recognizing wholes composed of sequential parts.

Core Idea
No single letter is speech, but their sequential whole is speech.

Imam Nasafi tackles a sophistical argument that denies the existence of speech because letters occur one after another. He shows the absurd consequences and uses a powerful analogy to expose the flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that since letters exist sequentially, only one letter exists at a time, and a single letter is not speech, so speech never exists.
2
Note the absurd consequences
Nasafi points out that this leads to denying truth, falsehood, divine speech, and even the validity of laws.
3
Examine the army analogy
Nasafi uses the example of an army to show that the opponent's logic would deny the existence of an army because no single soldier is the army.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحروف توجد على التوالي والترادف لا وجود للام الحمد وقت وجود الهمزة ولا وجود للهمزة وقت وجود اللام لاستحالة بقاء الهمزة إلى وقت وجود اللام، وكذا هذا في اللام مع الحاء والحاء مع الميم والميم مع الدال، فإذا لا وجود في كل زمان إلا لحرف واحد، والحرف الواحد لا يكون كلاما عندك، ولا كلام عندك إلا من جنس الحروف، فإن وجود الكلام عندك غير متصور، وكان قولك المتكلم من فعل الكلام - ولا تصور لفعل الكلام عندك، ولا تحقق لوجوده البتة - ضربا من التجاهل. ثم هذا منك إبطال الكلام والصدق والكذب وما يتعلق بكل واحد منهما من الجزاء، وإنكار أن يكون الله تعالى كلام وأمر ونهي، وهو العناد الظاهر والسوفسطائية المحضة وإبطال للشرائع والحظر والوجوب والإباحة، وهذا هو عاقبة من حاد عن سنن الصواب في دين الله تعالى وأعرض عن الحجج وتمسك بما يميل إليه هواه - نعوذ بالله من قول هذا عقباه.
ثم قيل له: لو كانت محال الحروف من أجزاء اللسان مختلفة، فلم زعمت أن لا وجود للكلام ؟ وأن كل حرف وإن لم يكن كلاما فلم زعمت أن مجموعها لا يكون كلاما قائما بمجموع أجزاء اللسان ؟ فإن قال: لأن كل فرد ليس بكل، فكذا الجملة، وكذا كل فرد من أجزاء اللسان ليس محل للكلام فكذا الجملة فيقال له: ما أنكرتم على من زعم أن لا وجود للعسكر لأن كل فرد من أفراده ليس بعسكر وجملة المعسكر ليس بمعسكر ولا مكان للعسكر لأن كل جزء من أجزائه الذي حل به رجل واحد ليس بمعسكر ولا مكان للعسكر فإن التزم ظهرت مكابرته، وإن لم يلتزم ناقض وأبطل كلامه ثم الغرض من هذا الفصل إبطال كلامه وإفساد ما ظنه دليلا له وإن لم يكن كذلك لازما علينا لبنائه إياه على أصله في مائية الكلام في محله. والله الموفق.
Letters exist in succession and alternation: the lam of al-hamd does not exist at the time of the hamza's existence, and the hamza does not exist at the time of the lam's existence, due to the impossibility of the hamza remaining until the time of the lam's existence. And likewise this [applies] to the lam with the ha, the ha with the mim, and the mim with the dal. Thus there is no existence in every time except of a single letter, and a single letter is not speech according to you, nor is there speech according to you except from the genus of letters. Therefore the existence of speech according to you is inconceivable, and your saying 'the speaker is the one who performs speech'—while there is no conception of the performance of speech according to you, nor any verification of its existence at all—is a kind of willful ignorance. Then this from you is an invalidation of speech, truth, falsehood, and the consequences belonging to each of them, and a denial that God Almighty has speech, command, and prohibition; and it is manifest stubbornness, pure sophistry, and an invalidation of the revealed laws, prohibition, obligation, and permission. This is the consequence of one who deviates from the path of correctness in the religion of God Almighty, turns away from proofs, and clings to what his whims incline to—we seek refuge in God from the end of such a statement.
Then it was said to him: If the places of the letters from the parts of the tongue are different, why did you claim that speech has no existence? And why did you claim that each letter—even if it is not speech—does not constitute speech as a whole existing through the totality of the tongue's parts? If he says: Because every individual is not the whole, and likewise the whole; and likewise every individual from the parts of the tongue is not a locus for speech, so likewise the whole. It is said to him: What do you say to one who claims that an army has no existence because every individual of its individuals is not an army, and the totality of the army is not an army, and there is no place for the army because every part of its parts which a single man occupies is not an army and there is no place for the army? If he accepts [this], his stubbornness becomes evident; if he does not accept, he contradicts and nullifies his statement. Then the purpose of this chapter is to nullify his statement and corrupt what he supposed to be a proof for him, even if it is not binding upon us, for our building it upon his principle concerning the quiddity of speech in its locus. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who denies that speech can exist because it is composed of letters that exist sequentially, not simultaneously. The author first presents the opponent's view: letters like the lam of al-hamd do not exist at the same time as the hamza, so only one letter exists at any moment, and a single letter is not speech, yet speech is nothing but these letters, so speech can never exist. The author then points out the absurd consequences: denial of truth and falsehood, denial of divine speech, etc., calling it stubbornness and sophistry. Then the author responds with an analogy: if the opponent claims that a single letter is not speech and the whole is not speech because each part is not, then it would follow that an army does not exist because each soldier is not the army, etc. This reductio ad absurdum shows that the opponent's premise is untenable. The author concludes that the purpose of this chapter is to refute this false argument, though it is not binding on us because it is built on the opponent's own mistaken principle about the nature of speech.

Key Terms
حروف
كلام
توالي
ترادف
سوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because letters exist sequentially and no single letter is speech, speech cannot exist at all.
Correction: The existence of a whole does not require simultaneous existence of all parts; sequential existence is sufficient for the whole to exist.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to refute the claim that letters cannot form speech?
A) A house
B) An army
C) A tree
D) A river

Key Takeaway
The existence of a whole does not require simultaneous existence of all its parts; sequential existence is sufficient, as shown by the army analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent's argument against speech was built on a seemingly logical premise about sequential existence. Are there beliefs you hold that you have never critically examined, simply because they 'make sense' to you? How does this passage challenge you to test your assumptions against revealed truth and rational proof?
?
The passage warns that turning away from proofs and following whims leads to manifest stubbornness and sophistry. Where in your daily life do you prioritize your own inclinations over seeking the truth? What specific proof from scripture or reason will you revisit today to correct a possible blind spot?

Proof That the Act of Creation Cannot Be Eternal
Quranic and linguistic proof that fi'l (act) cannot be eternal due to transitivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse and linguistic analysis of the transitive verb 'fa‘ala' prove that God's act of creation is temporal, not eternal.

Core Idea
A transitive verb requires an object; an eternal act would require an eternal object, which is impossible.

This lesson examines a combined proof from the Quran and Arabic linguistics. Imam al-Nasafi uses the verse 'Fa‘‘ālun limā yurīd' (Doer of what He wills) and the grammatical nature of the verb 'fa‘ala' to argue that the act of creation cannot be eternal. We will follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proof
Notice the intensive form 'Fa‘‘āl' and its connection to will. Consider why this implies the act is temporal.
The verse shows that God's actions are encompassed by His will, and whatever is willed is temporal.
2
Understand the linguistic principle
The verb 'fa‘ala' is transitive and requires an object (maf‘ūl). Grasp why this is crucial for the argument.
A transitive verb cannot exist without an object; the act and its object are correlated.
3
Explore the three logical possibilities
If the act were eternal, its object would have to be either eternal, non-existent, or both. Consider each case.
The first is impossible (world is not eternal), the second violates transitivity, the third is contradictory.
4
See the conclusion
Since all possibilities are invalid, the act cannot be eternal. It must be temporal, proving creation in time.
This argument is foundational for establishing the temporality of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن الله تعالى قال فَعَالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ والفعال للمبالغة والتكثير ثم علق به الإرادة دل على أن الإرادة متناولة لأفعاله، وما تناولته الإرادة فهو حادث مخلوق قال: ومن حيث اللغة أن قولهم فعل متعد المفعول، وإذا ثبت أنه لابد من أن يتعدى إلى مفعول، فلو وجد الفعل في الأزل لكان لا يخلو: إما أن يكون ما يتعدى إليه موجودا في الأزل أو كان معدوما في الأزل أو كان موجودا معدوما معا، أما ما شك أن المقدور معدوم في الأزل وإما ما يتعدى إلى الموجود والمعدوم كقولنا: عالم وله علم. فلا يخلو ما نحن فيه من الفعل إما أن يكون ما يتعدى موجودا في الأزل والمعتدي إليه معدوما، ولو كان كذلك لاقتضى أن يكون العالم موجودا في الأزل، إن كان موجودا ومعدوما لزم الأمران جميعا. وإذا بطلت هذه الأقسام ثبت بطلان وجود الفعل في الأزل.
And because Allah, may He be exalted, said: 'Doer of what He wills' and 'Doer' (al-Fa''al) is for exaggeration and multiplicity; then He attached will to it, indicating that the will encompasses His actions, and whatever the will encompasses is temporal, created. He said: And from the linguistic perspective, their saying 'he did' is transitive to an object. And if it is established that it must be transitive to an object, then if the act existed in pre-eternity, it would not be free from either: that what it is transitive to exists in pre-eternity, or it is non-existent in pre-eternity, or it is both existent and non-existent together. As for that which is transitive to the non-existent, it is certain that the object (maqdur) is non-existent in pre-eternity. And as for that which is transitive to the existent and the non-existent, like our saying: 'knower' and 'he has knowledge'. So what we are dealing with concerning the act: either what it is transitive to exists in pre-eternity and the recipient is non-existent, and if that were the case, it would necessitate that the world exist in pre-eternity. If it were both existent and non-existent, both matters would be necessary. And when these divisions are invalidated, the invalidity of the existence of the act in pre-eternity is established.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage provides a combined Quranic and linguistic proof that the act of creation cannot be eternal. It begins by citing the verse 'فعال لما يريد' (Doer of whatever He wills), noting that the intensive form 'فعال' implies repetition and abundance. The fact that the act is connected to will (al-irada) indicates that will encompasses all of God's actions, and whatever is encompassed by will is temporal and created. Then a linguistic argument is presented: the verb 'فعل' is transitive and requires an object (maf'ul). If the act existed in pre-eternity (al-azal), its object must also be in pre-eternity, but there are three possibilities: the object exists in pre-eternity, it is non-existent, or both. The first is impossible because the world is not eternal; the second is impossible because a transitive verb cannot have no object; the third is contradictory. Therefore, the act cannot be eternal; it must be temporal. This establishes the temporality of creation.

Key Terms
فعل
أزل
قدرة
مقدور
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An act can exist without an object.
Correction: Linguistically, the verb 'fa‘ala' is transitive and necessarily requires an object; an act without an object is meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic argument, why can the act of creation not be eternal?
A) Because the verb 'fa‘ala' is intransitive.
B) Because an eternal act would require an eternal object, which is impossible.
C) Because the Quran says 'fā‘āl' not 'fā‘il'.
D) Because the will is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic connection to will and the transitive nature of 'fa‘ala' demonstrate that God's creative act must be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your existence is a temporal act of God's will, or do you sometimes act as if the world has always been here and will always be here without reference to the Creator? How does this affect your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
If God's acts are all encompassed by His will, then every moment of your life is willed by Him. Are you at peace with His will for you, or do you resist His decree?
?
The proof shows that the act of creation cannot be eternal. How does this deepen your understanding of God's absolute sovereignty and your own temporality? Will you live today with the awareness that you are a created being dependent on the Creator at every moment?

Refuting the Invalidity of Repeating Metaphorical Terms
Refutes the objection that repetition of 'khalq' for the created being is invalid because it is metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how frequent usage can transform a metaphorical term into a quasi-reality, allowing repetition, as illustrated by the terms 'khalq' and 'adl'.

Core Idea
Frequent usage can make a metaphor function like a reality, permitting repetition.

This passage directly challenges the claim that metaphorical terms cannot be repeated. The author not only provides counterexamples from Arabic usage but also explains that extremely frequent usage can elevate a metaphor to the status of a reality (haqiqa). Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping how theologians use language about God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the objection, refutation, and the author's positive argument.
Notice the initial objection: if 'khalq' is a metaphor, God would not repeat it.
2
Identify the refutation examples
The author cites 'al-da'if', 'al-'adl', and 'al-zawr' as examples of words that are originally verbal nouns but are used metaphorically for the agent, and Arabs repeat them frequently.
These examples show that repetition of metaphors is normal in Arabic.
3
Examine the author's own explanation
The author argues that 'khalq' for the created being has become so common that it is treated like a reality, similar to 'al-'adl' being used for a just person.
The key point is 'ghalabat al-isti'mal' (predominance of usage) transforms the term.
4
Compare the 'adl analogy
Just as 'adl' (justice) is used to mean a just person and repeating it is fine, 'khalq' for the created being is permissible to repeat. However, this does not make the created being identical to the act of creation.
The analogy reinforces the argument while preserving theological precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر أن الله تعالى كرر لفظة الخلق وأراد بها المخلوق، ولو كان هذا مجازا لما كرر، فليس من شأن العرب التكرير في المجازات كلام واه وادعاء على العرب ما هو خلاف عادتهم، أليس أن الضعيف مصدر وهو في الشاهد معنى وراء الفاعل يذكر ويراد به الفاعل مجازا، وكذا العدل والزور في نظائر كثيرة لا تحصى، ثم إنهم يكررون ذلك في مفاوضاتهم ومحاوراتهم ويريدون به الفاعل دون المصدر فكذا هذا، والتعليل بمثل هذه الكلمات إما غفلة وإما وقاحة يقع فيها الإنسان لإفلاسه. على أنا نقول: إن غرضنا من هذا أن نبين أن إطلاق اسم الخلق على المخلوق مغير عن أصله إذ هو اسم المصدر دون المفعول ثم في الجملة من الكلام المغير عن أصله ما لا يحسن أن يقال إنه مجاز لأنه قد كثر استعماله فظهر معناه كظهوره بالأصل أو أكد كلفظة العدل، وإن كانت في الأصل موضوعة للمصدر ولما كثر استعماله في الفاعل التحق بالحقيقة لظهور المراد به عند كثرة الاستعمال حتى إن من قال: إن الله ليس بعدل لسارع الناس إلى إنكاره وتخطئته. وإن كان الله تعالى في الحقيقة عادلا لا عدلا بل العدل صفته، والخلق مع المخلوق من هذا القبيل فيصير لغلبة الاستعمال كأنه حقيقة فيجوز تكريره كما يجوز تكرير الحقيقة، ولكن بهذا لا يتبين أن المخلوق في نفسه خلق، كما لا تتبين أن ذات الله تعالى في الحقيقة صفة العدل والعدالة، وهذا واضح بحمد الله تعالى.
And what was mentioned that God Almighty repeated the word 'khalq' (creation) and intended by it the created being [makhluq], and if this were a metaphor [majaz] He would not have repeated it. So it is not the habit of the Arabs to repeat in metaphors, [this is] a flimsy claim and an assertion against the Arabs that contradicts their custom. Is it not that 'al-da'if' (the weak) is a verbal noun [masdar] and in observable reality is a meaning beyond the agent, mentioned and intended by it for the agent metaphorically, and likewise 'al-'adl' (justice) and 'al-zawr' (falsehood) in numerous similar cases beyond count? Then they repeat that in their discussions and conversations, intending by it the agent without the verbal noun, so too this. And explanation with such words is either heedlessness or impudence in which a person falls due to his bankruptcy. However, we say: Our purpose from this is to show that applying the name 'khalq' to the created being is changed from its original meaning, for it is the name of the verbal noun rather than the object; then in general, among speech changed from its original meaning, there is that which it is not proper to call metaphor because its usage has become frequent so its meaning has appeared like its appearance in the original, or even stronger, like the word 'al-'adl'. And though originally it was placed for the verbal noun, when its usage in the agent became frequent, it became attached to reality [haqiqa] due to the clarity of what is meant by it upon frequency of use, to the point that if someone said 'God is not 'adl (just),' people would rush to deny him and consider him mistaken, even though God Almighty in reality is 'adil (just), not 'adl, rather 'adl is His attribute. And 'khalq' with the created being is of this category, so due to the predominance of usage it becomes as if it were reality, so its repetition is allowed just as repetition of the real is allowed. But by this it is not proven that the created being in itself is 'khalq', just as it is not proven that the essence of God Almighty in reality is the attribute of justice and fairness. And this is clear, praise be to God.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that repetition of the word 'khalq' (creation) for the created being (makhluq) is invalid because it is metaphorical. The author first states the objection: if 'khalq' were a metaphor, God would not repeat it, since Arabs do not repeat metaphors. He then refutes this by giving examples: the word 'al-da'if' (weakness) is originally a verbal noun (masdar) but is frequently used metaphorically to refer to the weak person, and Arabs repeat it in that metaphorical sense. Similarly, 'al-'adl' (justice) and 'al-zawr' (falsehood) are used metaphorically for the just person and the liar, and Arabs repeat them. So the claim is baseless and either reflects ignorance or insolence. The author then explains his own position: the application of 'khalq' to the created being is a change from its original meaning (it is originally the verbal noun, not the object), but due to extremely frequent usage, it has become like a reality (haqiqa). He compares it to 'al-'adl', which originally is the verbal noun 'justice' but when used frequently for the just person, it becomes so established that denying God's 'adl is considered wrong, even though God is 'adil (just) not 'adl. Thus, 'khalq' for the created being is like a reality due to prevalence, and hence repeating it is permissible, just as repeating a real term is. However, this does not prove that the created being is identical to the act of creation, just as frequent usage of 'adl for the just person does not prove that the essence of God is the attribute of justice. The conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
مخلوق (makhluq)
مجاز (majaz)
حقيقة (haqiqa)
مصدر (masdar)
عدل ('adl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Metaphorical terms cannot be repeated because Arabs avoid repetition in metaphors.
Correction: The author shows that Arabs do repeat metaphors, and if usage becomes very frequent, the metaphorical term becomes like a reality, so repetition is permitted.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's main argument regarding the repetition of the term 'khalq' for the created being?
A) It is invalid because it is a metaphor and Arabs do not repeat metaphors.
B) It is allowed because frequent usage has made it like a reality, similar to 'al-'adl' used for a just person.
C) It is allowed because the word 'khalq' is always real when applied to the created being.
D) It is invalid because repetition changes the meaning of the term.

Key Takeaway
Due to frequent usage, the term 'khalq' for the created being has become like a reality, making its repetition permissible, just as 'adl' is repeatedly used for a just person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use theological terms like 'creation' or 'justice' in your daily life, have you ever considered whether your understanding is based on the original meaning or on common usage? How might this affect your conception of God?
?
The author warns that those who argue with weak explanations fall into heedlessness or impudence. Reflect on a time you defended a belief with a weak argument. What does that reveal about your attachment to truth versus your own pride?
?
If the term 'adl is so closely associated with God's justice that denying it is considered wrong, even though God's essence is not literally 'adl, how does this shape the way you speak about God? Are you careful to distinguish between what God is and how we describe Him?

Proof from Opponents' Inconsistency
Exposes inconsistency in opponents' claim that human acts are metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the opponents' position on human actions being metaphorical is inconsistent with the widespread usage of 'f'l' in the Quran and everyday speech.

Core Idea
Frequent usage of 'act' for humans disproves its metaphorical claim.

The author begins by exposing an inconsistency in the opponents' claim. They assert that human actions are merely metaphorical, yet the word 'act' appears countless times in the Quran and common speech for human deeds. This lesson examines how this very frequency undermines their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author quotes three Quranic verses to emphasize the repeated usage.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
Identify what the opponents say about human actions: they deny real human action and claim it is only acquisition and metaphorical.
3
See the inconsistency
The author points out that even though the term 'f'l' appears many times in the Quran and in speech for humans, the opponents still call it metaphorical. Why is this a problem for them?
The author uses their own principle against them: if frequency does not change metaphor, then their claim stands but is arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على بطلان هذا الكلام وكون المتمسك به متحكما، أنهم يقولون: لا فعل للعبد بطريق الحقيقة، وإنما له الاكتساب وما يضاف إلى العباد من الفعل فهو مجاز وإن كان موجودا في كلام الله تعالى وكلام الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وكلام جميع الناس بطريق التكرير قال الله تعالى: وَافَعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ وقال: فَإِن لَّمْ تَفْعَلُوا وَلَن تَفْعَلُوا وقال: بِمَا كَانُوا يَفْعَلُونَ في مواضع لا تحصى، ومع ذلك لم يخرج ذلك عن أن يكون مجازا فكذا هذا، على أن الخلق لو كان هو المخلوق للغت فائدة ذكر الخلق في قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ لأن السماء كالسماء فيصير كأنه قال إن في السماوات والأرض فيصير ذكر الخلق لغوا ضائعا.
And among what indicates the invalidity of this speech and that the one who adheres to it is arbitrary: that they say, 'There is no act for the servant in the way of reality [haqiqa]; rather, he has only acquisition [kasb], and what is attributed to the servants of act is metaphorical [majaz], even though it exists in the speech of God Almighty and the speech of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and the speech of all people in a repeated manner.' God Almighty said: 'And do good' [al-Hajj 22:77], and He said: 'But if you do not, and you will never do' [al-Baqara 2:24], and He said: 'Because of what they used to do' [al-An'am 6:159] in countless places, and despite that, it did not remove that from being metaphorical. So too this. Moreover, if 'khalq' (creation) were the created being [makhluq], then the mention of 'khalq' would be useless in His statement: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth' [Al Imran 3:190], because the heaven would be like the heaven, so it would become as if He said 'Indeed, in the heavens and the earth,' so the mention of 'khalq' would become futile and wasted.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage provides two arguments against the opponents' position. First, the author points out the inconsistency of the opponents who deny real human action and consider it merely metaphorical, yet they themselves use the word 'f'l' (act) repeatedly in the Quran and in everyday speech. If frequency of usage does not remove metaphoricity in their view, then by their own standard, 'khalq' used for the created being could also remain metaphor. But the author uses this to show their arbitrariness. Second, the author presents a linguistic argument: if 'khalq' (creation) meant the created being (makhluq), then the Quranic verse 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth' would become 'Indeed, in the heavens and the earth' — the word 'khalq' would be redundant and meaningless. This demonstrates that 'khalq' cannot be synonymous with makhluq; rather, it must refer to the act of creation itself, which carries the intended meaning.

Key Terms
فعل
اكتساب
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'f'l' for humans in the Quran is always metaphorical.
Correction: The author shows that the opponents' claim that human acts are metaphorical is inconsistent with the widespread real usage of the term in the Quran.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to expose the opponents' inconsistency?
A) The Quran commands humans to do good.
B) The opponents themselves use the word 'f'l' for human actions repeatedly despite calling it metaphorical.
C) The word 'khalq' means created being.
D) The opponents deny acquisition.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' insistence that human actions are metaphorical is contradicted by the very texts they accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic commands like 'وَافَعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ' (do good), do you treat them as literal, personal commands from Allah to you, or do you find yourself philosophically distancing yourself from their direct authority? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that your actions are real and fully accountable before Allah?
?
The opponents held onto a theory despite the Quran's clear and repeated use of 'فعل' for humans. Is there any belief or habit in your life that you cling to, even though it contradicts what Allah has clearly stated in His Book? Ask yourself: am I willing to submit to the literal import of revelation, even if it challenges my comfort or intellectual pride?
?
If the very words of Allah are not enough to convince you of the reality of your own actions, then what would be? This lesson exposes the danger of imposing philosophical frameworks over divine speech. Right now, identify one area where you have rationalized away a clear command. What step will you take today to reverse that and act upon it as a real deed for Allah?

Proof from Redundancy if 'Khalq' Means Created
Argues that if 'khalq' meant makhluq, Quranic phrase becomes redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument that interpreting 'khalq' as the created being would render Quranic verses redundant and meaningless.

Core Idea
If khalq were makhluq, then 'creation of heavens' would be 'heavens' — redundant.

Having exposed the opponents' inconsistency, the author now presents a direct linguistic argument. If the word 'khalq' (creation) meant the created being (makhluq), then verses like 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth' would become tautological. This lesson explores why 'khalq' must refer to the act of creation, not the product.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation of this short but powerful argument. Focus on the logical structure: assume, then derive consequence.
The author uses a conditional statement: if X, then Y leads to absurdity.
2
Understand the consequence
The consequence is that the phrase 'in the creation of the heavens and the earth' would be equivalent to 'in the heavens and the earth', making 'creation' redundant. Ask yourself why that is absurd.
Redundancy in the Quran is not permissible; every word carries meaning.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Since the Quran cannot be redundant, 'khalq' cannot mean the created being. It must mean the act of creation. This shows that the opponents' interpretation is invalid.
This argument is a clear refutation of the anthropomorphic tendency to equate creation with created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على بطلان هذا الكلام وكون المتمسك به متحكما، أنهم يقولون: لا فعل للعبد بطريق الحقيقة، وإنما له الاكتساب وما يضاف إلى العباد من الفعل فهو مجاز وإن كان موجودا في كلام الله تعالى وكلام الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وكلام جميع الناس بطريق التكرير قال الله تعالى: وَافَعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ وقال: فَإِن لَّمْ تَفْعَلُوا وَلَن تَفْعَلُوا وقال: بِمَا كَانُوا يَفْعَلُونَ في مواضع لا تحصى، ومع ذلك لم يخرج ذلك عن أن يكون مجازا فكذا هذا، على أن الخلق لو كان هو المخلوق للغت فائدة ذكر الخلق في قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ لأن السماء كالسماء فيصير كأنه قال إن في السماوات والأرض فيصير ذكر الخلق لغوا ضائعا.
And among what indicates the invalidity of this speech and that the one who adheres to it is arbitrary: that they say, 'There is no act for the servant in the way of reality [haqiqa]; rather, he has only acquisition [kasb], and what is attributed to the servants of act is metaphorical [majaz], even though it exists in the speech of God Almighty and the speech of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and the speech of all people in a repeated manner.' Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage provides two arguments against the opponents' position. First, the author points out the inconsistency of the opponents who deny real human action and consider it merely metaphorical, yet they themselves use the word 'f'l' (act) repeatedly in the Quran and in everyday speech. If frequency of usage does not remove metaphoricity in their view, then by their own standard, 'khalq' used for the created being could also remain metaphor. What would be the consequence if 'khalq' meant the created being?
