?
The argument shows that Allah's speech is precise and free from futility. In your own life, do you treat the Quran with that same precision, or do you sometimes read carelessly? What is one verse you have been glossing over, and what would change if you truly understood it?
?
If 'khalq' is the act of creation, then every moment of existence is a direct act of Allah. How does this reality change how you view the world around you? What will you look at differently today as a result?

The Inductive Proof for the Eternity of Takwin
Presents the inductive argument that if all but one division are impossible, the remaining is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how inductive reasoning is used in kalam to prove the eternity of the attribute of Takwin (creative power).

Core Idea
When all alternatives are impossible, the remaining one must be true.

Inductive proofs (dalalat istiqra'iyya) are a powerful tool in Islamic theology. By eliminating all impossible positions, the remaining possibility becomes necessary. In this passage, the author applies this method to argue that the attribute of Takwin (creative power) is eternal and subsisting with Allah's essence. Let's follow the logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the inductive method
Read the first part of the passage where the author explains the inductive proof. Notice the conditional logic: if all divisions are impossible and one is possible/necessary, then it is established.
The author presents two scenarios: one where a single division is possible, and another where multiple are possible but one is necessary.
2
Apply the method to Takwin
Now see how the author applies this method to the attribute of Takwin. He argues that all alternative positions have been shown impossible, so the remaining one (eternal Takwin) is necessary.
The 'remaining division' is the belief that Takwin is an eternal attribute subsisting with God's essence.
3
Respond to objections
The author anticipates an objection: what if one only grants possibility, not necessity? He clarifies that possibility alone is sufficient to establish Takwin's eternity, and then promises to refute all doubts.
This prepares the reader for a detailed refutation of opposing arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدلائل الاستقرائية إذا امتنعت الأقسام كلها وتعين قسم للإمكان تعين للثبوت، ولو استوت الأقسام في الإمكان وتعين قسم للوجوب كان هو المتعين للثبوت لرجحانه على غيره من الأقسام، فإذا امتنعت الأقسام كلها فيما نحن فيه وتعين القسم الباقي للوجوب كان هو الثابت ضرورة. ثم نقول لهم: لو لم يكن ما ذكرنا من وجوب وجود التكوين قائما بذات الباري في الأزل، كان ثبوت الإمكان كافيا لتعينه للثبوت، ودليل الإمكان دفع ما ذكرتم من الشبه وكلها مندفعة فاسدة. ولابد من أن نشتغل ببيان بطلانها في أنفسها فنقول:
The inductive proofs: if all the divisions are impossible and one division is determined as possible, it is determined as established. And if the divisions are equal in possibility and one division is determined as necessary, it is the one determined for establishment due to its preponderance over the other divisions. So if in our subject all the divisions are impossible and the remaining division is determined as necessary, it is the established one necessarily. Then we say to them: if what we mentioned of the necessity of the existence of takwin subsisting with the Essence of the Creator in pre-eternity were not the case, then the stability of possibility would be sufficient for its being determined as established, and the proof of possibility repels what you mentioned of the doubts, and all of them are repelled and invalid. And we must occupy ourselves with explaining their invalidity in themselves, so we say:
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage presents an inductive logical method (dalalat istiqra'iyya) to prove the eternity of the attribute of takwin (creative power). The author explains the method: when investigating a subject, if all possible divisions except one are impossible, the remaining division is necessarily established. Alternatively, if multiple divisions are equally possible but one is determined as necessary, that one is established because it preponderates. In the case of takwin, all alternative positions (e.g., that takwin is temporal, or that there is no real attribute) have been shown impossible, so the remaining division—that takwin is an eternal attribute subsisting with God's Essence in pre-eternity—is necessarily true. The author then addresses opponents: if they reject this necessity, they must accept that mere possibility would be sufficient; but the proof of possibility (i.e., that takwin is possible as an eternal attribute) already refutes their doubts. The passage concludes by announcing a detailed explanation of the invalidity of those doubts.

Key Terms
الدلائل الاستقرائية
التكوين
الإمكان
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility alone suffices to establish a doctrine, without needing necessity.
Correction: The author argues that when all alternatives are impossible, the remaining division is necessary, not merely possible. However, even if one only grants possibility, that possibility is sufficient to establish the doctrine in this context.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does the author use to argue for the eternity of takwin?
A) If all divisions are impossible, the remaining is possible.
B) If all divisions are impossible, the remaining is necessary.
C) If divisions are equal, all are false.
D) If one division is possible, it is true.

Key Takeaway
Inductive proof establishes the eternity of Takwin by showing that all alternative positions are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on logical certainty in your belief about Allah's attributes, or do you follow blind acceptance? Examine your own heart: when you assert that Allah has the attribute of Takwin (eternal bringing into existence), is it because you have reasoned through the impossibility of alternatives, or because you heard it? Commit to studying the proofs for every attribute you affirm.
?
The author says if all alternatives are impossible, the remaining is necessary. Are there areas in your own life where you keep entertaining impossible alternatives instead of surrendering to the truth? Specifically, regarding Allah's decree, do you accept that His action is eternal, or do you allow doubts based on human analogy? This lesson demands that you cut off false alternatives and submit to the necessary truth.
?
When you face an evident truth about Allah that leaves no room for doubt, do you still hesitate because of worldly attachments? Reflect: your hesitation is from yourself, not from the proof. Will you now let go and affirm what reason and revelation demand, even if it costs you comfort or pride?

Pre-Eternity of Takwin Does Not Imply Pre-Eternity of Created Things
Refutation of the claim that pre-eternity of takwin entails pre-eternity of created things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming the pre-eternity of Allah's attribute of takwin does not force one to affirm the pre-eternity of the created things, and how the distinction between independent existence and dependent existence resolves the confusion.

Core Idea
What depends on another for its existence cannot be pre-eternal; only the self-sufficient is truly eternal.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a common confusion in kalam: if we say that Allah's attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) is pre-eternal, does that mean the things He brings into existence are also pre-eternal? He argues no, because pre-eternality means independence, while created things are dependent on that attribute. Let's read his clear refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Look for the claim that saying takwin is pre-eternal leads to saying created things are pre-eternal. Notice the author's astonishment.
3
Understand the definition of 'pre-eternal'
The author defines al-qadim as 'independent in its existence from other than itself'. This is key. See how he applies it.
4
Follow the refutation analogy
The author compares to those who claim the world is pre-eternal but dependent on a Maker. He shows that dependence contradicts pre-eternity.
5
Reflect on the concluding point
The author ends by asking where the contradiction is in his own position. He asserts that only ignorance leads to insisting on pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم التكوين لا يؤدي إلى قدم المكونات]: ثم العجب كل العجب أن قوما يدعون الفراهة في علم الكلام والتبحر في معرفة الدلائل يزعمون أن القول بقدم التكوين يؤدي إلى القول بقدم المكونات، مع علمهم أن ما تعلق وجوده بسبب من الأسباب فهو المحدث لا القديم، لأن القديم هو المستغني في وجوده عن غيره، فما لم يستغن عن غيره وتعلق وجوده به كان محدثا ضرورة، والمكون وجوده بالتكوين فكيف يكون قديما؟. يحقق هذا : أنا جميعا ندعي على القائلين بقدم العالم المعللين لذلك بقدم ما تعلق وجود العالم به من ذات الباري أو صفة من صفاته المناقضة حيث ادعوا قدمه مع تعلق وجوده بغيره، وهذا الذي قرن هذا القول بمقاله ابن قلس قال في الرد عليه: أنكرنا قوله لأنه قول متناقض في نفسه، لأنه بقوله العالم قديم، ينفي أن يكون له صانع ويقوله : له صانع ينفي أن يكون قديما. ونحن بقولنا : العالم له صانع أثبتناه قدمه، وبقولنا: إنه محدث وإنه غير مستغن في وجوده عن غيره بل تعلق وجوده بغيره ادعينا حدوثه فأين التناقض؟ وأي شيء يوجب القول بالقدم لولا الجهل والحماقة.
The pre-eternity of takwin does not lead to the pre-eternity of the things created: Then the utmost wonder is that a group who claim skill in kalam and deep knowledge of proofs claim that saying takwin is pre-eternal leads to saying the created things are pre-eternal, despite their knowing that whatever is existence is attached to a cause is the originated (al-muhdath), not the pre-eternal (al-qadim), because the pre-eternal is the one independent in its existence from other than itself. So whatever is not independent from other than itself and its existence is attached to it is necessarily originated. And the created thing (al-mukawwan) has its existence by takwin, so how can it be pre-eternal? This is verified by [the fact that] we all claim against those who say the world is pre-eternal, who justify that by the pre-eternity of that to which the existence of the world is attached from the essence of the Creator or an attribute of His attributes that are contradictory [to that claim], where they claim its pre-eternity along with the attachment of its existence to other than itself. And this is what [Ibn Qals?] coupled this statement with his saying in refutation of it: We denied his statement because it is a self-contradictory statement, because by his statement 'the world is pre-eternal' he negates that it has a Maker, and by his statement 'it has a Maker' he negates that it is pre-eternal. And we, by our statement 'the world has a Maker', we have affirmed its [the Maker's] pre-eternity; and by our statement 'it is originated' and that it is not independent in its existence from other than itself, rather its existence is attached to other than itself, we claim its origination. So where is the contradiction? And what thing necessitates the statement of pre-eternity except ignorance and foolishness?
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that affirming the pre-eternity of takwin (the attribute of bringing into existence) necessarily entails affirming the pre-eternity of the created things themselves. The author begins by expressing astonishment that people who claim expertise in kalam could make such an error. He states the principle: whatever has its existence attached to a cause is originated (muhdath), not pre-eternal (qadim), because the pre-eternal is that which is independent in its existence from others. Since the created thing (mukawwan) has its existence by means of takwin, it cannot be pre-eternal. He then draws an analogy: his opponents (who affirm the pre-eternity of the world on the grounds that what the world depends on is pre-eternal) are inconsistent because they affirm both the pre-eternity of the world and its dependence on something else. He quotes a certain Ibn Qals (possibly Ibn Kullab) who argued that such a position is self-contradictory: if the world is pre-eternal, it has no Maker; if it has a Maker, it cannot be pre-eternal. The author then defends his own position: by affirming a Maker, they affirm His pre-eternity; by affirming that the world is originated and dependent, they affirm its origination. There is no contradiction. The only reason for insisting on pre-eternity is ignorance and foolishness.

Key Terms
قدم التكوين
قدم المكونات
القديم
المحدث
المكون
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute is pre-eternal, then what it produces must also be pre-eternal.
Correction: Pre-eternality means independence; created things depend on the attribute and thus are originated.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the defining characteristic of the pre-eternal (al-qadim)?
A) It is uncaused.
B) It is independent in its existence from others.
C) It is the cause of all things.
D) It is the first in time.

Key Takeaway
Affirming the pre-eternity of takwin does not imply the pre-eternity of created things because pre-eternality requires independence in existence, while created things depend on takwin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says that insisting on the pre-eternity of the world stems from ignorance and foolishness. What 'worlds' in your life—habits, comforts, relationships—do you treat as if they are eternal, never expecting them to end? Will you consciously loosen your grip on one of them today, acknowledging that only Allah is truly independent and eternal?
?
If your very existence depends entirely on Allah's takwin, do you truly live as a dependent servant, or do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? Name one area where you have been pretending to be independent, and commit to a concrete act of humility before Allah today.
?
The opponent's self-contradiction was claiming the world has a Maker yet is pre-eternal. What self-contradictions do you hold—professing faith in Allah's power but living as if you are in control of your future? Which specific contradiction will you confront and resolve starting now?

Rational Proof: Cause of Vision Cannot Transfer to the Unseen
Rational evidence that the cause and conditions of vision cannot apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God cannot be seen because the cause of visibility in the witnessed world (being a body) cannot apply to God, who is not a body.

Core Idea
What causes vision in the seen world cannot transfer to the unseen God.

This passage presents the core rational argument of those who deny the possibility of seeing God. The argument is based on a principle of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen (al-shāhid to al-ghā'ib). It asserts that the cause of visibility in the witnessed world is being a body, and since God is not a body, this cause cannot transfer, and therefore the judgment of being visible does not apply to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure.
2
Identify the key principle
The passage states a principle: what applies in the witnessed can only transfer to the unseen if the cause applies. Mark this principle.
3
Trace the argument
Follow how the cause (being a body) is identified, and why it cannot apply to God. Conclude that vision cannot be attributed to God.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة العقلية للنافين للرؤية: والمعقول لهم أن للرؤية في الشاهد علة وشروطا، لن يتصور تعدية ذلك إلى الغائب، وما يثبت في الغائب أو ينفى عنه يكون بطريق الاستدلال من الشاهد على الغائب وما تعدت العلة فيه، تعدى الحكم وما لا، فلا، وقد مر هذا فيما تقدم، فإذا لم يمكن تعدية علة الرؤية وشروطها لن يمكن تعدية الرؤية. وإنما قلنا ذلك لأن علة كون الشيء في الشاهد مرئيا كونه جسما، إذ كل جسم يرى ويستحيل رؤية ما وراء الجسم من الموجودات وهي الأعراض، ولا تعدي للجسم فلن يتعدى الحكم، وهو كونه صالحا لأن يرى.
The rational evidences of the deniers of vision: And their rational argument is that vision in the witnessed world has a cause and conditions; it is inconceivable to transfer that to the unseen. What is affirmed in the unseen or negated from it is by way of drawing inference from the witnessed to the unseen. And what the cause applies to, the judgment applies; and what not, then not. And this has been mentioned previously. So if transferring the cause and conditions of vision is not possible, then transferring vision is not possible. We only say that because the cause of a thing being visible in the witnessed world is its being a body, for every body is seen, and it is impossible to see what is beyond the body from among existing things, which are accidents. And the body does not transfer, so the judgment does not transfer, and it is its being suitable to be seen.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage presents the rational evidence of those who deny the possibility of seeing God (the Mu'tazila and others). It begins by stating that vision in the witnessed world has a cause (illa) and conditions (shurut). The key principle is that what is established in the unseen must be inferred from the witnessed, and the judgment (hukm) applies only when the cause applies. Since the cause of visibility in the witnessed is being a body (jism), and God is not a body, this cause cannot be transferred to God. Therefore, the judgment of being visible cannot apply to Him. The passage emphasizes that all bodies are seen, but accidents (a'rad) are not seen directly. Since God is not a body, He cannot be seen.

Key Terms
العلة
الشروط
الغائب
الشاهد
الاستدلال
الجسم
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the cause of visibility could be something other than body, but the argument assumes that in the witnessed world only bodies are visible.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the cause of something being visible in the witnessed world is its being a body. This is a premise of the argument, not a conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the cause (illa) of something being visible in the witnessed world according to this passage?
A) Having color
B) Being a body
C) Being at a distance
D) Having light

Key Takeaway
The rational argument against seeing God holds that since visibility in this world depends on being a body, and God is not a body, the cause cannot transfer, so vision of God is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument says your intellect, based on this world, cannot conceive of seeing God. Does that disturb you, or does it remind you that your relationship with God is not limited to what your senses can grasp? How will you seek to know Him beyond physical sight?
?
The argument draws a strict line between the witnessed and the unseen. Are there areas in your life where you mistakenly assume that God must be like something you've experienced? What false analogies might you be making about His mercy, justice, or presence? Resolve to correct them using revelation.
?
If you truly accept that the cause of visibility does not transfer to God, how does that change the way you long for the Hereafter? Does it make you focus more on the spiritual vision of the heart, or does it challenge your understanding of the Beatific Vision? Commit tonight to learning the orthodox position on seeing God.

The Shahid-Gha'ib Analogy for Vision
The author argues using shahid-gha'ib analogy to refute the opponents' vision claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the analogy between the seen and unseen (shahid-gha'ib) to argue that the opponents' claimed vision of God is not true vision.

Core Idea
What we affirm for the unseen must conform to the rational categories of the seen.

In this passage, the author engages in a rational argument using the well-known method of shahid-gha'ib (analogy from the seen to the unseen). He criticizes the opponents' claim about vision as conceptually impossible and then establishes a principle: whatever is affirmed for the unseen must be within the same limit as what is affirmed for the seen. This argument is crucial for understanding how rational theology approaches the attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument. The author starts by dismissing the opponents' claim as unintelligible, then presents the analogy.
The key phrase is 'وما يثبت في حق الغائب ينبغي أن يكون في حد ما يثبت في حق الشاهد'.
2
Identify the analogy
The author gives the example of knowledge: in the seen world, knowledge is either necessary or acquired. For the unseen, it is likewise inferential (istidlali) for most, but necessary for some. By analogy, vision must also follow the same rational categories.
Notice how the author uses 'ألا ترى' to draw attention to the analogy.
3
Understand the conclusion
Based on the analogy, the author concludes that what the opponents claim as vision does not fit any rational concept of vision, so it is not true vision. Their speech is 'غير معقول' (unintelligible).
This refutes the claim that God can be seen in a way completely unlike any vision we know.
4
Reflect on the method
The shahid-gha'ib method is a foundational tool in kalam. Ask yourself: why is it valid to reason from the seen to the unseen? What assumptions does it make?
The method relies on the consistency of rational categories across different domains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوقوف على كله أو بعضه، لن يعرف رؤية، فإذا ما يقولون كلام يقال وليس لفحواه في الأفهام ولا لمعناه في الأوهام وتصوره محال، وما يثبت في حق الغائب ينبغي أن يكون في حد ما يثبت في حق الشاهد ألا ترى أن العلم منا المتعلق بمعلوم شاهد لما لم يخرج من أن يكون ضروريا أو مكتسبا، فكان العلم منا بالغائب كذلك حتى كان العلم بالغائب عندكم وعند أكثرنا استدلاليا وعند بعض رؤسائنا ضروريا، فكذا الرؤية ينبغي أن لا تخرج في حق الغائب عن المتعارف المعقول. وبهذا التحقيق يتبين أن ما تزعمون أنه رؤية ليس برؤية وكلامكم في ذلك غير معقول.
And observing all of it or part of it, he will not know vision. So when they say a word that is said, and there is no meaning in minds nor concept in imaginations, and its conceptualization is impossible, and what is established regarding the unseen should be within the limit of what is established regarding the seen. Do you not see that knowledge from us pertaining to a known object that is seen, as long as it does not exit from being either necessary or acquired, so knowledge from us of the unseen is likewise, such that knowledge of the unseen with you and with most of us is inferential, and with some of our leaders is necessary. Likewise, vision should not exit in the case of the unseen from the conventional and rational. And by this investigation it becomes clear that what you claim to be vision is not vision, and your speech regarding that is unintelligible.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage presents an argument using the method of shahid-gha'ib (analogy from the seen to the unseen). The author criticizes the opponents' claim about vision as meaningless. He states that what is affirmed for the unseen must be within the same category as what is affirmed for the seen, giving the example of knowledge which is either necessary or acquired. By analogy, vision of the unseen must also conform to what is conventionally understood as vision. Therefore, the opponents' claimed vision does not meet rational criteria, so it is not true vision.

Key Terms

شاهد
غائب
ضروري
استدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That vision of God can be completely different from any concept of vision we know from the seen world.
Correction: The author argues that what is affirmed for the unseen must stay within the rational categories of the seen; thus, any claim about vision must conform to the conventional understanding of vision.

Checkpoint
What method does the author use to argue against the opponents' claim of vision?
A) Proof from impossibility
B) Analogy from the seen to the unseen (shahid-gha'ib)
C) Scriptural evidence
D) Logical contradiction

Key Takeaway
The author uses the shahid-gha'ib analogy to show that any claim about God's vision must fit rational categories; otherwise, it is unintelligible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's vision, do you ever imagine it in a way that has no basis in any concept of vision you know from the created world? Would you be willing to abandon that imagination if it contradicts the principle that the unseen must conform to the seen?
?
The author says the opponents' claim is 'a word that is said' with no real meaning. Are there aspects of your faith that you affirm only as empty words, without a genuine concept in your mind? What would it take to ensure your beliefs are truly intelligible?
?
If knowledge of God is primarily inferential (istidlali), what does that demand from you in terms of study and reflection? Are you content with relying on necessary knowledge alone, or will you actively seek to strengthen your intellectual understanding of God?

Two Rational Arguments Against 'Nazar' as Waiting
The author presents two rational proofs that 'nazar' cannot mean 'waiting'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments proving that 'nazar' in the verse cannot mean 'waiting'.

Core Idea
Waiting implies absence, but Paradise has present reward and no anxiety.

This lesson presents two clear rational arguments from Imam al-Nasafi against interpreting the word 'nazar' (looking) as 'waiting' (intizar) in the verse about the believers seeing Allah. The first argument focuses on the object of waiting—the reward—which is already present in Paradise. The second argument focuses on the state of the one waiting, which involves unease incompatible with Paradise. Together, these arguments firmly establish that 'nazar' means sight, not waiting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the English translation. Note that the author presents two distinct 'aspects' (wajhan) from reason.
The passage starts with 'والذي يدل على...' indicating a proof structure.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find where the first argument ends and the second begins. The first relates to the awaited thing (al-muntazar), the second to the one waiting (al-muntazir).
The author uses 'أحدهما راجع إلى...' and 'وأما الراجع إلى...' to separate them.
3
Analyze the first argument
Understand why waiting is impossible when the reward already exists. The author uses a linguistic analogy: 'wait for Zayd' means wait for his arrival, not his existence.
Key point: The non-existent is awaited; the existent is not.
4
Analyze the second argument
Understand why waiting causes anxiety, which contradicts the state of the people of Paradise. The proverb 'waiting is a red death' illustrates the distress.
The description 'fresh faces' (nādira) indicates joy of attainment, not anguish of waiting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على أن حمل الآية على الانتظار فاسد وجهان من المعقول: أحدهما راجع إلى نفس المنتظر فهو أن الثواب يومئذ يكون موجودا لأن الجنة دار وقوع الثواب لا دار الانتظار لذلك موجود في الدنيا، والمعدوم هو الذي ينتظر لا الموجود، وما يقال في مبتذل الكلام انتظر زيدا معناه انتظر حضوره وقدومه، ألا ترى لو فسر فقال: انتظر حضوره كان مستقيما ولو قال: انتظر وجوده كان فاسدا، فدل أن هذا التأويل باطل. وأما الراجع إلى حال المنتظر فهو أن ولي الله تعالى في الجنة مصون عما يوجب اعتراء وحشة في صدره أو ضيق وقلق في قلبه، وفي الانتظار ذلك وتنغيص للنعمة، وقد قيل في المثل السائر: الانتظار موت أحمر. وهذا مما لا يليق بحال بأهل الجنة، ألا ترى كيف وصفهم بنضارة الوجوه بقوله تعالى: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذ ناضرة إلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَة وذلك وصف من نال الثواب لا وصف من هو بعد يقاس ألم الانتظار وكربه.
And that which indicates that interpreting the verse as waiting is invalid are two aspects from reason: the first relates to the awaited thing itself: that the reward on that day would be existent because Paradise is the abode of receiving reward, not the abode of waiting; therefore it is existent in the world, and the non-existent is what is awaited, not the existent. What is said in common speech, 'Wait for Zayd,' means wait for his presence and arrival. Do you not see that if he explained and said, 'Wait for his presence,' it would be sound, but if he said, 'Wait for his existence,' it would be unsound. So that indicates that this interpretation is false. As for the aspect relating to the state of the one waiting: that the friend of Allah in Paradise is protected from anything that would cause an unease in his chest or distress and anxiety in his heart, and in waiting there is that and the spoiling of bliss. It has been said in the well-known proverb: 'Waiting is a red death.' This is not befitting the state of the people of Paradise. Do you not see how He described them with freshness of faces in His saying: 'Faces on that day are fresh, looking towards their Lord.' And that is a description of one who has attained reward, not a description of one who still endures the pain and anxiety of waiting.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage presents two rational arguments against interpreting 'nazar' in the verse as 'waiting' (intizar). The first argument focuses on the object of waiting: if the verse meant waiting, then the reward (thawab) would be something expected in the future. But the people of Paradise are already in Paradise, where reward is present, not awaited. In everyday language, 'wait for Zayd' means wait for his arrival, not his existence, because he already exists. Similarly, the reward in Paradise exists, so waiting for it is meaningless. The second argument focuses on the state of the person waiting. Waiting causes unease and anxiety, which is incompatible with the bliss of Paradise. The proverb 'waiting is a red death' illustrates how distressing waiting can be. The verse describes the people of Paradise as having fresh, joyful faces—a sign of having attained reward, not of enduring the pain of waiting. Thus, interpreting 'nazar' as waiting is rationally untenable.

Key Terms
انتظار (intizar)
ثواب (thawab)
منتظر (muntazir)
نضارة (nadaara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'nazar' in the verse could reasonably mean 'waiting' because the context of Paradise excludes anxiety.
Correction: The rational arguments show that waiting implies absence and anxiety, both incompatible with the presence of reward and the bliss of Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to the first rational argument, why is interpreting 'nazar' as 'waiting' invalid?
A) Because waiting requires the awaited thing to be non-existent, but reward in Paradise exists
B) Because waiting is physically impossible in Paradise
C) Because the verse does not use the word 'waiting'
D) Because the people of Paradise always have been rewarded

Key Takeaway
The two rational arguments establish that 'nazar' in the verse means 'looking' (seeing), not 'waiting', because waiting implies non-existence and anxiety, which are absent in Paradise.

The lesson shows that waiting implies doubt and anxiety. Where in your heart do you feel uncertainty about Allah's promise, and what would it take for you to be as certain as the people of Paradise whose faces are fresh and free of worry?
?
The proverb 'waiting is a red death' describes the pain of anticipation. In your own life, what are you waiting for—from Allah, from others, from yourself? Are you allowing that waiting to cause you anxiety, forgetting that Allah's reward for His friends is already granted? How can you shift from a mindset of waiting to one of present gratitude?
?
The source argues that waiting implies non-existence of what is awaited. Reflect on your relationship with Allah's attributes: do you treat His mercy, forgiveness, and reward as 'not yet here'? What would change if you lived as if they are already present, as the people of Paradise do?

Methodological Order of Proofs for Ru'ya
Nasafi explains his methodological choice to present scriptural proofs first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi presented scriptural proofs before rational proofs for the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs for ru'ya are self-sufficient; rational proofs are optional.

In this opening passage, Nasafi clarifies his unique approach: although logical order would place rational proofs first, he has already presented scriptural proofs because they simultaneously establish both the possibility and actual occurrence of ru'ya. This allows readers content with scripture to avoid deeper rational inquiry. After completing that, he now turns to rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's stated order.
2
Identify the reason for reversal
Find why Nasafi reversed the expected order: scriptural proofs contain both actual occurrence and permissibility together.
3
Consider the benefit for the reader
Note that whoever is satisfied with scriptural proofs does not need to delve into rational arguments.
4
Observe the transition to rational proofs
After finishing scriptural proofs, Nasafi now occupies himself with rational proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نشتغل بعد هذا ببيان جواز رؤية الله تعالى في العقول وإن كان الترتيب يقتضي إثبات الجواز أولا بالدلائل العقلية ثم الثبوت بالدلائل السمعية إلا أنا قدمنا الدلائل السمعية لما مر أن فيها الثبوت والجواز جميعا معا، فمن اكتفى بها كان ذلك كافيا له فلا يحتاج إلى الخوض في الدلائل العقلية. ثم بعد فراغنا من إثبات ما رمنا إثباته بالدلائل السمعية نشتغل بالدلائل العقلية فنقول: كان أوائلنا وأوائل المعتزلة سبق منهم الإجماع أن الأعراض مستحيلة الرؤية، واختلفوا بعد هذا الإجماع أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية في الشاهد ما هي؟ فزعمت أوائلهم أنها الجسمية فكل ما هو جسم كان مرئيا، وكل ما لم يكن جسما فهو مستحيل الرؤية وقالوا: إن البارئ جل وعلا ليس بجسم فيستحيل رؤيته وزعموا أن كل من قال : إن الله تعالى مرئي فقد زعم أنه جسم إذ لا فرق بين قول القائل هذا جسم وبين قولهم هذا مرئي كما لا فرق بين قولهم: هو عشرة وبين قولهم : هو قدر مائة وأصحابنا - رحمهم الله - قالوا: إن العلة المطلقة للرؤية هي
Then after this we occupy ourselves with the exposition of the permissibility of the vision of Allah the Exalted in the intellects, even though the order requires establishing the permissibility first by rational proofs, then [the actual occurrence] by scriptural proofs, except that we have presented the scriptural proofs first because, as has passed, they contain both [actual] occurrence and permissibility together. So whoever suffices with them, that suffices for him, and he does not need to delve into rational proofs. Then after our finishing from establishing what we intended to establish by scriptural proofs, we occupy ourselves with rational proofs and say: Our early scholars and the early Mu'tazila preceded [with] consensus that accidents are impossible to be seen. Then after this consensus, they differed as to what is the absolute cause of vision in the witnessed [world]. So their early ones claimed that it is corporeality: so everything that is a body is visible, and everything that is not a body is impossible to see. And they said: The Creator, Exalted and High, is not a body, so His vision is impossible. And they claimed that whoever says that Allah the Exalted is visible has claimed that He is a body, because there is no difference between one's saying 'this is a body' and their saying 'this is visible,' just as there is no difference between their saying 'it is ten' and their saying 'it is the measure of one hundred.' And our scholars — may Allah have mercy on them — said: The absolute cause of vision is
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage serves two functions: it explains the author's methodological choice and then introduces the rational argument. First, Nasafi notes that although one might expect rational proofs for permissibility to precede scriptural proofs for actual occurrence, he has reversed the order because scriptural proofs simultaneously establish both aspects. This allows the reader who is satisfied with scriptural proofs to avoid the complexities of rational argumentation. After completing the scriptural proofs, he turns to rational proofs. He begins by noting an early consensus between Maturidi scholars and Mu'tazila that accidents cannot be seen. However, they differed on the underlying cause (ʿilla) of visibility in the empirical world (al-shāhid). The early Mu'tazila claimed the cause is corporeality (al-jismiyya): only bodies are visible. Since God is not a body, they argued, He cannot be seen. They further claimed that affirming vision of God necessarily implies affirming His corporeality, drawing an analogy: saying 'this is visible' is equivalent to saying 'this is a body,' just as saying 'it is ten' is equivalent to saying 'it is the measure of a hundred.' The passage ends as the Maturidi response begins, cutting off at 'the absolute cause of vision is'.

Key Terms
جواز الرؤية
الدلائل السمعية
الدلائل العقلية
الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must study rational proofs to be certain of ru'ya.
Correction: Nasafi asserts that scriptural proofs alone suffice; rational proofs are for those who seek further demonstration.

Checkpoint
Why did Nasafi present scriptural proofs before rational proofs?
A) Because rational proofs are invalid
B) Because scriptural proofs contain both actual occurrence and permissibility together
C) Because he prefers scripture
D) Because the Mu'tazila accept scripture

Key Takeaway
Nasafi reverses the expected order of proofs to emphasize that scriptural evidence is sufficient, while rational argument is an additional depth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek certainty about Allah, do you find yourself demanding rational proofs before accepting what He has revealed? This lesson shows that scripture alone can suffice. Are you willing to let Allah's words be enough for you, even when your intellect struggles to grasp?

Nasafi presents scriptural proofs first, not because rational arguments are worthless, but because they are not necessary. What would change if you truly trusted that scripture contains both the permissibility and reality of the unseen?

Consider one belief you hold about Allah that you think you need rational evidence to justify. This lesson challenges that priority. Can you bring yourself to rest on the scripture's authority alone, even if you cannot fully explain it? In what area of your faith are you most tempted to seek rational proof before surrender?

The Mu'tazila Argument Against Ru'ya from Corporeality
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila argument against ru'ya based on the cause of vision being corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that only bodies can be seen and its implication that God, being non-corporeal, cannot be seen.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila equated visibility with corporeality, so denying God's body meant denying vision.

After completing his methodological note, Nasafi introduces the rational debate. He first records an early consensus that accidents cannot be seen, then presents the Mu'tazila claim: the cause of visibility is corporeality. Since God is not a body, vision is impossible. The passage also records the Mu'tazila's bold claim that affirming vision of God implies affirming Him as a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the initial consensus
Find the early consensus: accidents are impossible to be seen.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's causal claim
Identify what they claim as the absolute cause of vision in the empirical world: corporeality.
3
Follow the syllogism
Since God is not a body, it follows that He cannot be seen. Also note the equivalence argument: visible equals body.
4
Examine the analogy
The Mu'tazila draw an analogy: saying 'this is visible' is like 'this is a body' just as 'it is ten' is like 'it is the measure of a hundred.'
5
Observe the incomplete Maturidi response
The passage ends as the Maturidi position begins: 'The absolute cause of vision is...'

Rational Proof That Accidents Are Not Visible
Proof from disagreement: if accidents were visible, there would be no rational dispute about their existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that accidents cannot be seen, and why the existence of disagreement among rational people about accidents demonstrates their imperceptibility.

Core Idea
If accidents were visible, no rational person would disagree about their existence.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' presents a key rational proof used by early Maturidi and Mu'tazili scholars to argue that accidents—non-enduring attributes like colors or sounds—are not visible. The argument is striking: the very fact that people have debated accidents using rational proofs shows that accidents cannot be perceived by the senses. Let's explore this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Allow the author's argument to unfold naturally.
Notice that the passage refers to 'our early scholars and their early scholars'—a reference to both Maturidis and Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the premise
What is the observed fact that forms the foundation of the proof? The passage mentions disagreement among rational people about accidents.
This disagreement is historical and ongoing.
3
Follow the hypothetical
The author imagines what would happen if accidents were visible. Trace the consequences: no disagreement, no rational proofs, recourse to senses, accusing deniers of sophistry.
Why would there be no need for rational arguments if accidents were perceptible?
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the hypothetical consequences do not occur (people still debate with reason), the original assumption (accidents are visible) is false. Accidents are known through intellect, not senses.
This is a classic argument from the absence of expected consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أوائلنا وأوائلهم احتجوا لكون الأعراض مستحيلة الرؤية أن الخلاف بين العقلاء قديما وحديثا موجود في ثبوت الأعراض وانتفائها. اشتغل المثبتون بإقامة الدلائل على ذلك ولو كانت هي مرئية محسوسة لما جرى الخلاف فيها بين أرباب الحواس السليمة، ولما جرت المحاجة بالدلائل العقلية ولما اشتغل العقلاء بالمحاكمة إلى دلائل العقول بل فزعوا إلى الحواس، ونسبوا المنكرين إلى العناد وجحد الضروريات والمكابرة للحواس وألحقوهم بالمتجاهلة من السوفسطائية، وحيث لم ينسبوهم إلى ذلك وناظروهم وأثبتوا ذلك بالدلائل العقلية علم أنها ليست بمرئية، وأن طريق ثبوت العلم بها العقل لا الحس
Then our early scholars and their early scholars argued for the impossibility of seeing accidents (a'rad) by [pointing out] that disagreement among rational people, anciently and recently, exists regarding the existence and non-existence of accidents. Those who affirm [accidents] have busied themselves with presenting proofs for that. But if they were visible, perceptible, there would be no disagreement about them among people with sound senses, nor would there be disputation by rational proofs, nor would rational people have recourse to intellectual arguments; rather they would resort to the senses, and they would ascribe the deniers to obstinacy, denial of necessities, and opposition to the senses, and they would liken them to the ignorant among the sophists. But since they did not ascribe them to that, and they debated them and established [accidents] by rational proofs, it is known that they are not visible, and that the way of establishing knowledge of them is the intellect, not the sense.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof that accidents are not visible. It is presented as an argument used by both early Maturidis and early Mu'tazila. The proof is based on the observation that rational people have long disagreed about the existence of accidents. Those who affirm accidents (the 'provers') have had to provide rational proofs for their existence. If accidents were visible or perceptible, there would be no such disagreement among people with sound senses. People would instead rely on their senses, and those who denied accidents would be accused of obstinacy or sophistry. But because the dispute continued and was settled by rational arguments, it is clear that accidents are not visible or perceptible; knowledge of them comes from the intellect, not the senses.

Key Terms
أعراض (a'rad)
رؤية (ru'ya)
دلائل عقلية (dalā'il 'aqliyya)
حواس (hawās)
سوفسطائية (sūfisṭā'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sensory perception is the only way to know existence.
Correction: The passage shows that some entities, like accidents, are known only through rational proof, not through the senses.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that accidents must not be visible?
A) Because no one has ever seen an accident
B) Because if they were visible, there would be no need for rational proofs to establish their existence
C) Because the senses are unreliable
D) Because the Mu'tazila said so

Key Takeaway
The existence of rational debate about accidents proves that they are not perceptible by the senses but are known through intellectual proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth of Islam that you cannot see or touch—like the reality of accidents, the attributes of Allah, or the unseen—do you instinctively doubt because it is not physically observable? Or do you actively seek rational proofs to strengthen your conviction? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in the intellect as Allah's gift?
?
The early scholars engaged in rational debate with those who denied accidents, rather than dismissing them as stubborn or foolish. Think of a person in your life who rejects a core Islamic belief. Do you have the patience and knowledge to engage them with rational argument, or do you resort to labeling them as obstinate? What is one rational proof you could learn today to defend that belief without accusing them of sophistry?
?

Does it prove that Islam is false, or that its truths are known by reason and revelation, not by sensory consensus? How does this lesson empower you to respond to skepticism about unseen realities?

Proving Visibility of Bodies via Consensus on Accidents
Imam Nasafi establishes the method of proving visibility of bodies by consensus that accidents cannot be seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses consensus on the invisibility of accidents to argue for the visibility of bodies, and how he handles the exception of Abdullah ibn Sa'id.

Core Idea
Consensus on accidents is the key to proving bodies are visible in kalam.

Imam Nasafi presents a refined methodology for arguing that bodies can be seen. He acknowledges that a simple transfer of ruling from witnessed to unseen based on self-subsistence is problematic due to disagreements about accidents in the unseen. Instead, he grounds the argument in a point of consensus: all parties agree that accidents cannot be seen in the witnessed world. This passage explains the proper method and addresses an exceptional view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the problem with the simple argument
The author mentions that the argument based on 'self-subsistence' is not universally valid. Why? Because there is disagreement about whether accidents exist in the unseen.
3
Understand the consensus method
The author proposes using the agreed-upon point that accidents cannot be seen in the witnessed world, then transferring the ruling to the unseen based on whether something is an accident or a body.
This is a methodological shift from relying on self-subsistence to relying on consensus.
4
Note the exception of Abdullah ibn Sa'id
If an opponent holds that accidents can be seen (Abdullah ibn Sa'id's view), then one must engage with that specific proof. Otherwise, consensus suffices.
This shows the author's thoroughness in addressing potential counterarguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يطرد اعتلالهم في إخراج الأعراض من أن تكون مرئية بجريان الخلاف في ثبوتها بين العقلاء، فإذا التعويل على القائم بالذات غير ممكن إلا على طريق التعليق بالإجماع مع الخصوم في استحالة رؤية الأعراض فيقال لهم : اتفقنا أن ما هو مستحيل الرؤية في الشاهد هو العرض، وأن كل جسم جائز الرؤية، وكل كما هو جسم فهو قائم بالذات فبنا أن نتأمل أنه لأنه جسم فلا يعدى إلى الغائب لأنه ليس أو لأنه یری بجسم قائم بالذات فيعدى إلى الغائب لأنه قائم بالذات فينبغي أن نتكلم على هذا الوجه ولا نشتغل بدليل استحالة رؤية الأعراض إلا إذا كان المثبت للرؤية يرى في الأعراض رأي عبد الله بن سعيد فحينئذ يمكنه أن يتعلق بذلك الدليل، فأما من لم يكن يتابعه في ذلك ويجوز استدراك شيء من الأعراض بشيء من الحواس، فلا يمكنه التعويل على الدليل فينبغي أن يعتمد على إجماع الخصوم على ذلك.
Thus their argument [about accidents] is not universally valid because there is disagreement among rational people about the existence of accidents [in the unseen]. So reliance on what is self-subsistent is not possible except by way of appealing to consensus with the opponents on the impossibility of seeing accidents. So it is said to them: we agree that what is impossible to see in the witnessed world is the accident, and that every body is possible to see, and everything that is a body is self-subsistent. Therefore we should reflect that because it is a body does not transfer [the ruling] to the unseen because it is not [a body?], or because it is seen as a body self-subsistent, so it transfers to the unseen because it is self-subsistent. So we should speak in this manner and not busy ourselves with the proof of the impossibility of seeing accidents unless the one affirming vision holds the view of Abdullah ibn Sa'id regarding accidents, in which case he may rely on that proof. As for one who does not follow him in that and allows perceiving some accidents by some senses, he cannot rely on that proof, so he should depend on the consensus of the opponents on that.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage presents a methodological argument for proving that bodies are visible by relying on a point of consensus: all parties agree that accidents cannot be seen in the witnessed world. The author argues that since there is disagreement about whether accidents exist in the unseen (i.e., as divine attributes), one cannot simply transfer the ruling from witnessed to unseen based on self-subsistence. Instead, the proper method is to note the agreed-upon principle: in the witnessed world, what is impossible to see is the accident, and every body is possible to see. Then, when moving to the unseen, if something is self-subsistent (like a body), it is possible to see; if it is an accident, it is not. However, if an opponent holds the minority view (like Abdullah ibn Sa'id) that accidents may be seen, then one must engage with that claim using specific proofs. Otherwise, one should rely on the consensus that accidents are invisible.

الجسم
القائم بالذات
الرؤية
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus alone settles the issue without need for further argument.
Correction: The passage shows that consensus is used as a premise only when opponents agree on the impossibility of seeing accidents, and there is an exception that requires additional proof.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, on what basis should one primarily argue for the visibility of bodies?
A) Direct sensory experience of bodies in the unseen
B) The consensus that accidents cannot be seen
C) The self-subsistence of accidents
D) The opinion of Abdullah ibn Sa'id

Key Takeaway
The method for proving bodies are visible relies on consensus that accidents cannot be seen, but one must be prepared to address the minority view that accidents may be visible.

Why Colors Are Accidents, Not Bodies
Nasafi defines accident and applies it to colors to refute Nazzam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why colors are classified as accidents in kalam, based on the definition of accident and the impossibility of permanence in non-self-subsistent entities.

Core Idea
Colors are accidents because they need a substrate and cannot endure.

In this passage, Nasafi refutes Nazzam's claim that colors are bodies. He begins by defining an accident and showing that colors meet that definition. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the kalam distinction between bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read Nazzam's claim
An-Nazzam argues that colors are bodies and visible; note that he separates visibility from self-subsistence.
This is the view being refuted.
2
Study the definition of accident
Nasafi defines an accident as something that cannot subsist by itself, needs a substrate, and cannot endure.
This definition is used throughout kalam.
3
Apply definition to colors
Colors display all these characteristics, so they are accidents.
They are not self-subsistent.
4
Understand the permanence argument
Even if colors were claimed to endure, endurance cannot reside in a non-self-subsistent entity; this is intuitively known.
This reinforces their accidental nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن النظام منهم يبني الكلام على أصله أن الألوان مرئية وهي أجسام وليست بقائمة بالذات، فإذا انفكت الرؤية عن القيام بالذات وما انفكت عن الجسمية، ووجه إبطال كلامه أنه بناه على أصلين كل واحد منهما غير صحيح أحدهما ادعاؤه أن الألوان أجسام، وهذا ممنوع بل هي أعراض لوجود حد العرض فيها فإن الحركة التي هي عرض بالإجماع إنما هي عرض لاستحالة قيامها بذاتها وافتقارها إلى محل واستحالة بقائها (إذ العرض هذا هو يفارق الأجسام لاستحالة قيامها بذاتها وافتقارها إلى محل واستحالة بقائها) ويفارق صفات الله تعالى لاستحالة بقائها وهذا الحد بعينه موجود في الألوان ثم هو وإن كان يدعي بقاءها إلا أنا نثبت بالدليل الذي ثبت به استحالة بقاء سائر الأعراض وهو أن الباقي يكون باقيا ببقاء، واستحالة قيام البقاء بما لا يقوم بنفسه ثابتة بالبديهة، فلم تكن الأعراض باقية على ما نبين ذلك إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الاستطاعة - إن شاء الله تعالى والألوان غير قائمة بذواتها فيستحيل قيام البقاء بها فلم تكن باقية فكانت أعراضا لا أجساما، ومع ذلك جوز النظام رؤيتها دل أن الرؤية تنفك عن الجسم فبطل تعليقه الرؤية بالجسم، وكذا هو يقول باستحالة رؤية الأصوات وإن كانت عنده أجساما فإذا بطل تعليقه جواز الرؤية بالجسم طردا وعكسا.
Then an-Nazzam among them builds his argument on his principle that colors are visible and they are bodies, not self-subsistent. So he separates visibility from self-subsistence while not separating it from corporeality. The way to invalidate his argument is that he built it on two principles, each of which is unsound. One is his claim that colors are bodies; this is rejected—rather they are accidents because the definition of accident applies to them. Movement, which is an accident by consensus, is an accident because it is impossible for it to subsist by itself, its need for a substrate, and the impossibility of its permanence. (For the accident differs from bodies by the impossibility of its self-subsistence, its need for a substrate, and the impossibility of its permanence.) And it differs from the attributes of Allah because of the impossibility of its permanence. This same definition exists in colors. Then even if he claims their permanence, we prove by the proof by which the impossibility of permanence of other accidents is established—namely, that the enduring thing endures by a permanence, and the impossibility of the subsistence of permanence in that which does not subsist by itself is established by intuition. So accidents are not enduring, as we will explain when we reach the question of capacity (al-istita'ah)—if Allah wills. And colors are not self-subsistent, so it is impossible for permanence to subsist in them, so they are not enduring, thus they are accidents not bodies. Nevertheless, an-Nazzam allowed their vision, indicating that vision separates from body, so his linking of vision to body is invalid. Similarly, he holds the impossibility of seeing sounds even though they are bodies according to him. So his linking the permissibility of vision to body is invalid both positively and negatively.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage refutes an-Nazzam's claim that colors are bodies and therefore visible. The author identifies two flawed principles in Nazzam's argument: first, that colors are bodies; second, that visibility can be separated from self-subsistence. The refutation focuses on the first principle: colors are accidents, not bodies. The proof is that they meet the definition of an accident: they cannot subsist by themselves, they require a substrate, and they cannot endure (since endurance would require a permanence that itself cannot subsist in an accident). The author also argues that even if Nazzam claims colors endure, it is impossible because endurance is a quality that cannot reside in what is not self-subsistent—this is intuitively known. Therefore colors are accidents, and since Nazzam himself admits that accidents (like sounds) cannot be seen, his view is contradictory. Consequently, his linking of visibility to corporeality is invalid, as vision can exist without a body (in the case of colors, if they were bodies) and can be absent from bodies (in the case of sounds).

Key Terms
العرض
الجسم
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of accident is arbitrary or conventional.
Correction: It is based on logical necessity: anything that cannot subsist by itself and cannot endure is an accident. This is a key kalam principle.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider movement an accident?
A) Because it is visible
B) Because it cannot subsist by itself and needs a substrate
C) Because it is a body
D) Because it is self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
Colors are accidents, not bodies, because they depend on a substrate and cannot endure; even if permanence were claimed, it cannot inhere in non-self-subsistent entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the visible world around you—colors, shapes, sounds—as if it is self-sufficient and independent? What would it mean for your daily reliance on Allah if you truly internalize that every color you see is a dependent accident, sustained only by its substrate and ultimately by Him?
?
Nazzam insisted colors must be bodies because he couldn't accept their contingency. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you cling to because admitting its dependence would be too uncomfortable? What would change if you let that belief be refuted?
?
The proof that colors cannot endure relies on the impossibility of permanence inhering in what is not self-subsistent. In what areas of your faith or practice are you hoping for 'permanence' from something that is inherently fragile—like a relationship, a role, or a feeling? How can you anchor your hope only in the Self-Subsistent?

The Contradiction in Nazzam's View on Visibility
Nasafi points out the inconsistency in Nazzam's position on visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nazzam's contradictory stance on visibility (allowing for colors but denying for sounds) undermines his claim that vision requires corporeality.

Core Idea
If colors are bodies and visible, then sounds (also bodies) should be visible; Nazzam's denial shows his error.

Nasafi concludes his refutation by pointing out an inconsistency in Nazzam's position: he claims colors are bodies and visible, but he denies the visibility of sounds despite also considering them bodies. This contradiction invalidates his linking of vision to corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Observe Nazzam's inconsistency
Nazzam allows vision for colors (which he considers bodies) but denies it for sounds (also bodies).
This is a logical contradiction.
2
Analyze the implication
If vision required corporeality, all bodies would be visible; his denial of sounds shows this is false.
Linking visibility to body is invalid.
3
Conclude the refutation
Thus, linking vision to body is invalid both positively (colors) and negatively (sounds).
Nazzam's own position refutes itself.

Maturidi View on Visibility of Atoms and Composite Bodies
Nasafi states the Maturidi position that atoms are seen individually.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that atoms are intrinsically visible and that composition does not confer visibility but merely allows perception of multiple atoms together.

Core Idea
Atoms are visible by themselves; composition merely reveals them.

Nasafi presents the Maturidi doctrine on the visibility of atoms and bodies. He argues that atoms are inherently visible, so when we see a composite body, we are actually seeing each constituent atom. This counters views that deny atom visibility. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, noting key terms like الجوهر (atom) and الجسم (body).
The passage is short and cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the Maturidi claim
They claim that when a body is seen, every atom within it is seen, even though an atom alone is not a body.
3
Understand the reasoning
The argument: if an atom were not visible by itself, combining it with others would not make it visible. Visibility must be intrinsic.
This is a causal argument based on the nature of composition.
4
Consider the missing conclusion
The passage ends abruptly. Think about what the next part likely argues: that therefore atoms are visible in themselves.
Use the logic provided to infer the completion.
5
Connect to broader kalam debates
This position relates to discussions on divine attributes, specifically the visibility of Allah, as atoms serve as a model for visibility.
The Maturidis affirm that Allah can be seen in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصحابنا :يقولون : إن الجسم هو المتركب من الجواهر وعند رؤيته نرى كل جوهر وإن لم يكن كل جوهر جسما، وهذا لأن الجوهر لو لم يكن في نفسه مرئيا قبل انضمامه إلى غيره لما صار مرئيا بالتركيب وانضمامه إلى غيره، لأن جواز
And our companions say: Indeed the body is that which is composed of atoms, and when it is seen we see every atom, even if every atom is not a body. This is because if the atom were not in itself visible before its conjunction with another, it would not become visible through composition and conjunction with another, because the permissibility...
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position on the visibility of atoms and bodies. They define a body as a composite of atoms (jawahir). When we see a body, we actually see each constituent atom, even though an individual atom is not considered a body (since a body requires composition). The reason is that visibility is an intrinsic property of the atom: if the atom were not visible by itself, combining it with other atoms would not make it visible. The argument is cut off, but the implication is that the atom's visibility is inherent and not dependent on composition; thus, the visibility of a body is due to the visibility of its atoms. This contrasts with the view of some Mu'tazila who denied visibility of atoms.

Proving the Visibility of Substances
Nasafi proves substances are visible and refutes those who only accept color visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that substances (jawahir) are visible, using both consensus and the argument from visual discrimination, and how he refutes opponents who limit visibility to colors.

Core Idea
Substances are visible because we see them, and consensus proves it.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a fundamental issue: are only colors visible, or are substances and accidents also visible? He argues for the latter, using a combination of consensus (ijma of the Mu'tazila with us) and the fact that we visually discriminate between kinds of substances just as we do with colors. He then challenges objectors like al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim to explain their positions consistently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Nasafi starts with a definition and then moves to proofs.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the consensus proof and the discrimination proof. Notice how he says the consensus alone suffices, but then he adds another proof for emphasis.
The consensus is among all Mu'tazila, which is a powerful argument.
3
Follow the refutation
See how Nasafi challenges the opponent who only allows color visibility. He forces them to apply the same criterion (discrimination) to substances and accidents, and then shows the inconsistency.
Pay attention to the names: al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim are key figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرف كون الجواهر مرئية إلا بالتمييز بالبصر بين أجناسها وحصول العلم بوجودها، وهذا المعنى بعينه في حق الأكوان والألوان موجود فكانت مرئية، ولو لم تكن مرئية لعرف بالرؤية وجود الجواهر ولكان لا يوقف على كونه متحركا أو ساكنا أو مجتمعا أو متفرقا كما لا يوقف على الطعوم بمجرد الرؤية لانعدام تعلقها بالطعوم على ما أجرى الله تعالى العادة، وإن كانت مرئية عندنا لكونها موجودة على ما نبين بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى. والدليل على أن الجواهر مرئية موافقة جميع المعتزلة إيانا على ذلك فيغنينا الإجماع عن إقامة البرهان على ذلك ، ثم الدليل على ذلك حصول التمييز بين أجناس الجواهر بالرؤية كحصوله بين أنواع الألوان. ثم يقال لهذا الذي لا يجوز رؤية الجواهر ولم يجوز إلا رؤية الألوان: بم عرفت أن الألوان مرئية ؟ فإن قال بالتمييز بين أنواعها بالرؤية فكذا يلزم في الجواهر والأكوان والطول والقصر والحركة والسكون وغير ذلك. وبعين هذا يطالب النظام فيقال له : لم قلت إن الألوان مرئية؟ فلا يمكن أن يتعلق بالتمييز بين أنواعها فيلزم رؤية الحركة والسكون ، وكذا أبو هاشم يطالب بالعلة التي لأجلها جعل الألوان مرئية ثم يطالب بالتفرقة بينها وبين الأكوان ويسوي بينها وبينها بعين تلك العلة. وإذا ثبت رؤية الجواهر والأكوان والألوان فنقول: ما هي العلة المجوزة للرؤية؟ لابد من سبر الأوصاف ليتبين العلة المجوزة للرؤية من الأوصاف الاتفاقية المقترنة بها المسماة عند المتكلمين أوصاف الوجود، ثم نعدي الرؤية بتلك العلة من الشاهد إلى الغائب إذ التعدية من الشاهد إلى الغائب تكون بأوصاف العلة دون أوصاف الوجود والفرق بين أوصاف الوجود وأوصاف العلة قد مرت في مسألة الصفات فنشتغل بسبر الأوصاف ليتبين وصف العلة من أوصاف الوجود فنقول : لا يخلو المرئي في الشاهد من أن يكون مرئيا لكونه جسما أو لكونه عرضا أو لكونه لونا أو لكونه متلونا أو لكونه قائما بالذات أو لكونه كونا أو لكونه متكونا أو لكونه معلوما أو لكونه مذكورا أو لكونه محدثا أو لكونه موصوفا أو لكونه باقيا أو لكونه موجودا. ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه جسما لأنا أقمنا الدلالة على رؤية اللون والكون، وهما ليسا بجسمين، ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه عرضا لقيام الدلالة على كون الجسم مرئيا وليس هو بعرض على أن عند الخصوم يستحيل رؤية كلها. ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه لونا لأنا رأينا ما ليس بلون وهو الجوهر والكون على ما أثبتنا ذلك بالدليل وساعدنا على ذلك الجبائي. ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه ملونا أو متلونا لقيام الدلالة على رؤية الألوان والأكوان وليست هي بمتلونة لاستحالة قيام اللون بها. ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه قائما بالذات أو لكونه موصوفا لأن الخصوم لو علقوا الرؤية بكونه قائما بالذات أو لكونه موصوفا فقد سلمت لنا المسألة وأمكن التعدية إلى الغائب، ثم إنا بينا أن اللون والكون مرئيان وليسا بقائمين بذواتهما ولا موصوفين بصفة تقوم بهما.
It is known that substances are visible except by visual discrimination between their kinds and the acquisition of knowledge of their existence, and this same meaning exists with respect to accidents and colors, so they are visible. And if they were not visible, then the existence of substances would be known by sight, but one would not be able to know whether it is moving or stationary, or combined or separated, just as tastes are not known by mere sight due to the lack of their attachment to tastes, according to what God Most High has established as custom, although they are visible to us because they exist, as we will explain after this—if God wills. The proof that substances are visible is the agreement of all the Mu'tazila with us on that, so the consensus suffices us from establishing a proof on that. Then the proof for that is the occurrence of discrimination between kinds of substances by sight, just as it occurs between types of colors. Then it is said to the one who does not allow the visibility of substances and allows only the visibility of colors: How did you know that colors are visible? If he says by discrimination between their types by sight, then the same is required in substances, accidents, length, shortness, motion, rest, and other things. And by the same token, al-Nazzam is challenged, and it is said to him: Why did you say that colors are visible? Then it is impossible to depend on discrimination between their types, so the visibility of motion and rest would be required. Likewise, Abu Hashim is challenged regarding the cause on account of which he made colors visible, then he is challenged to differentiate between them and accidents, and he would equalize between them and them by that same cause. And once the visibility of substances, accidents, and colors is established, we say: What is the cause that permits visibility? It is necessary to survey the attributes to clarify the cause that permits visibility from the accidental attributes that accompany it, called by the mutakallimun 'attributes of existence.' Then we transfer visibility by that cause from the witnessed to the unseen, since transfer from the witnessed to the unseen is by means of attributes of the cause, not attributes of existence. And the difference between attributes of existence and attributes of the cause has already been mentioned in the question of attributes. So we occupy ourselves with surveying the attributes to clarify the attribute of the cause from the attributes of existence, and we say: The visible thing in the witnessed cannot be free from being visible due to its being a body, or due to its being an accident, or due to its being a color, or due to its being colored, or due to its being subsistent in itself, or due to its being an accident, or due to its being the locus of accidents, or due to its being known, or due to its being mentioned, or due to its being originated, or due to its being qualified, or due to its being enduring, or due to its being existent. And it is not permissible to be seen due to its being a body, because we have established evidence for the visibility of color and accident, and they are not bodies. And it is not permissible to be seen due to its being an accident, because evidence has been established for the body being visible, and it is not an accident, especially since according to the opponents it is impossible to see all accidents. And it is not permissible to be seen due to its being a color, because we have seen what is not a color, namely substance and accident, as we have established that by evidence and al-Jubba'i agreed with us on that. And it is not permissible to be seen due to its being colored or having color, because evidence has been established for the visibility of colors and accidents, and they are not colored due to the impossibility of color subsisting in them. And it is not permissible to be seen due to its being subsistent in itself or due to its being qualified, because if the opponents were to attach visibility to its being subsistent in itself or to its being qualified, then the matter would be conceded to us and transfer to the unseen would be possible. Then we have shown that color and accident are visible and they are not subsistent in themselves nor qualified by a quality subsisting in them.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla. It addresses two main points: first, establishing that substances (jawahir) are visible; second, beginning the investigation into the attribute (ʿilla) that makes something visible. The passage begins by stating that we know substances are visible through visual discrimination between their kinds, just as we do with colors and accidents (akwan). If they were not visible, one could not distinguish motion, rest, combination, etc., by sight. The proof for visibility is the consensus of all Mu'tazila with the Ash'aris/Maturidis, which suffices, and additionally the fact that we do discriminate between kinds of substances by sight. The author then refutes a hypothetical opponent who only allows visibility of colors, using the same principle: if the criterion is discrimination by sight, then it applies to substances and accidents too. He mentions al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim as opponents who are forced into inconsistencies. Once visibility of substances, accidents, and colors is established, he asks: What is the cause (ʿilla) that permits visibility? He says we must survey (sabr) the attributes that accompany visible things to find the cause, distinguishing between accidental attributes (awṣāf al-wujūd) and attributes of the cause (awṣāf al-ʿilla). The transfer from the witnessed (shāhid) to the unseen (ghā'ib) is based on the cause-attribute, not on accidental attributes. He then enumerates possible attributes: being a body, being an accident, being a color, being colored, being subsistent in itself, being an accident (kawn?), being the locus of accidents (mutakawwin?), being known, being mentioned, being originated, being qualified, being enduring, being existent. He then rejects each: not body because color and accident are visible but not bodies; not accident because body is visible and is not an accident; not color because we see substances; not colored because colors and accidents are not colored; not subsistent in itself or qualified because that would concede the issue to the opponents and allow transfer to the unseen, but color and accident are visible yet not subsistent in themselves nor qualified. The passage ends here, leaving the rest of the survey for later discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only colors are visible by sight.
Correction: Nasafi argues that substances and accidents are also visible, as proven by consensus and visual discrimination.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof Nasafi gives for the visibility of substances?
A) Direct sensory experience of substances.
B) The consensus of all Mu'tazila on this point.
C) The impossibility of knowing motion without seeing substances.
D) The argument that substances are colors.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi establishes the visibility of substances through consensus and the fact that we visually discriminate between them, refuting those who limit visibility to colors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly trust your sight to perceive reality, or do you, like al-Nazzam, restrict it based on a flawed assumption? Where in your life have you limited what you are willing to see or accept?
?
The consensus of the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna suffices as proof. Are you part of a consensus that embraces truth, or do you isolate yourself with a distorted view? Think of a belief you hold that goes against the clear consensus of the scholars — are you willing to reconsider?
?
If you cannot see God now, does that mean He is not visible? Nasafi argues that we see substances by discrimination, not by color. How does your limited perception affect your understanding of the unseen? What does that demand of your faith?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Searching for the Cause of Visibility
Nasafi surveys possible attributes to find the cause of visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi surveys potential attributes to identify the cause (ʿilla) that makes something visible, and why he rejects each candidate.

Core Idea
What makes something visible? Not its body, color, or existence, but something else we must find.

Having established that substances, accidents, and colors are all visible, Nasafi now asks: what is the cause that permits visibility? He embarks on a systematic survey (sabr) of attributes, distinguishing between accidental attributes and attributes of the cause. The goal is to find the attribute that can be transferred from the witnessed to the unseen to prove God's visibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to see the structure
First, Nasafi states the need for a cause. Then he distinguishes between types of attributes. Finally, he enumerates and rejects 13 possible attributes. Read carefully to follow the logic.
This is a survey (sabr) method common in kalam.
2
Identify the two categories of attributes
Nasafi distinguishes between attributes of existence (awṣāf al-wujūd) and attributes of the cause (awṣāf al-ʿilla). Only the latter can be used for transfer from the witnessed to the unseen.
This distinction is key to his method.
3
Follow the rejections
Nasafi lists 13 possible attributes (body, accident, color, etc.) and rejects each one. For each, note his reason—e.g., we see things that are not bodies, not colors, etc.
The passage ends before concluding what the real cause is; it only eliminates candidates.

يعرف كون الجواهر مرئية إلا بالتمييز بالبصر بين أجناسها وحصول العلم بوجودها، وهذا المعنى بعينه في حق الأكوان والألوان موجود فكانت مرئية، ولو لم تكن مرئية لعرف بالرؤية وجود الجواهر ولكان لا يوقف على It is known that substances are visible except by visual discrimination between their kinds and the acquisition of knowledge of their existence, and this same meaning exists with respect to accidents and colors, so they are visible. And if they were not visible, then the existence of substances would be known by sight, but one would not be able to know whether it is moving or stationary, or combined or separated, just as tastes are not known by mere sight due to the lack of their attachment to tastes, according to what God Most High has established as custom, although they are visible to us because they exist, as we will explain after this—if God wills. Then the proof for that is the occurrence of discrimination between kinds of substances by sight, just as it occurs between types of colors. Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla. It addresses two main points: first, establishing that substances (jawahir) are visible; second, beginning the investigation into the attribute (ʿilla) that makes something visible. He mentions al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim as opponents who are forced into inconsistencies. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visibility is caused by being a body or a color.
Correction: Nasafi shows that we see things that are neither bodies nor colors, so these cannot be the cause.

Checkpoint
How many possible attributes does Nasafi list for the cause of visibility?
A) 10
B) 13
C) 7
D) 5

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the cause of visibility cannot be being a body, an accident, a color, or any of the other common attributes, paving the way for the real cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson forces you to examine what you truly consider the basis for knowing God. Are there attributes you casually assume about God (e.g., that He is 'visible' because He is 'existent') without rigorous proof? Will you commit to tracing every claim about God back to its proper cause, as Nasafi does here?
?
Nasafi eliminates superficial attributes and demands a deeper cause. In your own spiritual life, have you mistaken temporary emotional states (like feeling close to God) for the essence of faith? Will you now distinguish between accidental attributes and the true cause of your relationship with God?
?
The method of 'sabr' means surveying all possibilities without laziness. What belief about God or the unseen have you held without ever testing it against revelation and reason? Are you willing to submit that belief to the same thorough examination Nasafi performs here, even if it unsettles you?
